                                 Books by the Author
1. 3D Unconditional Security—graphical overview of principle perspectives regarding unconditional security.
2. 4D Forgiveness—examines four levels of biblical forgiveness and explains how it is possible for God to
    forgive and forget our sin in one stratum while not forgiving or forgetting our sin in another stratum.
3. Breaking the Rocking Horse—introductory book regarding the distinction between having eternal life as a
    gift versus obtaining it as a reward in relation to the outer darkness and the parable of the soils.
4. Carnal Corinth—an affirmation of the categorical existence of carnal Corinthian Christians.
5. Destined for Salvation—evaluates five major models for interpreting 1Thess 5:9-10 and takes the passage
    as promising an unconditional rapture (and thus unconditional security) to all believers in Christ, even to
    those who are morally asleep. However, an incentive for being morally awake is provided by making
    salvation (in the form of millennial and eternal rulership) contingent upon being morally alert.
6. Fallen from Grace but Not from Perfection—an advanced defense of unconditional security which affirms
    that believers can fall from grace but not fall from perfection.
7. Is God a Communist?—by comparing Paul’s teaching of the workmen with Jesus’ parable of the laborers,
    this book refutes communistic views of Heaven which think that Jesus is building heavenly mansions in
    New Jerusalem for all believers regardless of the way they live.
8. John in Living Color—a color-coded translation and reference manual of the Gospel of John that focuses
    on the grammatical and aspectual indications of unconditional security.
9. Like a Tree Planted by the Water—expounds upon the OT background for the outer darkness by examining
    the precursor texts in Psalms, especially Ps 1-2.
10. Logic Tables and Diagrams—illustrations of logic with scriptural implications pertaining primarily to the
    soteriological and misthological arenas.
11. Mere Christianity and Moral Christianity—a more comprehensive discussion regarding perseverance (and
    other performance-related themes) as being needed for rewards in heaven rather than for entrance into
    heaven. This book provides both introductory material and advanced treatments.
12. Monogamous Sex in Heaven!—at the same time in which the present author was reading books by Marrow
    and Kreeft posing that sexual intimacy continues in Heaven, he discovered that his wife had stage-3 breast
    cancer. As a therapy to help him deal with this news, he poured himself into research to see if Marrow and
    Kreeft might be correct, thereby enabling him to believe that no matter what happened, the best was yet to
    come. This is the conclusion and comfort of this book.
13. New Birth by the Sovereign Wind—refutes the Calvinistic interpretation of Jn 3:16 that limits God’s love
    to the elect. An excursus deals with the Calvinistic claim from Jn 3:3 that one must be born again before
    one can see (i.e., believe) the gospel. An additional excursus entertains a possible duality to what it means
    to see the kingdom.
14. Predestination and the Open View—a discussion of predestination that focuses on free will and the book
    of life from an open view.
15. Rewards are Eternal—is a defense of the eternal nature of biblical rewards against the proposition that
    Rev 22:5 indicates that all believers will rule in the eternal state and against the premise that Rev 21:4
    proves that misthological death will no longer exist in the eternal state.
16. Sealed and Secure—a thorough defense of a popular argument for unconditional security that also defends
    the premise of unconditional promises.
17. The Race of Grace—introductory book in which performance-related themes (such as perseverance) are
    considered essential for rewards in heaven. Entrance into heaven, in contrast, is regarded as free. A strong
    defense of unconditional security derived from believers being given to Jesus is a strength of this book.
18. The Outer Darkness—a magnum opus that ties together the author’s arguments for unconditional security
    and conditional rewards.
19. Trichotomy—an advanced holistic dichotomous discussion of trichotomy with an integration of nikology.
20. Woolly Wolves and Woolless Sheep—a rejection of the notion that profession of salvation is proven genuine
    by the way a person lives. This book focuses on the parable of the sheep and the goats and shows the
    inadequacy of conditional security in interpreting this parable.
                      For books by the author, see http://misthology.org/?page_id=35.
                        For additional material, see www.UnconditionalSecurity.org.

Crawford, must be thanked for spending much time acting in an advisory capacity. My wife, Dianne, is deserving

                          Chapter 1. Love and Forgiveness
                                                       Introduction
        A connection between love and forgiveness is natural enough. Forgiveness is an innate expression of God’s
lovingkindness: “Pardon, I pray, the iniquity of this people according to the greatness of Thy lovingkindness, just
as Thou also hast forgiven this people, from Egypt even until now” (Num 14:19). “Thou art a God of forgiveness,
gracious and compassionate, slow to anger, and abounding in lovingkindness” (Neh 9:17). Many simply assume
that God’s love and forgiveness are unconditional or at least free. After all, God loves the whole world and sent His
Son to die for the whole world, even while we were yet in a sinful state (Jn 3:16; 1Jn 2:2; Eph 2:4-5; Rom 5:8). And
our forgiveness is offered freely to us via redemption in Christ (Eph 1:6-7; Col 1:14). Clearly, the unconditional
nature of God’s love is certainly affirmed in such passages, and the freeness of His forgiveness is surely confirmed
for those willing to receive it.
        However, Scripture does not leave the matter there. Even the context of the first verse quoted above, which
is the first explicitly stated connection in Scripture between lovingkindness and love, demonstrates the conditional
nature of God’s lovingkindness and corresponding forgiveness: “The Lord is slow to anger and abundant in
lovingkindness, forgiving iniquity and transgression; but He will by no means clear the guilty, visiting the iniquity
of the fathers on the children to the third and the fourth generations” (Num 14:18). The “but” throws a red flag into
the simplistic notion that God’s lovingkindness and forgiveness are always unconditional. Even a simple verse like
Ps 86:5 gives the impression that God’s lovingkindness and forgiveness can be conditioned on our calling upon
Him: “For Thou, Lord, art good, and ready to forgive, and abundant in lovingkindness to all who call upon Thee.”
In fact, the first verse listed above was just that: a call upon the Lord to pardon the people. Without the call, there
would be no pardon. Sometimes God’s love and forgiveness are not only conditional but also costly, requiring more
than a mere call.
        Understanding the dynamics of the Lord’s love and forgiveness will help us understand the dynamics of our
own love and forgiveness as well. After all, we are expected to love (Jn 15:12; Eph 5:2,25) and forgive (Eph 4:32;
Col 3:13) just as God does. And quite frankly, if we fail to forgive others just as God has forgiven us, then He will
refuse to forgive us (Mt 6:12-15). Nor can we love God if we do not love other believers (1Jn 4:20). The Bible says,
“If anyone does not love the Lord, let him be accursed” (1Cor 16:22). On the other hand, rewards are promised to
those who love the Lord (Jam 1:12; 2:5). Part of loving the Lord is to keep His commandments, and His foremost
commandment is to love Him, and His second is to love others. Thus, great rewards are offered to those who love
their enemies. Obviously, understanding how this dynamic works is critically important.
                                          Conditional Agape-Phileo Love
       In the Ten Commandments, God states that He shows “lovingkindness to thousands, to those who love Me
and keep My commandments” (Ex 20:6; cp. Dt 5:10; 7:9). Daniel likewise surmises: “O Lord, the great and
awesome God, who keeps His covenant and lovingkindness for those who love Him and keep His commandments”
(Dan 9:4). In this verse, the Lord’s lovingkindness is for those who love Him. The Lord’s lovingkindness is
conditioned on our love for Him in such OT passages. In other words, God loves those who love Him. The NT
teaches the same thing. Abiding in Jesus’ agape love in Jn 15:10 involves keeping His commandments: “If you
keep My commandments, you will abide in My love [agape]; just as I have kept My Father’s commandments, and
abide in His love [agape].” This conditional agape love is associated contextually with His conditional phileo love
on a personal level: “You are My friends [philos] if you do whatever I command you” (Jn 15:14). “For the Father
Himself loves [phileo] you, because you have loved [phileo] Me” (Jn 16:27). The reason the Father loves these
believers in this capacity is because these believers love His Son. In the OT, God, speaking via the personification
of His attribute of wisdom, puts the matter succinctly: “I love [agapao] those who love [phileo] me” (Prov 8:17;
LXX). Jesus said much the same thing: “He who has My commandments and keeps them, he it is who loves
[agapao] Me; and he who loves [agapao] Me shall be loved [agapao] by My Father, and I will love [agapao] him,
and will disclose Myself to him” (Jn 14:21). In short, Jesus says that “he who loves Me...I will love him.” This is a
warm affectionate love pictured as abiding in intimate fellowship and friendship with the Lord: “If anyone loves
[agapao] Me, he will keep My word; and My Father will love [agapao] him, and We will come to him, and make
Our abode with him” (Jn 14:23).
       The notion advanced by many, that God’s agape love is unconditional and simply volitional, is mistaken.
Finley is a case in point. Concerning the Law’s injunction to “love [agapao] your neighbor as yourself” (Gal 5:14),
Page 2                                                                              Degrees of Love and Forgiveness
he provides a common explanation:

             The Greek word here for love is agape. This word in the Greek language did not specifically
      mean “God’s love,” or God’s kind of love.” The Greek word agape did not have the qualities of
      affection and emotion connected to it as did other Greek words for love. This word fits best with
      Christian love, as God’s love is not based upon emotion, but comes from an act of the will and an
      attitude of the mind.
             In God’s love, there is selfless care for the welfare of another. Its action in loving or giving is
      not based upon the other’s worthiness or the other’s qualities. Unfortunately, human love usually
      operates based upon an evaluation of the other party’s qualities. Human love has “conditions” on its
      being exercised. But God’s love is unconditional. A line from a gospel song by Dottie Rambo puts this
      well, “He looked beyond my fault and saw my need.” When men were living a sinful lifestyle,
      offending God’s holiness, He demonstrated His own love (agape) towards us by sending Christ to die
      for us (Rom. 5:8). So we also see that God’s love is willing to sacrifice self to an unimaginable extent
      for the sake of others.1 (Emphasis his.)

      Considering the above conditional texts on agapao plus the texts where it is used interchangeably with phileo,
Finley’s argument that God’s love is necessarily unconditional is necessarily false. How about his argument that
God’s love is unemotional? Peck, at www.Bible.org, thinks that genuine love is necessarily devoid of emotion:

             Sacrificial love has transforming power. Genuine love is volitional rather than emotional. The
      person who truly loves does so because of a decision to love. This person has made a commitment to
      be loving whether or not the loving feeling is present. If it is, so much the better; but if it isn’t, the
      commitment to love, the will to love, still stands and is still exercised.
             Conversely, it is not only possible but necessary for a loving person to avoid acting on feelings
      of love. I may meet a woman who strongly attracts me, whom I feel like loving, but because it would
      be destructive to my marriage to have an affair, I will say vocally or in the silence of my heart, “I feel
      like loving you, but I am not going to.” My feelings of love may be unbounded, but my capacity to be
      loving is limited. I therefore must choose the person on whom to focus my capacity to love, toward
      whom to direct my will to love. True love is not a feeling by which we are overwhelmed. It is a
      committed, thoughtful decision.2

       This argument crashes against biblical analysis as well—at least if the term sacrificial love is being assigned
simplistically to all usages of certain Greek words such as agapao and its cognates. Considering the above texts,
where agape is used interchangeably with phileo plus the usage of phileo in relation to God, the sacrificial love
denoted by agapao can take on the affectional connotations denoted by phileo. Peck poses a superficial and
unnecessary thorough-going dichotomy. The Greek language in which the NT was written (Koine Greek) had a
variety of words denoting love, not all of which are found in the NT. Kenneth Wuest discusses four of them.

      1. Agape is presumed to be an “unconditional love” that is “called out of one’s heart by the preciousness of
         the object loved. It is a love of esteem, of evaluation. It has the idea of prizing....not kindled by the merit
         or worth of its object.”3 Really? But he also says, “It is a love that recognizes the worthiness of the object
         loved. Thus, this love consists of the soul’s sense of the value and preciousness of its object, and its
         response to its recognized worth in admiring affection.” Wuest has not only misconstrued some
         significant biblical texts in asserting that agape is unconditional and unmerited, but he has also refuted
         himself. Basically, he is saying that agape is an unconditional love that is conditioned on the preciousness
         of the objected loved. It is not kindled by the worth of the object but is kindled by the worth of the object.4

  Thomas W. Finley, Galatians: A Verse-by-Verse Commentary (n.p., n.d., 2016), 101.

  Kenneth S. Wuest, Wuest’s Word Studies from the Greek New Testament (Grand Rapids, MI: Wm. B. Eerdmans
Chapter 1. Love and Forgiveness                                                                                  Page 3
         He engages in doublespeak. One would be better advised to pay closer attention to Wuest’s own
         acknowledgment: “It should be kept in mind, however, that all the shades of meaning in each word will
         not be applied on each occasion of its use. A study of the context will guide one in ascertaining just what
         distinctive meaning the word will have in each passage.”5 He then goes on to acknowledge that in some
         texts, like Jn 3:19 and 1Jn 2:15, agapao obviously does not have the NT meaning he expects. He attributes
         its meaning to classical Greek rather than the Koine Greek of the NT (p. 116). Then he asserts, “Aside
         from such exceptional cases like these, agapan is to be given its full-orbed New Testament meaning.”
         Nonsense. Doing so commits illegitimate totality transfer in a variety of texts by erroneously importing
         the full semantic range of the word into contexts where only one shade of the word’s meaning is
         intended.6 Peck makes the opposite error. Certainly, Jn 3:16 uses the sacrificial-love shade of meaning
         for agape. But to pigeonhole all genuine love into this mold does great violence to other biblical texts.
         To make a blanket statement like he does that “genuine love is volitional rather than emotional” would
         lead to the horrid conclusion that when the Father and Son love us with phileo love, they are not
         expressing genuine love! Perish the thought! The Father even loves the Son with an agapao love (Jn 3:35)
         and a phileo love (Jn 5:20), and the Father’s agapao love for His Son can be conditional.
      2. Phileo is a word that typically speaks of affectionate, personal intimacy, love-of-liking, such as the
         conditional give-and-take mutual attraction between friends. Jesus had this type of love for Lazarus
         (Jn11:3).7 At least here, Wuest catches on to the conditional and affectual nature of God’s love: “Philein
         is used in John 16:27 where God the Father takes pleasure in and loves those believers who take pleasure
         in His Son and therefore love Him. It is a love of friendly affection.”8 This genuine love is genuinely
         affectional and conditional.
      3. Eros is an erotic, passionate love. In secular writings, it refers to sexual love. In Christian writings, it was
         used in other capacities, though it was not used in the NT.

      Wuest observes the love-of-liking (phileo) occurs between Jesus and Lazarus, but he fails to note the semantic
overlap between Jesus and John: love-of-liking phileo (Jn 20:2); love-of-liking agapao (Jn 13:23; 21:7, 20).
                                       Illustration 1. Semantic Love Overlap




Obviously, agapao and phileo have a semantic overlap in several texts. Although agape can be used of God’s
unconditional-volitional-sacrificial love, it can also be used of God’s conditional-emotional-beneficial love. Verses
like Rom 5:8 give us grounds to conclude that God’s love is unconditional and volitional, rather than conditional
and emotional. However, the extrapolation made by Finley and others in assuming that God’s love is always
unconditional is false, and the deduction that God’s love is necessarily devoid of an emotional/affectional dimension
is likewise a grievous error. Contrary to Finley, God’s love for His children can be very affectionate. Paul testifies:
“For God is my witness, how I long for you all with the affection [splagchnon] of Christ Jesus” (Phil 1:8). Several

Publishing Co, 1984), 112.

  Illegitimate totality transfer is the expression popularized by James Barr to describe the faulty lexical procedure
of importing a word’s full semantic range of meaning from multiple contexts into any given context.

different words are translated as affection in the Bible. Going into a word study or differentiating between such
words as agapao, much less phileo, is unnecessary in the present study. Since the fact of the overlap between agape
and phileo has been established already, we can acknowledge that God’s love for us can be volitional or affectual.
Context is king in determining which meaning is intended. In Phil 1:8, the emotional affection that Paul was
experiencing for these believers was that “of Christ Jesus.” In other words, the Lord Jesus was expressing His own
personal emotional affection for these believers through Paul. Jesus can love others in like manner through us. We
need not pose a thoroughgoing dichotomy between our affection and the Lord’s affection.
        God’s love, like ours, is frequently dynamic rather than static. The flipside of verses like Jn 3:16 (which show
a relative unconditional, unchanging, volitional love) are verses like Jn 14:21-23; 15:14; 16:27 (which clearly show
a conditional, dynamical, affectual love). In the latter group of passages, if we lovingly continue to obey Him, then
we will continue to be objects of His agape love and experience His agape love. Moreover, the more we love Him,
the more He will love us. In turn, the more He loves us, the more we should love Him. As pictured in The Outer
Darkness (TOD), this love is an infinite, dynamic, 3D, love-spiral burrowing infinitely deep down into an infinite,
unconditional-conditional, 4D container.
                                            Illustration 2. 4D Love Spiral




       As explained in TOD, the only point of entry into this multidimensional experience of God’s love is found
in the unconditional plane. The unconditional plane is therefore foundational.9 Picturing the multiple aspects of this
relationship via simpler illustrations will allow us to juxtapose a healthy tension between the foundational nature of
the unconditional-volitional-impersonal-immutable fact of God’s love described by Jesus to Nicodemus in Jn 3 with
the supplemental conditional-affectional-personal-mutable experience of God’s love promised by Jesus to His
disciples in the upper room in Jn 14-16. Doing so is very practical in that it will help us understand God’s love and
our love. Our love, if healthy, will be modeled after God’s love. Understanding how love works will clear up
misunderstandings regarding both His love and ours.
                                        Strata of Love and Forgiveness
      Before exploring a dynamic tension between the conditional and unconditional aspects of God’s love,
however, a simpler model will be implemented that will be helpful in working our way up to a more advanced
discussion. The relationship between love and forgiveness has already been noted above. Forgiveness is a natural
expression of love. In my book, 4D Forgiveness (4DF), forgiveness was shown to exist in four basic strata or planes,
with some texts running through multiple strata. Even though a sin is previously forgiven in one stratum, it can still
remain an issue in another stratum.

                               Illustration 4. Four Planes of Vertical Forgiveness




       Since forgiveness is an expression of love, one naturally would expect that love could be divided into similar
strata or planes. As demonstrated at length in that 4DF discussion, both God’s merciful lovingkindness and His
forgiveness are rewards in Ps 103:1-18, reserved for those who fearfully keep His commandments. Finding love
and forgiveness intertwined at the various strata is not surprising.
Page 6                                                                                 Degrees of Love and Forgiveness
                                 Illustration 5. Four Planes of Vertical Forgiveness




       The epistle of 1John intertwines the discussion of forgiveness and love in various ways. In 1Jn 2:12-14, John
addresses the readers at three levels to describe their present positive experience and then presents a potentially
positive outcome in 1Jn 2:28 at the Bema (i.e., the Judgement Seat of Christ). Addressing them at their lowest level
of maturity in this sequence,” John says, “I am writing to you, little children [teknion], because your sins are
forgiven you for His name’s sake...because you know the Father” [Gr. pater for Daddy]. Addressing them at their
next level of maturity, he explains, “I have written to you, young men, because you are strong, and the word of God
abides in you, and you have overcome the evil one” (1Jn 2:14). Addressing them at their next level of maturity, he
affirms, “I have written to you, fathers, because you know Him who has been from the beginning.”10 Addressing
the potential of their future positive outcome if they overcome, he exhorts them: “And now, little children, abide in
Him, so that when He appears, we may have confidence and not shrink away from Him in shame at His coming.”
       At the lowest level, all their sins have been forgiven soteriologically. Thus, all their sins (past, present, future)
have been forgiven positionally in terms of their relationship with God as their Father. More mature levels of
forgiveness, however, are based conditionally on their interaction with their Father and provide increasing levels of
fellowship with Him. Even in the natural realm, the conditional fellowship that children have with their daddy in
their childhood is foundational to the fellowship that they can have with him as they mature into adults. But they
can understand their father even more when they grow up and have children of their own. These maturing levels of
fellowship are conditioned, of course, on their remaining in fellowship with their father. Children, young adults,
and parents can be in fellowship or out of fellowship with their own parents. Fellowship is conditional at all levels.
The highest level of fellowship that John pictures in this chapter is eschatological. God’s love and forgiveness are
increasing conditional as one moves higher on the vertical scale. If we abide in fellowship with our Father at these
three present levels in the here-and-now, we can be confident that we will be in fellowship with Him in the hereafter.
        As discussed in 4DF, 1Jn 1:7 and 1Jn 1:9 pertain to the familial level of fellowship. They should not be
placed in the soteriological level. They are dealing with experiential fellowship, nor legal relationship. The context
is clearly talking about fellowship: a familial forgiveness pertaining to one’s experience within the family, not to

10
   For the literary purpose of his discussion, John does not use this child—young-man—father sequence but rather
a child—father—young-man sequence to represent our being children (denoting dependency) and fathers (denoting
intimacy) while providing the means for us to be young men (denoting victory). Which comes first, the chicken or
the egg in the cycle of dependency, intimacy, and victory? Typically, it would be dependency, leading to a
dependency-intimacy-victory spiral. Children start off as dependents. As children mature, greater degrees of
intimacy are possible. When children mature, they tend to manifest the nature of their parents, especially if they
have had an intimate child-parent fellowship with them as they were growing up. In the case of the believer, this
means that we will victoriously manifest God’s nature as we mature.
Chapter 1. Love and Forgiveness                                                                                    Page 7
one’s legal position in the family. The passage is not addressing positional forgiveness whereby we legally become
part of the family. Without going into the details provided in 4DF, we can simply observe the repeated reference to
fellowship in the immediate context:
       5
         And this is the message we have heard from Him and announce to you, that God is light, and in Him
       there is no darkness at all. 6 If we say that we have fellowship with Him and yet walk in the darkness,
       we lie and do not practice the truth; 7 but if we walk in the light as He Himself is in the light, we have
       fellowship with one another, and the blood of Jesus His Son cleanses us from all sin. 8 If we say that
       we have no sin, we are deceiving ourselves, and the truth is not in us. 9 If we confess our sins, He is
       faithful and righteous to forgive us our sins and to cleanse us from all unrighteousness. 10 If we say
       that we have not sinned, we make Him a liar, and His word is not in us. (1Jn 1:5-10)

        Building upon that foundational 4DF study herein, though, a possible distinction between the fellowship
stated in v. 7 and described in v. 9 may be entertained. Verse 7 promises that if we walk in the light, we have
fellowship with God. Verse 9 demonstrates that confessing our sins is an expected and necessary part of that walking
in the light, thus a requisite for this type of fellowship. Suppose, however, that a believer rips v. 9 out of its intended
context and treats it as a blanket promise, “some sort of button they push” to be filled with the Spirit and forgiven.
In that case, Anderson surmises, “this type of glib ‘agreement with God probably won’t even restore these people
to fellowship.”11 As Hodges notes, the promise in v. 9 depicts the ongoing forgiveness and fellowship experienced
by believers who are walking in the light and thus do not need repentance, just confession.12 What about a believer
who is walking in the darkness and thus needs to repent but instead just gives glib confession? Does such a believer
experience the forgiveness promised in v. 9? Possibly not.
                                          Little Children Versus Children
       Then again, the extended context might leave some wiggle room. Anderson (pp. 94-97) argues for a
soteriological/positional understanding of the forgiveness in 1Jn 2:12 because of its perfect tense, which is translated
as have been: “I write to you, little children, because your sins have been forgiven.” I used the same argument in
The Outer Darkness (TOD):

              All sins of all believers are forgiven in regards to their being a child of God. Their forgiveness
       in terms of this relationship is complete and already accomplished. This forgiveness is not conditioned
       upon confession of sin. These little newborn children (teknion, cp. teknon in Jn 1:12) stand in a
       perfectly forgiven state. The perfect tense of forgiven in 1Jn 2:12 is used concerning these little
       children. In the next verse (1Jn 2:13), the perfect tense of know is used in relation to their more mature
       knowledge as children (paidion) who are old enough to experience its cognate discipline (paideia; cp.
       Heb 12:7). Unfortunately, the KJV and NKJ fail to note the transition from little children to children.
       John uses this transition to mark a progression from infancy to early stages of Christian maturity in
       their moving from an infantile to a juvenile knowledge of God.
              The use of the perfect tense for forgiveness in v. 12 also has significance regarding the scope of
       forgiveness. Some NT texts describe a forgiveness that is associated with reconciliation (Eph 1:7;
       Col 1:14). The scope of this soteriological/positional forgiveness is even stated to cover all sins in
       Col 2:13. To be sure, many conditional securitists object that such texts only indicate the forgiveness
       of all past sins. Such a restriction would make no sense in the present text, however. John is not
       indicating that only their past sins have been forgiven and that only those past sins remain in a forgiven
       state. What about their present sins which he just mentioned in the previous chapter? Are they not also
       being forgiven? Most certainly. John does not exclude these sins, yet he excludes the present action of
       forgiving in 2:12. John does not imply, “I write to you, little children, because your sins are being
       forgiven.” Instead, he includes the present sins (that are being forgiven via 1:9) in the present state of
       already having been forgiven (in 2:12). The sins being forgiven presently from ch. 1 are subsumed in

        On the other hand, Hodges seems to link this soteriological forgiveness in 1Jn 2:12 with experiential
forgiveness. Soteriologically, he says that the forgiveness they have experienced in 1Jn 2:12 “marks them as little
children of their heavenly Farther (cf. verse 13). This forgiveness has been granted for His name’s sake...Saving
faith is belief in His name.” Experientially, however, he proceeds to say, “But as 1:9 indicates, the forgiveness of
sins is an ongoing need in the believer’s life....They have had the experience of forgiveness” (emphasis his).13 In
GNTC, he surmises regarding 2:12, “They have experienced true forgiveness (cf. 1:5-2:2).” In this summation, he
seems to equate the forgiveness they have experienced in 2:12 with that of 1:15-2:2. Dillow simply equates the two
passages and thus takes both passages as dealing merely with fellowship forgiveness.14 Perhaps the truth lies
somewhere in between, as Hodges seems to suggest in Walking in the Light of God’s Love. I would suggest that to
the degree we walk in the light we have fellowship with God, and this degree of walking fellowship may not be
quite as binary as it first appears in 1John.
                                      Little Children Can Have Fellowship
       The reason for suspecting degrees of fellowship, even for little children who may be struggling to walk in
the light, is because of what John proceeds to say:

           •   I am writing to you, little children (teknion), because your sins are forgiven you for His name’s sake
               (1Jn 2:12).
           •   I have written to you, children (paidion), because you know the Father (1Jn 2:13).
           •   And now, little children (teknion), abide in Him, so that when He appears, we may have confidence
               and not shrink away from Him in shame at His coming (1Jn 2:28).
           •   See how great a love the Father has bestowed upon us, that we should be called children (teknon) of
               God; and such we are. For this reason the world does not know us, because it did not know Him.
               Beloved, now we are children (teknon) of God, and it has not appeared as yet what we shall be. We
               know that, when He appears, we shall be like Him, because we shall see Him just as He is (1Jn 3:1-
               2).

       Even as children (paidion) they know the Father; they have some level of immature fellowship with Him. At
this paidion age, they are naturally subject to His paideia discipline. But in the context of 1Jn 1:5-2:2, John
acknowledges that even as younger little children (teknion) they can have fellowship with God, even though this
fellowship is conditioned on confession while walking in the light: “My little children (teknion), I am writing these
things to you that you may not sin. And if anyone sins, we have an Advocate with the Father, Jesus Christ the
righteous” (1Jn 2:1). Yet little children are known not to walk very well. They do a lot of stumbling as they toddle.
Their walk is not consistent. For this reason, John’s addressing them as little children in his exhortation to abide in
Him (1Jn 2:28), that is in the light (cf. 1Jn 1:5), is completely understandable and indicates that he envisions the
possibility that they may not be walking in the light as consistently as they should. Consequently, their fellowship
with the Father as little children would not be as consistent as one might expect in a simply binary text. Instead of
forgiveness and fellowship being strictly one or the other (soteriological or experiential, light or darkness, all or
nothing), the imagery of little children walking in the light—while having an inconsistent, immature degree of
fellowship with God in a context showing higher gradients of mature fellowship for more mature believers under
the imagery of older children, young men, and fathers—suggests that fledgling, toddling believers can have a very
basic and inconsistent fellowship with their Father, even while they are stumbling in their walk with the Lord and
using 1Jn 1:9 as a push-button text. The expanded context suggests that 1Jn 1:9 can be used in this push-button
capacity by little children but that push-button usage will fail to save them from shame at the Bema.
       After all, John proceeds to affirm that God loves all His children and that all His children will see Him and
be like Him—even those who shrink away in shame from before Him (or are shamefully put away by Him into the

playpen). Accordingly, even after the Bema one would expect some rudimentary degree of intimacy being accorded
to those little children who walk in the darkness. Since they can have some limited degree of fellowship with the
Father after the Bema, we would do well to expect that His little children who are trying to crawl and toddle to Him
in the light with a push-button immaturity are being granted what degree of fellowship is possible to them in that
capacity. John apparently wants us to perceive the common-sense implications of his analogy.
       As parents, we know that we cannot always have fellowship with our children when they are little. When
they are crying, whining, pouting, or rebelling in their self-absorbed immaturity, fellowship with them is impossible.
Then again, many times even when they are in that immature state, we can have fellowship with them—at a level
permissible to their limited degree of development and obedience. As they grow out of that immaturity and become
responsible young adults, an increasingly mature degree of unbroken fellowship with them is possible. John seems
to envision a similar potential maturation and communion with his illustration. If we abide in Him in childlike
dependency, we can increasing experience maturing intimacy with Him and stand in mature victory before Him.

                                                Immature Fellowship
        If this understanding of John’s analogy and context is correct, which seems reasonable, then a text like
1Jn 2:12 can be seen from two different vantage points, which is a common literary device in 1John and especially
in this context (cp. 1Jn 2:7-8).15 From a pastoral perspective, it allows us to assure immature believers that they can

15
  The validity of multiple vantage points has been demonstrated repeatedly in my writings. Paul even affirms that
Jews can be viewed simultaneously as enemies or beloved, depending on one’s vantage point: “From the standpoint
of the gospel they are enemies for your sake, but from the standpoint of God's choice they are beloved for the sake
of the fathers” (Rom 11:28). Even a passage like Rom 8:29-39 might be seen from two different vantage points. In
TOD, both soteriological and misthological vantage points were entertained and intertwined. Paul might have
simultaneously intended both vantage points. Romans 8:1 shows that there is no condemnation in the present for
Page 10                                                                              Degrees of Love and Forgiveness
have some degree of fellowship with God even during their struggling to walk in the light. John does not expect us
to misconstrue him as saying that immature believers must reach the victorious experience which he describes by
the analogy of young men before believers can have fellowship with God. For he assures us that even as little
children we have advocacy with Daddy. A gradient vantage point is therefore prudent, at least as a secondary
vantage point. While the segregated view is primary, which sharply distinguishes the distinct maturity levels of
believers into compartmentalized categories, a secondary vantage point is permissible and advisable that
acknowledges that the transition from one category to another is not so sharp so as to disallow any meaningful
transition from one to the other or application of one to the other. Those immature believers struggling to mature
can be assured of some degree of fellowship with God even though their ability to walk in the light, as demanded
by 1Jn 1:7, is seriously impaired and their implementation of 1Jn 1:9 is much too push-button for the taste of more
mature believers. Even those believers who are still crawling and toddling in their walk with the Lord can have
some degree of fellowship with their Daddy when they are not doing childish things that make fellowship
impossible, such as: whining and wallowing in self-pity, pouting and rebelling in self-assertion, or refusing
assistance in self-reliance.
                            Illustration 7. Static versus Dynamic Forgiveness and Love




those believers who are walking victoriously, which harmonizes very well with the end of the chapter if the ending
is taken misthologically. Juxtaposed between the beginning and end of the chapter is an extended discussion,
affirming both soteriological and misthological realities. Rom 8:17 stands out as a mountain peak in that interim
terrain, juxtaposing being children soteriologically with potentially co-ruling sons misthologically. Compare the
co-glorying (sundoxazo) in Rom 8:17 with the co-morphing (summorphos) in Rom 8:29. Read from this perspective,
Rom 8:29-30 is an expression of the high degree of confidence that victorious believers can have in the present for
being victorious at the Bema. They can face the Bema with a confident expectation of hearing, “Well done.” Paul
says that believers can presently know that they will do well at the Bema (Col 3:24). Wilkin cites other examples
and concludes, with appropriate caveats, “We can be confident that if Christ returned today, we would rule with
Him.” Bob Wilkin, “Can We Be Sure in This Life That We Will Rule with Christ?” Available at

       Not all alleged vantage points are valid, however. The vantage point of a neurotic cannot be accepted as a
valid vantage point. They think their vantage point is a true reflection of reality when in fact it is not. Then they try
to change their vantage point into an advantage for the sake of the argument in which they are engaged. As discussed
subsequently herein, a neurotic vantage point must be rejected. Nevertheless, I write the present section to give
hope to neurotics that even if they are very immature in their Christian walk, they can have some degree of intimacy
and fellowship with God, even if they are using 1Jn 1:9 in a push-button mentality.
Chapter 1. Love and Forgiveness                                                                                  Page 11
       Another way we might try picturing this fuzzy static-dynamic maturity and fellowship is to combine the
gradient graph with an X-Y-axis graph to give some rough idea of what one’s progression might potentially look
like. One shortcoming with the gradient graphs is that the distinction between a gift and reward is blurred. The static
section represents free aspects while the dynamic material represents costly aspects. To assist conceptually with
keeping the free aspects distinct from the costly aspects, a dashed threshold with a zero beside it on the vertical axis
has been added to clarify that a gift cannot be a reward or vice versa. Therefore, at the point in time when one is
born again and becomes a little child by saving faith, the horizontal clock starts ticking and the fluctuating line starts
moving, going up and down on the graph. The vertical axis, however, has a range of zero—from the horizontal axis
to the threshold—to remind us that the static underlying blue relationship is unaffected. The binary distinction
between a gift and reward must be maintained. However, in terms of the terminology and analogy that John
employees, we must leave room for some exegetical gradation. John uses the term children in both capacities—to
refer both to our relational new birth and to our experiential fellowship. Conceptually, also, we must admit that
although a child is not a young adult, one does not graduate from childhood to adulthood in an instant. The process
takes time.16
                                                  Duality of Carnality
       However, because of the binary dichotomy between carnality and spirituality, some teach that a new believer
can become instantly spiritual and conversely that a spiritual believer can become instantly carnal.17 Such teachers
even think that when spiritual Christians sin, they immediately become carnal Christians. Wilkin rightly objects:
“Just as it takes time to move from carnality to spirituality, so it takes time to make the return trip.” “Backsliding
may indeed be a slippery slope. However, it is a slope and not a cliff. People rarely, if ever, move immediately from
spirituality to carnality.” “If we think that we become carnal the moment we sin, then we become like a human yo-
yo, bouncing back and forth between carnality and spirituality.” “Maturity is not all or nothing. Once a person has
become spiritual, he has plenty of room for growth.” Inversely, one can “in fact fall into carnality,” but it takes “an
extended period of time” of being out of fellowship with God.18




16
   As depicted in the graph in Illustration 7, love can be: static and/or dynamic; unconditional and/or conditional;
volitional and/or affectional; personal and/or impersonal; factual and/or experiential; immutable and/or mutable.
Some of these items are mutually exclusive. For example, God’s love cannot be both immutable and mutable in the
same way at the same time. However, if God’s love is understood as simultaneously operating at different levels or
different planes, then we can easily see how His love may be both immutable and mutable at the same time—
because it is not being immutable and mutable in the same way. This is not merely theoretical or theological. This
is how love works in real life. I may love one person impersonally and another person personally. Or I may love
the same person personally on one occasion or impersonally on another occasion. Or I may love the same person
both personally and impersonally on the same occasion in different ways. For instance, one action I perform for my
wife, Dianne, may be because of my affection for her (like buying her a dozen roses on Valentines); the other action
I perform for her may be because of or despite my affection or lack of affection for her at that moment or pertaining
to that particular activity (some items on a honey-do list might fall into this category at times). In fact, sometimes
my volitional love for her will be at odds with my affectional love for her, such as when I do or say something for
her best interest that I know she will not like. Regardless of all such variations of my love for Dianne, my love for
her as Dianne is immutable. I will always love her in terms of our relationship even though the affectional fellowship
is subject to mutable up-and-down moments.

                   Illustration 8. Binary and Fuzzy Distinction Between Carnal and Spiritual




       Paul uses the analogy of passing from drinking milk in childhood to eating meat in adulthood to make a
binary distinction between carnal and spiritual (1Cor 3:1-4; cp. Heb 5:12-14), yet Wilkin makes an appropriate
fuzzy distinction. Both vantage points are legitimate. A child is not an adult. A carnal Christian is not a spiritual
Christian. Still, passing from one to the other is a process. Additionally, Wilkin notes yet another type of Christian:
“There are at least three kinds of Christians. The three kinds are the spiritual (mature), the carnal (baby Christians),
and those who are out of fellowship with God, sometimes called backsliders (like the prodigal son in Luke 15:11-
32)” (emphasis his).19 Three words thus describe these three types of Christians:

     1. Carnal = immature
     2. Spiritual = mature
     3. Prodigal = backslider/apostate

Wilkin describes this latter type as a non-church goer:

              The third type of Christian is one who is likely not in church. He has backslidden and is in the
       spiritual far country (Luke 15:11-24). That person is not really carnal in the way in which Paul uses
       the term. Paul reserves that term for people who are in church, walking with the Lord, but who have
       yet to advance beyond baby status. While the backslider is indeed acting and thinking like unbelievers,
       he is not walking with Christ and hence it is better to think of him as a different type of Christian.20

The prodigal son wanted to strike out on his own and live independently of his father, even while he was living with
his father. He was a prodigal in attitude even before he was a prodigal in action.


19
     Bob Wilkin, “Is There More Than One Type of Christian?” (October, 2017). Available at:
https://faithalone.org/blog/is-there-more-than-one-type-of-christian. Accessed 4/10/2018.

                   Illustration 9. Binary and Fuzzy Distinction Between Prodigal and Spiritual




        The spiritual Christian walks in obedient dependency upon Christ, acting in cooperation and dependency on
the Spirit. The prodigal Christian, in contrast, walks independently of Christ and eventually becomes carnal as a
result. Certainly, the prodigal and spiritual Christian can be pictured as polar opposites. Thus, the top vantage point
in the illustration is correct. By his action, the prodigal falls out of spirituality, back into stark carnality. Still, this
type of carnality is different than that which marked his childhood, when he lived at home with his father as a child.
This subsequent fall from maturity into carnality is a rebellious carnality.21 This prodigal is a rebel. Nevertheless,
his rebellion starts with an attitude before it manifests itself as an action. In a movie of the prodigal son I had for
my children when they were young, it depicted the prodigal son hounding his father day-after-day to divide the
inheritance until his father finally relented and did so.22 The prodigal son did not wake up that morning a prodigal
son. The process took time. The fuzzy potential for prodigal attitudes is pictured in the lower part of the illustration.
Such attitudes have the potential to lead to the prodigal actions pictured in the upper part of the illustration. Even
without factoring in the older son, the parable encourages both vantage points—a duality pertaining to carnality.
                                              Walk Determines Delight
        Was the prodigal son’s father delighted in his son while his son was in his rebellious state? Obviously not. Is
God delighted in His children when they are in a state of full-grown adult rebellion? No. Is God delighted in His
children when they are in a state of infantile or juvenile rebellion? Again, no. Regardless of our state or stage of
development, God is not delighted in our rebellion. Nor do earthly parents delight in their children when their
children are being rebellious. The fact that John conditions our fellowship with God on our walk with Him in the
light is intuitively what we should expect. Fellowship is conditional, in both the natural and supernatural realms.
        Jesus had fellowship with sinners, to be sure. But it was with repentant sinners. We have all heard the adage:

satisfaction or delights with a cold indifference to God. They could even become atheists in their physical adulthood.

                                 23
Love the sinner but hate the sin. Thus, we are not necessarily surprised to hear Scripture say, “The sacrifice of the
wicked is an abomination to the Lord, but the prayer of the upright is His delight” (Prov 15:8). As this pertains to
the present discussion, we can conclude that God detests the supposedly godly service of ungodly rebellious
believers. But does God always make a differentiation between His emotions toward the actions of the sin versus
the person of the sinner or between the actions of the walker and the walker himself? No. Let’s hear from God on
the matter. He says, “The perverse in heart are an abomination to the Lord, but the blameless in their walk are His
delight” (Prov 11:20). God finds the former group of people to be abominable and the latter group to be delightful.
As this pertains to the present discussion, we may conclude that God dislikes those believers who walk in the
darkness but likes those believers who walk in the light. Does this mean that God does not love His children who
walk in the darkness? No. God can love the sinner while hating the sin because love does not equal like.
                                                 Love versus Like
       Again, common sense should lead us to this conclusion. God can love us without liking us. Similarly, we can
love others whom we do not like. Imagine the nonsense that we would derive if we concluded otherwise and
translated Jn 3:16 as, “For God so liked the world, that He gave His only begotten Son, that whoever believes in
Him should not perish, but have eternal life.” Did God like the sinful world or the sinners in the world? Is that why
He sent Jesus to die for us because He liked us so much? Hardly! God decided to send His Son to die for us despite
His dislike of us in our present condition. Calvinists might respond that God loved us because of our future
condition. There is some truth to such a claim. God sent His Son to die for us knowing that He would get a wife out
of doing so and that His Son would get a Bride. Nevertheless, Calvinists are wrong when they pose that God did
not love the entire world in Jn 3:16, just the world of the elect. Not true. God loved the entire world and sent His
Son to die for the entire world (1Jn 2:2).
                                         Love Because of Future Value
     Notwithstanding, part of the reason the Father sent the Son to die for us is because of the future Bride His
Son would acquire as a result. We catch something of this rationale in Paul’s exhortation:
      25
        Husbands, love your wives, just as Christ also loved the church and gave Himself up for her; 26 that
      He might sanctify her, having cleansed her by the washing of water with the word, 27 that He might
      present to Himself the church in all her glory, having no spot or wrinkle or any such thing; but that she
      should be holy and blameless. 28 So husbands ought also to love their own wives as their own bodies.
      He who loves his own wife loves himself. (Eph 5:25-28)

        Christ did not love the church in this manner solely because of her present condition but because of what His
love could do for her and to her. He loved the church in this manner because of Her resultant future holy and
blameless condition. His love for Her would make Her valuable.24 Even the OT anticipates His love for us in this
manner: “Forget your people and your father’s house. Then the King will desire your beauty” (Ps 45:10-11).25 His
loving us in this manner is due to our response to Him in this passage. Part of His love for humanity was because
of the loving intimacy that He would experience with part of humanity in return. He loved us to make us lovable.
Still, this does not mean that He does not love the unlovable. This affirmation is not illogical because love can be
experienced and expressed at different levels. He loves His Bride at one level. His love for those who are not His
Bride is at a lower level. There is nothing unnatural or sinful about that. Far from it! We fully expect husbands to
love their own wives more than they love their neighbor’s wives. They better! Husbands are rightfully expected to
have a more affectionate love for their own wives than for other women. Likewise, we should not be surprised to
learn that God loves bridal believers more than nonbridal believers. Yes, in some ways, God loves some believers
more than other believers.

23
   Adams says this “is a very misleading slogan that has absolutely no biblical basis....Answer me this: What does

Forgiven to Forgiving (Wheaton, IL: Victor Books, 1989), 113. Adams is partially correct. Surely, Jn 3:16 suggests
that God can love the sinner without loving the sin. Yet, God might simultaneously love and hate the sinner while
exclusively hating the sinner’s sin. God’s attitude toward sin is uniform. His attitude toward the sinner is varied and
complex.

                                     God’s Conditional Loving Affection
       Let’s consider the above passages along with some additional passages that explicitly demonstrate that God
loves some people more affectionately than other people. This love is conditional:

           •   “Yet on your fathers did the Lord set His affection to love them, and He chose their descendants after
               them, even you above all peoples, as it is this day” (Dt 10:15).
           •   “The perverse in heart are an abomination to the Lord, but the blameless in their walk are His delight”
               (Prov 11:20).
           •   “Lying lips are an abomination to the Lord, but those who deal faithfully are His delight”
               (Prov 12:22).
           •   The sacrifice of the wicked is an abomination to the Lord, but the prayer of the upright is His delight.
               The way of the wicked is an abomination to the Lord, but He loves him who pursues righteousness.
               (Prov 15:8-9)
           •   When you make a vow to God, do not be late in paying it, for He takes no delight in fools. Pay what
               you vow! (Ecc 5:4)
           •   It will no longer be said to you, “Forsaken,” nor to your land will it any longer be said, “Desolate”;
               but you will be called, “My delight is in her,” and your land, “Married”; for the Lord delights in you,
               and to Him your land will be married. (Is 62:4)
           •   “Oh that there were one among you who would shut the gates, that you might not uselessly kindle

               from you. (Mal 1:10)
           •   They are not pleasing to God (1Thess 2:15).
           •   But My righteous one shall live by faith; and if he shrinks back, My soul has no pleasure in him.
               (Heb 10:38)
           •   By faith Enoch was taken up so that he should not see death; and he was not found because God took
               him up; for he obtained the witness that before his being taken up he was pleasing to God. (Heb 11:5)

        Some people pose that God loves everyone equally. They attempt to justify this conclusion by deducing that
God loves everyone unconditionally. They further attempt to bolster their argument by saying that God’s love is
volitional rather than emotional. Such arguments tend to be half-truths. The argument that God’s love is totally void
of emotion is obviously false, as attested by such passages as those above. Obviously, an emotional demission to
God’s love is present in such instances. On the other hand, the love expressed in Jn 3:16 certainly displays a high
volitional element. God did not send His Son to die for us because it made Him feel good. He volitionally decided
to act in our best interest despite His affectional love for His Son at the time He was being crucified. Love frequently
must act against its feelings. God’s volitional love for the whole of humanity took volitional action against His Son.
Understanding this tension in God’s love can assist in understanding the tension in our love. We were created in
God’s image. And our love, if healthy, will reflect His. For this reason, we are expected to love others in the same
way God loves us. We need a healthy understanding of how His love operates to understand how healthy love, His
love, should operate in our lives. We need to understand both His love for us and His love expressed through us to
others.
Page 16                                                                             Degrees of Love and Forgiveness
                             Illustration 10. Binary-Fuzzy, Affectual-Volitional Scale




        One way to picture the volitional-affectional interplay would be with a scaled graph. The more you move
horizontally to the right on the scale, the more your love is volitional and the less it is affectional. Conversely, the
more you move to the left, the more it is affectional and the less it is volitional. At one end of the spectrum, our
love on occasion may be purely volitional. At the other end of the spectrum, it may be purely affectional. To
illustrate, suppose you and your spouse had a spat, and you were not feeling affectionate toward your spouse;
nevertheless, you decided to do something nice for your spouse simply because you love your spouse. Your
volitional decision to act on behalf of your spouse could be devoid of any emotional affection at the time. Still, it
would be an act of love—volitional love. On another occasion when your spouse is away, you are thinking warm
loving thoughts about your spouse and feel very affectionate about your spouse, but you might take no volitional
action to express those loving thoughts. Even so, those loving thoughts and feelings would be a form of love—
affectional love. Later that night if those affectional feelings carry over into an act of making love, then your
lovemaking would be both: affectional-volitional love. Even common sense would tell us that this is how love
normally works. Biblically, we can deduce that God’s love works similarly.
                                                       Love Less
       Some parents love some of their children more than their other children. Isaac and Rebekah are a case in
point: “Now Isaac loved Esau, because he had a taste for game; but Rebekah loved Jacob” (Gen 25:28). We should
not conclude that Isaac had no love for Jacob or that Rebekah had no love for Esau. Surely, both parents loved both
their children. Yet they had their favorites. Each parent loved one of their children less than the other child. Is this
wrong? Not necessarily. God also loves some believers less than other believers, as is naturally deduced from this
study. One popular explanation for Rom 9:13 asserts just that, when God says, “Jacob I loved, but Esau I hated,”
God loved Esau less than He did Jacob. This interpretation is good insofar as it goes.26 Probably, an emotional
element is involved. God liked Esau less than He did Jacob. Certainly, a volitional element is involved. God
volitionally rejected Esau for the birthright which normally would have been his. For that matter, because of the
circumstances, even Isaac volitionally had to reject Esau for the inheritance (Gen 27:34-38; Heb 12:6-17). Even
though Isaac affectionately loved Esau more than he did Jacob, Isaac volitionally had to love Jacob more than he
did Esau on that occasion. Isaac’s volitional love for Esau had to be circumscribed by the circumstances.
       So even though the categories for static and dynamic love in Illustration 7 picture volitional love as
unconditional and affectual love as conditional, we must acknowledge that in real life things are not so easily
compartmentalized. Both parents and God can have a greater volitional and affectual love for some of their children
than they do others. God will volitionally disinherit His disobedient children from inheriting the kingdom (1Cor 6:9-
10; Gal 5:21; Eph 5:5). Further, He emotionally takes no pleasure in them (Heb 10:38). God can be quite displeased,
emotionally, with some of His kids. They can be estranged from Him in terms of fellowship. God is justified in
loving some of His children more than others. He is not unjust to have greater affectual fellowship with those of
His children who affectionately love Him.27 Some carnal believers pout and complain when they do not sense an
26
 I do not limit Esau nationally to Edom. See my book, Salvation, when released.

  Daniel is a well-known example demonstrating that God has degrees of love (Dt 10:11,19). The heavenly
