intimate affection with God. However, God is not under any obligation to shower them with intimate affection when
they are living in disobedience. Fellowship and intimacy with God can be hindered by carnal immaturity or mature
apostasy. Likewise, earthly parents can understandably have less intimacy with some of their children than others.
When some of their children are estranging themselves from their parents, obviously parents are not under moral
obligation to love all their children equally.
                                              Fluctuating Affection
       Notwithstanding, the ongoing affectual love experienced by mature believers with the Lord must not be
confused with never-ending affectual feelings. My wife, Dianne, and I have a forty-five-minute drive to church.
While driving to church with Dianne the morning I was writing this part of the book, it occurred to me that I was
not experiencing any affectionate feelings toward her or the Lord, but it did not bother me in the least. I simply had
my mind on other matters. Regardless of my feelings, or lack of them, I knew that I was affectionately loved by
both Dianne and the Lord and that I love them both affectionately. When I got to church, tears of affection for the
Lord ran down my face as we sang a congregational song of praise. During another song, I affectionately reached
over to hold Dianne’s hand during a song that I knew would be particularly meaningful to her. During the service,
I was experiencing affectual feelings for both the Lord and Dianne. Next Sunday I might not have particularly
strong affectual feelings for either of them. It does not matter. I don’t care.28 I am not concerned about these
fluctuations in my affection. My affirmation and knowledge of the affectual love that exists between us are not
dependent upon the natural ebb and flow of emotions.29 Mature believers live by faith, not by keeping their eyes
fixed on the bouncing ball of emotions. Love, even affectual love, cannot be tied inseparably to emotions.30
                                            Affectual-Volitional Tension
       Understanding the affectual-volitional nature of love can help us understand certain problematic texts. For
instance, how do we explain the fact that Father was pleased to kill His Son? “But the Lord [the Father] was pleased
[chaphets] to crush Him [His Son], putting Him to grief; if He would render Himself as a guilt offering, He will see
His offspring, He will prolong His days, and the good pleasure [chaphets] of the Lord will prosper in His hand”
(Is 53:10). The Hebrew word for pleased here is chaphets and means to delight in. Does this mean that the Father
delighted in killing His Son? Was the Father sadistic? Did the Father find emotional pleasure in killing His Son?
Intuitively, we sense there is more to the story. The LXX translates it as boulomai which means to be willing. The
ESV appropriately translates this part of the verse as: “It was the will of the Lord to crush him.” The Father was
willing to sacrifice His Son, but He did not enjoy it. Understandably, many conclude that the love expressed in
Jn 3:16 was purely volitional love, not emotional love. The Father did not send His Son to die for us because He
liked us or because He liked killing His Son. Even though God took this action, in part, because of the affectual
love He would have for bridal believers in the future after that event, His love for humanity as a whole at that point

loved like everybody else.” Thinking that Daniel was merely loved like everyone else would be utter nonsense. A
special preciousness was attributed to God’s feelings about Daniel and to God’s esteem of Daniel. John is a well-
known NT example who is repeatedly called the disciple whom Jesus loved (Jn 13:23; 20:2; 21:7,20—using both
phileo and agapao). The Lord liked and loved John. In turn, John had a greater degree of intimacy with the Lord
and was one of the inner core of disciples with more privileges in terms of intimate access to the Lord. Undoubtedly,
the Lord had His favorites. But the Lord was not guilty of favoritism or partiality. In Dt 10:15, the Lord singles
Israel out for His “delighted affection,” yet the text immediately affirms that He “does not show partiality”
(Dt 10:17) because the Lord’s affection and volitional action are not based on a person’s “social or economic
standing” (NICOT); the Lord is not guilty of social injustice or lack of love, even for the alien not covered by His
special selection (Dt 10:18).
28
   See related I-don’t-care example in Rejection Induced Neurosis, 63.
29
   In this experience, I am just describing the natural ebb and flow of emotions. Dobson is even more pointed when
he acknowledges that there are times when good, loving parents do not necessarily like some of their own children
and times when loving spouses in a good marriage do not necessarily like each other for a little while. His solution
is tough love: love that hangs tough, love that is volitionally committed to the relationship. James Dobson, Parenting
Isn’t for Cowards: Dealing Confidently with the Frustrations of Child-Rearing (Waco, TX: Word Books, 1987),
204.
30
   Intimacy with the Lord is a sense of closeness, care, and communion (CCC). This CCC intimacy transcends our
earthly circumstances and emotions in that CCC is a tranquility that comes from deeper within, is grounded upon
Scripture, and rises above our temporal turmoil and emotional disparity.
Page 18                                                                              Degrees of Love and Forgiveness
in time was purely volitional. The Father was volitionally pleased, not emotionally pleased, by the excruciating
crucifixion of Jesus.31 This is a valid point, a valid vantage point, though even this binary volitional-versus-affectual
explanation may not be the whole story.
       How about the use of chaphets and boulomai in the second half of Is 53:10? While translations like the NAS
err in translating both occurrences in terms of emotional pleasure, the ESV probably errs in translating both
occurrences in terms of volitional decision: “The will of the Lord shall prosper in his hand.” This latter part of the
verse probably includes both elements of love—emotional and volitional. An appropriate translation would be:
“The joyful will of the Lord shall prosper in his hand.” However, one may object that this imports an interpretation
into the translation. Even so, it is a reasonable understanding of the text.
       How about rape? Is God delighted over rape? Some say so. They put two verses together from Deuteronomy
and think that they have rightly put two and two together to conclude that in some cases God is delighted by rape.
Here are the two verses:

     •   “You shall betroth a wife, but another man shall violate her [i.e., rape her]; you shall build a house, but you
         shall not live in it; you shall plant a vineyard, but you shall not use its fruit” (Dt 28:30).
     •   “And it shall come about that as the Lord delighted [sus] over you to prosper you, and multiply you, so the
         Lord will delight [sus] over you to make you perish and destroy you; and you shall be torn from the land
         where you are entering to possess it” (Dt 28:63).

Their logic runs like this:

     1. The Lord was delighted to punish them.
     2. Part of that punishment included rape.
     3. Therefore, the Lord delighted to punish them with rape.

The Hebrew word used here is sus and is normally translated as delight. However, WBC states that a better
understanding in this context is volitional—that sus, “translated here as ‘took delight,’ carries a volitional sense.”
Just as an exclusively volitional interpretation of chaphets is best for the first part of Is 53:10, but does not
necessarily exclude an emotional satisfaction enjoined with volitional decision in the second part of the verse, so
the exclusively volitional use of sus in the second part of Dt 28:63 should not preclude us from seeing an inclusively
volitional-emotional usage of sus in the first part of the verse. Just because a word is used with its full dual meaning
in one context or even twice within the same verse does not prove that it must be understood with its full dual
meaning in every context or even when used twice within the same verse. That the emotional aspect is missing in
the second usage of sus is deductively clarified elsewhere:

     •   “Do I have any pleasure in the death of the wicked,” declares the Lord God, “rather than that he should turn
         from his ways and live? (Eze 18:23)
     •   “For I have no pleasure in the death of anyone who dies,” declares the Lord God. “Therefore, repent and
         live.” (Eze 18:32)
     •   “Say to them, ‘As I live!’ declares the Lord God, ‘I take no pleasure in the death of the wicked, but rather
         that the wicked turn from his way and live. Turn back, turn back from your evil ways! Why then will you
         die, O house of Israel?’ (Eze 33:11)

       Although chaphets is used in these verses, the point is clearly applicable to sus. From the use of chaphets in
these verses, we must conclude that, at least in some aspect, God does not take emotional pleasure in the death of
the wicked. When seen from this vantage point, God does not take emotional delight in making the wicked perish
or in destroying them. Consequently, we may safely deduce logically from this vantage point that the second use of
sus in Dt 28:63 cannot be referring to His emotional delight in causing the wicked to perish or in destroying them.

31
  Concerning the first occurrence, WBC comments that it “is a term used of sovereigns. Their pleasure is equivalent
to their will in a matter.” That volitional interpretation is a good explanation for the first occurrence, but it should
not be thought that the term necessarily excludes emotional satisfaction enjoined with volitional decision in every
context.
Chapter 1. Love and Forgiveness                                                                                   Page 19
At the very least, translators and interpreters must show discretion to know whether a passage talking about God’s
delight, pleasure, or love is describing His response from a perspective that is: (1) emotional or (2) volitional or
(3) emotional-volitional. Sometimes the Lord’s response will be binary: emotional or volitional. Sometimes it will
be fuzzy: emotional and volitional. Sometimes it may even depend upon whether we are looking at God’s emotional
or volitional or emotional-volitional response from one vantage point versus another vantage point versus multiple
vantage points. The same is true of our responses to others as well. Sometimes our love for others will be emotional;
sometimes it will simply be volitional; at other times it will be emotional-volitional. Returning now to Finley’s
statement quoted earlier in this chapter, he is wrong to limit God’s love to the unconditional dimension:

                 In God’s love, there is selfless care for the welfare of another. Its action in loving or giving is
         not based upon the other’s worthiness or the other’s qualities. Unfortunately, human love usually
         operates based upon an evaluation of the other party’s qualities. Human love has “conditions” on its
         being exercised. But God’s love is unconditional. A line from a gospel song by Dottie Rambo puts this
         well, “He looked beyond my fault and saw my need.” When men were living a sinful lifestyle,
         offending God’s holiness, He demonstrated His own love (agape) towards us by sending Christ to die
         for us (Rom. 5:8). So we also see that God’s love is willing to sacrifice self to an unimaginable extent
         for the sake of others. In 1 Cor. 13 we see an expanded Biblical definition of how God’s love operates
         in its care towards others.32 (Emphasis his.)
                                Illustration 11. Binary-Fuzzy, Affectual-Volitional Love




       Yet we have seen that God’s love is frequently conditional. Moreover, while it is true that God looked beyond
our fault and saw our need, that does not tell the whole story. The rest of the story is that He looked beyond our
need and fulfilled His need, a need for a Bride. If that statement is too strong for the sensitivity of some readers,
then at least acknowledge that God looked beyond our need and saw a Bride. In any event, the above illustration is
yet one more way to show the binary-fuzzy, affectual-volitional nature of love—both God’s love and ours. We have
seen that God loves us in this manner from a variety of texts as discerned by their contexts.
                                           Our Conditional Affectional Love
       We have also deduced that, since we are created in God’s image and expected to be like Him and exhorted
to love others as He loves us, we should love others in this same manner as well: with a binary-fuzzy, affectual-
volitional love that is sensitive to its context. A few texts demonstrating the appropriate conditional nature of our
affection will suffice for illustration:

             •   And his affection abounds all the more toward you, as he remembers the obedience of you all, how
                 you received him with fear and trembling. (2Cor 7:15)
             •   3
                   I thank my God in all my remembrance of you, 4 always offering prayer with joy in my every prayer
                 for you all, 5 in view of your participation in the gospel from the first day until now. 6 For I am
                 confident of this very thing, that He who began a good work in you will perfect it until the day of
32
     Finley, Galatians, 101.
Page 20                                                                              Degrees of Love and Forgiveness
                              7
               Christ Jesus. For it is only right for me to feel this way about you all, because I have you in my
               heart, since both in my imprisonment and in the defense and confirmation of the gospel, you all are
               partakers of grace with me. 8 For God is my witness, how I long for you all with the affection of
               Christ Jesus. (Phil 1:3-8)
           •   10
                  I appeal to you for my child, whom I have begotten in my imprisonment, Onesimus, 11 who
               formerly was useless to you, but now is useful both to you and to me. 12 And I have sent him back
               to you in person, that is, sending my very heart, 13 whom I wished to keep with me, that in your behalf
               he might minister to me in my imprisonment for the gospel. (Phlm 1:10-13)

       In the first passage, Paul explains that Titus’ abounding affection for this group of believers is appropriated
because of Titus’ reflection on their obedient response. His affection for them is tied to their obedience. This type
of affectional response on his part is thus justified by the response on their part. His affection is conditional. In the
second passage, the affectional feeling which Paul has for this group of believers is due to their participation and
partnership in promoting the gospel with him. His affection is not only conditional but equated with Christ’s
affection. The affection which Christ was expressing through Paul for them on this occasion was because of their
evangelistic participation. In the third passage, Paul’s loving affection is attributed dually both to Onesimus’
conversion and to Onesimus’ usefulness as a minister. However, his usefulness is the foremost consideration. Paul
has some affection for Onesimus because of his conversion; therefore, this aspect of his affection is unconditional
in that it is not conditioned on Onesimus’ performance. Notwithstanding, the primary source of Paul’s affection for
Onesimus is attributed to Onesimus’ personal ministry to Paul, consequently conditioned on Onesimus’
performance. From these three passages, we can conclude that conditional affection is considered a natural,
reasonable, reciprocal response that is equatable with the supernatural affection of Christ in that it is one of the ways
that Christ loves us and loves others through us.
                                                        Conclusion
        To recap the discussion in this chapter, God’s agape love for us can be conditional or unconditional, also
volitional or affectual. The context must be consulted to ascertain what aspect of this love is intended. Arguments
that agape love has nothing to do with the value of the object loved need to be reevaluated since statements made
in that regard are contradictory. Part of the problem is that agape love overlaps the phileo love, and the significance
of this overlap is not always freely admitted. On the other hand, God’s phileo love is more easily and consistently
perceived as tending to be conditional and affectual and thus reciprocal. Although eros love is not found in the NT
writings, it certainly has a fitting place in Christian writings.
        Forgiveness and love both occur in four primary strata. The foundational stratum is soteriological. Love, at
this level, tends to be primarily volitional. Progressive growth and maturity can likewise be perceived at four
primary strata with an additional stratum for regressive growth and maturity:

        (1) Little children—(teknion) are younger children who have a basic soteriological and push-button familial
forgiveness;
        (2) Children—(teknon/paidion) are older children who know their Daddy and are more mature in their
confession and response to His discipline;
        (3) Young men—are victorious in their fighting the good fight and have higher degrees of forgiveness;
        (4) Fathers—have a mature knowledge of the Father and the highest degree of forgiveness. Relationship is
stable; fellowship is conditional.
        (5) Backsliders—are retrogressive in their growth and thus must be distinguished from the four growing
categories of Christians. These backsliders can either be formerly mature or chronically infantile or juvenile. The
fellowship forgiveness environed in 1Jn 1:9 is for believers who are walking in the light. They need only to confess
their sins as the occasion arises to continue unhindered in their walk in the light. For those believers not walking in
the light, confession may not suffice, repentance would also be an expected requirement.

       Love cannot necessarily be equated with like. God loves/likes some believers more than He loves/likes other
believers. God’s love and our love moves naturally in volitional-affectual tension: sometimes being one, sometimes
the other, many times both. Affectional love is greater for those in fellowship, but even then, it comes and goes
naturally in ebbs. Love, whether it is divine or human, may be: (1) emotional or (2) volitional or (3) emotional-
volitional. Greater affectional love for some rather than for others is completely natural, desirable, and justifiable.
                           Chapter 2. Practical Application
                                                   Introduction
      The proceeding chapter was written to lay the foundation to meet a pressing need. Dianne and I were
counseling a young lady, whom I will call Sue (not her real name), who had experienced a lot of emotional
estrangement from her earthly father and therefore was having extreme difficulty in feeling loved by her Heavenly
Father. She also projected this lack of feeling loved toward us, sometimes claiming that we did not love her. She
acknowledged during one session that the problem was probably on her part. On various previous occasions, I had
counseled her to read a couple of books by Charles Solomon: Handbook to Happiness and The Ins and Out of
Rejection. The second book is particularly applicable to Sue’s situation. But the first book lays the foundation.
                                             Solution to Neurosis
       How do you change your feelings? In the first book, Solomon appropriately answers:

               It goes without saying that we cannot change our emotions, but in Christ we are not called upon
       to change our emotions. This also is God’s job, and if we try to do it, it is just so much more futile self-
       effort. When we experience the Lord Jesus Christ as our life, he changes our emotions as a result of
       renewing of our minds. All of these changes do not occur instantaneously. The transformation is
       effected by the Holy Spirit as we begin to place all of our confidence in “the instruments,” the infallible
       Word of God. We must realize with our minds that God’s promises are true and then determine with
       our wills to act upon them. God will honor his Word and keep his promises to us, even if we feel that
       they are untrue or that he won’t keep them (2 Tim. 2:13). As we consistently will to place our
       confidence in our infallible “instruments,” the Word of God, our feelings will increasingly come in
       line with the facts. When this happens, we no longer fit the label of neurotic, and our feelings are
       consistent with our faith in the facts.33 (Emphasis his.)

       The neurosis Solomon is discussing in this context is a mental-emotional disparity in which one’s feelings
are inconsistent with the facts. This is not a mental illness but a moral issue,34 in which the neurotic distorts the facts
to agree with his or her emotions:

               As Christians, we often face the same dilemma. If we are well adjusted psychologically, there
       is not as much disparity between our feelings and the facts. However, people who feel inferior,
       insecure, inadequate, and unacceptable are continuously faced with a set of emotions that are at
       variance with the facts or reality. Therefore, they must distort the facts to agree with their
       feelings (commonly known as a neurosis) or employ some defense mechanism to permit them to
       cope with an untenable situation. Someone has said that the neurotic builds castles in the air, the
       psychotic lives in them, and the psychiatrist collects the rent!
               People may feel inferior when they really aren’t. In fact, most who have extreme inferiority
       feelings excel in many areas. Because they feel inferior, there are many things they will not attempt,
       or they withdraw from certain situations—not because they cannot perform but because they feel they
       cannot. Naturally, this causes much frustration and internal conflict, and these are compounded
       because these people know with their mind that they can perform, but their emotions cry out, No!35
       (Italics his.)

      As will be explained in a subsequent chapter dedicated to Solomon’s counseling approach,36 Sue suffers from
this mental-emotional neurotic disparity because of her being caught in a rejection syndrome.37 Her perception of

reality is distorted by the emotional grid through which she perceives reality. Solomon’s solution to such neurosis,
in a nutshell, is to change one’s behavior by volitionally doing what is necessary to trust and obey the Scripture and
the Spirit. The Spirit enables us to do so and renders transformation as a result. Long story short, our Spirit-enabled
change in behavior will result in a change in our emotions.
                                               Purpose of Present Book
       The purpose of the present book is not to reproduce the details provided by Solomon to resolve this mental-
emotional neurotic disparity. He is the expert in this area of nikology,38 and I will defer to his excellent treatment.
Nevertheless, despite my deferral, Sue insisted that my wife and I were being unloving and rejecting if we did not
render personal counseling in this area. And she put a six-page letter in our mailbox, Friday (1/11/2019),39 telling
us that we were being uncaring since she had a right to come to us for counseling. Therefore, reluctantly, I have
been drawn into having to respond to her letter and other related comments Sue has made. Notwithstanding, rather
than rehash the solution already provided by Solomon, I will focus on a particular aspect of it and integrate it with
my specialty—misthology.40
       After Sue sent us this letter, she and her husband, whom I will call Sam (not his real name), said that they
would like to come over to our house and discuss it with us. Dianne, wanting to be a gracious hostess, suggested
that they come over to our house the following Saturday evening (1/19/2019) to discuss it, and then we could have
dinner. So Dianne prepared a dinner. I had a foreboding that it would not turn out well. Given Sue’s propensity for
emotions, I anticipated that she might get upset and not want to eat. Oh well, I guess Dianne was hoping for the
best, and I did not want to discourage her from her optimistic outlook. Unfortunately, it did not go as she had hoped.
       When Sue and Sam came, we gave Sue the opportunity to express herself more fully. She acknowledged that
the problem might be with her but then would say nothing else about the situation. Neither Sam, Dianne, or I could
get her to say anything further. So eventually I proceeded to express my views on the situation in a rather long
monologue. I believed it was time for a tough love approach and told Sue that I believed she was correct in
perceiving the problem to be with her perception and stressed that her feelings of rejection were not in accord with
the facts. Not surprisingly, she did not like what I had to say, and she said so quite emotionally. At times she would
intellectually acknowledge that I was right about her emotions not being factually correct and then turn right around
and vehemently deny it emotionally. With her thinking being controlled by her emotions, she could not think with
logical consistency.
       Solomon has given a helpful outline above for bringing our emotions “in line with the facts.” The facts are
that Sue’s Heavenly Father loves her even when she does not feel loved. And Dianne and I love Sue even when she
is angrily accusing us of not loving her. However, both God’s love for her and our love for her need not be one of
emotional intimacy when she is rejecting the means to such intimacy. As we have seen, biblical intimacy, both on
God’s part and ours, is frequently conditional, especially as one moves higher up the graph in Illustration 7. At
times, during Sue’s emotional outbursts against me and Dianne, my love for Sue was reduced to volitional love.
Likewise, God’s love for us may be reduced to volitional love when we are rejecting His counsel and disbelieving
the facts that He has recorded for us in Scripture. Such is the nature of volitional love, as seen in the previous
chapter. Volitional love can continue, endure, and tolerate (1Cor 13:7), even though affectual love does not.
                                                    Feeling Loved
       Volitional love is still love, though. Sue was angry when I pointed this out. She did not like my affirming my
love for her when I did not link it ball-and-chain to my affectual feelings about her or to her feelings about me. If
she did not feel loved, she did not want me telling her that I loved her. But the fact of the matter is that it is not
necessarily my responsibility to make Sue feel loved. My responsibility is to love her, not necessarily to make her
feel loved. Dianne and I had tried the empathetic approach with Sue on previous occasions, and it had not worked.
Of course, my expectations from reading Solomon’s book was that it would not work; still, the empathetic approach
was worth a try and was a reasonable place to start. Still, it was not a reasonable place to stay. My tolerating Sue’s
self-absorbed emotional outbursts that Saturday evening—while she was insisting that we focus on her emotions
rather than on the biblical truths that address those issues—was a volitionally tolerating love on my part, as Paul

says, “showing tolerance for one another in love” (Eph 4:2; NAU). However, I did not show emotional empathy to
her pity party on this occasion. She would have to look somewhere else for empathy for her pity party since I was
no longer going to be party to it. Dianne and I, particularly Dianne, had tried a more emotionally empathetic loving
approach to Sue previously. Certainly, the level of Dianne’s affectual empathizing love for Sue is much greater than
mine. Dianne is a more empathetic listener than I am. She responds more on the affectual scale than I do. My

to both approaches.
       Dianne’s empathetic approach is subject to rather wide vertical emotional variations depending on how angry
and unloving Sue might be at a particular moment. At times Sue’s volatile emotional reaction would be projected
upon or reflected in Dianne’s response as they had texted in the past. Such is the nature of affectual love. Those
who reject God’s love and then experience an offended emotional reaction from God, then turn around and accuse
God of being unloving. Sue was certainly charging both God and Dianne with being unloving.
                                                Emotional Blackmail
        On the other hand, my volitional approach can seem unkind and uncaring because it focuses on applying a
biblical solution rather than indulging a counselee’s protracted indulgency in self-pity. Sue wrote to us and said that
in coming to us she was seeking to have her problems “remedied by the faith and encouragement of older and wiser
Christians.” Yet, when she came, she would then turn around and express contempt for our counseling. She just
wanted us to continue listening empathetically to her emotional problems. She wanted to make her emotional issues
the focus of attention as she had done in the past. Preferring a more biblically orientation and thus a solution, I
stressed the need to walk by faith rather than by emotions, to apply biblical truth to the underlying problems that
were causing the emotional issues.41 She was thoroughly resistant to the proposal, being more driven by her
emotional frame of mind. She criticized Dianne for telling her that she just needed to trust the Lord, and she
criticized me for telling her that she needed to learn how to trust the Lord.
        I had provided material that would be helpful in that capacity. But she craved pity under the guise of asking
for empathy. She retorted to me that her emotions should be the focus of our discussion rather than biblical truth by
citing the adage: People do not care how much you know until they first know how much you care. I was not going
to be emotionally pressured by her playing that game and allow her to hijack the conversation, placing it in bondage
to her demanding empathy for her self-pity. She was trying to take the conversation hostage to her emotions by
claiming that I was cruel and did not care because I did not care to hear any more of her self-indulgent sorrow. Her
emotional blackmail would not work on me, though, because I was not willing to pay the ransom price she
demanded, namely, prove that I cared on her terms, by apologizing for not continuing to listen to her sob story. As
Dobson notes, not only children but adults can use emotional blackmail to manipulate others in their power games.

   At the suggestion of a pastor friend who counseled me to practice the faith-rest drill in response to some other
issue that I was going through last year, I recently had read The Faith-Rest Life by Thieme. I found the material in

comfort those who are in any affliction with the comfort with which we ourselves are comforted by God” (2Cor 1:4).
However, I pointed out that the book would have limited usefulness unless this practice was implemented in a

Of the twelve manipulative devises Dobson cites, emotional blackmail was at the top of the list with this definition:

      Emotional Blackmail: “Do what I want or I’ll get very angry and go all to pieces.”42 (Emphasis his.)

       Sue was doing just that—performing emotional blackmail, going all to pieces and getting angry because I
would not allow her to use her emotions to control the conversation. At times she had expressed frustration because
she did not feel that God cares about her. I assured her that He did because the Bible tells us so (1Pet 5:7).43 Doubting
God’s care for us is irrational. Doubting God’s care for us is to make Him out to be a liar. If she doubts that God
cares about her, then it is no surprise that she doubts that I care about her. Would she be justified in demanding that
God make her feel that He cares about her before she would be expected to listen to Him? Of course not! If God
had failed to make her feel cared for, then was I supposed to succeed where God had failed before I could expect
her to listen to me? Such line of argument is foolish. God’s expectation that we believe that He cares for us is not
dependent upon our feeling that He cares for us. God has not placed His expectations for us at the mercy of how we
feel. Likewise, I was not going to base my expectation that Sue believes that I care on her feeling that I care. We
know that God cares because of what He has written. The question is whether we believe what He has written.
Likewise, I have proven that I care by what I have written (and by other means as well). Discipline and love are not
antithetical. Rather discipline is a form of love and proof that one cares (Heb 12:6-8).
       Granted, the you-must-first-prove-that-you-care adage has some truth to it. Still, it can certainly be overdone.
And she had overdone it. God had proved that He cared, and I had proved that I cared. Yet she did not believe either
of us. Some people play this you-don’t-care card to make an excuse for their I-will-not-listen-to-anything-you-have-
to-say attitude. She wanted to play her you-don’t-care card to force the conversation back to her emotions. Since I
was not willing to play her game, she was not willing to listen to anything I had to say. I did not care to play her
game, but that did not mean that I did not care. Not surprising, she refused to acknowledge the difference.
       During a much earlier counseling session, when Sue had come over to apologize for some similar unkind
things that she had said, Dianne and I had forgiven her. Dianne had given her a hug, and I had given her an
affectionate pat on the cheek. She felt loved because we listened, yet again, to her recount her emotional
drama/trauma, and then we gave her a hug and a pat. Fine. Emotional empathy has its time and place. As another
adage goes, based on Ecc 3:1-8, “There is a time and place for everything.” Yet the point of that passage is that,
just as there is a time and place for one thing, so there is a time and place for its opposite.44 And we had come to
the time and place for the opposite of empathetic listening. There is a time to move beyond empathetically enduring
a rehash of self-pity to an exhortation which requires that appropriate action be taken. Sue attributed this insistence
for action on my part to my being cold and uncaring. Actually, my insistence on appropriate action is a volitional
form of caring as opposed to the previous affectual approach. In response, she now said that I was cold and uncaring
toward her because I hurt her feelings.
       My pity for Sue’s self-pity was conditioned on her taking appropriate action. Likewise, God’s pity for His
children is conditional (Ps 103:13): “As a father pities his children, so the Lord pities those who fear Him” (NKJ).
This word for pity can also be rendered as compassion: “Just as a father has compassion on his children, so the Lord
has compassion on those who fear Him” (NAS). Scripture shows us that God’s fatherly pity and compassion are
more reasonably expected toward those of His children who “fear Him,” that is, who treat Him with respect.

manner.” H. Norman Wright, Helping People in Crisis and Stress (San Bernardino, Here’s Life Publishers, 1989),

changing her emotions. If she would conform her behavior to reality, then her emotions could eventually follow
suit and conform to reality. On the other hand, if she would not conform her behavior to reality, then there was no
reason to think that her emotions would conform to reality.
Chapter 2. Practical Application                                                                                Page 25
Disrespectful children have no right to expect this level of pity or compassion for their emotional dissatisfaction.
Moreover, the Scripture expects us to see a clear connection between our Heavenly Father’s pity and compassion
for us as His children and that of earthly fathers for their children. A natural correlation between the two is expected.
Sue had shown Dianne and I a considerable degree of disrespect. Following the example of the Heavenly Father,
my pity for her self-pity was consequently terminated.
       Citing Solomon’s second book, The Ins and Out of Rejection, I shared with Sue that my trying to make her
feel that I cared before she would listen to me was not the solution. Because of her rejection syndrome, she was
emotionally programmed to see me and Dianne as unloving and uncaring regardless of what we said or did. If we
caved in to her demand—that we not seek a cure until she felt loved—then she might never reach the point of
seeking a cure because she might never feel loved—until the cure was applied first. In other words, cure first, feeling
loved second. Biblically, we are to speak the truth in agape love (Eph 4:15). But as seen herein, agape love can be
either affectional, volitional, or a combination thereof. Knowing from Sue’s background and Solomon’s analysis
that Sue would probably never have a long-term feeling that we cared affectionately for her until a cure was first
applied volitionally, I moved from the passive role of the affectionate listener to the role of a volitional active
counselor—a role she was adamant that I not play. But how do you change the feelings of emotional estrangement
by someone who is caught in rejection syndrome? Not by continuing to listen to their self-pity and feelings but by
addressing the underlying cause of that self-pity by appropriating the death of self. The death of self must be
accepted for self-pity to die. Thus, I continued to stress Solomon’s books as a critical component of the solution.
       She then attributed her lack of willingness to listen to such exhortations for applying biblical truth to my
body posture. We were sitting in our living room, so I was leaning back comfortably on my couch, relaxed, with
my feet on my footstool. She was angry and upset, evidently mad because I did not share her emotional state or
distress. I insisted that we should apply Scripture and logic to the problem rather than indulge the problem by
focusing on emotions. Certainly, emotions have their place. But we need to learn the place that emotions have from
Scripture, put them in their proper place, and understand how they operate, which is one reason for explaining the
role of affection herein. My responsibility is to speak the truth in love, not to make someone who is rejecting that
love feel loved.
       Likewise, God has taken the initiative upon Himself to love us. The fact is God loves us. If we do not feel
loved, we cannot turn around and blame God for our not feeling loved. Our feelings are not corresponding to the
factual reality that we are loved. We are the ones to blame for not feeling loved by God. If we accept the truth of
God’s word, then we would have to conclude that we are loved. And if we dwell upon that factual truth by the
power of the Spirit, then our emotions will eventually start to come around to being consistent with that factual
reality. As noted above, Solomon said, “Our feelings will increasingly come in line with the facts.” Likewise, if
Sue were to dwell upon the factual reality of the love which Dianne and I have for her rather than on the emotional
distress generated by her rejection syndrome, then eventually her feelings would increasingly accord with the fact
that we do love her. However, nothing we can say or do, nor tone of our voice, is going to be able to effect a long-
term change about how Sue feels until she takes the appropriate action. Thus, I urged her to move from a focus on
her emotions to a focus on applying biblical truth.
                                                   Hurt Feelings
       Sue and Sam came back to our home the next evening, Sunday (1/20/2019). Sue wanted me to apologize for
having hurt her feelings. I would not. I explained why, yet again, but added that I would explain the matter more
thoroughly in the material I was writing. She left upset again that night also. Throughout the next day, Monday
(1/21/2019), she kept sending me texts, telling me what an unloving, uncaring, and cruel person that I am. To this
string of texts, I simply kept responding that I would respond in the material I was writing. (At this juncture it was
becoming increasingly clear that my response would have to be book length, which led to the present book.) She
then came back to our home that Monday evening, expecting me to apologize to her after she had spent the day
sending me those texts. I responded rationally.45 I was not going to apologize for doing something wrong when I

45
   Ad hominem arguments are directed against the person rather than against what the person is saying. Such
arguments are frowned upon both in debating and counseling. Debates and counseling confrontations should seek,
as much as possible, to target the person’s behavior (words and actions) rather than the person him or herself. Sue
tends to make no differentiation between attacking the person and the person’s behavior. For her to say that my
behavior hurt her feelings would be a true statement. Then, since we are to speak the truth in love (Eph 4:15), an
appropriate question would be: “Were my statements false or unloving?” Just because my statements hurt her
Page 26                                                                               Degrees of Love and Forgiveness
had done nothing wrong. She at least wanted me to say that I was sorry for having hurt her feelings. I refused. I was
not the least bit sorry for hurting her feelings. Sometimes the truth hurts. And sometimes speaking the truth in love
necessitates hurting feelings. My responsibility is to speak the truth in love, not necessarily to avoid hurting her
feelings. Caring about a person under normal circumstances would entail caring about how they feel and trying not
to hurt that person’s feelings. However, these were not normal circumstances. When Sue gains victory over her
emotional volitivity, then sensitivity to her hurt feelings would be a more reasonable expectation. But showing
sensitivity to her hypersensitivity would be ill-advised. Insisting on sensitivity to her hypersensitivity when her
hypersensitivity is being used to justify sinful activity would be a pathetic sympathy, as will be demonstrated
subsequently herein via the counseling advice of others.
        When neurotics irrationally use their feelings as a tool to control a conversation, then caring about how they
feel would be irrational, playing into their irrationality. Enough is enough. It was time for Sue’s accusations against
me and my wife, that we were uncaring and unloving, to come to an end. She may not be able to control her emotions
at this juncture, which is perhaps understandable, but she should be held responsible for her actions. Sinful behavior
must not be allowed to continue unchecked. The focus should be on behavior rather than emotions. Emotions are
changed by a focus on behavior rather than by a focus on emotions. A degree of counseling tolerance might be
allowed for counselees working to overcome their sinful emotions while working on overcoming their sinful
actions. But a line must be drawn when counselees use their sinful emotions to avoid taking the appropriate steps
for overcoming their sinful actions. Put more simply, people should not have to walk on eggshells around her.
        When someone is going to use their emotions and hurt feelings as a shield to avoid taking the appropriate
action, those hurt feelings must not be allowed to stand as an obstacle to appropriate action. Hurting feelings is
frequently necessary to apply discipline effectively. The relationship is not hard to follow logically.
                                       Illustration 12. Hurt Feelings Syllogism
      1. Hurting feelings is frequently a necessary component of discipline.
      2. Discipline is frequently a necessary component of love.
      3. Therefore, hurting feelings is frequently a necessary component of love.

       My expectation is that this book will hurt her feelings. Even so, hurting her feelings is not my goal; rather,
my goal is for her to face the truth—for both our sakes. My overriding concern at this point is the truth, not feelings.
Hurt feelings are collateral damage, not the target. She is the one who owes me an apology (confession of guilt),
who needs to repent of her behavior. I have not sinned against her, but she has sinned against me. Her ranting about
me being cruel is actually cruel behavior on her part. My reason for writing this is not because my feelings got hurt
or are hurt. Realistically, in the short term, it would hurt less to just call a truce, put the matter to bed, and let the
sleeping dogs lie—until they wake up again in the future and start biting that is! My present feelings are not the
driving force for not allowing those dogs to sleep. Pretending that her protracted rants did not happen would be
pretentious. It was not the first time that she has acted in this manner, and it will not be the last. It has been a
reoccurring pattern. Without a confession on her part, the Bema is probably not going to be a pleasant experience
for her on its part. My holding her accountable now may help her deal with Bema accountability then. Plus, it may
help her deal more responsibly with future emotions. She may not be able to control her emotions directly, but she
has direct control over her actions. She should be able to control what she says even when she gets emotional.
                                      Introspection and Self-Examination
       Additionally, another reason for writing this book is that I am certainly not beyond being accountable at the
Bema. I will be held accountable for what I have said and what I have written. Making this interaction and
understanding public subjects me to peer-to-peer review. My readers, including pastors and counselors, can make
their own judgment. If they believe that I am wrong, I am sure that some of them will do the loving thing and
confront me with my error. After all, I do not want to persist in error to the Bema. We should address whatever
errors we can now, before the Bema occurs, so that things can go well for us when we stand before the Bema.


feelings does not prove that they were false or unloving. But for her to say that I am an uncaring person is an ad
hominem argument, directed against me as a person. Since it is false, it is a transgression against me. Tolerating
such behavior would be a sin on my part, a transgression against her by allowing her to continue unchecked in sinful
behavior. In certain circumstances, God does not forgive us for tolerating sinful behavior, as will be explained
herein.
Chapter 2. Practical Application                                                                                  Page 27
Nevertheless, presently and generally, it is a very small thing to me that Sue, or even other pastors or counselors,
may find me in the wrong if they cannot do so without clear scriptural or logical explanation. I am much more
inclined to be subject to the Scripture and Spirit than to mere human opinions. We must be objectively in accord
with the Scripture and subjectively in accord with the Spirit (not necessarily in accord with the judgment of others)
to do well at the Lord’s judgment at the Bema (1Cor 4:3-4). Finley gives an excellent explanation as to why this is
so:

         Paul writes in I Cor. 4:3, “1 do not even examine myself.” Although the Greek word for “examine” in
         1 Cor. 4:3 is a different word from the one used in Gal. 6:4 it still carries the thought of discovering
         what the quality is of the thing examined. The word in 1 Cor. 4:3 is used of a judge or a court official
         investigating a person for the purpose of judgment.
                 So how do we reconcile the idea in Gal. 6:4 of one examining his own work and Paul’s statement
         that he does not examine himself in 1 Cor. 4:3? I believe that there is reason to believe that the answer
         is found in the passage in 1 Cor. 4. There Paul states: “For 1 am conscious of nothing against
         myself.” Instead of examining himself, or being examined by other humans or a human court, Paul
         seems to be indicating that he is fully open to God to be examined by Him - “the one who examines
         me [note the present tense] is the Lord” (see 1 Cor. 4:3-5). Paul learned how to walk in the light of
         God’s presence and God’s word, which gave him an indication where he was right before God and
         where he had a shortcoming or made a misstep.
                 When we humbly seek God with our whole heart and are willing to hear His voice, then we can
         receive His evaluation and correction. As we seek Him and spend time in His word, then the Holy
         Spirit enlightens us concerning our actions, our attitudes, our motives, etc. The light of His
         presence and His speaking from His word approves or disapproves our actions (Heb. 4:12-13; 1
         Jn. 1:5, 7). We might say that placing ourselves humbly under God’s enlightenment is similar to
         placing ore into the fire, bringing forth the impurities and the qualities of a metal by such testing.
                 The examination in this verse certainly does not involve introspection. Introspection
         involves the practice of one looking within himself to examine his thought processes, emotions
         and motives. It is self-analysis. Some sincere believers have mistakenly employed introspection
         as a supposed path to purifying their walk before God. However, introspection is a false and
         dangerous way for a Christian to determine the quality of his actions or his spiritual condition.
         In conclusion, to “examine our work” means that we place ourselves willingly and humbly under
         God’s enlightenment, open to hear His approval or correction.
                 When 6:4 states that “then he will have a reason for boasting in himself alone.” it is not
         suggesting that we should be prideful or boastful about what “we have done.” The word for boasting
         in this verse really means rejoicing or exulting. There is a wonderful rejoicing and thankfulness within
         when we find that Christ has worked through our lives (Rom 15:17-18). Such rejoicing should only be
         in regard to ourselves and what God has done in us, never looking to compare ourselves to others.
                 Verse five is again connected with the whole train of thought, as shown by the connecting “for”
         at the beginning of the verse. It gives the reason that each person should focus on examining his own
         work: “For each one will bear his own load.” This verse points to the responsibility of each believer
         before God as respects his conduct and the carrying out of his ministry. Importantly, the “load” in
         verse five should not be confused with the “burdens” in verse two. Two different Greek words are
         used here. The word in verse two places emphasis on the weight of the burden - there it means it is too
         heavy for one person. The word in verse five was commonly used of a soldier’s pack and thus it is
         fitting to speak of each one bearing his own load. Verse five is speaking of the responsibility of each
         believer to live uprightly and to carry out his assigned ministry before God. This thought certainly has
         in view the coming Judgment Seat of Christ where each believer will stand before Christ and be judged
         for the things he has done. It is for this sobering reason that each Christian should take care to “examine
         his own work.”46 (Italics his.)

      Thinking along the same lines from a well-known OT text in Ps 139:23-14, Solomon focuses on the fallacy
of morbid introspection:
46
     Finley, Galatians, 112-113.
Page 28                                                                             Degrees of Love and Forgiveness

             Almost without exception, many Christians, and those who fit the description of neurotic
      behavior, spend much time looking inward. They may be searching for sin or evaluating this or
      that behavior or trying to justify their existence. Those who feel exceedingly inferior and worthless
      may spend most of their time contemplating their hopeless plight. The result? The more they look
      inward, the more miserable they become, in spite of the fact that they may view their self-
      searching and self-condemnation as proof of their sincerity or as a kind of punishment by which
      they get even with themselves. They may conclude that God surely will reward them for their
      futile attempts to probe and purge and somehow make themselves acceptable in his sight.
             This searching process is not only interminable and depressing to the believer, but it is
      also repugnant to God. The psalmist highlighted the fact that it is Gods job to do the searching:
      “Search me, O God, and know my heart: try me, and know my thoughts: And see if there be any
      wicked way in me, and lead me in the way everlasting’1 (Ps. 139:23-24). The psalmist is praying,
      “You search me; you know me; you try me.” But all too many Christians, by their actions, are
      trying to do the searching themselves.
             At best, our searching can turn up only garbage, and sorting garbage is a most depressing
      avocation. In addition, if we could ever complete the interminable task, we would wind up with
      neatly sorted piles of garbage, but we would still be blind to the source of all the garbage—self.
      When God turns on the searchlight, he is much more interested in dealing with the source than
      with the results. And he is always ready to “forgive us our sins, and to cleanse us from all
      unrighteousness” (1 John 1:9). But it must grieve him to see us plagued by the same besetting sins year
      after year. He always abhors sin and desires to rid us of it by putting it under the blood of Christ. Self—
      the flesh—is also the object of his abhorrence, and he yearns to rid us of its control and dominion
      through the cross of Christ.
             When an overly introspective person ceases or attempts to cease this useless and harmful
      indulgence of self, he usually feels guilty for falling down on God’s job! And yet he always fails
      at it because he is playing God. That is a sure way to fail.47 (Italics his.)

       Spiritual maturity entails growing up by giving up on such morbid introspections. I made that transition many
years ago. Spirit-analysis is far superior to self-analysis and is appropriate to those who walk by the Spirit rather
than by focusing on self. And as Paul shows, David’s words have applicability to others who want to take the place
of God and do the searching. The words, “You search me; You know me; You try me,” are to be addressed to God,
not to self or to others. Paul was not going to let himself or the Corinthians be his God by performing an examination
role that belongs to God. Nor was I going to allow Sue to play the role of God in her examination of me.
        Paul’s attitude in 1Cor 4:3-4 is consistent with what he wrote in Rom 8:1: “There is therefore now no
condemnation to those who are in Christ Jesus, who do not walk according to the flesh, but according to the Spirit”
(NKJ). Those who walk according to the Spirit will not be condemned by the Spirit and thus should not listen to
the condemnation imposed by fleshly self or fleshly people. As Solomon further explains, those who walk according
to the flesh are subject to condemnation by the flesh: “Though the scripture enjoins us to love our neighbors as we
love ourselves, many Christians never recognize this activity as sin. If such Christians loved their neighbors as they
do themselves, their neighbors would be in mortal danger!” “Upon the inquiry as to whether she had confessed the
sin of self-hatred, she replied that she had never recognized it as sin. She was shown that she had no more right,
scripturally, to hate herself than to hate others. Confession and learning who she was in Christ resulted in
freedom.”48

             Another source of false guilt is that a person can’t forgive himself even though God’s Word has

verse and what Paul wrote and concludes: “So here too John is telling his readers that they can safely entrust
themselves to the judgment of God who knows all about them, and consequently they can set their hearts at rest,
even though they feel self-condemned.” The emotional conflict can be put to rest. Self-condemnation can be quieted
when it is not in accord with the Spirit and Scripture. After all, what is it that God knows in the context of the
Pauline and Johannine corpus that can silence self-condemnation?

     •    For Paul, those believers who walk by the Spirit are free from all condemnation, including self-

     •    For John, those believers who walk in the light are cleansed from all sin and shame, even at the Bema.

Those who are led by the Spirit via the Scripture do not have to second guess themselves or listen to condemnation
that is not in accord with the Spirit or Scripture. By trusting themselves to the Spirit’s present judgment as
administered by the Scripture, they can be confident both now in the present and in the future. They can walk in
spiritual stability rather in emotional instability. We can have both nikological and misthological assurance.
                                                Place of Emotions
       The blue book, as it is popularly known, is a small booklet-sized tract used by Campus Crusade for Christ.50
I used this blue-covered tract for witnessing back in my high school days and still take that basic approach to my
Christian walk, though I have modified and further developed some of its explanations over the years and in my
writings. Finley likewise provides a suitable modification after explaining the typical approach to emotions:

                 Now, putting aside for the present the matter of seeking the Lord, let us look at the factors of
         the word, faith, and the Holy Spirit. Many teachers have illustrated the reality of the believer’s
         experience as a train of “fact - faith - feelings.” These teachers point out that the engine (the first car
         in the train) is the word of God (fact), faith is the middle car, and feelings is the last car. The word of
         God is absolute truth, so we take what it says as “Fact.” In this “train” of Christian experience, the
         word must come first, then our faith in that word, and, as a result, feelings may follow. If we look for
         some type of feelings first we will fail. “Feelings” would be emotions or sensations. Actually, what
         should be the final car of this train of Christian experience is not “feelings,” but experience of the Holy
         Spirit. We must place our faith, our full trust, in what the word says, and then experience will follow.
         Actually, when the stage of experience is realized, it is just the operation of the Holy Spirit in our
         living.
                 So, we must start with our trust in what the word declares, and this means we must disregard
         our feelings, or what seems to be our experience contrary to the word. The Bible says: “for we
         walk by faith, not by sight” (2 Cor. 5:7, KJV). The term “walk” here means to have our conduct, our
         experience of living. We must not put our belief in what appears to be our situation....
         ....Feelings, or sensations, will follow from time to time as we use our faith. Feelings, however, are

    Finley’s entire discussion and book are highly recommended. But this quick excerpt will have to suffice to
provide the basic principle. To be consistent with the biblical terminology, wherein Paul described the operation
and experience of the Holy Spirit within us as being filled with the Spirit (Eph 5:18), I have used the term filling in
my adaptation of Finley’s explanation. My FFFF train thus has four cars, all beginning with the letter “F.”
                                              Illustration 13. FFFF Train




The first three cars are necessary. (1) The first car represents our Spirit-enabled understanding and appropriation of
the facts presented by God’s word. (2) The second car is our Spirit-enabled faith in God’s word. (3) The third car
is the part of the train where we experience the filling of the Spirit: the part of the train that we walk in (representing
our Spirit-enabled walk in Gal 5:16,25) and that we live in (representing our Spirit-enabled living in Gal 5:25).
Sometimes this passenger car is practically empty; sometimes it is practically full. As we become more spiritually
mature, it will become more consistently full. (4) The fourth car, the caboose representing our feelings, is optional.
Sometimes it is present; sometimes it is not. When feelings are present, sometimes the feelings being expressed in
the caboose will be consistent with what is going on in the passenger car; sometimes they will not. Mixed feelings
can happen on a train, and it certainly happens in real life. We must live by our filling, not by our feelings. Do not
confuse filling with feelings! This is spirituality 101—healthy spirituality. Frankly, it is also common sense—
healthy psychology. Emotions have their place, as seen in the train illustration. But emotions must be placed within
the context of biblical analysis, thus made last and least for determining the course of our lives and our assurance
of reality.52 Sometimes, as Finley says, we must discard those feelings, adopting an I-don’t-care attitude toward
them.

51
   Thomas W. Finley, The Victorious Christian Life: A Lesson Series for the Earnest Christian, revised edition (n.p.,
2013), 37-38. For free online version, see http://seekersofchrist.org/VCL/victory.html. Accessed 1-23-2019.
52
   The feelings of the soul can run counter to the feelings of the spirit, to the feelings of the Spirit, and to the filling
of the Spirit. Therefore, we must live by faith in the Spirit rather than by our subjective soulish feelings. Likewise,
our soulish reasoning can be counter to the Spirit’s scriptural leading. Thus, the objective focus of our faith must
be in the facts of Scripture. Human logic is not meant to be an end unto itself (which would be circular, soulish
reasoning) but a means to an end—a tool to trust in the revelation given by the Spirit. Living by faith is living in
dependency upon the Holy Spirit. God asks us, “Do you trust Me?” Faith is our way of saying, “Yes.” Doing so is
an act of love. Love believes that God causes all things to work for the good of those who love Him (Rom 8:28).
       Rather than enjoying wrongdoing, by unfailingly incorporating faith and hope in God, mature love endures
wrongdoing (1Cor 13:6). Such love “bears all things, believes all things, hopes all things, endures all things”
(1Cor 13:7). This “love never ceases to have faith; it never loses hope” (NICNT). This “love believes and hopes”
(NAC). Since this love rejoices in the truth rather than in unrighteousness (1Cor 13:6), it “does not naïvely “believe
the best about everybody or everything” (NAC). Rather, even now, it believes the best about God. Both temporally
and logically, since such love incorporates both faith and hope and outlasts both, it is greater than faith and hope
(1Cor 13:13). When faith becomes sight (Heb 11:1) and hope is realized (Rom 8:24), both faith and hope (when
defined as a sense of anticipation) must necessarily cease, for anticipation gives way to eschatological realization.
The love, however, which faith and hope will have built up and fostered (cp. 2Pet 1:7), will abide forever and result
in the misthological salvation of loving souls (1Pet 1:7-9). Developing a loving spirit will misthologically save your
loving soul. Such perfected love casts out fear of doing poorly at the Bema (1Jn 4:17-18). We should rejoice in our
trials and give thanks for them, knowing that they only come to make us strong and perfected in love (Jam 1:2-4).
Believers must grow strong in faith and hope if they are to mature in love. Faith and hope are stepping stones to a
mature love. Conversely, since faith and love are necessary components of the fullest degree of love, Jesus’ love
for those who do not love Him must necessarily be limited. Jesus could not entrust Himself to immature believers
(Jn 2:23-24). Eschatologically, the Lord will not be able to entrust unfaithful believers with true riches (Lk 16:11).
Chapter 2. Practical Application                                                                             Page 31
     Just as our spiritual life is begun by faith rather than emotions, so it must be lived by faith rather than by
emotions, as Solomon wisely explains:

      Just as in accepting Christ as Savior there is to be no dependence on feelings for confirmation that God
      has done a work in the human spirit, there is to be no emphasis on feelings in appropriating Christ as
      our life....
             Assurance…that the Christ-life has been appropriated (identification) based on the Word
      (Romans 6:6,11,13) is equally as important as assurance of salvation. Although there is generally a
      new freedom from the problem of the flesh as the Christ-life is appropriated, assurance is not based on
      the life changes but on the unchanging truth of God. When our assurance is based on the Word, we
      can never be shaken when we have temporary “down times.”53
                                                  Feeling Unloved
       In an earlier counseling session that Dianne and I had with Sue, I had shared a personal real-life example of
the deceptiveness of emotions. When Dianne was undergoing an extended period of treatment and surgery for stage-
3 breast cancer, we were unable to experience sexual intimacy. As a result, I felt unloved by Dianne. Was I really
unloved by Dianne during that time? Far to the contrary! Even in terms of loving affection, our going through that
experience together was a very emotionally bonding experience, even during that time, and our volitional love for
each other was as strong, if not stronger, than ever. Enduring the trial together was emotionally endearing. My
feelings of being unloved were completely irrational, and I knew it intellectually. Yet our lack of sexual eros love
prevented me from feeling that I was being loved affectionately by Dianne at a phileo level. Even so, I did not jump
to an unhealthy psychological conclusion that I was unloved. Rather, I put faith in the fact that I was loved
affectionately, even while being unloved sexually (and thus feeling unloved affectionately), and I was still able to
feel a considerable degree of endearing love affectionately. My feelings of affectual love were divided. An
emotional war was going on in my caboose, but it did not affect the rest of my train. My train of thought remained
as factually on track as ever. The same thing is possible at the supernatural level as well. Do not let your emotions
dictate your thinking; otherwise, you will draw very erroneous conclusions, and your train will certainly crash.
                                                Counseling Legalists
        From past counseling sessions, Sue had made it clear that her focus on living the Christian life was focused
on performance—do’s and don’ts. I shared that our focus should instead be Christ-centered rather than works-
centered. She said that she did not understand how that could be since our works, good and bad, count very much
at the Bema. Since the Bema is a preeminent concern and focus in my writings, I certainly share her concern about
having works that will qualify for hearing, “Well done,” at the Bema. However, my conviction is that dependency
on the Spirit and intimacy with Christ through the Spirit are the means by which we live victoriously and are enabled
to hear, “Well done.” My concern was that, in leaving the Spirit out of the equation, Sue was failing the test and
would not receive a passing grade at the Bema. I urged her to pursue material that would be helpful in that area.
        For example, I recommended Solomon’s books and similar resources. One problem she had with such
materials is that they tend to focus on the Spirit but fail to integrate concerns concerning the Bema. Finley is an
exception in that he integrates both perspectives very well. His book on Galatians would be particularly helpful to
legalists like Sue who need help in integrating the two truths. I suggested the book to her but acknowledged that
there were a couple of issues where I disagreed with Finley, concerning which she would have to read with
discernment. On the intimacy side, the book simplistically states that God’s love is unconditional. On the Bema
side, it wrongly asserts that rewards are only millennial. Since I have demonstrated the fallacy of unconditional


Jesus will not be able to express a level of love that permits Him to entrust them with such riches. In order to
maximize our love for God, we need to trust Him. Doing so will allow Him to maximize His love for us. We place
a limit on how much God can love us when we distrust Him. His love for believers will endure their distrust, of
course, but not even His love can rejoice in or thrive in an atmosphere of distrust. Since love rejoices in the truth,
love can only thrive in an atmosphere of mutual trust. We must trust God so that He can trust us. If we do not trust
Him, why do we think that He would be able to trust us? Just as love is the fitting reward for love, so trust is the
fitting reward for trust. Stewards, those entrusted with riches, must “be found trustworthy” (1Cor 4:2). To be
entrusted and trusted, we must be worthy of such entrustment and trust.
53
   Solomon, Rejection, 91-92.
Page 32                                                                               Degrees of Love and Forgiveness
equation herein and the fallacy of the millennial limitation in my book, Rewards are Eternal, we need not deal with
those weaknesses here. Rather, because of the strengths of Finley’s commentary on Galatians and its multiple
expositions showing that our focus should be on Christ, I used an eating-fish analogy in recommending his book:
When you eat fish, spit out the bones. If you practice discernment, you can profit from a good book. You merely
need to reject or correct any errors that you might encounter. The same rule will apply to the books I have written,
including this one. I make no pretense to being infallible. I make mistakes. However, I am not a pushover. So, when
Sue went on and on complaining about how unloving and unkind I was, it had a negligible impact on me because I
simply evaluated her accusations against the Scripture while trusting in the Spirit. To me, her extended outbursts of
anger that she exhibited with her slanderous texts throughout that next day, Monday (1/21/2019), sounded like the
temper tantrum of a child who was upset because she did not get her way and got her feelings hurt.54
       A couple of criticisms that she employed in those texts are worth noting, though, since she sought to base
those criticisms on Scripture and since the material regarding Galatians is very applicable. In fact, I had just finished
reading Finley’s commentary on Galatians. The Galatians were carnal Christians of the legalistic variety. As Finley
points out, the translation of Gal 2:19 as referring to the Law would be better translated simply as law because the
Greek text is missing the article the in both cases (p. 34). Paul is not limiting his reference to the OT Law. Rather,
he is using the word law to refer to the whole underlying principle of performance. Therefore, living by law would
mean: living by performance, living by rules, living by regulations, living by a list of do’s and don’ts, living by a
checklist mentality, living by legalism—living by living up to God’s legal demands.55
       We are to understand Paul as saying, “For through God’s legal demands I died to God’s legal demands, that
I might live to God.” Trying to live by focusing on doing your best for Jesus is legalism (p. 47). Likewise, we might
translate Gal 5:4 in a similar manner: “You have been severed from Christ, you who are seeking to be (declared)
righteous by living up to God’s legal demands; you have fallen from grace” (TM).56 Trying to live up to God’s
legal demands by focusing on God’s legal demands is legalism. To cross a bridge, you do not focus on looking
down but by looking straight ahead, so it is with performance and the Spirit. In the Christian life, you do not focus
on your performance but on the Spirit. The Galatians were performance-driven rather than Spirit-focused.
       Conversely, some people are emotionally-driven rather than performance-driven. In his book, Trade Like A
Stock Market Wizard, Minervini stresses time and again that in trading stock you must have a plan and stick with it
rather than allow your emotions to control your investing. Emotional discipline is achieved through following the
plan. Similarly, living by emotions, rather than by faith in the Spirit’s plan, is a carnal activity. Since emotions tend
to follow the leadership of behavior, it is not surprising that the performance-driven Galatians also had emotion-
related problems. At least Sue had acknowledged her propensity to be performance-driven rather than Spirit-led.
Hopefully, she will come to see that her emotional issues are not surprising in that case. According to Paul, biting,
devouring, and outbursts of anger are expected results of a performance-driven mentality (Gal 5:15,19-21). Dealing
with the performance-driven mentality will help with obtaining emotional stability. Thus, the present book
addresses both issues.
       Very early in Paul’s opening comments to the Galatians, he makes some very strong accusations against the
Galatian believers because of their fall into Christian legalism. He tells them that they have left the true gospel for
