   Finley says that the first thing that we need to do is translate the word normally rendered as justified as something
that avoids soteriological misperceptions since the Galatian epistle is dealing with experiential sanctification rather
than soteriological justification (p. 79). His point is well taken—to a point. The book of Galatians stresses the
experiential pitfalls of legalism, to be sure, but it also stresses the misthological pitfalls. This verse is best seen as
dealing with both aspects. One need not force a dichotomy between the middle and passive voice. (1) In the middle
voice, it refers to seeking to be righteous in terms of making yourself experientially righteous regarding your present
sanctification. (2) In the passive voice, it means seeking to be declared righteous by God at the Bema—
misthological justification. (3) Placing declared in parenthesis—seeking to be (declared) righteous—makes both
understandings applicable, which is probably what Paul intended since he used a verb form that allowed both
understandings in a context that suggests both meanings.
Chapter 2. Practical Application                                                                                  Page 33
an entirely different, distorted gospel—taught by those who must be considered accursed (Gal 1:6-9). Consequently,
Paul calls these Galatian believers foolish. Furthermore, he publicly rebuked Peter to his face over the matter
(Gal 2:11-21). Then Paul rebukes the Galatians again, repeatedly calling them foolish (Gal 3:1,3). Paul calls them
his children (teknon), not little children, because he is taking them to the woodshed. He is verbally disciplining
them very strongly, warning them that if they receive circumcision, Christ will be of no benefit to them (Gal 5:2).
Then he tells them that, even if they don’t go that far, they already have fallen from grace and have been severed
from Christ because of their legalistic mindset (Gal 5:4).57 And he wishes that those who were leading them astray
into this mindset would even castrate themselves (Gal 5:12). Talk about strong language! Then he warns them that
as a result of their legalistic mentality, they would manifest fleshly traits, such as “outbursts of anger,” and not
inherit the kingdom as a result (Gal 5:21), but rather reap destruction (Gal 6:8). By the soft standards of some, Paul
would be considered harsh.
       By confronting Sue with the reality of the situation, I was seeking to follow Paul’s example, as manifested
in Galatians, while talking to Sue during that Saturday session. Since Sue could not object to the factual accuracy
of my statements, she kept harping about my tone of voice. She said it was not sensitive enough. She wanted a
softer, affectionate tone. Imagine the Galatians whining to Paul:

               Well, Paul, what you say about us may be factually true, but the tone of your letter shows that
       you are unkind and unloving since you hurt our feelings with your harsh comments. Furthermore, since
       you are not using a tone of voice that is kind and loving, this shows that you are not filled with the
       Spirit, since you said that the fruit of the Spirit is love and kindness (Gal 5:22). Therefore, you are the
       one who owes us an apology since you are being fleshy, as proved by the tone of your letter.

       Would the Galatians get an apology from Paul for such a foolish response to his tone? Not hardly! Rather,
what they got was an explanation. Paul says: “I could wish to be present with you now and to change my tone
[Gr. phone], for I am perplexed about you” (Gal 4:20). The Greek word for tone in this verse is phone—the Greek
word for voice. At times, a stern tone of voice is necessary when rebuking. Paul was being kind and loving to use
such a stern tone with them. His tone of voice was required for the occasion. Sue’s whining about my tone of voice
was as irrational as the fictional response I have attributed to the Galatians above. To be sure, I was using a stern
tone of voice in responding to Sue’s accusations against me and my wife. A stern tone was fitting for the occasion.
       Even so, compared to Paul, both my statements and my tones were as tame as a kitten. During that session, I
toned down my response to Sue several notches by purposefully using Paul’s imagery in 1Cor 3:1-3 instead of using
his terminology from Galatians. I told her that she was being immature. But even that angered her. I wonder if it
angered the Corinthians when, in 1Cor 3:1-3, Paul told them that they were being carnal spiritual babies! Was Paul
overly concerned about hurting their feelings? No! He even asked them, “What do you desire? Shall I come to you
with a rod or with love and a spirit of gentleness?” (1Cor 4:21). Sometimes an affectionate response is appropriate;
at other times a rod is appropriate. Paul was not going to spare the rod, if it was needed, for these spiritual children.58
       How about when Paul confronted the Galatians as strongly as he did? Was avoiding hurting their feelings his
top priority? Obviously not. Certainly, restoration is to be done by the spiritual in a spirit of gentleness (Gal 6:1).
But as seen in how Paul deals with carnal spiritual babies and carnal legalists, gentle restoration may have to be

   Paul does not stop at calling them fleshly babies in 1Cor 3:1-3 and threatening with a rod in 1Cor 4:2. He proceeds

(1Cor 11:30). He protests their behavior and accusations (1Cor 15:31). This letter to the Corinthians has been termed

preceded by very strong confrontation—even a rod. Sue accused me of being cruel because, in her opinion, I was

Yet, as seen in how Paul dealt with the legalistic Galatians and immature Corinthians, avoiding hurting her feelings
was not to be my priority. Indeed, given how Paul approached the carnality at Corinth and Galatia, one might
conclude that he believed it was necessary to hurt their feelings in order to confront their carnality and deal
effectively with the situation. Obviously, Paul did not love hurting their feelings, but it was the loving thing to do.
                                                     Childish Anger
        Now, as to the application of Galatians to our counseling session, Sue said in her texts that next day, Monday
(1/21/2019), that I was in the wrong since I provoked her to anger. She cited Eph 6:4 as a case in point since it is
an example in which the one causing the other person to be angry is, in her mind, definitely at fault: “And, fathers,
do not provoke your children to anger; but bring them up in the discipline and instruction of the Lord.” She used
this as a prooftext to say that her anger was my fault. But even in that context, one might ask, “Is a parent necessarily
at fault when a child gets angry in response to parental discipline?” Parents know the answer to that question. So
does the Bible. Does anybody remember the adage, “Spare the rod, spoil the child”? Well, it is derived from
Prov 13:24. Parental discipline, like God’s discipline, is not pleasant: “All discipline for the moment seems not to
be joyful, but sorrowful; yet to those who have been trained by it, afterwards it yields the peaceful fruit of
righteousness” (Heb 12:11). Discipline is not fun but necessary. Love is not necessarily lovey-dovey.
        When my brother, Phil, and I were very young, we had both done something that provoked our father’s
discipline. So our dad sent us to our room, where he would come and administer a spanking. When dad came into
the room, Phil responded that he wanted to say something first, which was, “I wish you were not my father.” My
brother, as an adult, is very intelligent. But that instance, as a child, was not his brightest moment—not by a long
shot! What can I say? Children will be children. So, when Sue responded the next day that she wished that I was
not her counselor because I upset her, my assessment was the same: Children will be children. Despite such childish
displeasure and the unpleasant nature of discipline for a parent, discipline is necessary. Otherwise, the children will
be spoiled.
        Even Eph 6:4 instructs fathers to discipline their children. It does not make the unreasonable and
contradictory demand that fathers make the discipline pleasant. The NICNT comments, “Fathers (or parents) are
urged not to assert their authority over children in a manner more calculated to provoke resentment than ready
obedience.” Unfortunately for Sue’s accusation against me, my verbal discipline was more calculated toward trying
to invoke obedience to the Scripture rather than disobedience to it. Consequently, her attempt to use Eph 6:4 to
blame her anger on me was not biblically justified. Tough Love, as Dobson calls it, does not make sugar coating the
truth its top priority. NICNT explains, “Parents can easily misuse their authority either by making irritating or
unreasonable demands which make no allowances for the inexperience and immaturity of children.” However,
demanding that a child apply Scripture to a problem or exhorting the child to practice spiritual warfare to a demonic
attack is not an attack on the part of the parent against the child. When under demonic attack, I would not want my
child to take it like a punching bag but learn to fight back! But Sue, in her self-pity, insisted that there was nothing
demonic about her out-of-control emotions. Frankly, I don’t believe it. Her problems should not be presumed to be
all in her head, all psychological. Certainly, her issues are also spiritual.59 And what father would not want his
daughter to learn how to protect herself by practicing spiritual warfare? A better understanding of Eph 6:4 is that it
is addressing a situation in which the father might be potentially at fault in causing the child to be angry. One does
not need to be a theologian to discern that the verse is not saying that every time children get angry at parental
discipline the parent is necessarily at fault. Not by a long shot! Sue’s accusations completely missed the mark.
        Sue was upset that Saturday night and wanted to leave without eating dinner with us because I did not pity

                                                     Sinful Forgiveness
       But, in the interim, it would be a sin for me not to point out the danger to Sue that her unrestrained words
may have for her at the Bema. We have a responsibility to rebuke and warn when a brother or sister sins in such a
way that it cannot simply be covered in love (Eze 3:18-19; Mt 18:15). As Adams explains, love covers a multitude
of sins (1Pet 4:8), but it does not cover all sins.60 It would be a sin for me to ignore her sin in such a case. Sometimes
it can be a sin to forgive.61 For her own welfare, I must refuse to forgive her—at upper levels. I cannot pretend that
she is forgiven when the more likely reasonable deduction is that she is not forgiven. If God has not forgiven her at
the misthological level, then I would be very unloving to forgive her on all levels. I must point out her sin.
       As to the conditional nature of forgiveness on the horizontal level, we all know this verse: “If your brother
sins, rebuke him; and if he repents, forgive him” (Lk 17:3). What if a brother or sister refuses to repent? Then,
obviously, you are not to forgive such a brother or sister—not at all levels. (See 4DF for further discussion of levels.)
For present purposes, it suffices to conclude with a verse which shows that a lack of forgiveness by God in the
vertical direction directs us to withhold forgiveness in the horizontal direction:
      20
        ’But I have this against you, that you forgive the woman Jezebel, who calls herself a prophetess, and
      she teaches and leads My bond-servants astray, so that they commit sexual immorality and eat things
      sacrificed to idols. 21 ’And I gave her time to repent; and she does not want to repent of her sexual
      immorality. 22 ’Behold, I will cast her upon a bed of sickness. (Rev 2:20-21)

       Some translations render this opening phrase as: I have this against you, that you tolerate the woman Jezebel.
However, there is good reason to translate it as forgive: I have this against you, that you forgive the woman Jezebel.62
In any case, both ideas are present. In some cases, it is a sin to forgive on all levels those whom God does not
forgive on all levels. Lovingly tolerating such behavior would be intolerable. Tolerating such behavior is a sin. We
should not tolerate such behavior. So even though Sue came back the next night and we eventually moved beyond
her emotional confrontations, my expectation is that she has not been acquitted misthologically. Accordingly, my
responsibility is to follow through with this book and provide a warning. This is the loving thing to do. I will have
what degree of fellowship with her that I can in the interim, but I must risk another yet outburst from her by
following through on my promise to her to write this book, even though during her outbursts she said that she had
no interest in reading it. At least I will have done my due diligence in seeking to avoid God’s displeasure—both for
me and her. If she does not repent, then it would be a sin for me to forgive her and not hold her accountable at a
familial level. Another reason for my continuing to write this book even during that time of relative calm after that
subsequent night was because this was unlikely to be her last outburst. For those driven by emotions, periodic
emotional outbursts can be expected. Hopefully, my writing this book for her would assist her in learning not to let
her emotions drive her to do or say things she will later regret.
       Therefore, I could not sweep this episode under the rug, let sleeping dogs lie, or leave well enough alone
because this will come up at the Bema and probably happen again and again as she has subsequent pity parties and
resulting temper tantrums with my refusing to participate in such parties.63 Plus, I might as well make this experience

                                           Professional Weaker Brother
      Another counterpoint Sue made in her Monday (1/21/2019) texts to my counseling approach was the example
of how the stronger brother in Christ is to treat the weaker brother in Christ—in an understanding and considerate
manner—and she said that I had failed to do so. She wanted to use her weakness to dictate the parameters of the
conversation. I was not the least bit impressed by her argument. Rather, I was reminded of the advice that Aldrich
had given when dealing with professional weaker brothers and sisters in Christ. Although Aldrich was applying his
exposition to lifestyle evangelism, the underlying principles are applicable to an infinite number of situations. Thus,
an extended quote will be helpful not only for this present situation but also for others.
              Paul had to publicly rebuke Peter for his vacillation. I’m sure Peter protested, “But Paul, I’ll
      offend the Jewish believers if they see me eating with Gentiles!” Paul’s reply? “Eat with them
      anyway.” He could not allow Peter to distort the saving grace of God by erecting again the racial wall
      that the Cross had eliminated. To have accommodated Peter’s inconsistency would have compromised
      the integrity of the gospel. Peter’s memory needed to be refreshed by another sheet from heaven. Sound
      familiar?
              Gray Area Options
              What do you do when you’re faced with some “gray areas”? If you believe your participation
      in some event or activity has redemptive potential, and you’re sure Scripture doesn’t condemn it, what
      do you do when fellow believers look askance at your proposal? One thing to do is to determine, as
      best you can, the nature of the objectors. Let me offer a few descriptions that may help.
              1. The professional weaker brother/sister.
              A legalist by nature, a “professional” weaker brother/sister has taken the easy way out. First,
      he/she has settled for a simplistic view of spirituality. He believes the mature believer is the one with
      the smallest comfort zone. Gray doesn’t exist for him; his world is black and white. Second, he thinks
      you demonstrate spirituality by adhering to a narrow, self-imposed list of “do’s” and don’ts.” Third,
      and most serious, all others are expected to conform to this list.
              Fourth, this believer is usually a “mature” saint who will not stumble because of the actions of
      those who reject his petty legalisms. He won’t fall into sin because of your deeds; he just doesn’t like
      what you do. Fifth, these folks do more harm than good. They kill joy, resist beauty, and produce
      ugliness in the name of truth. Many Christians live in mortal fear of such deadpans. Don’t! More than
      anything else, they want to control. Don’t let them! They must be confronted in love.
              2. The genuine weaker brother/sister.
              Folks who are still babes are susceptible to stumbling in the area of doubtful things. We are
      warned not to put a stumbling block in their path. Children, teenagers, and new believers need careful
      attention. I think, however, the church as a whole has spent too much time trying to protect our weaker
      brothers/sisters and not enough time trying to educate them. The weaker brother/sister position is not
      a lifetime option. It is a developmental period on the way to spiritual maturity.
              Susceptible Christians need to understand that they will encounter many within the body of
      Christ who have lesser or greater amounts of freedom, depending on the issues involved. Once they
      understand the principle of conscience and the diversity of freedom, they have little excuse to remain
      a weaker brother/sister. These folks need to be educated. We must not allow them to paralyze our

She had not acknowledged that she was wrong in making those accusations, nor did she ask for forgiveness. Dianne
and I were able to hug her and affirm our love for her that Monday night, but the underlying issues still had not
been resolved. Consequently, I told Sue that I would continue writing this book. For one thing, until she could
accept the difference between volitional and affectual love, my perception was that she would not be able to repent.
Accepting the difference between the two would be necessary for her to believe that I was telling the truth when I
said that I loved her even though my affection for her was running low. Without her firmly acknowledging that
difference between volitional and affectual love, her equation of affection with love would continue to yield the
erroneous conclusion that she was unloved when she felt unloved. She would continue to let her emotions dictate
as to whether she was loved regardless of what the facts had to say. Neurotically, her emotions would remain out
of tune and out of touch with reality.
Chapter 2. Practical Application                                                                                Page 37
      evangelistic efforts.
             3. The mature, nonparticipating brother/sister.
             These folks limit their liberty for one of two reasons. First, they limit it because they don’t have
      freedom in a particular area. Perhaps they refrain from dancing because they don’t have the freedom
      to dance. That is as it should be. Second, they may limit their liberty despite freedom to exercise it.
      Wisdom has taught them that it is not necessary, and sometimes is not prudent, to exercise all the
      liberty which is theirs to enjoy. ‘“Everything is permissible’—but not everything is beneficial.
      ‘Everything is permissible’—but not everything is constructive. Nobody should seek his own good,
      but the good of others” (1 Corinthians 10:23-24). Depending on the issue, we are probably all
      nonparticipating brothers/sisters.
             4. The immature, participating brother/sister.
             This person is not necessarily immature in the sense of lacking in knowledge. His immaturity
      shows up in his injudicious practice and proclamation of freedom. He/she becomes a champion of
      liberty and attempts to get others to join the larger circle of his/her conscience. These folks harm the
      cause of Christ by injuring the lambs.
             5. The mature, participating brother/sister.
             This individual understands the biblical doctrine of liberty and is balanced and mature in its
      application. He/she is consistent in applying his/her freedom in Christ. Yet, this person is able to
      exercise freedom in a quiet, nonthreatening manner which attempts to avoid unnecessary offense.
             Sometimes, however, offense is inevitable. Peter must keep on offending the Jews by eating
      with Gentiles. Mature individuals realize that to be consistent, redemptive Christians, they may have
      to make some difficult choices. Sometimes our involvement with non-Christians must take precedence
      over our concern for other believers. These individuals are sensitive to such occasions and do their
      best to relate effectively to both the Christian and the non-Christian.64 (Bold and italic his; highlight
      mine.)

        Genuine weaker believers would be young believers and new believers. But even they should not be given a
lifetime pass to stay in that condition. Remaining weak should not be a lifetime option but should be remedied with
education. Sue, however, has been a believer for a long time. She is not a genuine weaker believer. Rather she is a
professional weaker believer who has not progressed to spiritual maturity. Part of her stunted growth is attributable
to her thinking that we live the Christian life by focusing on a list of do’s and don’ts. She did not know how to walk
by the Spirit. Thus, I had advised her to seek counsel from a group that specializes in this area. To her credit, she is
doing so. However, earlier she had said that she would do it so that she could check it off her list! Checklist mentality
is antithetical to spirituality. We are to walk by the Spirit rather than by obeying rules.65 Other areas prohibiting her
growth include walking by emotions rather than by the Spirit and continuing to allow her rejection trauma to
traumatize her. More than anything else, she wants to use her emotional drama to control the conversation. Aldrich’s
advice regarding such controlling legalist is: “Don’t let them! They must be confronted in love.”
        Part of Sue’s problem is that she does not acknowledge that love must at times be confrontational. In her
texts, she said that “I was a cruel man...of the worst sort.” She appealed to Paul’s discussion about how we should
treat those weaker in the faith to justify her conclusion. In her assessment, I had completely failed to respond to her
in the manner directed by these weaker-believer texts because, as she continued to chastise me, “love is supposed
to be kind and considerate,” and, according to her, I mocked and laughed at her tears instead, thus failing to “speak
the truth in love.”66 There are multiple weaknesses in her weaker-believer argument, however.
        First, if the occasion demands it, speaking the truth in love (Eph 4:15) means to speak the truth in
confrontational love. Speaking the truth in love is not limited to speaking the truth in lovey-dovey affection. Paul
is a splendid example. As noted previously, “When Cephas came to Antioch,” Paul says, “I opposed him to his
64
   Joseph C. Aldrich, Gentle Persuasion: Creative ways to introduce your friends to Christ (Multnomah Press, OR:
Portland, 1988), 212-215.
65
   As the song says, “Trust and obey, for there is no other way to be happy in Jesus, but to trust and obey.” Trust in
the Spirit and in Scripture (i.e., faith), followed by obedience (i.e., appropriate behavior) results in happiness (i.e.,
appropriate emotions). Appropriate emotions follow the trust and obedience. Emotions follow behavior. And
behavior is changed by a focus on the Spirit and Scripture, not by a focus on rules or regulations.

   Personal correspondence, 1-21-2019.
Page 38                                                                              Degrees of Love and Forgiveness
face, because he stood condemned” (Gal 2:11). Likewise, when Sue came to our home, after sending me and my
wife a six-page letter accusing us of being uncaring and unloving people, I opposed Sue to her face, because she sat
condemned. Love has two natures: one affectionate, the other disciplinary. Speaking the truth in love may, on
occasion, be highly disciplinary rather than affectionate.
       Second, what is good for the goose is also good for the gander. The charge to speak the truth in love does not
only apply to me but also to Sue. Is she speaking the truth in love? Obviously, she does not have any problem
dishing out confrontational counsel. She is being logically inconsistent in dishing it out but crying foul when it is
dished back.
       Third, we “are not to repay evil for evil or insult with insult” (1Pet 3:9). Has Sue complied with that directive?
No. Have I? I believe so. I was confronting her unwillingness to deal with the situation biblically when she was at
our house. My major problem with what Sue was dishing out in her letter and texts was that her accusations are
factually untrue, not that it was confrontational. At least in her texts, she was attempting to rebuke me with Scripture.
This is a step in the right direction. Granted, she did not want me to rebuke her with Scripture. Still, by trying to
rebuke me with Scripture, she has acknowledged that scriptural rebukes are applicable to the situation, which is a
step in the right direction.
       Fourth, as to her actual use of Scripture, her texts do not prove her case. The weaker-believer passages to
which she alluded are found in Rom 14:1-23; 1Cor 8:1-3. They do not apply to Sue’s situation. In his book, A
Theology of Christian Counseling, Adams explains why:

             Note, the principle is to refrain from any legitimate practices that may cause a brother to sin.
      (The principle is not to refrain from those right acts about which your brother may take offense.
      In such cases vss. 1-9 must be applied to him.) To “cause to offend” (i.e., to lead by example into sin)
      is not the same as to take offense.67 (Italics his.)

       In other words, offending Sue is not necessarily a sin, even if she is a genuinely weaker believer. The offense
would have to be of such a nature to cause her to sin if she followed my example. Yet the example I was setting
was to lead her to base her actions on Scripture, an example worthy of imitating. The principle being given in the
passage is not that I must refrain from using Scripture or biblical discipline just because the so-called weaker
believer may take offense by my using Scripture or using biblical discipline. As Aldrich also points out in the case
of the professional weaker sister, Sue real issue was just that she didn’t like what I did. But what did I really do? I
insisted that we deal with the situation scripturally rather than emotionally. She refused, appealing to her tears
instead of Scripture. Would she have sinned or stumbled if she had followed my example by insisting that the issue
be dealt with biblically rather than emotionally? Hardly! That was the very objective I was trying to accomplish. In
those weaker-believer passages, you do not eat meat if the weaker believer might follow your example and thus eat
meat without being fully persuaded that it was the proper thing to do, thus violating his or her conscience, which
would be wrong. However, in my case, it was fitting for me to try to get Sue to follow my example and deal with
her problem biblically rather than emotionally.
       Fifth, Sue is not a genuinely weaker believer in the sense described in those passages but a professional
weaker believer, as described by Aldrich. It is time for her to grow up and start acting responsibly and biblically.
This was the goal of my counseling.
       Sixth, given Sue’s behavior and Adams’ nouthetic counseling advice (to be explained later),68 it would have
been a sin for me not to confront Sue, thus offending her if necessary. Again, the point of the weaker-believer
passages is not that it is wrong to do something that might offend weaker believers. Rather, it is wrong to do
something that might cause them to sin by offending their conscience if they followed your example.
       Seventh, even if I was mocking Sue’s tears as being a poor cop-out for dealing with the situation biblically,
such mocking would not necessarily be sinful. Nouthetic mocking and laughing can be part of a loving rebuke, as
is demonstrated by Jesus, and is plausibly useful in extinction behavior modification for both counseling situations
and real-life integration.69

   Jay E. Adams, A Theology of Christian Counseling: More Than Redemption (Grand Rapids, MI: Zondervan
Publishing, 1979), 32, n. 31.

   For Dobson, see Disciplinary Extinction, 75. For Adams, see Chapter 6. Adams’ Nouthetic Counseling, 89;

                                                        Guilt Trip
       As we know from Scripture, God does not indulge our extended indulgences in self-pity. A reasonable
deduction is that the Lord would likewise expect biblical counselors to follow His example and have limited
patience with counselees who indulge in self-pity. Indeed, if biblical counselors are to be vehicles of the Father’s
love, then biblical counselors should also be vehicles of Father’s displeasure. We expect God to love others through
us. Yet we have also seen that His affectual love for those wallowing in self-pity tends to be rather limited. Thus, it
should be no surprise when the affection we feel for those wallowing in self-pity has its limits as well. Counselors
should not let a self-indulgent counselee throw them into a guilt trip when counselors express the Lord’s displeasure
over a counselee who insists on continuing to wear spiritual diapers and who is not willing to take the necessary
steps to victory and Christian maturity. Some children resist moving on to the potty-training stage. Nevertheless, a
good parent will insist that they do so and at times be stern about doing so.
       Nevertheless, just as the mom and dad may approach the potty training differently, without either being in
error, so two counselors may approach the attempts of a guilt-inducing counselee from different levels of empathy
without either counselor being in error. The Holy Spirit is free to use varying amounts of affectual-volitional love
from various counselors dealing with the same counselee. For instance, when Sue was insisting on my continuing
to be empathetic to her emotional trauma, I was insistent that we move on volitionally beyond her focus on emotions
to focus on scriptural principles that could help her proceed beyond her emotional hang-ups to a more spiritually,
grown-up perspective. My understanding of the situation, from my vantage point, indicated that I should deal with
Sue’s emotional resistance in the manner in which I did. Dianne, on the other hand, wanted to cling on to her more
empathetic approach. Fine. The Lord can use both me and Dianne from different points on the emotional-volitional
spectrum. The Lord does not expect us to be cookie-cut copies or clones of one another. Nor does He expect us to
live by a set of rules that are so rigid that we must approach the situation from the same vantage point when multiple
vantage points are permissible—and probably even advisable. We want to walk by the Spirit, not by rules.
Furthermore, the Godhead may have mixed emotions and might want to use the variety in counselors to provide
some of the Godhead’s own spectrum of emotions. For example, Dianne and I frequently did not agree on the level
of discipline that we should administer to our children as they were growing up. Both approaches were legitimate,
though tension naturally existed at times between our two different disciplinary approaches. As seen in the
volitional-emotional graphs, healthy tension is possible in the spectrum of love. This spectrum variation would
apply to discipline as well. After all, discipline is a form of love (Heb 12:6-8). Discipline involves action. It is
volitional. Lack of discipline would indicate a lack of love. Those counselees who insist that counselors merely
listen empathetically rather than respond volitionally are ironically asking the counselor to respond unlovingly, all
the while accusing the counselor of being unloving for doing what is loving in responding volitionally.
                                                         Diapers
       To illustrate the difference between loving someone volitionally and loving someone emotionally, during
that Saturday’s counseling session, I used the example of changing my children’s diapers when they were babies. I
changed thousands of diapers. I did not enjoy doing so. Changing and tolerating their messy diapers was a necessary
part of parenting. During particularly messy diapers, my affectual love for my children may have been reduced to
a tolerating volitional love. In such a case, I would tolerate changing their diapers because of my volitional
commitment to do so, not because of some warm emotional feeling I was experiencing at the moment. I did not
love the diapers, but I did love the children within the diapers. I looked forward to the day that they would be mature
enough that I did not have to change any more diapers, and as a parent I did what I could to move them along to
that stage sooner rather than later. Remember the potty-training stage for your children, parents? We still love our
children even when they are in the diaper stage, of course, but as they get older, more mature intimacy with them
is possible, and you can do more things together with them and understand one another better.
       Likewise, putting up with Sue’s spiritual and emotional immaturity was not something I enjoyed or that gave
me warm affectionate feelings, but I did it, not only because of my volitional commitment to love her but because
I looked forward to the affectual love that would be possible if she grew out this spiritual and emotional immaturity.
And I was going to do what I could to help her get to that stage. She did not want to hear that she was being
immature, but that is exactly what she needed to hear. I am sure the Corinthians did not like hearing that they were
immature either. But just as earthly parents are not reasonably expected to enjoy changing diapers, spiritual parents
are not biblically expected to enjoy the immature, self-absorbed pity parties or temper tantrums of the spiritually
and emotionally immature.
Page 40                                                                              Degrees of Love and Forgiveness
                                                  Loveless Marriage
        During our dinner conversation that following Monday evening (1/21/2019), Sue told me and Dianne that
she did not feel affectionate love for her husband, alias Sam, when they got married. Because of Sue’s rejection
syndrome, it was hard for her to feel love for others or to feel loved herself. Nevertheless, she married Sam in the
belief that the affectual love would come in time since he is such a loving young man. Does this mean that she did
not love him at all at the time? She did not think that was the case, and neither do I. Rather, an emotional disconnect
was occurring between her volitional love and affectional love. She loved him volitionally because of the affectual
love she believed they would experience eventually. As I pointed out to her, her own marriage was an excellent
illustration of the very point I had been making when she claimed that I did not love her when I stopped
affectionately empathizing with her emotional distress.
        She believed that in the future she would affectionately start loving her husband at some point in time.
Conversely, in my case, even though my affectual love for her had stopped momentarily at that point in time, while
she was ranting, my volitional love for her was as strong as ever. Moreover, just as she presumably loved Sam
volitionally at the point she married him—not just because she was volitionally determined to do so for his sake but
also because she believed future affectual love between them would follow—so I loved her volitionally, even during
her ranting, not just because I was determined to do so but because I believed that future affectual love between us
was possible.
                                               Love Versus Like
     Chay begins one of his articles with an applicable quote from Fedor Dostoyevsky: “To Love a Person is to
See Them as God Intends Them to Be” (emphasis his).70 He expounds in this article by saying,
                Lately, I have been studying my words and surveying my thoughts about certain people. (I am
         sure you have your own list!)
                I have also been reflecting on what it means to be like Jesus and what it looks and sounds like
         to respond like Jesus. There are many illustrations from the words and works of the Lord as to how to
         live in the world. But one night a scene from the gospels struck me, words that I know quite well and
         a scene from scripture that is pungent with meaning.
                 There Jesus was - flogged, beaten, scourged within an inch of His life, condemned by the
         Romans, cursed by the Jews, hanging from the cross. Experiencing alienation from both God and man,
         mocked by onlookers, taunted by bystanders, waiting to die.
                 And then He said it. I can hardly believe He said it.
                “Father Forgive THEM for they know not what they are doing.”
                 I know what I would have said, “To HELL with ALL of YOU! NOW!”
                But that is not what He said. He said, “Father I wish to hold out Heaven to them.”
                 As Pastor John Mitchell has said, “Love is sincerely wishing God’s very best for someone
         else and doing what you can to see it come about.”
                 I do not like the people on my list. But I can love them and I can pray for them, for God’s very
         best for them.
                How about your list? (Emphasis his.)
         Jesus told us to love our enemies; He did not tell us that we have to like them (Mt 5:44). As LaHaye notes,
                When Jesus said, ‘Love your enemies,’ He didn’t say we simply should have a loving feeling
         toward them. It would probably be a long time before you felt like loving your enemies. The apostle
         Paul, quoting from the Old Testament, wrote, “If your enemy is hungry, feed him; if he is thirsty, give
         him something to drink” (Romans 12:20; see Proverbs 25:21). True love is, at its core, an activity, not
         merely a feeling.71

        As already noted, the difference between like and love spills over into the distinction between affectionate
love and volitional love. We are not required to have affectional love for our enemies, but we are required to love
them and volitionally express that love by praying for them. Doing so frees us to care for and about someone
volitionally without caring for someone affectionately. In other words, recognizing that volitional love is not

70
     Fred Chay, “Friday with Fred” (November 9, 2018).
71
     Tim LaHaye, Anger Is a Choice, Kindle Edition (Zondervan, MI: Grand Rapids, 2002), 113.
Chapter 2. Practical Application                                                                                 Page 41
inseparably tied to affectual love allows us to take loving action on behalf of those whom we do not necessarily
like, thereby fulfilling the biblical mandate to love, without feeling guilty about not having the feelings that others
would erroneously deem necessary when they try to induce a guilt trip.
        Affectual love goes up-and-down and even comes-and-goes. Even married couples might think that they
have fallen out of love in a protracted absence of affectual love. In this case, they need to be educated about the
difference between volitional and affectionate love. By acting in volitional love, they can restore their affectual
love. The lack of one type of love need not indicate or dictate the absence of the other type of love. Rather, the
presence of one type of love can lead to the presence of the other type of love. Volitional love can be used to
generate or regenerate affectual love. Or, in the case of Sam, the reverse would have been true. His affectionate
love for Sue led to his eventually making a choice to love her volitionally via the act of marriage.
        I did not like Sue’s slanderous accusations, especially when she kept sending me those long text messages
throughout my workday on Monday, going on-and-on about how unloving, uncaring, and cruel I am. I just
responded that I would deal with her allegations in this book. Factually, her accusations were untrue. But she and I
had two different perspectives as to what it means to be loving and caring. Quite frankly, I not only did not like
Sue’s accusations that day, I did not like Sue. So what? What did my not liking Sue or her behavior at that time
necessarily have to do with my loving her or caring about her? Nothing. The love Dianne and I have for Sue is not
inseparably tied to our emotions about her. The facts of the matter are that I was praying for Sue, cared about her
well-being, wanted God’s best for her, was doing my best to write this book to help bring that about, and was
envisioning a future in which she might grow up spiritually and be all that God intends for her to be. In short, I was
fulfilling the biblical mandate to love those at enmity with me.
        When Jesus died for us, we were His enemies (Rom 5:8-10). He loved us as His enemies, but that does not
mean that He liked us at the time or that He liked those crucifying Him. Still, He could look beyond His present
circumstances and see that some of His enemies would become all that God intends them to be—His Bride. I write
this book in hopes that it will help Sue and others like her qualify to be a part of that Bride.
                                                     Book Format
       Sue did not want me to write this book. She just wanted to talk about her feelings. I was unwilling. One way
she tried to discourage me from writing this book is by saying that she would have no interest in reading it. I told
her that whether she read it was her choice. Whether I wrote it was my choice, not hers. I was not going to let her
control the conversation by keeping it focused on emotions. Putting our perspectives in a book format would provide
a more objective biblical and logical context in which to examine our emotions. Examining such emotions in a
merely vocal, emotional context was circular and getting us nowhere. Another way that she tried to discourage me
from writing this book was to say that it would not make any sense to write this book just for her. I responded that
I would not be writing this book just for her.
       For one thing, I do not write any of my books for a single person. And I especially would not write a book
solely for someone who says that she has no interest in reading it. Even when I wrote my book, MSIH, in response
to Dianne’s cancer and put her picture on the cover, I did not write the book just for her. In fact, due to the complexity
of the discussion, Dianne still has not read the book. She tried to read the first page of MISH and gave up because
of the advanced nature of the discussion. And I knew when I wrote MSIH that there was a good chance that she
would not read it. However, this did not deter me from writing because I was not writing it just for her. The same
applies to Sue. After Sue failed to discourage me from writing the book and stated that I did not need to write the
book just for her, she then became mad as a result of my telling her that I would not write the book just for her but
would seek to be obedient to 2Cor 1:4 by making the book helpful to others as well. Apparently, she did not want
me to write, but if I was going to write, she wanted it to be all about her and all for her. And then she criticized me
for hurting her feelings when I would not comply with her wishes. Obviously, I am not submitting to her self-
centeredness.
       As I informed Sue, when I write, one reason I do so is to be obedient to 2Cor 1:4, which says that God
“comforts us in all our affliction so that we may be able to comfort those who are in any affliction with the comfort
with which we ourselves are comforted by God.” In other words, I write to pass these insights on to others who may
find them useful. Counselees seeking help with some of these issues may find this book useful. Likewise, counselors
dealing with some of these types of counselees may find such insights and approaches applicable. General readers
may find some of this discussion helpful as well. Even advanced readers may find thought-provoking material. For
such reasons, Sue’s insistence—that if I write this book, I must write it exclusively for her—was nothing but childish
and selfish.
Page 42                                                                            Degrees of Love and Forgiveness
                                                     Conclusion
        This second chapter has been focused on a counseling session with one counselee. Indeed, the first chapter
was written to supply the groundwork for this second chapter and thus address the needs of this counselee to
recognize the difference between volitional and affectional love and between love and like. In turn, this second
chapter has demonstrated the practical outworking of the first chapter. Discussions of strata and types of love and
forgiveness are not merely theological, they are immensely practical. A common feature of several of Paul’s epistles
is to lay a theological foundation and then build upon that foundation with practical exhortations. The same approach
has been adopted in the first two chapters of the present book. Nevertheless, the scope of this book is not limited to
the needs of a single immature neurotic. Indeed, the next chapter will offer something for the advanced reader.
        The basic premise of how to deal with mental-emotional neurotic disparity was briefly outlined. However,
the focus has been on how to apply this approach to the neurotic who insists on focusing on emotions. Insisting that
the neurotic must first feel loved before corrective action is taken was shown to be impractical and self-defeating,
focusing on feelings when the focus should be on behavior. Emotional blackmail should not be allowed as a means
to take the focus off of behavior. Appealing to hurt feelings and angry feelings to circumvent taking appropriate
action should not be allowed either. Such appeals are a smokescreen to turn the attention away from the true issue—
behavior. Moral therapy, not sympathy for self-pity, is needed. A tough love approach is appropriate when dealing
with a counselee who is making sinful allegations. Such allegations are behavioral issues calling for repentance.
Appealing to emotions to avoid facing the need for repentance is morally unacceptable. A confrontation is
necessary, as will be demonstrated in subsequent chapters drawing upon the counseling experience of Solomon,
Dobson, and Adams.
     Chapter 3. Advanced Considerations Regarding Love
                                                      Introduction
       My writings are for: (1) the Lord, (2) others, and (3) myself. The Spirit uses my writing to cause me to think
more clearly, consistently, and completely. During the process of writing this book, my own insights were increased
with further insight. I thank the Spirit of revelation for such spiritual enlightenment in the knowledge of God’s love
(Eph 1:17). Of course, I still have much to learn. As part of my endeavors to learn, I pose questions and seek to find
answers. Actually, I think the Spirit poses these questions in the first person within my mind so that they seem like
they are my questions, just as demonic forces pose temptations in the first person within my mind to make it seem
like they are my thoughts. I accept the former and credit the Spirit for them. I reject the latter and rebuke the dark
forces interjecting them, practicing spiritual warfare 101.
       In the Hall of Faith chapter, the Bible concludes by praising OT believers who “from weakness were made
strong, became mighty in war [polemos], put foreign armies to flight” (Heb 11:34). The Greek word here, polemos,
can also be translated as battle or warfare. In the secular arena, the term polemology is used to refer to the study of
human conflict and war, especially international war.72 Yet it must be expanded to include spiritual warfare. The
present study delves into human conflict. Nouthetic counseling, for example, presupposes human conflict.
Nevertheless, Paul informs us that “our struggle is not against flesh and blood, but against the rulers, against the
powers, against the world forces of this darkness, against the spiritual forces of wickedness in the heavenly places”
(Eph 6:11). Therefore, our conflict with other human beings must be presumed to involve a conflict with spiritual
beings as well. We cannot limit polemology to the physical dimension. You cannot have spiritual victory (nikology)
without victorious spiritual warfare (polemology). Therefore, the present study integrates nikology with
polemology.
       Although the discussion in the previous chapter integrated theory with practice, a more strictly theoretical
discussion will be posed in the current chapter for the sake of advanced readers. Contemplating God’s love for
angelic spiritual beings, both those who are supportive of us and opposed to us, will help us integrate our discussion
of nikology with polemology. Like us, they too were created in God’s image as objects of God’s love. We can learn
much by considering the implications of God’s love for them into deductions regarding God’s love for us. Consider
the following questions as brainteasers:

      1.   Does Jesus love everyone equally?
      2.   Does Jesus love everyone infinitely?
      3.   If Jesus loves everyone infinitely, must He love everyone equally?
      4.   Is Jesus’ soteriological love strictly volitional?
      5.   Is Jesus’ love for the lost in hell finite?
      6.   Is Jesus’ love for angels conditional?
      7.   Would Jesus have died for a single individual rather than for humanity as a whole?

       Pause here. Think through the questions. Take time to jot down an answer. You can either write yes or no
beside each question or provide further elaboration in the space below. Once you have written your answer, proceed
on to the next page, and let’s think through these questions together—at a more advanced level than has been taken
thus far in this book.




72
  Polemology is a secular expression derived from polemos (warfare) and logos (teaching) to refer to the study of
human warfare. However, biblically it must be expanded to include the doctrine of spiritual warfare. Human
conflict is but a symptom of the deeper spiritual conflict.
Page 44                                                                                  Degrees of Love and Forgiveness
       Counter to popular opinion, the answer to the first question is a no-brainer for a misthological thinker.
Obviously, as already demonstrated herein and elsewhere in my writings, Jesus does not love everyone equally. A
soteriologist might object and pose that Jesus at least loves everyone equally soteriologically. Even if that were the
case, it would still be true that Jesus does not love everyone equally misthologically. Yes, Jesus did love everyone
equally in terms of dying for them and offering them the free gift of eternal life—both of which are infinite in value.
In that case, however, misthologists may contend that He loved them sufficiently, not equally. In any case, when
all dimensions are factored in, both soteriological and misthological, we may confidently answer the first question
negatively: Jesus does not love everyone equally. Even soteriologically, Jesus does not love everyone equally when
the eschatological dimension is taken into consideration. The value of the payment made for the lost and the value
of the gift offered to the lost are infinite, but since they reject His infinite gift, they reject His infinite love and reject
being objects of His infinite love.
       The second question is not as intuitive as it may seem: “Does Jesus love everyone infinitely?” The answer
seems to be a simple, “Yes.” Many assume that Jesus at least loves everyone infinitely, as expressed in the song
affirming that we are redeemed by His infinite mercy and verses such as Jn 3:16. Assuming that the answer to the
first question is no and that the answer to the second question is yes, though, causes a problem when we reach the
third question. If Jesus loves every person infinitely, which is possible since His capacity to love is infinite and the
atonement is infinite in value and made for everyone, then seemingly He must love each person equally because
degrees of infinity are impossible. For example, because 80%-of-infinity is still infinite, the Lord cannot love
someone 80%-of-infinity as much as He loves someone else. Any number multiplied by infinity is infinite.
       This presents a problem with the law of noncontradiction. Jesus’ love for a person cannot be both finite and
infinite in the same way at the same time. We can solve the dilemma, of course, if we think in terms of planes. On
the soteriological plane, Jesus’ love for us may be infinite while His love for us on another plane may be limited.
The law of noncontradiction is not violated because we are not saying that He loves us both finitely and infinitely
in the same way at the same time. Just because God’s love is finite in one capacity does not mean it cannot be
infinite in another capacity and vice versa. The following section is an abbreviated excerpt from my last chapter in
TOD, where I used spheres to illustrate this principle.
                                         Conditionally Loved in Christ
       Jude instructs us: “Keep yourselves in the love of God, waiting anxiously for the mercy of our Lord Jesus
Christ to eternal life” (Jude 1:21). The parallel to Jn 15:10 is obvious and often cited: “If you keep My
commandments, you will abide in My love.” The biblical deduction is rather straightforward: Those believers who
keep Christ’s commandments are kept in God’s love. In these contexts, we keep ourselves in the love of God by our
works—by keeping His word on an ongoing basis. This is not a soteriological keeping by works. Any keeping that
is by works is misthological, as Jude proceeds to indicate: “Now to Him who is able to keep you from stumbling,
and to make you stand in the presence of His glory blameless with great joy” (Jude 1:24). God wants to keep
believers from stumbling out of the sphere of His misthological love (i.e., love that results in rewards) so that they
will be able to stand misthologically blameless at the Bema. Constable is correct in regard to its spherical nature:
“We should keep ourselves in the sphere of God’s love.”73 Jude’s dative of sphere is misthological. Paul, in contrast,
describes a soteriological sphere in which the believer is unconditionally secure. Paul and Jude are talking about
two different spheres of God’s love.




73
   Thomas L. Constable, L. Jude, 19. See Dr. Constable’s Bible Study Notes, 2004 ed. Available at
http://www.soniclight.com
/constable/notes.htm. Accessed on September 14, 2004.
Chapter 3. Advanced Considerations Concerning Love                                                           Page 45
                              Illustration 14. Conditionally Loved by God in Christ




       A more precise translation of the Judean text would be: “But you, beloved, building yourselves up in your
most holy faith; praying in the Holy Spirit; keep yourselves in the love of God, waiting anxiously for the mercy of
our Lord Jesus Christ resulting in eternal life” (Jude 1:20-21; TM). Beloved (agapetos) is the adjective form of the
noun and verb for love (agape and agapao respectively). In other words, beloved means agaped ones. Believers are
beloved. Jude commences his letter to these believers with the affirmation that they are “beloved in God the Father,
and kept for Jesus Christ” (Jude 1:1; MT). Paul says that the beloved are the elect, so does Jude (Col 3:12;
1Thess 1:4; Jude 1:1). While it might be noted that Rom 11:28 makes it impossible to invert our statement and
claim that all beloved are believers, it is nevertheless true that even in this verse the beloved are the elect. Thus,
with this exception noted, Jude’s harmony with Paul (and with the rest of the NT writers) may be inferred so that
Jude 1:1 is to be understood as affirming that these believers are beloved because they are unconditionally loved
in Christ and unconditionally kept in Christ. Or as the KJV expresses Jude 1:1, they are preserved in Jesus
Christ. The beloved are the preserved. With his affirmation of their status as beloved, Jude wishes to confirm their
unconditional security as objects of God’s loving protection. John expresses it this way: “Beloved, we are God’s
children” (1Jn 3:2; NAB). The term beloved is used in the NT to convey the sense of God’s unconditional love
and security to those who are believers simply because they are loved as God’s children.
       Naturally, it will be asked, “Then why does Jude urge these believers to keep themselves in the love of God
Page 46                                                                              Degrees of Love and Forgiveness
in 1:21?” Such a question is, however, quite unnatural. Jude is using the same technique exhibited in John’s writing:
“Beloved, let us love one another” (1Jn 4:7). John supplies the cohortative to love one another after providing the
indicative that we are loved as God’s children. John’s thought, like that of Jude, is clearly: You are loved so love
(1Jn 4:10-11). Be what you are! You are loved so be loving. God’s unconditional love for us is the basis for our
unconditional love for others. God wants us to be like Him and follow His example. The reason parents should
unconditionally love their children is because their heavenly Father unconditionally loves them. It would be foolish
for conditionalists to assert parents should unconditionally love their children, much less each other and their
enemies, if it were not for the fact that God unconditionally loves them. We are to love others unconditionally
because this is the manner in which God loves us. The affirmation of God’s unconditional love for us in Christ
necessarily carries with it the realization of our unconditional security in Christ.
       Neil Anderson relates how much of his success as a Christian counselor has come from trying these two twin
truths together. Believers are unconditionally secure in Christ because they are unconditionally loved in
Christ. Understandably, these powerful truths can be expected to have profound impacts on our lives. Anderson
does well to interlace OSAS with God’s unconditional love for us. A popular Christian song, What If, keeps asking
God, “Would You love me even more?”—if I could perform up to various expectations. The obvious answer
expected by the song is, “No.” Having hit upon the powerful positive results that come from unconditional love,
our Christian subculture pragmatically demands that love be unconditional. Unfortunately, in doing so, a half-truth
is being perpetuated as if it were the whole truth. The whole truth of the matter is that believers are not only loved
unconditionally, they are also loved conditionally. Imagine how much more powerful and positive the whole
truth would be than just a half-truth.
       Anderson asserts, “We don’t follow Him in order to be loved; we are loved, so we follow Him.”74 According
to Christ, however, we follow Him in order to be honored by Him (Jn 12:26). From this statement, one would
deduce that if we wish to be lovingly honored as a follower of Christ, we must be a follower of Christ. If one wishes
simply to be loved as a believer in Christ, one needs only to believe in Christ. But if one wishes to be loved as a
follower of Christ, then one needs to follow Christ. These two truths are not expected to act independently of one
another. For example, my realization as a child that Christ loved me so much as to die for me was the primary
motivating factor that led me to believe in Christ for eternal life. I was led to believe in Christ as a result of
contemplating Christ’s unconditional love for me as a lost child. However, after believing in Christ, I became a
child of God. Instantly, I was no longer loved unconditionally as a lost child; rather, I now was loved unconditionally
as a saved child. Furthermore, having tasted of Christ’s unconditional love for me as a redeemed child, I wanted to
experience the depth of Christ’s love for me as an obedient child. The truth of the matter is that God cannot love
His disobedient children as His obedient children any more than He can love the children of the devil as His own
children. To be sure, God unconditionally loves the lost children of the devil. But let us not imagine that God loves
them as His own children. He does not. His own children are loved in Christ and protected in Christ for the day of
redemption. The lost children of the devil are not. If you want to be loved as a child of God, you must be a child of
God.
       Likewise, if you want to be loved as an obedient child of God, you must be an obedient child of God. Such
children are loved as Christ’s friends. Jesus asserts, “If you keep My commandments, you will abide in My
love….You are My friends, if you do what I command you” (Jn 15:10,14). If we want to be loved as Christ’s friend,
then we must be Christ’s friend. Being loved in this manner requires keeping Christ’s word. For this reason, my
youngest son, Jonathan, is pictured as standing on Christ’s word and grasping Christ’s word. To the degree he does
so, he is loved as Christ’s friend. Yet one foot is pictured outside this loving sphere to depict various truths:

         •   Some areas of our lives may be outside the bounds of God loving us as His friends.
         •   Being loved as God’s friend is experienced in degrees.
         •   Being loved as God’s friend is not necessarily all or nothing.

     Yet in a Who-Am-I list, Anderson lists Jn 15:15 as if it means that all believers are unconditionally friends of
God because they are in Christ.75 The truth of the matter, to the contrary, is that not all believers are friends of God.
Some believers are in fact enemies of God (Jam 4:4). Granted, God unconditionally loves His children who are at

74
     Neil T. Anderson, Victory over the Darkness: with Study Guide (Oxford: Monarch Books, 2007), 278.
75
     Ibid., 52.
Chapter 3. Advanced Considerations Concerning Love                                                              Page 47
enmity with Him, but let us not suppose that God loves them as His friends. Being loved as God’s friend requires
being God’s friend. As parents, we hope that when our children grow up, we will be able to love them not only as
our children but also as our friends. God has similar aspirations for His children. Should this not motivate us to be
friends of God? Of course, it should. Will the conditional nature of this love cause some to brood? Perhaps.
                        Illustration 15. Unconditionally and Conditionally Loved in Christ




       If we want to be loved as Christ’s Bride, then we must be Christ’s Bride. Those believers who live their lives
in a love affair with the world will not be loved as Christ’s Bride since they will not be a part of that bridal company.
Will those believers who fail to attain to this experience of love brood over not being loved in this manner? Yes,
they will. They will weep and gnash their teeth over being rejected from this experience of intimacy. Will they be
able to cry out to God that it is not fair for Him to love His obedient children more than He loves His disobedient
children? No, they will not. First, God already has clearly stated the terms of being loved in this conditional manner
in His word. Christians who wish to ignore the conditional aspects of God’s love in favor of pop music and pop
psychology and shallow theology have no right to blame God. Second, God loves all His children equally and
infinitely when it comes to their position in Christ. Even if believers step completely out of the conditional sphere
of God’s love, they may rest in the knowledge that they cannot step out of the larger unconditional sphere of God’s
love, which encompasses the smaller conditional sphere. As believers, we can rest psychologically in knowing that
we are loved unconditionally in Christ. God is gracious. As obedient believers, we can further rejoice in realizing
that we have a fuller experience of God’s love than we would have had as disobedient believers. God is fair.
Page 48                                                                               Degrees of Love and Forgiveness
       Jude gives believers two means by which to keep themselves in the conditional sphere of God’s love: building
themselves up in their faith and praying in the Holy Spirit. Remaining in this mistholic sphere is based on believers’
performance—their works. The Greek verb for building is epoikodomeo, the same verb Paul uses three times in
1Cor 3:11-15 to describe believers as building upon Christ in a manner worthy of being rewarded. In both contexts,
believers are the ones actively engaged in the building process. They are not passive pawns in God’s building
program. Their active participation in building themselves up in the faith through energized prayer is likewise to be
compensated with two rewards: misthological mercy resulting in (eis) misthological life. Like Jesus and James,
Jude indicates that the future mercy, which such believers eagerly anticipate, is a reward for their present
performance (Mt 5:7; Jam 2:13). That this future acquisition of eternal life as a reward is a crowning experience on
the part of loving believers who already have received eternal life as a gift is straightforward inference:

       1. Keeping the faith results in eternal life (Jude 1:20-21).
       2. Keeping the faith results in a reward (2Tim 4:7-8).
       3. The eternal life that comes as a result of keeping the faith is a reward.

       To reiterate, OSAS is the foundational truth for Jude. All believers have become beloved (MT) and
positionally sanctified (CT) so that they are perfectly protected in Christ (Jude 1:1). Jude originally had planned on
writing about this aspect of their salvation shared in common by the beloved (Jude 1:3) before he decided to advance
to another matter. Even so, he provides enough information to justify the deduction that the beloved are
unconditionally secure believers. In the soteric sphere, such believers are loved unconditionally by their heavenly
Father. Yet, rather than limit his discussion to an affirmation of loving security that is true of all believers regardless
of their performance, Jude advances beyond this elementary truth to warn these soteriologically secure believers
that false teachers, nevertheless, pose a real danger in that even soterically secure believers can be led astray from
the purity of their devotion to Christ and fail to experience the full depths of God’s love in the mistholic sphere. It
almost goes without saying that one must first become a beloved child of God before one can become loved as an
obedient child of God. Nevertheless, Jude does not leave the matter unsaid. He clearly indicates that before one can
engage actively in keeping oneself in the love of God (that results in the reward of eternal life), one must first be
numbered among those who are beloved unconditionally in Christ (as a result of having received the gift of
unconditional security). For John, being beloved is the precursor to being loving. For Jude, being beloved soterically
is the grounds for being loved mistholically.
       A children’s song at the opposite end of the spectrum from What If misrepresents God’s soteric love for His
children as if it were conditional with its lyric: “If I love Him till I die, He will take me home on high.” Reaching
Heaven and remaining an object of God’s saving love requires the believer’s preserving in love according to this
accursed stanza. What If is a far superior song and completely accurate regarding the soteric sphere, which it is
clearly addressing, as noted in its chorus line: I belong to You apart from the things I do. In terms of belonging to
God, our Daddy could not love us any more, regardless of how good we are. He cannot love us any more than He
already does because He loves us infinitely. Nor can He love us any less. He will always be our loving Daddy, and
we will remain His children, even if we do not love Him the way we should, even if we lose our first love.
Nevertheless, the Bible clearly indicates that a reciprocal and misthological dimension to God’s love is experienced
by those of His children who love Him. A biblical harmony between these two songs and these two truths is attained
easily by a modification to the children’s song. When the chorus is first sung, it should express the unconditional
security that is granted when a believer first believes. When the chorus is repeated, it should stress the additional
conditional security that is available if a believer expresses undying love. Together, these two affirmations would
be:
        • If I trust Him before I die, He will take me home on high.
        • If I love Him till I die, He will give me a throne on high.
                                            Affectual Soteriological Love
       Returning now from TOD to the present discussion, the X-Y graph in Illustration 7. Static versus Dynamic
Forgiveness and Love in the present book (p. 10) and the above discussion from TOD regarding spheres both
acknowledge that God loves us affectionally as Daddy, even in the soteriological plane or sphere. A limited range
of affectual soteriological love is seen in God’s love for us as His children. God has a degree of affectual love for
us even when we are immature, diaper wearing children. His love for us as humans is conditioned on our being
Chapter 3. Advanced Considerations Concerning Love                                                             Page 49
                                                                                      76
humans. His love for us as His children is conditioned on our being His children. In the same manner, His love
for us as His friends is conditioned on our being His friends. Accordingly, this level of intimacy is not unconditional.
Not all of God’s children are His friends. Some of His children are His enemies: “You adulteresses, do you not
know that friendship with the world is enmity with God? Therefore, whoever wishes to be a friend of the world
makes himself an enemy of God” (Jam 4:4; TM). Instead of experiencing intimacy with God, estranged children
may be experiencing enmity toward God and thus be out of fellowship with God. Even though God loves all His
children infinitely in terms of the horizontal axis, does not mean that He loves all His children infinitely or equally
regarding the vertical axis. The answer to the third brainteaser is, “No.” Just because Jesus loves us infinitely does
not mean that He loves us equally. Infinity in the horizontal direction does not guarantee infinity in the vertical
direction. The gift of His love in the horizontal direction must not be confused with the reward of His love in the
vertical direction. A gift must not be confused with a reward: soteriology 101. Nor will everyone receive the same
reward: misthology 101. Thus, not all believers will be loved equally vertically.
        Moving now to the fourth question, despite my acknowledging the obvious volitional nature of God’s love
in verses such as Jn 3:16, insisting that those verses limit God’s soteriological love to the volitional arena is not
justified. Certainly, at times, God has varying degrees of affectionate love even for the lost, even those who may
never believe. Undoubtedly, in eternity future, God will have varying degrees of affectionate love for His children,
even for those believers who commit apostasy and wind up in the remotest portion of the outer darkness. In the
meantime, we may reasonably infer that God has varying degrees of affection for all believers, even those who are
walking in the darkness out of fellowship with Him and thus at enmity with Him. His affection for them is
attributable to the simple fact that they are His children. Therefore, since soteriological love is not necessarily
completely devoid of affectual love for believers, we may reasonably infer that His soteriological love for the lost
is not necessarily limited to the volitional arena either.
                                                   Love for the Lost
       As we all know, emotions can be mixed, even contradictory, simultaneously bitter and sweet. Anderson
approvingly uses Yancey’s description of God as likewise having the irrational, emotional, mode swings of a jilted
lover in Hosea, as God’s affectional feelings flowing from His love clash in volatile dynamic tension with the anger
springing up from His righteous indignation when His beloved believers reject His love.77 Given that we as humans
are created in the image of God, our mixed human motions may be considered a mirror image—though pale and
distorted—of God’s emotional complexity. Might God have mixed emotions even about the lost? We already have
surmised that God’s love for believers, as well as for the lost, must be volitionally-emotionally complex, being a
combination of both volitional and emotional aspects, sometimes in tension. Nonetheless, it was suggested that even
this explanation is not the whole story but only a part of the story, a valid vantage point but not the only vantage
point. The full story is probably more complex. In explaining passages such as Dt 28:63 (where God takes delight
in punishing the wicked) versus Eze 18:32 (where God takes no pleasure in killing the wicked) or Is 53:10 (where
the Father was pleased to sacrifice His Son) versus Rom 8:32 (where it was emotionally difficult for the Father to
sacrifice His Son), Piper explains this tension is not merely volitional-emotional but also emotional-emotional:

             God’s infinite complexity is such that he can look at the world through two lenses. He can look
      through a narrow lens or a wide-angle lens. And when God looks at a painful or wicked event through
      a narrow lens, he sees the tragedy of sin for what it is in itself, and he is angered or he is grieved at
      what he sees. “I have no pleasure in the death of anyone” (Ezekiel 18:32) — when God looks at a

76
   In his negative critique of conditional love, Solomon states, “A child who is loved because he performs according
to criteria set by his parents is accepted only on the basis of performance. Hence, he is rejected even when he is
accepted! He is ‘accepted’ for what he does, not for what he is” (Rejection, 48; italics his). His point is well taken
as long as the actual wording of his point is maintained. To think that God’s love for His children is “only”
conditional would be very unhealthy spiritually and psychologically. Conversely, to think that God’s love for His
children is “only” unconditional would be very unhealthy misthologically. The pivotal word is only. Balanced
discussion would acknowledge that God’s love for His children is both unconditional and conditional.
77
   David R. Anderson, Free Grace Soteriology (USA: Xulon Press, 2010), 82. This wide-angle vantage point is

might produce a Bride who brings Him delight. He does not delight in sin, per se, but He does delight in the big-
picture result of sin, namely, the production of a Bride who has become beautiful because of Her fight against sin.

      painful or a wicked event through his narrow lens.
            But when he looks through his wide-angle lens, he sees the tragedy of sin in relation to
      everything leading up to it and everything flowing from it. He sees it in relation to all the
      connections and all the effects that form a pattern or a mosaic, stretching into eternity. And that
      mosaic and all of its parts, good and evil, bring him delight. Psalm 135:6, “Whatever the LORD

      reasons for doing it in the wide-angle lens of things.
            So that is my effort to stay faithful to the Scriptures and come to terms with the infinitely
      complex emotional life of God. Through one lens, he does not delight in the death of anyone, and
