       Piper’s wide-angle versus narrow-angle lens is a good way to describe different vantage points, such as an
emotional-emotional vantage point. Emotional-volitional is another valid means of doing so. These vantage points
are not mutually exclusive; nor are they exhaustive. Another emotional-emotional vantage point would perceive
that God’s loving feelings flowing from His mercy can clash with His angry feelings springing up from His
righteousness. The song, Mercy Came Running, by Phillips, Craig, and Dean vividly depicts God’s mercy coming
to meet our need once the demands of His righteous anger have been propitiated via redemption:

                                   Once there was a holy place
                                   Evidence of God's embrace
                                 And I can almost see mercy's face
                                     Pressed against the veil

                                  Looking down with longing eyes
                                     Mercy must have realized
                                 That once His blood was sacrificed
                                       Freedom would prevail

                                     And as the sky grew dark
                                   And the earth began to shake
                                  With justice no longer in the way

                                      Mercy came running
                                     Like a prisoner set free
                           Past all my failures to the point of my need
                                    When the sin that I carried
                                       Was all I could see
                               And when I could not reach mercy
                                    Mercy came running to me
       A practical implication of this observation is that it would not be surprising if two counselors might have
different vantage points as to the best means to counsel. God’s attribute of mercy might be more prevalent in one
counselor; whereas, His attribute of righteousness might be more predominant in another counselor. For example,

78
    John Piper, “Does God Delight in Destroying Sinners?” (Episode 713; 10/23/2015). Available at
https://www.desiringgod.org/interviews/does-god-delight-in-destroying-sinners. Accessed 1/29/2019.
Chapter 3. Advanced Considerations Concerning Love                                                                  Page 51
Dianne leans more toward mercy in her evaluation as to the best means of counseling, while I am more inclined to
righteousness. All factors being equal, neither approach is necessarily better than the other. Indeed, a healthy tension
could be expressed in our joint counseling. In the present case, Dianne and I have different temperaments toward
Sue. No surprise there. The tension between God’s attributes of mercy and love can result in healthy tension within
God’s own emotions. Therefore, finding this tension expressed in His counselors is to be expected. We all know
what this feels like, even in a single counselor. Experiencing opposite love-hate emotions simultaneously are
nothing new or unique to us as humans.
                                     Illustration 16. God’s Emotional Complexity




       One reason for encapsulating God’s affectual-volitional X-Y love in Illustration 11 (p. 19) was so that it
could be combined with the illustrations using planes to form a joint illustration, suggesting that God’s love is
capable of simultaneous affectual-volitional variations at various levels. God’s love, as is ours, is complex and
complicated. Simplistic views of God’s love are for the immature, for little children, who draw a picture of a red
heart and think that it tells the full story, or who pick daisy petals, saying, “He loves me; He loves me not.”
Sometimes love is that simple; many times it is not.
       Does Jesus love the Devil? The answer to that question is simple: “No, He does not.” Does Jesus love the
lost? The answer is, “Yes, He does.” Of course, much more could be poured into that answer, as the present book
attests. Still, the answer is true, insofar as it goes. But it does not tell the whole truth. Let us consider a more difficult
question: Does Jesus love the lost in Hell? My answer is, “No.” In SAS, I used that negative answer to defend
unconditional security for the believer. My assessment is that the lost cease to be objects of God’s love once they
are eternally consigned to hell. The beauty of God’s image that was once exhibited in Lucifer as God’s image bearer
was destroyed by Lucifer’s fall. Likewise, the beauty of God’s nature that we see manifested in our fellow human
beings who are created in the image of God will be lost when they are confined to eternity in hell. We will no longer
find any beauty in them or find them loveable. They will no longer be God’s image bearers. They will no longer
display His image. Thus, my response to the fifth brain teaser is that Jesus’ love for the lost in Hell is finite. He
loves the lost currently, but He will not love them eternally if they die in a state of having rejected His loving offer
of eternal life as a free gift.
Page 52                                                                              Degrees of Love and Forgiveness
                                                  Love for Angels
      As to the next brain teaser, yes, Jesus’ love for the angels is conditional. If He no longer loves Lucifer, then
His love for the angelic host is contingent upon their obedience to Him. Furthermore, Jesus did not die for angels.
He does not offer redemptive help to angels (Heb 2:14-16).79 We already have seen texts such as Prov 8:17 where
God says, “I love those who love Me.” In eternity future, God’s love will be limited to those who love Him. All
believers will love Him, and all the heavenly host will love Him. Those in Hell will not love Him, and they will not
be loved by Him. Presently, God loves His enemies, to be sure. And we are to imitate His behavior by loving our
enemies as well (Mt 5:44; Lk 6:35)—for now. But the time will finally come when the proper thing to do is call
upon the Lord to destroy His enemies and ours (e.g., Rev 6:10). His love and our love for the lost has an expiration
date.
                                                     Love for Individuals
       As to the last brain teaser, some preachers say that even if there had been one person for whom Jesus’ death
had not sufficed, He would have died for that one person. Purportedly, He would have died for a single individual,
rather than just for humanity as a whole, had it been necessary. Is this true? Apparently not. Granted, Jesus died for
the whole world and thus for all humanity. And His love for the whole world is infinite. Accordingly, a person
might reason that if His love for humanity was infinite, then it must be infinite for each person because a finite
percentage of infinity is still infinite. For example, suppose that 108 billion people have lived since the dawn of the
human race. An infinite value divided by 108 billion people is still an infinite value per person. Thus, one might
assume that His love for each person is infinite. However, we have strong reasons to believe that God’s love for
the lost is finite, not infinite, in both its altitude and amplitude. In terms of the X-Y axis, His vertical love for the
lost is less than His affectual love for His children, and the Lord’s horizontal love for the lost is less than His
volitional love for His children. The Lord has given His children eternal life and love. All of God’s children will
know something of His love through all eternity (Jn 17:3). This is not true of the Devil or his children. They will
know nothing of God’s love through all eternity future.
                                                Love for the Bride
      We all know the parable of the pearl of great price: “Again, the kingdom of heaven is like a merchant seeking
fine pearls, and upon finding one pearl of great value, he went and sold all that he had, and bought it” (Mt 13:45-
46). Misthologists such as Chitwood, Whipple, Hollandsworth are correct to recognize that the pearl resents the

79
  For extended discussion, see sequential sections by Heiser: “Can ‘Fallen Angels’ Be Redeemed?” and “Are Fallen
Angels Included in Reconciling All Things” and “Are Angels Denied Redemption?” Michael S. Heiser, Angels:
What the Bible Really Says About God’s Heavenly Host, Kindle Edition (Bellingham, WA: Lexham Press, 98225),
KL 2816-2989. Heiser argues that the clause to reconcile in Col 1:20 must refer to the past and already be completed
because it is an aorist, like created in Col 1:16. Yet his argument is faulty because the latter is an indicative while
the former is an infinitive, and time is lost outside the indicative mood. Nevertheless, his other argument—that
restoration of all things means that all things will be in subjection to God’s authority—is well received. Everything
will be brought into harmony with God’s will (cp. Phil 2:10-11; Col 2:15). For some, submission will be voluntary,
for some involuntary. But all will be in subjection. For fallen angels, this subjection will mean eternal death. An
alternate explanation is that reconciliation purposefully does not include things currently “under the earth” (BKC,
cp. Phil 2:10). MMP2 places the Lake of Fire outside of His kingdom (cp. Mt 13:41-42,50), thus outside of the
heavenly multiverse. Hades, which is currently under the earth, will be thrown into the Lake of Fire (Rev 20:10),
and thus not be included currently or eschatologically in the scope of reconciliation. Those in the Lake of Fire would
be excluded from a reconciliation limited to “things on earth or things in heaven.” The Lake of Fire will be outside
of both the earthly and heavenly realms.
     Heiser’s argument that Jesus cannot die for angels because angels cannot die is also weakened by the fact that
Heiser acknowledges that angels can die like men (cp. Ps 82:6-7). Nevertheless, Heiser’s overall argument is valid.
For example, we might conjecture that Ps 82:6-7 indicates that the fate of both unredeemed men and angels will be
eternal death. In any case, Heiser is correct that Jesus’ death for humans in Jn 3:16 could not apply to angels because
Jesus partook of human nature in that death and died for all men, not for angels. I would add that cooperate death
was possible for humanity because humanity died corporately through one sin (Rom 5:18-19). Corporate death is
not possible for angels because they did not die corporately through one sin. Even if it were possible for Jesus to
die for angels, He would have to die for each angel individually, a theoretical possibility at best, nevertheless, one
that is easily and rightly dismissed (Rom 6:9; Heb 10:10,14).
Chapter 3. Advanced Considerations Concerning Love                                                          Page 53
      80
Bride. What is not so readily recognized, though, is that God had to allow sin in order to create this bridal pearl.
What is even more amazing to grasp is that God allowed sin not only to maximize our love for Him but also to
maximize His love for us! To make a pearl of this supreme value (which He could in turn love for its unbridled
value) cost Him not only His life for sin but the allowance of sin. He had to allow sin in order to maximize our
beauty and thus His love for us. The maximization of our beauty maximizes God’s love for us. God allowed sin to
make us all the more beautiful so that He could love us all the more. Sin not only makes our beauty jump out like
placing a diamond on a black velvet cloth, but sin was also used to produce our beauty, to turn the back coal into
an exquisite diamond.81
      But not all of God’s children will know equal amounts of God’s love through all eternity. The following
excerpt from MMP3 shows that the epidemy of God’s love is reserved for a portion of humanity.
                                     Illustration 17. The Pearl of Great Price




             In commanding us to lay up treasure for ourselves in Heaven (Mt 6:20), God has commanded
      us to follow His example. The Lord is not commanding us to be selfish—but to act in our own best
      interest which is His best interest. He wants us to lay up treasure so that we might become His treasure
      (Mt 13:44)—His Bride. The ultimate purpose for which God created this universe was not to
      create a kingdom, but to create a Queen. Creating a kingdom was a means to an end. God could
      create a universe and a kingdom without sin. But He could not create a Queen without allowing
      sin. Sin was the necessary price, which cost His blood, to produce a Queen worthy of Himself.
      God allows sin and suffering so that He might create the Bride of Christ and so that we might
      qualify to be the Bride of Christ. Doing so was the only way to produce the Bride of Christ. Why
      does God allow good people to suffer? So that they might become beautiful members of the Bride
      of Christ. Suffering could not be limited to them but was for them. Allowing an opponent to
      come about who would cause a war in which collateral suffering would occur was necessary to
      accomplish the bridal objective.

80
   Arlen L. Chitwood, Mysteries of the Kingdom, Second Edition (Norman, OK: Lamp Broadcast, 2011), 144. Gary
T. Whipple, The Matthew Mysteries: A Revelation of the Higher Wisdom Concerning the Church, Israel and the
Gentiles in Prophecy (Hayesville, NC: Schoettle Publishing Co., 1995), 199. Hollandsworth, Keys, 44-45.
81
   Also see Love Creates Value 115; He Loves Her Because She is Beautiful 117; Infinite Beauty 122.
Page 54                                                                            Degrees of Love and Forgiveness

       When Jesus died for humanity, it was primarily for His Bride. In the upper room, He told His disciples:
“Greater love has no one than this, that one lay down his life for his friends. You are My friends, if you do what I
command you” (Jn 15:13-14). Jesus’ greatest affectual love was in laying down His life for His friends, not for His
enemies. He loved them to the “full extent of His love” (Jn 13:1; NIV)—in full measure, fully, completely (FB), to
the uttermost (GD). Yes, Jesus died for His enemies. He laid down His life even for His enemies. At the very least,
He had volitional love for His enemies, even for those who crucified Him and for those who would never believe
in Him. In like token, He also died for believers who would be at enmity with Him and who would fail to qualify
to be His Bride. Nevertheless, His greatest love was for those believers who would obey Him and thus qualify to
be His friends—His bridal companions. These bridal saints are the Queen whose beauty King Jesus will seek (Ps
45:11). He values Her beauty. Just as the merchant valued the pearl of great price because of its valuable beauty, so
Christ will value the Bride because of Her compelling beauty. The compelling motivation moving Jesus to die for
the world in Jn 3:16 was not to save the whole world, even though His death made the whole world savable. Rather,
His primary reason for dying for the whole world was in order to lay down His life for His friends—for those
believers who would ultimately compose the Bride. As the Gospel of John progresses from Jn 3:16 to Jn 15:13, we
learn that Jesus’ love for the world was collateral to His love for His friends. Collateral suffering by the world and
His friends was unavoidable if He was to produce a Bride. MMP3 provides the following additional illustration and
to explain why this is so.
                             Illustration 18. The Pearl of Great Price is not the Body




             As stated in MMP2, moral evil was allowed for the greater good (GG), and misthology is a
      large part of that greater good. The only way for the Father to form a Bride for Christ was to allow
      the presence of moral evil. Believers form the body of Christ, but the Bride of Christ is formed
      from the body of Christ. Just as Adam’s bride, Eve, was formed from his body and distinct from
      his body, so the Bride of Christ is formed from the body of Christ and distinct from His body.
      Even though the oyster uses the same substance to form its shell as it does the pearl, no one would
      mistake the pearl for the shell. Even so, no one should confuse the Bride of Christ with the body
      of Christ. Only by overcoming evil do believers become misthologically suitable to be a Bride for
      Christ. Just as the pearl is formed from the body of the oyster but removed from the oyster, the
      Bride of Christ is formed from the body of Christ but removed from the body of Christ. Just as
      it takes heat and pressure to turn coal into a diamond and takes an irritant to cause an oyster to
      produce a pearl, moral evil was necessary for believers to be made into a mature Bride. Oysters
      produce pearls as a defense mechanism because something is hurting them. Likewise, only by
      overcoming painful evil can faithful believers become experiential and misthological partakers
      in the divine nature (2Pet 1:4-8). The Bride is God’s pearl of great price. And that price required
      permitting moral evil.
Chapter 3. Advanced Considerations Concerning Love                                                               Page 55

        An observation being added in the present book is that just as collateral suffering was necessary to produce
the Bride so was collateral love. Jesus could die for the whole world, that is, for every human being in the world,
but He could not die for them with the same degree of affection. Necessarily, even at the point of offering up His
life for the whole world, He loved those who would comprise His Bride more than He loved those who would not.
Love is proportional to value. Firefighters risk their lives indiscreetly to save those in need. Understandably, though,
if the call that came into the station was for the home of one of the firefighter’s and he realized that his own wife
was in danger and they rushed to his house and he rushed in to save his wife, he would have a greater affectional
love on this occasion in risking his life than he would when risking his life to save another man’s wife. We would
not fault him for having greater affectual love in risking his life to save his own wife than when he was risking his
life to save another man’s wife. Affectionately, he values his wife more than another man’s wife. Noting is immoral
in the differentiation in value. Neither should we fault the Lord for having greater affectual love for those who
would compose His Bride than those who would not, especially since He equally laid down His life for both. The
degree of affectual love and motivation was different, but the volitional love expressed in the sacrificial love was
equivalent. Jesus’ love for each individual person in the world was not equal nor was it infinite for each person, but
it was sufficient to make them savable. He genuinely offers eternal life to everyone who hears the gospel message.
Nevertheless, just as Sue’s volitional love at the time she married Sam was based on the affectionate love that she
foresaw eventually occurring between them, so Christ’s greater love for future bridal believers at the point He died
for them was based on the affectionate love that He foresaw eventually occurring with them.
                                 Illustration 19. Finite versus Infinite Love Graph




        A verse like Jn 3:16 looks at God’s love through a narrow-lens vantage point: “For God so loved the world,
that He gave His only begotten Son, that whoever believes in Him should not perish, but have eternal life.” This
vantage point zooms in on Jesus’ love at the point of His death for the entire human race and acknowledges that
Jesus died for the whole world and that He offers eternal life to the whole world. Jesus loved and died for all of
humanity, every individual human. He gave His physical life for every single human and offers His life—eternal
life—to every single human. Nevertheless, before they die, they must believe in Him for eternal life to receive the
free gift of eternal life. There is an expiration date to His offer of eternal life. The reception of this life would make
them objects of His love eternally. Their ultimate rejection of this life will make them objects of His wrath eternally.
Even acknowledging that the love which Jesus expressed for the entire world in Jn 3:16 is infinite does not nullify
this statement. Infinite love for the whole world does not necessarily indicate infinite love for every person in the
world, as can be deduced from Illustration 19. Finite versus Infinite Love Graph.
        Many will refuse to believe in Jesus for eternal life. Even so, God demonstrates patience toward such
unbelievers, but not the same degree of patience that He demonstrates for those who believe in Him for eternal life.
“His perfect patience” is “for those who would believe in Him for eternal life” (1Tim 1:16). Comparatively
Page 56                                                                              Degrees of Love and Forgiveness
speaking, solely from a wide-angle vantage point, God’s patience for those who will not believe is only much, not
perfect/complete, since unbelievers will be objects of wrath rather than objects of His love in eternity future:
      22
         What if God, although willing to demonstrate His wrath and to make His power known, endured
      with much patience vessels of wrath prepared for destruction? 23 And He did so in order that He might
      make known the riches of His glory upon vessels of mercy, which He prepared beforehand for glory,
      24
         even us, whom He also called, not from among Jews only, but also from among Gentiles. (Rom 9:22-
      24)

           •   Nonelect: God’s love is horizontally and vertically finite.
           •   Soteriological elect: God’s love is horizontally infinite and vertically finite.
           •   Misthological elect: God’s love is horizontally and vertically infinite.

        But what about the mathematical computation which asserts that because God’s love is infinite and because
He loves each person in the world (so Jn 3:16), then even a fraction of His infinite love for an individual must be
infinite since a fraction of infinity is still infinite? Those thinking this way conclude that God loves everyone
infinitely. However, they are not thinking clearly. God’s infinite love for every person in the world does not extend
to those in Hell. The X, Y, Z values in Illustration 19 represent God’s love, respectively, for: (1) nonelect
unbelievers, (2) soteriologically elect believers, and (3) misthologically elect bridal believers. For sake of the
illustration, suppose that the vertical axis has a scale so that: X = 20; Y = 40; Z = ∞. To compute the percentage of
X to Y you would divide X by Y and multiply by 100%. For example, (20/40)*100 = 50%. Twenty is fifty percent
of forty. To compute the percentage of X to Z, you would divide X by Z and multiply by 100%. In this case,
(20/∞)*100 ≈ 0. Any number divided by infinity approximates zero. The proper mathematical formula to compute
the percentage of X as a part of infinity does not multiply X by ∞; rather, it divides X by ∞. Paul’s statements
above reflect excellent mathematical reasoning: Much patience is much less than perfect patience.
                                Illustration 20. Finite versus Infinite Love Faucet
Chapter 3. Advanced Considerations Concerning Love                                                               Page 57
       For those less mathematically inclined, the water faucet illustration expresses the same concept. The supply
of water to the faucet may be infinite, but the amount of water coming through the faucet is subject to the handle
on the faucet. This handle is our free will. We have the freedom to respond to God’s love displayed on the Cross
for us by believing in Jesus for eternal life, and we have the freedom to reject God’s love for us displayed on the
Cross. If we die in a state of having never received God’s love, then we turn off God’s love for us. He has given us
that option. Carnal believers, on the other hand, will forever be the objects of God’s infinite love. However,
compared to the love that God will be able to lavish on those believers who qualify to be His Bride, carnal believers
can limit God’s infinite love for themselves to a trickle. God’s affection for the Bride is infinite. As humanity
spreads throughout the multiverse, His display of public affection for the Bride will increase infinitely as the scope
of the Bride’s co-rulership with Him increases infinitely. Additionally, the Bride’s participation in ruling with Him
will make Her increasingly beautiful throughout eternity. She will become increasingly valuable as His co-ruler.
And His love will increase accordingly.
       Jesus did not die for the angels corporately, much less die repeatedly so as to die personally for every single
angle. His love for the angels is finite and eternally contingent. He did not make the ultimate sacrifice for angels,
which are beings whom He loves finitely and contingently. His love for the lost portion of humanity who will be
consigned in Hell is also finite and contingent. Although He died for them, He did not die because of them. If all
humanity would have rejected His free offer of eternal life, would Jesus have wasted His life dying for them?
Apparently not. Notwithstanding, He died for all humanity knowing that a portion of humanity would believe in
Him and that a subset of those believers would qualify to be His Bride. Basically, He died for His Bride. Other
portions of humanity were not excluded from the scope of His death and love, of course, but neither were they the
focus of His death and love or the driving motive for His death. In the final analysis, from the scope of the wide-
angle lens that sees infinity, Christ’s love for the Bride was so great that, comparatively speaking, His death was
for the bridal portion of the church rather than for the rest of humanity, just as Paul indicates:
      25
        Husbands, love your wives, just as Christ also loved the church and gave Himself up for her; 26 that
      He might sanctify her, having cleansed her by the washing of water with the word, 27 that He might
      present to Himself the church in all her glory, having no spot or wrinkle or any such thing; but that she
      should be holy and blameless. (Eph 5:25-27)

       Jesus died to get a Bride. To make the last brain teaser harder, though, let’s ask the seventh question again,
but this time with a focus on the Bride: “Would Jesus have died for a single individual rather than for humanity as
a whole if that single individual would be a member of His Bride?” Notice that the question did not ask, “Would
Jesus have died for a single individual rather than for humanity as a whole if that single individual would compose
His Bride?” In that case, the answer would be, “No.” The Lord would not have died for a single individual because
a single individual cannot compose the Bride. A cooperate body is necessary to compose the Bride. Jesus died to
get a Bride, not a bridal toenail, not a part of a Bride, so to speak. On the other hand, what if that Bride would be
missing her toenail for all eternity if she were missing that constituent part? Speaking strictly theoretically, if Jesus’
death for corporate humanity sufficed for the completion of the Bridal body except for the most insignificant
member of the cooperate Bride, would Jesus have died a second time for this single, solitary, least significant, bridal
believer? Pause. Ponder the question. The answer is on this page. Don’t look ahead. When you have figured out the
answer, then proceed to the next page.
Page 58                                                                              Degrees of Love and Forgiveness
       Yes!!! Jesus would have died for this single, solitary, least significant, bridal believer because of His love for
the Bride as a whole. He wants the Bride to be complete, to be whole, to be without blemish. Paul as much says so.
And Paul is not the only biblical writer to write of the beauty of the Bride. Christ would not have died for just a
bridal toenail. He could not marry a toenail. Yet, if necessary, He would have died for the Bride and then died a
second time for her toenail to make His Bride faultless, if that is what it took, to make Her perfect in Her beauty.
The Lord not only wants a Bride that is completely beautiful for His sake but for Her sake. He wants her not only
to be completely beautiful, but He also wants her to feel completely beautiful. Feelings are important! God cares
very much how we feel. Nonetheless, those feelings must be put in their proper place and evaluated in a biblical
context. God wants us to feel loved—supremely loved. He wants us to feel beautiful—supremely beautiful.
       God has done and is doing everything necessary to make this misthological perfection possible. He supplies
us with an infinite source of grace to make this possible, but He is not going to turn the faucet on for us. He has
given us free will. Will you accept His offer to be His friend, to be a part of His Bride? If you want to be the object
of God’s infinite love, even to the extent that He would have left Heaven a second time to die personally just for
you, then you have a choice. Just turn the faucet open all the way without delay. John likens this to getting ready
for your wedding:
      7
        “Let us rejoice and be glad and give the glory to Him, for the marriage of the Lamb has come and
      His bride has made herself ready.” 8 And it was given to her to clothe herself in fine linen, bright and
      clean; for the fine linen is the righteous acts of the saints. (Rev 19:7-8)

       If you are not a believer, then believe in Jesus for eternal life as a free gift. From that point on, you will be
eternally and unconditionally secure as a child of God. But don’t stop there! Grow up! Become mature! Get ready
for your wedding day! To speak in terms of biblical analogies, the Lord will be looking for a mature woman, not
an immature girl, to be His Bride. Be a grownup when it comes to spiritual understanding and application. Be
mature in your thinking (1Cor 14:20), which includes being mature in your thinking about emotions and in your
dealing with emotions. Do not let your emotions control you. Let the Spirit control you instead.
       The Lord greatly desires our beauty because it is His beauty being manifested within us by the Holy Spirit.
God loves Himself. The Bride gets to participate in this trinitarian love. The Bride will love herself as well when
she sees how beautiful her victory has made Her, how much like Him it has made Her. The Bride will be one spirit
with the Lord just as a husband and wife are one flesh. The ugliness we must go through now is our beauty treatment.
We had to become ugly ducklings if we were to become the most beautiful of swans. God will use our suffering to
make us beautiful—if we let Him. But we are not passive couch potatoes in the process. He makes us beautiful by
teaching us how to fight. If you want to be like God, fight.
       As explained in TOD, the reason God allowed sin was to maximize our love for Him (cp. Lk 7:47). Then, as
explained in MMP3, this maximization occurs in the creation of the Bride. The reason God allowed sin was so that
He could create a Bride that would more fully share His nature experientially. All believers share God’s nature
ontologically, but bridal believers will share His nature/character more fully experientially. To maximize our
knowledge of Him, and thus maximize our participation in eternal life (Jn 17:3), God had to permit sin. Those who
co-suffer with Christ will be co-glorified with Christ, of course (Rom 8:17b). But why? Why do we have to suffer
with Him in order to be co-glorified with Him? One reason is that by suffering with Him we come to know Him
better; we know the fellowship of His suffering (Phil 3:10). By this means, we have hope of inheriting the out-
resurrection (Phil 3:11).
       Why did God ask Abraham to offer up Isaac (Gen 22:2)? To be sure, one reason was so that soteriologically
it would typologically foreshadow the sacrifice that God Himself would make when the Father offered up His own
Son. Indeed, the very place that Abraham prepared to offer up Isaac is where Jesus was actually offered up. Still,
the typology encompasses much more than mere soteriology. Further, more was at stake than just typology. Faith,
hope, and love were greatly increased as a result (cp. 1Cor 13:13). Because Isaac was the son of promise through
whom his subsequent generations would be raised (Gen 21:12), Abraham deduced that God would raise Isaac from
the dead. Abraham’s faith was put to the fiery test, and he passed the test because he believed that God would raise
Isaac from the dead if that is what it took for God to fulfill His promise (Heb 11:17-19). As a result, the Angel of
the Lord (i.e., Jesus in angel form as an OT theophany) expressed admiration and love for Abraham, while
pronouncing a misthological blessing upon him (Gen 22:11-18). As a result of this experience, Abraham was called
“the friend of God” (Jam 2:23). Times have not changed. Jesus (who had appeared to Abraham as the Angel of the
Lord) likewise told His NT believers: “You are My friends, if you do what I command you” (Jn 15:14). By calling
Chapter 3. Advanced Considerations Concerning Love                                                               Page 59
upon Abraham to offer Isaac, Jesus was giving Abraham the opportunity to trust Him. Surely, Abraham loved the
Lord much more as a result of the experience. His faith in the Lord would certainly have given him the capacity to
love the Lord more as a result of the trial, and the blessing bestowed upon him by the Lord afterward is proof that
he did so. Certainly, the Lord loved Abraham more as a result of Abraham’ faith and willingness to obey Him, even
to the point of sacrificing Isaac. Jesus loved Abraham as His friend.
       Moreover, Jesus endured darkness while dying on the Cross outside the city. “Now from the sixth hour
darkness fell upon all the land until the ninth hour. And about the ninth hour Jesus cried out with a loud voice,
saying, ‘Eli, Eli, lama sabachthani?" that is, ‘My God, My God, why hast Thou forsaken Me’?” (Mt 27:45-46) His
suffering outside the gate of the city has misthological and misthopological overtones (Heb 13:12-14).82 Jesus not
only suffered for us soteriologically by shedding His blood, but He also suffered misthologically in enduring the
outer darkness while dying on the Cross. His relationship with the Father could not be broken, but His fellowship
with the Father was certainly broken. As pointed out in BRH,

              Jesus did not cease to be the Son of God on the cross. His fellowship with His Father was broken;
      His relationship with His Father was not even strained. Believers can lose their fellowship with their
      Father, but their relationship with God is permanent. God still remains their God.
              Some might object that Jesus fully had to lose eternal life and His relationship with God His
      Father in order to provide full atonement. To the contrary, He who was infinite suffered finitely so that
      we who are finite might not suffer infinitely. Jesus did not have to stay dead so that we could remain
      alive. If we finite creatures pay for our sins, then we must do so infinitely in hell. The finite must suffer
      infinitely, or the infinite must suffer finitely. As God, Jesus provided the latter solution—a substitution.
      Infinity could suffer finitely (in a finite amount of time and to a finite degree) and yet suffer infinitely.
      Infinity times a finite number is still infinity.

       This truth is not limited to soteriology but includes misthology since both soteriological and misthological
results are eternal. The typology not only encompasses both soteriology and misthology but has special
misthological implications.83 As discussed in MMP4 and LTPW, Abraham was looking for the reward of living in
the Heavenly City (Heb 11:8-10). Offering up Isaac was his means not only to a temporal blessing but to a
misthological end. But the additional observation being added in the present book is that God allows us to suffer
not only to increase our love for Him, via faith in Him, but also to increase His love for us as we trust Him.
        Some people are fighters; some people are lovers. God wants His people to be both. Sue asked, “Why does
God allow us to suffer?” Her husband gave the typical freewill response. I did not. God cannot sin, yet God has free
will. In the future, we will not be able to sin, yet we will still have free will. God could have created us with free
will and yet not have given us the freedom to sin. However, God could not teach us to fight without putting us in a
fight. The Lord allowed Satan to sin so that we might learn to fight Satan. God is a fighter, and He wants us to learn
to fight. He wants us to engage in spiritual warfare so that we might become a warrior Bride, a beautiful warrior.
Polemology is a necessary component of bridal misthology. The Bride is composed of overcomers/fighters. By
fighting we develop a character more like God’s; we become more godlike, more beautiful. Something Beautiful,
by Bill Gaither, catches something of this spirit:
                                        Something beautiful, something good
                                          All my confusion He understood
                                  All I had to offer Him was brokenness and strife
                                    But he made something beautiful of my life
But this beauty is not automatic; it is not true of every believer. You must wrap up your sorrows and disappointments
and lay them at the Cross, as the song goes on to express. Or, as expressed in terms of the exchanged life, you must
appropriate the reality of your death with Christ.

For scars to be beautiful they must end in triumph—in victory—otherwise, they are just a tragedy. We must allow
the Holy Spirit to minister to our hurts and lead us into victory, to experience the power of Christ’s resurrected life,
so that our scars can be transformed into something beautiful in our spirit. We must be able to express the above
song in a genuine spirit of thankfulness rather than self-pity. We must be thankful for our scars. They allow us to
become His Bride. The Lord is thankful for His scars. They allow Him to have a Bride. Jesus did not have an attitude
of self-pity. We must not give in to self-pity either. Use the above song to overcome self-pity with thankfulness.

                                                  Conclusion
      Reposting the brain teasers with an answer key will conclude this chapter:

      1.   Does Jesus love everyone equally? No
      2.   Does Jesus love everyone infinitely? No
      3.   If Jesus loves everyone infinitely, must He love everyone equally? No
      4.   Is Jesus’ soteriological love strictly volitional? No
      5.   Is Jesus’ love for the lost in hell finite? Yes
      6.   Is Jesus’ love for angels conditional? Yes
      7.   Would Jesus have died for a single individual rather than for humanity as a whole? It depends.

        As to the last question, brain teaser number seven, the answer to this strictly theoretical question depends on
whether we are talking about a bridal believer. If not, the answer would be, “No.” After all, if Jesus were willing to
die for a single individual, would He have not been willing to die for every single angel? Conversely, since He did
not die for every single angel, does this not indicate that He is not willing to die for isolated individuals? Even if
the single individual were a bridal believer, the question still needs to be qualified as to whether the single individual
is being viewed as composing the Bride or as a composite member of the corporate bridal entity. Only in the latter
case would the answer to the question be, “Yes.” In short, Jesus would have died again a second time, if necessary,
for a single individual if that single individual would be a composite part of His corporate Bride. The Lord will do
whatever it takes to have a complete Bride. He allowed the fall for this reason. He also died on the Cross for this
reason. We have reason to conclude that the Lord’s love for even the least bridal saint is infinite, both affectionately
and volitionally.
Page 62   Degrees of Love and Forgiveness
                    Chapter 4. Solomon’s Spirituotherapy
                                                   Introduction
       Because of Sue’s background, in which she attributed her emotional issues to the rejection that she had
experienced from her father growing up, I had strongly recommended that she and her husband, Sam, read
Solomon’s book, The Ins and Out of Rejection, before they got married. I warned them that, based on Solomon’s
professional counseling experience, they were going to face considerable difficulty. Nevertheless, I was hopeful
that by reading such material and by applying it, their marriage might be successful. Solomon begins that book by
saying:

            In working with persons who have mental and emotional disturbances there is one trait which
      pervades most, if not all, of those seen in the counseling office. This trait is characterized by an
      impaired ability to give or receive love and is usually traceable to a person’s childhood and parents
      who had the same problem. Although there may have been a type of love in the home, a child sensed
      some degree of overt or covert rejection — hence, the categorization of symptoms which follow could
      be subsumed under the heading, the Rejection Syndrome.84
                                            Rejection Induced Neurosis
       Solomon devotes that book to describing this rejection syndrome, and I highly recommend his book and his
companion book, Hand Book to Happiness. He poses Spirituotherapy as the means to overcome the rejection
syndrome. Basically, Spirituotherapy is a biblically based therapy that sees the Holy Spirit as the Therapist who
renews our minds and transforms our lives through exchanged-life counseling and biblical thinking (Rom 12:2).
Solomon was awarded a doctorate in this area by the University of Northern Colorado.
       Solomon is a professional counselor, having a degree in this area and much experience in clinical analysis.
Having neither and not pretending to be a professional counselor, I deferred Sue and Sam to Solomon’s expertise
in this area. My area of expertise is more in the field of biblical analysis, especially in the field of misthology.
Whereas Solomon and counselors of his background would be more helpful in the field of nikology, I would be
more helpful in integrating nikology with misthology. Solomon calls his approach Spirituotherapy. Sue was having
trouble with the integration between nikology and misthology, so I volunteered to assist in that capacity. The present
book is one such endeavor to render such assistance. Solomon correctly believes that 1Jn 1:9 applies to believers.
Unfortunately, the nikological counselor Sue was seeing did not believe that 1Jn 1:9 applies to believers.85 I gave
Sue a copy of my book, 4DF, to assist in that area. Deferring to Solomon’s expertise in Spirituotherapy, however,
I will cite him repeatedly at length in this chapter, at least to a sufficient extent that the current reader may have
some sense of the rejection syndrome phenomenon. In Rejection, Solomon again uses the castle analogy to explain
the difference between the neurotic and psychotic:

             When the mind can no longer handle the internal and external stress, it can resort to distorting,
      or even denying, reality and shifting responsibility or blame to others. A person who distorts reality
      is described as neurotic; when he denies reality he is diagnosed as psychotic. Someone has said that
      a neurotic person builds castles in the air; a psychotic person lives in them; and a psychiatrist collects
      the rent!86

      In a section entitled, “Feelings vs. Knowledge,” Solomon provides the following diagnosis of those suffering
from a “disagreement between the intellect and emotion.” Such a person will

             have a discrepancy between what he knows to be true and what he feels to be true. And his

84
   Solomon, Rejection, 15.

   Originally, I recommended a Grace Life International counselor to Sam and Sue because this origination had an

       feelings will seem to be more real, so he conducts his life on the basis of the feelings rather than
       truth. This results in distorting reality to agree with emotions. This is known in psychological
       circles as neurosis. It follows that those who do not feel the same way will see their behavior as being
       illogical.
              Many parents of unwanted children attempt to convince their children (and themselves) that
       they do love them. Since a child cannot be fooled by the phony expressions of ‘love’ by the parents,
       he is faced with a double message. ‘I love you’ is the message in words to his mind but ‘I hate you’ is
       the message received by his emotions. Since the rejection is subtle, the child reacts to it without
       knowing what it is. This is the classic setup for the beginning stages of schizophrenia—a conflicting
       message to the mind and the emotions. He knows one thing with his mind and feels another with his
       emotions. This puts him in a double bind since the two messages cannot be reconciled. When it is no
       longer possible to tolerate the conflict, a retreat from reality occurs, known as a psychotic break. After
       the break, the mind and emotions ‘do their own thing’ with little or no cooperation. Thus, a person
       might laugh at news of a family tragedy or in other ways respond inappropriately to communication.
              Interpersonal relationships are grossly hampered by such conditions. When one person
       relates to a situation as it is and another as he feels it is, it is very difficult, if not impossible, to
       arrive at a common conclusion. Thus, communication cannot be conducted on a rational basis.
       Relationships also break down unless the person who is objective can tolerate the illogical behavior
       without extreme frustration.87 (Italics his.)

       Not surprisingly, my wanting to address the situation as it was rationally and Sue’s wanting to address the
situation as she perceived it emotionally could not be conducted on a rational basis because Sue was distorting the
reality to agree with her emotions. As mentioned earlier herein,88 in an earlier counseling session that Dianne and I
had with Sue, I had shared a personal real-life example in which, to some degree, I was able to empathize with those
suffering from this emotional delusion. When Dianne was undergoing an extended bout with stage-3 breast cancer,
we were unable to experience sexual intimacy. My emotional impression was that Dianne did not love me. My
rational assessment was that she loved me as much, if not more, than ever. Logically, I knew that she loved me even
though, emotionally, I did not feel loved. Speaking colloquially, we might call this the difference between head
knowledge and heart knowledge.89 Emotional knowledge (if we call it that) is the so-called heart knowledge, which
is determined by subjective impressions. Spiritual knowledge is head knowledge, which is in accordance with
objective truth. There was a disagreement between my reason and emotion, a discrepancy between what I knew to
87
   Ibid., 34.
88
   See Feeling Unloved, 31.
89
   Adams rejects the distinction between heart knowledge and head knowledge because heart knowledge, in his
opinion, is a function of the heart, and “emotion, in the Bible, is not related to the heart but to the viscera [intestines,
gut, entrails].” Jay E. Adams A Theology of Christian Counseling: More Than Redemption (Grand Rapids, MI:
Zondervan, 1979, 114). He has a point, to a point. Colloquially, we use the expression intestinal fortitude to refer
to someone who has courage and the endurance to go on. And the gut is referred to our second brain, housing our
enteric nervous system and immediately responding when we are stressed, sometimes with pronounced diarrhea.
English speakers say things like, “I have a gut feeling,” and ask others, “What is your gut telling you?” The Greek
word splagchnon refers psychologically to our seat of emotions and was physiologically associated with our inner
body parts in our bowels (i.e., our intestines, gut, entrails). Nevertheless, in our English translations “a transference
is made to the rendering heart” (BDAG). Rendering it as heart in Phlm 1:12, for example, certainly sounds better
to our English ears than saying “I have sent my bowels.” This popular literal translation makes it sound like Paul
had diarrhea, not the most suitable imagery. Moreover, the Greek word more specifically associated with the heart
is kardia and includes our emotions (BDAG). By the way, kardia is not used of the literal heart organ in the NT.
Heart knowledge (or emotional knowledge), as I am using the terms colloquially above, actually refers to what I
emotionally perceive to be true. Phenomenally, it appears to be a function of knowledge (thus a persuasion). In
reality, though, it is merely a function of emotion. Among the various practical benefits of making this distinction
is Solomon’s distinction between the feeling of doubt and actual doubt. Actual doubt would be a function of
persuasion (thus intellect), not emotions. We can have emotional doubt (so to speak) and not have actual doubt (i.e.,
intellectual doubt). In that case, we are having feelings of doubt, which, if we are not careful, can be unnecessarily
embraced as intellectual misgivings and thus become actual doubt, that is, intellectual doubt.
Chapter 4. Solomon’s Spirituotherapy                                                                           Page 65
be true rationally and what I felt to be true emotionally. A critical difference between me and the neurotic, however,
is that I did not distort reality to agree with my emotions. Rather, I maintained a rational basis, relating to the
situation as it was, not as I felt it was. My feelings of being unloved were completely irrational, and I knew it
intellectually. And I accepted the truth of that rational assessment rather than jump to an unhealthy psychological
conclusion that I was unloved. I put faith in the fact that I was loved affectionately, even while being unloved
sexually and thus feeling unloved affectionately. I chose to walk by faith in the rational fact that I was loved. My
intellectual-volitional-emotional sequence was: fact – faith – feelings. Because I was choosing to do so by the
enablement of the Spirit, I was moving through the experience aboard my FFFF train: fact – faith – filling – feelings.
That train is highly practical, not just theoretical. It helps with the problems of life, such as breast cancer. As
Christians, we are to live by faith, to conduct ourselves rationally in accord with reality, because to do otherwise
would make us a spiritual neurotic! The Christian is to be rational/logical instead of emotional/neurotical.
                                             Illustration 21. IFFF Train




         Neurotics ride a different train—an IFFF train instead of the FFFF train. Contrast this IFFF train with the
previous train: Illustration 13. FFFF Train (p. 30). The IFFF train has one car missing and has an inverted sequence,
hence its acronym is missing one “F” and begins with the letter “I” for inverted. Whereas the FFFF train is led by
facts, the IFFF has an inverted sequence in which facts come last and the train is led by emotions. The neurotics’
perception of factual reality is determined by what they feel to be true. They are allowing their feelings to determine
what they believe to be true. They ride life aboard their IFFF train: feelings – fact – faith. Such train rides can work
relatively well at the beginning, even be exciting at other times, and even have some smooth times. But overall, it
tends to be a roller coaster ride, especially when feelings get out of control, taking a toll on one’s physical, mental,
and spiritual health. What starts off as a happy adventure can turn into a rough, dark, volatile ride.
                                   Illustration 22. The IFFF Roller Coaster Ride




In his earlier book, Solomon describes how being a spiritual neurotic can be detrimental to one’s sense of assurance:

             It is possible to be saved but not be assured of that salvation. Our assurance must be based on
      the facts of Scripture rather than on fluctuating feelings. The person who is sometimes labeled
      neurotic most often wants to feel something rather than to believe it, and his style of living is to
      distort reality. As a result, there is no reason for him to believe that his feelings will be trustworthy
      in the matter of salvation.
             There is a profound difference between doubting one’s salvation (mental) and feeling unsaved
Page 66                                                                             Degrees of Love and Forgiveness
         (emotional). Most people who lack assurance feel unsaved. However, most of us do not make a
         distinction between the functions of the mind and the emotions. Neither do most personal workers,
         who attempt to help those who lack assurance. In our attempts to help, we often appeal to someone’s
         mind with the facts of the gospel when it’s really the emotions that are the cause of the confusion.
         When the emotions are being controlled by lies the person has been believing, there is no way the
         emotions will ever agree with truth. Therefore, a person may know (mentally) that he is saved, and
         never doubt his salvation, and yet feel (emotionally) unsaved for a lifetime.
                A veteran missionary who had felt unsaved off and on for more than a quarter century was
         greatly relieved when he finally saw this distinction. Although the feelings did not change
         immediately, having an understanding of the problem gave new light. The following day his testimony
         to a group of missionaries was, “Today I know I am saved, and I don’t care whether I feel saved
         or not!”
                The Bible, speaking to believers, tells us that we are to know that we have eternal life
         (1 John 5:13). It is not at all presumptuous to take God at his Word and simply rest in it. We can
         establish our assurance only by accepting God’s Word for what it is. After we have assurance of our
         salvation, we can go on to find security in that fact.90 (Italics his.)

       This missionary found victory over his emotional neurosis by adopting an I don’t care attitude toward what
he was feeling since what he was feeling was not in accord with what he intellectually knew to be true. I challenged
Sue by firmly expressing this same attitude. When she appealed to her emotions as her defense for what she believed
to be true, I flat out responded that I did not care how she was feeling because her feelings were not in accord with
reality. The above material from Solomon’s Happiness that I had recommended to Sam and Sue makes this I don’t
care attitude a key to victory. So, when a neurotic neurotically says that her feelings are hurt so she is unloved, my
response is, “I don’t care.”
       I don’t care about a neurotic’s emotions in those circumstances. For that matter, as a normal walk of life, I
don’t care about my emotions either. Because I live by faith rather than by emotions, I don’t care about my emotions
when they are counter to my faith. My emotions do not concern me on those occasions. For that matter, as I have
already related, I don’t care about my emotions when they fluctuate. They may not be counter to my faith, but, even
so, they are not in accord with my faith. They are not in accord with factual reality.91 During some worship services,
I may have affectual feelings for the Lord; during other worship services I may not. I don’t care. I am not concerned
about such fluctuating feelings. I worship God via spirit and truth (Jn 4:24), not via emotional highs. Likewise, I
am not alarmed if at some moment I am not feeling affectionate toward Dianne. I know that I love both her and the
Lord affectionately even if I am not feeling particularly affection at some given moment. Those who care too much
about emotions are living by emotions rather than walking by faith and are in for a wild ride.
       As I insert this note into the rough draft of this manuscript, I am sitting in the study typing. Dianne is on the
other end of the house watching TV, while she is washing clothes and taking a break from proofreading this
manuscript. I am not feeling any affectionate feelings toward her at this moment. So what? Earlier this week I took
flowers to her office for our anniversary. Two days later I took her more flowers at her office for her birthday. I felt
affectionate on both of those occasions. Next weekend we are going to Pigeon Forge for a belated celebration of
our birthday and anniversary. I know that I will feel affectionate then. Affection is great. Affectionate feelings are
great. But allowing affectionate feelings to determine whether you are loving or are loved is unwise and unhealthy
when your feelings are not in accord with factual reality.
       At best, rather than riding the FFFF train in the passenger car, this missionary was locked into its caboose,
until he moved up to live in the passage car. Or worse, riding the IFFF train, he was living by emotional feelings
rather than by spiritual filling until he made this transition. This transitional testimony of know-versus-care is
healthy. This missionary found victory by giving up on caring how he felt emotionally. Instead, he volitionally
focused on what he believed intellectually. The passage Solomon cites reads, “These things I have written to you
who believe in the name of the Son of God, in order that you may know that you have eternal life” (1Jn 5:13). It
does not read, “These things I have written to you who believe in the name of the Son of God, in order that you may
feel that you have eternal life.” Faith is an intellectual persuasion, as is knowledge. Feelings lie in another realm of

90
     Solomon, Happiness, 20.
91
     See my personal I-don’t-care example in Fluctuating Affection, 17.
Chapter 4. Solomon’s Spirituotherapy                                                                          Page 67
the soul. The verse is written to give assurance to us via our intellectual capacities, to discourage rather than
encourage spiritual neurosis. There is no reason to doubt one’s salvation just because one does not feel saved. We
are to believe it and know it even if we do not feel it. The question is, “What do you believe?” The question is not,
“How do you feel?” In assisting counselees, we must not only appeal to their minds with the facts but instruct them
that their emotions should not be allowed to cause confusion. We are to instruct them to accept the truth of what
they rationally know to be true rather than believe what they feel to be true. To do otherwise is to be neurotic.
       An unhealthy attitude of care-so-guilt can lock one into a vicious cycle:

              It is possible for a person to go for years feeling unsaved and never doubt his salvation.
      Feeling unsaved is a product of the emotions whereas doubting one’s salvation is a mental or
      intellectual problem. Too frequently, the counselor working with a person who feels unsaved vainly
      tries to prove to him from the Word that he is saved. The person who has no reservations about the
      Scriptures and knows that he accepted Christ at a point in the past, concludes intellectually that he is
      saved yet feels that he isn’t. When he is told that he is calling God a liar if he doesn’t believe what
      the Word says, he has more guilt heaped upon the ‘unsaved feeling’ in his emotions which
      compounds his problem. Thus locked into wrong emotions, the person is launched into his career of
      church membership.92

Neurotics frequently want to live by emotions rather than by logic, so they fail to distinguish mental doubt from
emotional feelings, or they distinguish them but give preference to the latter. Although I really enjoy the character
Spock in Star Trek, I obviously am not saying that there is no place for emotions on our track. Emotions have their
proper place, and they should be kept in that proper place, certainly not given first place. Lest my appeal to living
logically may seem too Vulcan to some, an abbreviated excerpt from MCMC should suffice:

             Among other things, being created in the image of God means that we were created as rational
      beings because God is a rational being. Logic is rooted in the nature of God and thus in our nature. At
      our deepest level, we do not accept contradictions or doubletalk. We have an innate need for truth—
      for logical consistency. The word theology is derived from the combination of two biblical Greek
      words: theos (God) and logos (logic). Biblical theology is logical teaching about God. Geisler defines
      theology as “the logic of God. Theology is rational discourse about God.”93 “God is the author of all
      logic.”94 Not even God can do what actually is impossible logically, such as making a round square,
      because that would go against His very nature. The truly mysterious goes beyond human reason, not
      against it (emphasis his).95
             Or as Gracely would express it, the human inability to know God comprehensively does not
      nullify the ability to know Him fundamentally (emphasis his).96 Just as A cannot be non-A, just as light
      is not darkness, just as sweet is not bitter, so it is wrong to “call evil good, and good evil” (Is 5:20).
             The Bible tells us to be rational (logikos) in our worship of God in Rom 12:1 (MRD). Most
      translations render the word in question as spiritual or reasonable. Of course, if the requested worship
      is in accord with reason, it is rational and reasonable. As to the rendering of spiritual, certainly this
      type of worship feeds our spirit (the metaphysical as opposed to the physical dimension of our being),
      and the context is using logikos in relation to dedicating our physical bodies spiritually to God. Still,
      the word in question is logikos (logical), the adjective form of logos (logic). Why not simply translate
      the verse as the CJB does, as referring to an act of worship that we are to perform because it is logical?
             Christians are supposed to be logicians, at least to a reasonable degree. They are expected to
      offer themselves as living sacrifices to God because doing so is logical, not mechanical or external. In
      the OT, no appeal was made to the intelligence of the sacrificial offering. You did not try to persuade

                                              Marriage to a Neurotic
        Not surprisingly, emotional neurosis can be highly detrimental to marriage:

                Many young people ‘date down’ or establish relationships with persons who are below them in
        age, education, appearance, social status, etc., because they do not feel inferior in such relationships.
        Marriages established under such conditions seldom endure....
        ....Mutual trust can seldom be established since individuals who have experienced rejection often
        marry each other. The situation is almost as bad when a well-adjusted person marries a person from a
        background of rejection. It is exceedingly difficult for this type of person to have empathy for a mate
        who makes illogical assumptions and distorts statements and actions to make them fit with the way he
        feels things are.99 (Italics his.)
                                               Neurotic Subjectivity
     Not surprisingly, living by emotions causes problems with subjectivity:

               Such a person is controlled to a great extent by his emotions; he may be said to “think with his
        feelings”. An observer may find his behavior illogical and inexplicable since It is based on feelings
        rather than facts. A person who argues a point with him based on logic may become very frustrated
        when the reply comes back, “But I feel this is the way it is!”100 (Emphasis his.)

      As a principle to live by, the neurotic prefers emotions over logic; whereas, the Bible prefers logic over
emotions. We are to live by faith in the facts, in accordance with reality, not out of touch with reality. Sue did not
want to live in accordance with the facts; rather, she wanted to live in accordance with her emotions.
                                          Neurotic Emotional Immaturity
   Necessarily, Christians who live by their emotions rather than by faith are carnal Christians. Such emotionally
induced carnality results in spiritual immaturity. Emotional immaturity is not limited to the spiritual arena, however:
“The fact that a person is physically an adult does not mean that his emotions have kept pace. An adult can be an
intellectual genius and an emotional moron. Trying to live with a person possessing this combination of traits can
be a very frustrating experience!”101

                                        Neurotic Emotional Displacement
       Emotional displacement was preventing Sue from interacting with me as someone other than her dad. She
projected her negative emotions concerning her dad onto me, but not only onto me, but also upon God, which is not
surprising for someone living in a state of the rejection syndrome:

             He learns to distrust his parents and finds it very difficult to trust anyone including God.
      He may know (intellectually) that he can trust God but feels that he can’t. Since his feelings are
      in the ascendancy, whatever is felt is the governing factor. Since the parents and others are seen as
      rejecting and condemning, he sees God in much the same way; and his attitude may be summed up
      towards his parents and God, “You’ve rejected me so I will reject you....
      ....When the mind and emotions function independently of each other the person could be described
      as psychotic.102 (Italics his.)

Sue’s intense reaction toward me was not surprising: “Those whom the person identifies with rejecting parents
are singled out for more intense rejection.” “The name of the game is ‘Rejection, pass it on!’”103 Over and over
again Sue would equate me with her father, despite Sam repeatedly saying that this was a completely inaccurate
comparison. Still, since her father is the one from whom she received the rejection, she continued to project that
rejection onto me as acting in an equivalent manner.
                                            Being Neurotic is a Choice
       As Solomon explains, once you know the truth, whether you remain neurotic is your choice. You can either
live in a defeated, victim mentality or you overcome it. Your choice.

             Once the new program, Truth, is implemented by the volitional choice of the will, the
      indwelling Christ empowers the believer to behave in accord with truth. John 8:32 states, “And ye
      shall know the truth and the truth shall make you free.” The converse of this statement would be, “And
      you shall know lies and the lies will enslave you.” This is the plight of the neurotic individual; the
      mind accepts as factual the lies embodied in the emotions and concocted by the flesh or self and the
      brain implements the lies into behavior.
             When instead, truth is volitionally accepted even though it is in contradiction to the
      emotions, the emotions will respond either gradually or suddenly to the truth.104

Because being neurotic is a choice, Solomon is being consistent to insist that the neurotic take personal
responsibility for their neurosis:

             The next pattern we consider is the person who has come for counseling because he has
      encountered severe problems. This person may have been away from the Lord, living in sin and having
      deep psychological problems. From the time of his salvation things have gone progressively downward
      compelling him to seek counseling. Although he may have minor victories in some areas, the overall
      pattern is one of defeat.
             ....During the counseling the first thing he must do is take the personal responsibility for
      what is transpiring in his life. Many persons come for counseling who place the blame for their
      failures or troubles on their parents, mates, their jobs or lack of jobs, circumstances, or others
      generally. After he realizes he must take the responsibility for his life and his failures, then he can
      begin to look at them objectively and see how God can meet his need. Once he is willing to face his
      personal responsibility, he must be ready and willing to confess all known sin in his life and trust God
      for grace to overcome it.105

Rejection Syndrome is Solomon’s follow-up book to Rejection. Again, he stresses the need for the counselee to take

                The fact that the genesis of such behavior, thinking, or feeling may be traced to past rejection
         in no way exonerates anyone from personal responsibility....

         promoting increased understanding of the damage that has taken place to the emotions as a result of
         rejection.....
