During counseling, the first task is to have someone suffering from rejection syndrome to take responsibility
for what is happening in his or her life. Counselees must take total responsibility for the sins they commit
and for allowing their emotions to continue uncontrolled. Accordingly, I held Sue accountable for her
outbursts. Blaming her actions on her dad or on me was not the solution. She must take personal responsibility
for her behavior. I was not going to continue to let her play the victim card as an excuse to blame others for
her lack of objectivity. We all have our somebody-done-me-wrong song. But that does not mean that we have
to keep on singing it! Ultimately, it all comes down to a choice as to which is more important: pity or victory.
Choose one or the other. You cannot choose both.
                                              Neurotic Demonic
       Choosing to live by emotions opens one up to demonically inflamed emotions. What a person thinks are his
or her own emotions might actually be demonically intensified emotions:

                 Very frequently it is during this first down time, this first regression or reversion to self, that
         Satan manifests himself in an overt way. Satan’s usual trick is to ally himself with the person’s
         emotions and/or flesh and start to emphasize and enlarge these out of proportion. For instance,
         along with the depression that a person may experience, if this has been a symptom of the self-life,
         Satan will bring on some oppression to amplify the depressive reaction.
                 Also, during this period of time, negative thoughts run rampant. Satan suggests a few of
         his own which are in agreement with the way a person is feeling at that time so the automatic
         tendency for the person is to agree with Satan. His thinking begins a downward spiral as he
         continues to agree with Satan against God. When Satan can get someone to agree with him against
         God he has fulfilled his role as “accuser of the brethren” (Revelation 12:10). The role of the counselor
         is to intervene in this process and help the person to see objectively what is happening and to
         deal with Satan in a direct manner.
                 James 4:7 gives us instructions as to what must happen before we begin to deal with Satan:
         “Submit yourselves therefore to God.” When self is in control and we are under Satanic attack, it
         does little good to rebuke Satan as long as we are still tolerating the reign of sin in our lives. The
         first step, therefore, must be to confess any and all known sins and then to submit unto God and
         to reckon ourselves to be dead indeed unto sin but alive unto God through Christ.107 (Italics his.)

       For such reasons, I persisted in directing Sue to practice spiritual warfare rather than give in to her volatile
emotional state. As believers we have a choice. However, we must exercise that free will, either that or be a spiritual
punching bag for demonic forces who seek to inflame our emotions and thoughts. Submit and rebuke. They go hand
in hand. Sue related that on one occasion, while trying to pray, she felt a dark presence in the room and rebuked it
in the name of Jesus. But then she told us that she had a dream in which she rebuked the demon and doing so did
not work. I told her that it was good that she was attempting to do spiritual warfare and that it would be good for
her to supplement her reading in nikology with reading in demonology (thus polemology). Even though my

106
      Charles R. Solomon, The Rejection Syndrome and the Way to Acceptance (USA: 1998), 32-33.
107
      Solomon, Rejection, 231.
Chapter 4. Solomon’s Spirituotherapy                                                                          Page 71
specialty is misthology, I have a lot of books specializing in both those other two fields of study. As I explained to
Sue, demons can have footholds or strongholds in our lives. Books on deliverance can provide a more advanced
discussion on how to deal with such problems. Basically, demonic forces can have legal rights to harass believers
if those legal rights are not revoked. Sue believed that they had such strongholds in her life, which is a reasonable
possibility. In any event, we need not go into such advanced discussions to be able to draw some basic conclusions.
       Solomon gives enough explanation to provide a reasonable answer as to why Sue may not have been
successful in rebuking demonic forces, even in the name of Jesus. Trying to rebuke demons in the name of Jesus
can be met with spectacular failure in certain cases (Acts 19:13-17). Submission to Jesus must proceed confrontation
with demons. If we have not submitted to God first, then we hardly have legal grounds to oppose Satan and suppose
that he will flee. Part of that submission to God includes confession. Solomon is superior to some nikologists in
recognizing that confession is necessary for victory. He correctly understands that 1Jn 1:9 conditions a believer’s
fellowship with God on the believer’s confession of sin. Moreover, he correctly conditions a believer’s victory over
demonic forces on our practicing 1Jn 1:9 as part of our submission to God. However, sometimes that confession to
God must include reconciliation with others when possible.
       For neurotics who have unjustly criticized others, this would require that they not only confess their sin to
God but confess to those whom they have unjustly criticized (cp. Mt 5:23). In my assessment, Sue owes me an
apology, and her harmony with God and her victory in demonology are probably not going to be all that they should
be until she renders a confession. It is important, for her sake, that I proceed with this book in hopes that she will
respond in such a way that she can experience the intimacy with God and victory over dark forces that she desires.
       I am not only morally obligated out of my love for her to point this out, the Bible explicitly requires me to
do so:
      15
        “Moreover if your brother sins against you, go and tell him his fault between you and him alone. If
      he hears you, you have gained your brother. 16 “But if he will not hear, take with you one or two more,
      that ‘by the mouth of two or three witnesses every word may be established.’ 17 “And if he refuses to
      hear them, tell it to the church. But if he refuses even to hear the church, let him be to you like a
      heathen and a tax collector. (Mt 18:15-17; ESV)

       To her credit, I believe Sue was honestly trying to reconcile the matter between us when she came to our
house and demanded that I apologize for hurting her feelings. Good for her in trying to seek harmony between us.
The problem is that I see her at fault, not me. Thus, since I believe she is the one who owes me a confession, we
were at an impasse. Harmony with God and victory over demons is at stake. This is not a matter I take lightly or
am dealing with superficially. I greatly value that harmony and victory. Hence, I have poured a lot of time and effort
into this book.
                                                      Conclusion
        One obstacle with my fulfilling this biblical mandate in Mt 18:15-17 fully, however, is that it takes time for
me to comply with the biblical mandate fully, to explain fully why I believe Sue is at fault. I have done so in a
limited capacity in our conversations, to be sure, even in the presence of two witnesses—Dianne and Sam—and
they concurred that Sue was being irrational. Nevertheless, they were not able to understand the full scope of my
response. Thus, the book is necessary for their sakes as well. Therefore, in order to comply with the biblical mandate,
as fully as possible, I persevere in writing this book in hopes that the present unreconciled state will be temporary,
not permanent. I want to gain Sue, not lose her. Yet it must be done on biblical grounds. Neither God nor demons
are going to be impressed with any other grounds. One cannot cry vantage point when the Bible cries foul. The
difference between us is not merely one of vantage point. Victory in resisting demonic forces is not going to be
achieved by trying to sweep this matter under the proverbial rug. The demons will see the dirt under the rug and
walk right over it, even if the rug has Jesus’ name on it. Therefore, I am taking time to write this book in hopes that
it will help move beyond the impasse. Otherwise, calamitous results may be expected in the days ahead. A train
being driven by feelings will, sooner or later, come to a fateful end—at the Bema, if not before.
Page 72   Degrees of Love and Forgiveness
                            Chapter 5. Dobson’s Tough Love
                                                    Introduction
       When our children were young, Dianne and I took different approaches toward disciplining them. Dianne
was inclined to scold and plead. I did neither. I instructed and disciplined. Books by James Dobson on parenting
and disciplining were among my favorite. This passage from his book, The Strong-Willed Child, is illustrative of
his approach:

                Should a spanking hurt? Yes, or else it will have no influence. A swat on the behind through
         three layers of wet diapers simply conveys no urgent message. However, a small amount of pain for a
         young child goes a long way; it is certainly not necessary to lash or “whip” him. Two or three stinging
         strokes on the legs or bottom with a switch are usually sufficient to emphasize the point, “You must
         obey me.” And finally, it is important to spank immediately after the offense, or not at all. A toddler’s
         memory is not sufficiently developed to permit even a ten-minute delay in the administration of justice.
         Then after the episode is over and the tears subsided, the child might want to be held and reassured by
         his mother or father. By all means, let him come. Embrace him in the security of your loving arms.
         Rock him softly. Tell him how much you love him and why he must “mind his mommie.” This moment
         can be the most important event in the entire day.108 (Emphasis his.)

       I wanted my boys to know two facts from their earliest ages: (1) I loved them; (2) I would discipline them.
Daily, I would hug them, kiss them, and tell them that I loved them. Plus, I would pray with them and play with
them. Yet I kept a flyswatter in each room of the house. If they disobeyed me, I would immediately administer the
flyswatter. My boys complained, “But, dad, flyswatters are for flies.” However, I was firm in taking what Dobson
had to say to heart, and I applied the flyswatter consistently and lovingly. At an earlier age, when our boys were
very small, they had the propensity to toddle over to the TV and start turning the knobs. I shook my head, “No,”
and promptly thumped them on the back of the hand.109 They quickly learned that when I told them they could not
do something or shook my head, “No,” this meant that they could not do so. When their mother told them that they
could not do something, on the other hand, it merely meant that they should not do so. They quickly discerned the
difference between her “No,” and my “No.” Since Dianne only followed up her statements with more statements,
her statements were not nearly as persuasive as mine. I followed up my statements with action.
       I vividly recall being at my dad’s house, and Jeremy, our older son, toddled over to my dad’s TV to play with
the knobs. Jeremy reached up to turn the knob, paused with his hand in midair, and turned to look at me. I was laid
back in the recliner. I simply gave one shake of my head, “No.” Jeremy put his hand down and toddled off crying.
I did not have to do anything. I remarked to my dad, “When Dianne tells them, ‘No,’ it means that they should not.
When I tell them, ‘No,’ it means that they cannot.”
       When they were a little older and could run a little, Dianne and I took the boys to a café. It was a rather long
café and had a door at the opposite end. One of our boys started running for the door at the opposite end of the café.
They were old enough by this time that they would have been able to open the door. In fear that our son would go
out the door, Dianne urged me to run after him and stop him. I just stood there and waited, as she frantically kept
urging me to stop him. I did not chase him. I just stood there quietly waiting. When he got to the door, he reached
up, paused with his hand in the air, and turned back to look at me. I simply gave one shake of my head, “No.” He
promptly turned around and came back. Dianne was perturbed with me and asked, “Why did you not stop him?” I
responded, “I did stop him. I shook my head, ‘No.’” I share this background to illustrate the differences in
temperament that my wife and I take when it comes to discipline. It has not changed over the years.
                                              Discipline is for Girls Too
       As Sue and her husband were sitting on our couch, I explained to Sue that I would not tolerate her rude
behavior toward Dianne or myself, that I believed in discipline and that this is the way I raised my two boys. Sue
retorted that it was too bad that I did not have any daughters because, if I did, I would realize that daughters must
be dwelt with differently, using this to justify her expectation that I just listen empathetically to her share her

108
      James Dobson, The Strong-Willed Child: Birth Through Adolescence (Wheaton, IL: Tyndale House, 1978), 47.
109
      Dobson advocates a thump, Parenting, 92.
Page 74                                                                              Degrees of Love and Forgiveness
emotions. True, I have not had any daughters, but I regarded her retort to be nonsense. Dobson has had much
experience counseling for parents of girls, and he opens his book, Dare to Discipline, with this illustration:

             A mother recently asked for help in handling her defiant three-year-old daughter, Sandy. She
      had realized that her tiny little girl had hopelessly beaten her in a conflict of wills, and the child had
      become a tyrant and a dictator. On the afternoon prior to our conversation, an incident occurred which
      was typical of Sandy’s way of doing business: the mother (I’ll call her Mrs. Nichols) put the youngster
      down for a nap, although it was unlikely that she would stay in bed. Sandy is not accustomed to doing
      anything she doesn’t want to do, and naptime is not on her acceptable list at the moment. On this
      occasion, however, the child was more interested in antagonizing her mom than in merely having her
      own way. Sandy began to scream. She yelled loudly enough to upset the whole neighborhood, fraying
      Mrs. Nichols’ jangled nerves. Then she tearfully demanded various things, including a glass of water.
      At first Mrs. Nichols refused to comply with the orders, but she surrendered when Sandy’s screaming
      again reached a peak of intensity. As the glass of water was delivered, the little tigress pushed it aside,
      refusing to drink because her mother had not brought it soon enough. Mrs. Nichols stood offering the
      water for a few minutes, then said she would take it back to the kitchen if Sandy did not drink by the
      time she counted to five. Sandy set her jaw and waited through the count. “. . . three, four, five!” As
      Mrs. Nichols grasped the glass and walked toward the kitchen, the child again screamed for water.
      Sandy dangled her harassed mom back and forth like a yo-yo until she tired of the sport.
             ....This mother had read that a child will eventually respond to patience and tolerance,
      ruling out the need for discipline. She had been told to encourage the child’s rebellion because it
      offered a valuable release of hostility. She attempted to implement the recommendation of the
      experts who suggested that she verbalize the child’s feelings in a moment of conflict....She has
      been taught that conflicts between parent and child were to be perceived as inevitable
      misunderstandings or differences in viewpoint. Unfortunately, Mrs. Nichols and her advisors
      were wrong! She and her child were involved in no simple difference of opinion; she was being
      challenged, mocked, and defied by her daughter. No heart-to-heart talk would resolve this nose-to-
      nose confrontation, because the real issue was totally unrelated to the water or the nap or other aspects
      of the particular circumstances. The actual meaning behind this conflict and a hundred others was
      simply this: Sandy was brazenly rejecting the authority of her mother. The way Mrs. Nichols handled
      this confrontation would determine the nature of their future relationship; she could not ignore it.110

Daughters need discipline just like boys, not just as toddlers, but also as teens:

             Mr. Holloway was the father of a teen-age girl named Becky. He came to see me in desperation
      one afternoon and related the cause for this concern. Becky had never been required to obey or respect
      her parents, and her early years were hectic for the entire family. Mrs. Holloway was confident that
      Becky would eventually become more manageable, but that improvement never came. This child held
      her parents in utter contempt from her youngest childhood. She was sullen, disrespectful, selfish, and
      uncooperative. Mr. and Mrs. Holloway did not feel they had the right to make demands on their
      daughter, so they smiled politely and pretended not to notice. Their magnanimous attitude became
      more difficult to maintain as Becky steamrolled into puberty and adolescence. She was a perpetual
      malcontent, sneering at her family in disgust. Mr. and Mrs. Holloway were afraid to antagonize her in
      any way because she would throw the most violent tantrums imaginable. They were victims of
      emotional blackmail.111

      Dobson’s description of emotional blackmail was provided earlier.112 Sue’s tearful protests—that her
emotions be made the paramount focus of our concern—reminded me of Sandy’s tearfully demands that Dobson
describes here. Such crying can be a “weapon. It becomes a tool of protest to punish the enemy” (p. 52). Sue’s tears
did not work on me, however. We had invited Sue and her husband over for discussion and dinner that Saturday.
110
    James Dobson, Dare to Discipline (Wheaton, IL: Tyndale House, 1970),11-12.

Since the only thing Sue wanted to discuss was her emotions, though, and I would not play along, she brought out
the tears. I was not impressed by the waterworks and invited her to sit in the living room crying, if she liked, or go
to the study and cry while Sam, Dianne, and I went into the dining room to eat. Sam, Dianne, and I thought this was
a reasonable course of action since Sue was determined to keep crying about her emotions not being made the focus
of the conversation. Apparently, Dobson would concur with our decision not to let her crying ruin our dinner.
                                               Disciplinary Extinction
       Dobson provides examples of how children, parents, adults, and teachers respond to a form of behavior
modification called extinction—by which certain forms of behavior are eliminated. The example he provides of a
daughter is applicable here, when the daughter says, “‘You don’t love me anymore.’ Most children know their
parents are anxious to convey their love, so they use this delicate issue to extinguish punishment. It often succeeds.”
Such children claim that their parents do not love them anymore to “manipulate their parents” (p. 86). Sue’s
manipulate emotional blackmail and tears did not work on me because I was not going to try to convey my love or
concern for her by caving in to her demands that I make her feelings the barometer of my love or concern. When
she said that I did not care for her anymore because I had stopped listening empathetically to her somebody-done-
me-wrong-song, I simply pointed out that her accusations were factually untrue. Granted, I was not going to
continue to listen empathetically to her worn-out tune. However, this did not prove that I did not care about her.
Instead, I would demonstrate my concern for her by taking loving disciplinary action, namely, proceed with writing
this book.
       As far as my inviting Sue to go cry/pout in another room while Sam, Dianne, and I ate, Dodson gives a rather
appropriate illustration:

                Extinction occasionally happens accidentally, as it did in the case of four-year-old Mark. His
        mother and father were concerned about his irritating habit of throwing temper tantrums. He would
        select the time when his parents least wanted him to misbehave; when guests were visiting in their
        home, he could be expected to explode at bedtime, if not before. Mark repeated the same emotional
        performance in restaurants, church services and other public places. His parents were no strangers to
        discipline, and they unloaded all their resources on their little rebel. They spanked him, stood him in
        the corner, sent him to bed early, shamed and scolded him. Nothing worked. The temper tantrums
        continued on a regular basis. Finally, they reached the point of exasperation. They didn’t know what
        else to do. Then one evening Mark’s parents were reading the newspaper in their living room. They
        had said something that angered him, and he fell on the floor in a rage. He screamed and whacked his
        head on the carpet, kicking, and flailing his small arms. They didn’t know what to do with him, so they
        did nothing. They went on reading the paper. That reaction was totally unexpected by villainous Mark.
        He got up, looked at his father, and fell down for Act II. Again his parents made no response. Mark’s
        yelling stopped abruptly. He went over to his mother and shook her arm, then collapsed for Act III.
        Still they made no move toward him. He apparently felt so silly flopping alone on the floor that he
        never threw another tantrum. In retrospect, it is clear that the reinforcement for Mark’s tantrums was
        parental manipulation. He was able to get those big, powerful adults upset and distraught through
        this violent behavior. Tantrums are more frequently a form of challenging behavior which can be
        eliminated by one or more appropriate spankings; for a few children, like Mark, the spanking itself is
        actually a form of reinforcement. For Mark, like the pyromaniac, it was rewarding to see how much
        commotion he could precipitate.113

Mark’s attempt at parental manipulation had no impact because his parents finally ignored his tantrum and
continued reading their newspaper. Similarly, Sue’s tears had no impact on me because she was trying to use her
tears to manipulate the conversation, seeking to keep it focused on her emotions. Such manipulation should be met
with disciplinary extinction. Dianne and I had told Sue months earlier that we would love her and treat her as a
daughter. Well, Dobson’s methodology is the way I would treat a son or a daughter. Such discipline is not gender
specific. Dianne intervened somewhat on Sue’s behalf by telling her I did not have experience with daughters, so
my response may not be as well suited for Sue as it was for our sons. However, Dianne and I did not always see
eye-to-eye in my discipline of our sons either when they were small. Consequently, Dianne’s reluctance to such

113
      Dobson, Discipline, 81-82.
Page 76                                                                              Degrees of Love and Forgiveness
discipline in Sue’s case was no surprise. Still, for the most part, Dianne is supportive and recognizes the need for
discipline, much like Mark’s mother did.

                                               Child-Adult Discipline
        “But Sue is an adult!” someone will exclaim. True, but Sue was acting like a child. In any event, discipline
is not just for children and adolescents. Adults need it as well. In his subsequent book, Love Must be Tough, Dobson
tells how he uses discipline in his counseling practice when dealing with adults. He relates a letter that he received
from Linda, whose husband was having an affair. Linda nagged and complained, to no avail. She endured his affair
out of love for him. In explaining her mentality, Dobson acknowledges that Eph 5:22 tells wives to be submissive
to their husbands and that 1Cor 13:4-7 tell us:
      4
        Love is patient, love is kind, and is not jealous; love does not brag and is not arrogant, 5 does not act
      unbecomingly; it does not seek its own, is not provoked, does not take into account a wrong suffered,
      6
        does not rejoice in unrighteousness, but rejoices with the truth; 7 bears all things, believes all things,
      hopes all things, endures all things. 8 Love never fails.

       Nevertheless, Dobson counters: “I am of the opinion that Linda’s tolerance and longsuffering will probably
be fatal to her marriage, and that her advisor has misinterpreted the Bible.”114 As already explained in the present
book, this Corinthian passage about love occurs in what many have termed a sorrowful letter because Paul certainly
disciplines the Corinthians in this epistle.115 Love does not exclude disciplinary action. To the contrary, biblical
love demands discipline. As to being submissive, Dobson goes on to explain: “But there is a vast difference between
being a confident, spiritually submissive woman and being a doormat” (emphasis his.)116 As this applies to the
present discussion, we should not allow ourselves to be a neurotic’s doormat. I certainly refused to be Sue’s
doormat. In such cases, one would do well to apply the title of Brown’s book to their situation, No More Mr. Nice
Guy! Saying Goodbye to ‘Doormat’ Christianity.117 Dobson explains the correlation between the discipline of adults
and children:

             Let me make one more attempt to explain why appeasement, even in the name of
      Christianity, can prove fatal to a marriage. Just as toddlers and teenagers will challenge the authority
      of their parents precisely for the purpose of testing their confidence and courage, a husband or wife
      will sometimes do the same. They, like children, want to feel the security of loving discipline which
      says, “Go this far and no farther.” There is safety in defined limits for human beings of all ages.
             In other books I have described what happens when a parent collapses in response to consistent
      challenges from a child. Respect is lost when the question is asked, “How tough are you?” and the
      answer comes back, “I’m made of Jell-O!” Not only does the child begin to feel that the parent is
      unworthy of his respect, but he senses a lack of love in the relationship, too. Genuine love demands
      toughness in moments of crisis. It’s true for grownups, too.
             As indicated above, adults will occasionally challenge one another for the same reasons
      they challenged their parents as children. Unconsciously, perhaps, they are asking the question,
      “How much courage do you have, and do you love me enough to stop me from doing this foolish
      thing?” What they need in that moment is loving discipline that forces them to choose between good
      and bad alternatives. What they don’t need, contrary to the suggestions offered above, is
      permissiveness, understanding, excuses, removal of guilt and buckets of tender loving care. To
114
    James Dobson, Love Must Be Tough (Waco, TX: Word Books, 1983), 16.
115
    See footnote 58 herein.
116
    Dobson, Tough, 25.
117
    Stephen Brown, No More Mr. Nice Guy! Saying Goodbye to ‘Doormat’ Christianity (Nashville, TN: Thomas
Nelson, 1986). As to the present discussion, a couple of quotes will suffice. “Your mind controls your actions and
emotions; your will controls your mind; you control your will. Therefore, you control your actions and your
emotions” (emphasis his, 28). This is another way of saying that you have a choice. You do not have to let your
emotions control you. “Love looks out for the interests of others. Love sometimes is harsh, strong, and even angry,
but it is never destructive” (p. 59). Just because you draw a hard line in the sand in dealing with a neurotic does not
mean that you are unloving. The Lord did not call us to be wimps but to take a stand.
Chapter 5. Dobson’s Tough Love                                                                                    Page 77
         dole out that kind of smother-love at such a time is to reinforce irresponsibility and generate disrespect.
         It deprives the marriage of mutual accountability!118 (Italics his.)

        Sue wanted me to appease her. Dobson says, “Appeasement...can prove fatal.” Sue wanted tender love.
Dobson says, “What they don’t need...is...tender loving care.” Dobson calls for tough love instead, a disciplinary
love that requires accountability. But doesn’t the Bible call for us to be tender-hearted? Sure. Paul says, “Be kind
to one another, tender-hearted, forgiving each other, just as God in Christ also has forgiven you” (Eph 4:32; cp. Col
3:13). But let it be noted that such tender-hearted forgiveness is not unconditional. Paul will elsewhere tell us to
reprove and rebuke those in error (1Tim 5:20; 2Tim 4:2; Tit 1:13; 2:15). In Eph 4:32 and Col 3:13, Paul merely
summarizes that God’s children are to forgive just as God has forgiven them. Where did he get that from? From
Jesus. Commenting on Col 3:13, NICNT rightly observes: “The point, however, is to reinforce the interdependence
between forgiving and being forgiven (as, again, in Matt. 6:12, 14–15; 18:23–35).” Likewise, TNTC comments that
Paul is “echoing (perhaps intentionally) the parable of the Unforgiving Servant in Matthew 18:23-35.” The
stratification and conditionality of forgiveness in that parable are self-apparent. As to the other passage, Jesus
summarized, “Forgive us our debts, as we also have forgiven our debtors....But if you do not forgive men, then your
Father will not forgive your transgressions” (Mt 6:12,15). Paul would have had access to this teaching from the
Lord via, Luke, his traveling companion (Lk 11:4). Jesus taught that God’s forgiveness of His children was layered
and conditional. Familial forgiveness, the forgiveness which takes place within the family, is not necessarily free.
        The Greek word for forgive in Eph 4:32 and Col 3:13 is charizomai, which means to graciously forgive.
Because the root is associated with grace, some commentators wrongly think that it means to freely forgive. NAC
is a case in point: “‘Forgiving’ is based on the root word for ‘grace.’ It carries the idea of a free forgiveness, perhaps
because of grace” (cf. Col 3:13). The duplicity of this simplicity has already been refuted sufficiently in TOD
regarding other texts. Two separate examples from TOD will suffice to illustrate the point:

         The only other occurrence of granted (charizomai) in Philippians is in 2:9 and would suggest that what
         is given is not necessarily freely given independently of one’s performance (cf. 2:8). More
         emphatically, in this other Philippian passage, what is given is a reward based on performance, where
         the Father graced the Son with an exalted name because of what the Son did. As will be discussed
         more fully concerning Gal 3:18, the promise of rewards is graciously given (charizomai). We do not
         deserve either the God-given opportunity or ability to earn rewards. Nevertheless, if we avail ourselves
         of this opportunity to earn rewards through God’s imparted ability, then we do in fact merit such
         rewards. That which is offered has to be received. It is not irresistibly forced upon us. What is freely
         given is the opportunity and ability to earn the reward, not the reward itself. Consequently, another
         reason the Calvinistic interpretation of this passage must be rejected is because it misconstrues the
         nature of charizomai. This verb does not prove that what is given is a gift, much less an irresistible
         gift.

                For example, Rom 8:31-32 is taken as proving that God will freely give (without merit) all
         believers (without exception) all things (including co-rulership with Christ). His position is faulty
         exegetically in that charizomai need not necessarily mean freely, it may simply mean graciously. God
         graciously gives overcomers rulership of the universe. Just as in Heb 2:8 we find God submitting all
         things to man, and yet understand this in context to be a limited class of men (i.e., faithful brethren),
         so too in Rom 8:32 we find contextual delineation of these co-rulers with Christ as the faithful elect
         who suffer with Christ.

        As we have seen in chapter one of the present book, once we have moved beyond the soteriological level and
become God’s children, His forgiveness at these higher planes is conditioned on our obedience. Not recognizing
this is destructive for Christian counseling. Do not grant tender-hearted forgiveness until after the conditions for it
have been met. Dobson appropriately surmises, “Total forgiveness” is “entirely appropriate after a
reconciliation has occurred” but is frequently destructive if granted before (italics his, 54). In a variety of
situations, “the best approach is to force a crisis that will bring the matter to a head. Then it can be treated
and resolved” (p. 56). When Sue came to our house with her husband after she wrote a six-page letter telling me
118
      Dobson, Tough, 55.
Page 78                                                                             Degrees of Love and Forgiveness
and Dianne how unloving and uncaring we are, I chose this force-a-crisis course of action, as Dobson advises:
“When the old patterns persist...I would urge her to precipitate a crisis of major proportions” (pp. 58-59). “By
making it clear that there are limits to what you can tolerate, you are showing self-respect and confidence” (p. 62).
We must set limits to how much ill behavior we will tolerate.

      Why else will a toddler or a five-year-old or a teenager deliberately disobey his parents for no other
      reason than to determine how far Mom and Dad can be pushed? That same urge to test the limits causes
      students to harass teachers, employees to challenge bosses, privates to disobey sergeants, and so on.
      And regrettably, it leads some husbands and wives to test the ones they love, too. What is required in
      each instance is discipline and self-respect by the one on trial....
             The precipitated crisis, first, must be accompanied by an entire change of attitude. Instead of
      begging, pleading, wringing your hands and whimpering like an abused puppy, you as the vulnerable
      partner must appear strangely calm and assured. The key word is confidence, and it is of maximum
      importance.” (Italics his, 63).

       I purposefully precipitated a crisis as Dobson instructed and am fully confident that (1) I took the best course
of action available to me in that situation and (2) that Sue’s accusations were unfounded. Consequently, I would
not waver despite her spite. Dianne and I being loving and caring were not the issue. Rather, the issue was Sue’s
temper tantrums. Her behavior, not our emotions, were the issue. As to Sue’s accusation that my taking such an
approach is unloving, Dobson responds that those following his advice are actually being loving:

      I certainly believe in the validity of unconditional love, and in fact, the mutual accountability I have
      recommended is an expression of that love! For example, if a husband (or wife) is behaving in ways
      that will harm himself, his children, his marriage and the family of the “other woman,” then
      confrontation with him becomes an act of love. The easiest response by the innocent partner would
      be to look the other way and pretend she doesn’t notice. But from my perspective, that is tantamount
      to a parent’s refusing to confront a fourteen-year-old who comes home drunk at 4:00 A.M. That mother
      or father has an obligation to create a crisis in response to destructive behavior. I’m trying to say that
      unconditional love is not synonymous with permissiveness, passivity and weakness. Sometimes it
      requires toughness and discipline and accountability. (Italics his, 81)
                                                    Enablers
       Dobson’s approach for dealing with addiction is applicable to those caught in a neurotic rejection since, in
both cases, “you can’t conquer a physiological problem with willpower. It’s like trying to stop diarrhea by making
up your mind to do so” (p. 170). Neurotic addicts must make a choice, but it is not simply a choice to rely on
willpower. Rather, they need to make a choice to learn how to rely on the power of the Holy Spirit. Family and
friends counseling them must make a choice too:

             1. Do not nag, complain, scream, cry, beg, plead, embarrass, label or berate the victim. He has
      a disease which he can’t control. It is not within his power to overcome it alone.
             2. Do not keep the alcoholic’s life together by lying to his boss, covering for his
      irresponsibilities, bailing him out of jail and paying his bills. Such a rescuer is called an enabler, and
      he may actually prolong and worsen the problem. (Emphasis his, 170-171.)

Family and friends taking non-disciplinary action are enablers. Such wimpy action can actually make the problem
worse. However, they are not called upon to resolve the problem without help:

             First, it is virtually impossible to resolve this problem without outside help. In a very real
      sense, the entire family shares the sickness of the alcoholic. They are affected by rage, depression,
      disillusionment, despair, financial fear, denial, low self-esteem and myriad other emotions that
      accompany this illness. They are wounded in spirit and need the loving concern of those who have
      been there. (P. 171.)

      I did not expect for me and Dianne to be able to resolve Sue’s issue. For one thing, we were the objects of
Chapter 5. Dobson’s Tough Love                                                                               Page 79
her rage. Therefore, I advised the neutral third-party counsel of those who have a background in working with those
suffering from rejection syndrome. Unfortunately, the counseling service that we thought would be helpful did not
live up to our expectations. However, I found that Solomon’s materials were available online and recommended
those resources in addition to his two books I had previously recommended. In the meantime, I worked on producing
the present book, partially as a form of discipline and instruction, despite knowing that third-party supplementation
would certainly be necessary. Thankfully, I don’t have to try to reinvent the wheel when excellent third-party
resources are available.
                                                Sinful Apology
      Dobson relates an episode in which his father was unjustly critical of him. His dad apologized and harmony
was restored. There have been times when I have had to apologize to my sons also. Nevertheless, Dodson believes
that most of the time, this would be unwise when dealing with a strong-willed person:

             Go back with me now to the story I told about my dad’s apology during our brief disagreement
      in the back yard. He took all the blame for that confrontation and in essence, “ate humble pie.” I must
      make it clear that it is not always wise to assume this posture. In fact, I believe most parents of very
      difficult teenagers go too far in that direction. There is a time for parents to get off their knees and
      quit apologizing. They have sought to avoid conflict by groveling and appeasing their strong-willed
      adolescents, and in so doing, they have made what turns out to be a tragic mistake. Please remember
      this fact: To a power-hungry tyrant of any age, appeasement only inflames his lust for more
      power. Behavioral research has now demonstrated this relationship between insecure, permissive
      parents and violent, delinquent teenagers.119 (Emphasis his.)

If you are not at fault, don’t take the blame. Don’t apologize. Do not appease the manipulator. To apologize in such
a case would be sin, enabling the sinner to continue sinning, leading to more destructive behavior on the
manipulator’s part, making the matter much worse.120 After making such warnings, Dobson then proceeds with an
extended praise of the TOUGHLOVE organization started by Phillis and David York.121 He relates an appropriate
response from Ann Landers regarding a 19-year-old who was blaming her parents for her maladjustments:

      She had no friends and couldn’t hold a job because she was always shooting her mouth off, telling
      acquaintances, colleagues and bosses exactly what she thought of them. “I was brought up that
      way...” she declared. “I was allowed to say anything to anybody. My parents raised me that way
      and now I am all messed up and it’s their fault. Any suggestions, Ann?”
             I [Ann Landers] replied, “Yes . . . accept responsibility and quit blaming your parents for
      your mean mouth and foul moods. If you don’t like the way you are—go to work on yourself and
      become something different. Enough of this ‘You damaged me. Now take care of me’ nonsense.
      It’s a cop-out. Guilt laid on parents by you kids is so thick you can cut it with a knife—and all it does
      is perpetuate financial and emotional dependence and create a climate of hostility and ultimate failure.”
             [Dobson responds:] So what can parents do with kids who have them backed against the wall?
      They can make a 180-degree turn and go the TOUGHLOVE route. Heaven knows reasoning, pleading,
      crying, threatening and bribing hasn’t worked. It’s time to try something else, and I believe self-help
      groups like TOUGHLOVE are the most effective approach to problem-solving....
             You need TOUGHLOVE if you feel helpless and unable to cope with your teenagers’ behavior
      or if you feel victimized by them, disappointed in yourself as a parent, guilty because you think you
      have done a rotten job and are frightened by the potential for violence in yourself and your children.
      These feelings are experienced by the affluent, the disadvantaged, middle income families, the
      uneducated, intellectual, single, divorced, married, permissive, repressive, black and white. Anybody.
             Remember, you have the right to a night’s sleep without worrying where your kid is—or being
      awakened by a phone call from the police or a hospital or a drunk teenager who’s stranded somewhere.
      It’s time you started taking care of yourself and letting your teenager be responsible for his or her

119
    Dodson, Parenting, 168.
120
    Ibid., 169.
121
    Ibid., 170ff.
Page 80                                                                              Degrees of Love and Forgiveness
         actions. That’s where the concepts of TOUGHLOVE come in.
               You must find the courage to withdraw your money, influence, affection, anger, guilt and
         pleas that he or she learn to shape up. You must begin to make real demands entailing severe
         consequences. You must make it clear that you will not live in a house with people who mistreat you
         and do not respect the rules you have laid down. You do not need your teenager’s approval. You’re
         the boss. The sooner your youngster understands this, the better.122

       Sue was intent on blaming her father and me (her counseling-surrogate father) for her emotional outbursts.
This nonsense was her cop-out for her not taking responsibility for her own actions. Does this mean that her father
was not as bad as she claimed? Not necessarily. He may have been every bit as bad as she claimed. It makes no
difference in terms of the necessity that she must take responsibility for her behavior. She, not her dad, was the one
sitting in our living room that Saturday evening. I was not going to allow her to use his behavior to justify her
behavior. We needed to deal with her behavior, not his. What she needed was accountability, not sympathy.
                                                Sinful Transparency
        When Sue wrote her six-page letter to me and Dianne, criticizing us for being so unloving and uncaring, she
justified it in the name of being transparent about her feelings. Appealing to transparency was supposed to justify
her hostility. It did not work. There was no justification for her unrestrained overcommunication. Dobson describes
this issue in an interview with a fellow counselor:

                LUSH: Over-closeness can cause a marriage to become very dry. When a couple becomes tied
         to one another in this way, when they feed off each other exclusively, they lose some of the magic.
         You see, I feel a woman should preserve what I call the “mystique” that is uniquely hers. There should
         always be a bit of “mystery” in her personality.
                DOBSON: Do you think it is possible for a husband and wife to destroy this sense of mystery
         by overcommunicating?
                LUSH: I certainly do. I have counseled couples that complained about not loving each other
         anymore, even though they communicated beautifully. When they told me how they communicated, I
         then understood why love had died. They had destroyed the mystique and left only the bare ugliness.
         That’s why I hate to see absolute honesty in marriage.
                DOBSON: Explain what you mean by that.
                LUSH: Well, let’s consider what happens to a woman throughout the menstrual cycle. She can
         have some pretty depressing days, especially during the premenstrual stage. Speaking personally, I
         had a very biting, vicious tongue at that time, and that is not unusual. Now, if a woman believes she
         has a right— even an obligation—to be totally honest even when she knows her perception is distorted,
         she can say some horrid things that she doesn’t really mean. That can be very hard on a marriage. It is
         simply not healthy to dump all the ugliness on your partner. We need to exercise some discipline in
         what we say to one another.
                DOBSON: When you say you are opposed to total honesty in a marriage, you’re not suggesting
         that husbands and wives be dishonest with one another, are you?
                LUSH: No. But I am saying we don’t have to verbalize every thought that comes in our heads.
         Love is a delicate flower that must be nurtured. It doesn’t like heat and it doesn’t like cold. Remember
         that husbands and wives live in fleshly bodies; we are not saints, and too much ugly reality can take
         the edge off our affection for one another.
                Dobson: You also worry about women who lean too heavily on their husbands in their thirties,
         expecting them to carry them emotionally—
                LUSH: I know of no better way to set up a mid-life crisis ten years later than for a woman to
         demand more of her husband than he can give in his thirties. (Emphasis his, 181-182.)

Carrying another person emotionally is not our responsibility, as Dobson goes on to explain in more detail: “But
every story has two sides, and John’s version should also be told. His wife is wrong to believe that her contentment
is exclusively his burden. No one should be expected to carry another person emotionally. Only Diane can make

122
      Ibid., 178-179.
Chapter 5. Dobson’s Tough Love                                                                              Page 81
herself happy! She has no right to lay that total load on John” (p. 178). Furthermore, we should exercise self-
discipline when being transparent. Otherwise, we should be disciplined by others if we fail to do so. In their book,
Happiness is a Choice, Minirth and Meier give boundaries for transparency:

             Most two- and three-year-olds, for example, are very much in touch with their angry feelings.
      When they feel angry, they know it and they express it, sometimes in appropriate and sometimes in
      inappropriate ways. When our children feel angry toward us, we encourage them to share their angry
      feelings in a respectful way. In fact, we thank them for doing so. If they show their anger by kicking,
      however, we spank them. However, many parents discourage their young children from sharing their
      angry feelings, even appropriately. In fact some parents punish their children for sharing normal angry
      feelings appropriately.
             Do you remember as a child ever telling your father, “Daddy, I’m really mad at you right now.
      Could we talk about it?” Many of you probably learned to fear your anger instead, because being aware
      of and sharing your anger resulted in rejection or punishment. You then learned to deceive yourself
      into thinking you weren’t really angry at all. You learned to repress your anger and displace it by
      kicking the dog or fighting with your siblings.123

One need not choose between emotional repression and emotional displacement. Respectful-restrained articulation
is a healthy mediation. Sue’s barrage against me and Dianne was reminiscent of a disrespectful emotional three-
year-old. I dealt with it by administering a verbal spanking, so to speak. In other words, I was firm in my response,
pointing out her irrational inconsistency and requiring that her issue be addressed scripturally rather than
emotionally by allowing her emotions to dictate the conversation.
       Returning now, for a moment, to Solomon’s Handbook to Happiness, his words on emotional catharsis are
applicable to the venting done in the name of transparency:

             One form of psychotherapy advocates letting out the feelings—emotional catharsis—as a
      means of venting hostility. This is workable in the therapeutic climate, but not everyone has a
      safe situation in which to dump their hostility. Society at large does not accept negative feelings
      with tender loving care. Many people who are filled with hostility have no acceptable means of dealing
      with it. They cannot verbalize it satisfactorily, and they cannot exercise violence toward other people
      to get it out. They keep all or some of it inside....
             ....A more homespun definition of depression is that of an internal temper tantrum. (Pp. 32-34).

Solomon does not advocate psychotherapy as the solution:

             In psychotherapy, of whatever persuasion, self is strengthened to cope with those problems.
      Herein lies the basic problem with psychotherapy. With enough psychotherapy, some of the symptoms
      will respond so that the person may become better adjusted and the symptoms may either diminish or
      leave. But, in order to cope with those symptoms, the person builds better defense mechanisms and
      learns more acceptable behaviors, and self becomes stronger. Thus, when symptoms improve as a
      result of pure psychotherapy, the real problem, self-centeredness, always gets worse. This result is
      diametrically opposed to what God does, because God’s way of dealing with self is that it must become
      weaker and weaker until its control is finally phased out. Self is reduced to nothing so that Christ can
      be everything. This is the process by which Christ becomes the center of the life. (Emphasis his, 35.)

Rather than using venting hostility as a form of psychotherapy, Solomon’s Spirituotherapy should be
employed to deal with Self, and Dobson’s tough love should be used to enforce self-control.124
      During the time frame in which I was working on this book, someone at work cited a scientific study
supposedly showing that people who use profanity are more honest.125 I immediately expressed my skepticism and
123
    Frank B. Minirth and Paul D. Meier, Happiness is a Choice: A Manual on the Symptoms, Causes and Cures of
Depression (Grand Rapids, MI: Baker Book House, 1978), 48.

    Also see Carnal Venting, 85; Foolish Venting, 105.

    See “People Who Swear Are More Honest.” Available at http://blogs.discovermagazine.com/seriouslyscience

promptly cited a counter scientific article. The point of interest as it relates to this book is that the pro-profanity
argument asserts: “Profanity is often used to express one’s genuine feelings and could therefore be negatively related
to dishonesty.” This double negative is claiming that profanity is often used to express one’s genuine feelings and
therefore could be positively related to honesty. In other words, people who use profanity are supposedly more
honest about their true feelings and therefore more honest people. As I pointed out to my coworker, this premise is
flawed in that it assumes a simplistic, straightforward connection between volition and emotion. Our volitional
choice as to whether to use profanity is not tied by honesty to our emotional state. For example, just because you
dislike something or someone does not mean that you have to use profanity to express your feelings. You do not
have to curse someone just because they upset you. Rather, you can choose to respond lovingly to them even though
you are upset with them. Furthermore, responding lovingly to them does not require that you feel lovey-dovey about
them. Being loving is not inseparably tied to feeling loving. As an act of love, you might choose to respond civilly
to someone even though you are feeling angry. The volitionally loving thing to do on certain occasions might be to
turn the other cheek rather than verbally slap their cheek. Honesty is not an issue. You are not being dishonest if
you turn the other cheek. Jesus was not advocating dishonesty when He advocated turning the other cheek (Mt 5:39).
Rather, He was demonstrating that volitional love does not require affectual love. You can love someone
volitionally without necessarily loving them affectionally. Sue sought to justify her harsh words by saying that she
was being “transparent.” However, at times emotional, transparency can be more closely associated with unloving
immaturity than loving honesty. Emotional transparency is not necessarily an expression of honesty. You can
volitionally love someone without emotionally liking them. Conversely, you can emotionally like someone without
volitionally loving them. Neither of these responses are necessarily a question of honesty. Of course, honesty can
be a factor. You can pretend to love or like someone when in fact you do not. Doing so would be dishonest. But
being pretentious is not necessary. The Lord is not calling us to be pretentious. Jesus did not say, “Pretend to love
your enemies” (Lk 6:27a). Rather, He commanded us to love our enemies, and then, in the next breath, He told us
how to do so honestly: “Do good to those who hate you” (Lk 6:27b). He did not say, “Feel good about those who
hate you.” Loving them does not require that we feel good about them. My way of doing good for Sue was to take
the time to write this book, even though she responded that this was proof that I don’t care. Thankfully, the Lord
will be the judge of that, not her. And I will be content with His judgment and prayerfully proceed to see this writing
project through to the end in anticipation of hearing His words, “Well done.”
                                           Belief is Volitionally Induced
       Happiness is a Choice is likewise a very good resource, written by Christian counselors, Minirth and Meier,
who implement Christian solutions to psychological needs. The authors insist that Christians can’t use I can’t as a
cop-out for saying things like, I can’t control my emotional outburst or I can’t believe. For the Bible says that we
can do whatever God desires for us to do through the power of the Spirit (Phil 4:13).127 God holds us accountable
to believe, for example:

             Belief in Christ starts with a choice of the will. The degree of the emotional conviction
      involved in the choice will differ from individual to individual. We have counseled quite a few
      individuals who were concerned whether they had believed enough. They seemed to be confusing
      belief with full emotional persuasion. We shared with them that their belief starts with the will.
      Emotions are sometimes evasive and hard to change because they may be set in psychological
      problems and roots of many years past. For example, a woman who could never trust or depend on her
      parents may have trouble feeling that she can always trust and depend on God. Belief for her should
      begin with the will; later, as she spends time growing in Christ and in His Word, her emotions
      will also change and she will become fully convinced.
             To repeat, belief must start with the will. Failure to accept this premise leads to the unresolvable
      problem of the degree of belief—what degree of belief or emotional persuasion does it take to save an
      individual? Where would the line be drawn, that is, the line determining whether belief is strong
      enough or still too weak? In Mark 9 is recorded the story of an epileptic boy. The father of the boy

      brought him to see Jesus. Jesus shared with the man that all things were possible if he believed. The
      man told Jesus that he did believe, but then he asked Him to help his unbelief. In other words, there
      were still some aspects to his emotions that were not fully convinced. However, with his will he
      was choosing to believe.128

Rather than embroil ourselves in nuances of the nature of faith, we can accept the proposition that faith is an
intellectual persuasion, not an act of the will, but this intellectual persuasion normally comes about as an act of the
will. “Faith comes from hearing, and hearing by the word of Christ” (Rom 10:17). But you have a choice as to
whether you will hear/listen to what the Bible has to say. Sue can believe that God loves and cares for her and that
we love and care for her. She can choose to listen to what we say or choose not to listen. She is not a helpless pawn,
left to the mercy of her emotions. She has a volitional role to pay and is thus accountable for the choice she makes.
Thus far, she has chosen not to believe us because she has chosen to allow her emotions to control what she believes
rather than choose to control her emotions through what she believes. We can choose to believe the facts by
meditating on the facts. Such faith is a passive persuasion that is actively and volitionally attained through the
appropriation of the supernatural empowerment of the Spirit as we focus on the truth (Rom 10:17). We determine
our mental programming, and then our mental programming determines our emotions.129
        Solomon likewise stresses the volitional choice we have as to whether we choose to walk by faith in the facts
rather than be led by our emotions. We do not have to let our caboose control our train:

             Again, by volitional choice, we appropriate Christ as our Life just as we appropriated Christ as
      our Savior. In response to our act of faith in appropriating Christ as Savior, the Holy Spirit regenerated
      us in our spirit; in response to our appropriation of Christ as our Life, the Holy Spirit renews us in our
      minds (Rom. 12:2). The will is the vital function since the emotions may be at variance with the
      facts. As we choose against the world, the flesh, and the devil and count upon the indwelling Christ to
      be our life, we are choosing to have our minds renewed by the Holy Spirit. Consequently, our emotions
      are brought into congruence with truth (Eph. 5:18) so that we can “walk not after the flesh, but after
      the Spirit” (Rom. 8:4) and abide in him (Jh 15:5).130 (Italics his.)

Willful disbelief is a sin because willful belief is possible.131 We are morally accountable for both. We are
responsible because we are able to make the appropriate response. Responsible = response-able. Granted,
belief is not an act of the will, and disbelief is not an act of the will. Nevertheless, both typically start with
an act of the will. We have a choice as to what we believe or disbelieve. Since our emotions may be at
variance with what the Bible says about justification and sanctification, it is vital that we choose to listen to
the Bible rather than our emotions.
                                              Accusatory Emotions
      Even the emotions of a Spirit-led believer are not necessarily trustworthy, much less the emotions of a carnal
believer. In his book, Emotions: Can You Trust Them? Dobson concludes that emotions are not necessarily
trustworthy, even under the best of circumstances. He begins by noting that biblical love should not be equated with
emotions:

      The greatest piece of literature ever written on the subject of love, the 13th Chapter of 1 Corinthians,
      includes not a single reference to feelings: “Love is very patient and kind, never jealous or envious,
      never boastful or proud, never haughty or selfish or rude. Love does not demand its own way. It is not

128
    Ibid., 134.
129
    Ibid., 136-147.
130
    Solomon, Happiness, 42.
131
    Likewise, when we use the expression, willful ignorance, we do not mean that ignorance is a function of the
volition. Obviously, ignorance is a matter of the intellect. Nevertheless, the state of ignorance might be attributable
in certain cases to an act of the will not to investigate or consider the matter more carefully or fully, making one
morally culpable for one’s state of ignorance. In a similar fashion, we are morally culpable for willful disbelief
because we could have chosen to investigate or consider the matter more carefully or fully. Biblically accurate
passive persuasion is often actively attained as a form of spiritually enabled, synergistic, self-persuasion.

      irritable or touchy. It does not hold grudges and will hardly even notice when others do it wrong.”
      (1 Corinthians 13:4-5 TLB)132

These are character traits, not emotions. Dobson makes this observation to counter today’s dangerous trend:

      Today’s Americans have become temperamental yo-yos....We live and breathe by the vicissitudes of
      our feelings, and for many, the depression of the “lows” is significantly more prevalent than the elation
      of the “highs.” Reason is now dominated by feelings, rather than the reverse as God intended.
      “But when the Holy Spirit controls our lives he will produce this kind of fruit in us: love, joy, peace,
      patience, kindness, goodness, faithfulness, gentleness and self-control....” (Galatians 5:22 TLB)
      ....nothing could be more dangerous than to permit our emotions to rule our destinies....
      ...emotions must always be accountable to the faculties of reason and will.133 (Italics his.)

       In a discussion of guilty feelings and our conscience, Dobson acknowledges that such feelings can be induced
by both the Holy Spirit and a satanic spirit. We must not be ignorant of such satanic devices. Even apart from such
influences, the conscience is an imperfect human faculty. Guilt can originate internally from our emotions or from
our intellect or from the Holy Spirit, or seemingly internally from Satan, or externally from someone trying to throw
a guilt trip on us. Regardless, Dobson suggests a series of three progressive tests before such emotions are deemed
valid:

      1. Emotionally, what do I feel as a result of my action?
      2. Intellectually, is this feeling reasonable and biblical in view of my action?
      3. Volitionally, was my action intentionally violational?

        Dobson is personally of the persuasion that no guilty feelings should be considered as coming from God
unless the action “was an expression of willful disobedience.”134 To err is human; to err willfully is sin. Even sinless
angels can err without sinning.135 Thus, more comprehensively, we might say that to err unintentionally is simply
to be fallible, to be a created being. To be culpable of transgression, we must transgress, not merely feel guilty.
Being fallible is not equatable with being guilty. We must not let our emotions run unchecked; otherwise, “guilt
will flow like a wild river!”136 Am I the perfect counselor? Hardly! I don’t want to do any more of it than I absolutely
have to because I am not Mr. Sensitive. Will I make errors in counseling? Undoubtedly. Will I be bogged down
with guilty feelings as a result? Nope. Like any other emotion, I analyze guilty feelings. If any tinge of guilt does
not pass the test, I flunk it. Dobson gives a very good test. So does Finley regarding 1Cor 4:3-4. My conscience
passes both tests. As Paul says, “I am conscious of nothing against myself.” So my conscience and I are getting
along quite well.
        Sue does not have the emotional stability nor I the emotional sensitivity to be a good match for counseling. I
know that, so does my wife. I make no pretense otherwise. My wife and I repeatedly tried to tell Sue that.
Nevertheless, Sue insisted that she should be able to come to us for counseling. To which I might say, “Be careful
what you ask for, you might get it.” My counsel is going to fit my biblical framework, not someone else’s emotional
framework.137

                                                    Carnal Venting
       Similar tests might be applied to the emotion of anger. In this discussion, Dobson notes that not all forms of
anger are sinful. Some forms of anger are natural and “have nothing to do with sinful behavior.”138 Such forms of
anger include (1) anger brought on as an involuntary biochemical response to being attacked externally and
(2) anger brought on by an internal state attributable to extreme fatigue, extreme embarrassment, or extreme
frustration. However, sinful anger, that is, carnal anger is unacceptable and “motivates us to hurt our fellow man—
when we want to slash and cut and inflict pain on another person....Nothing justifies an attitude of hatred or a desire
to harm another person” (emphasis his).139 When it comes to venting one’s anger,140 Dobson again offers wise
counsel:

              Many psychologists seem to feel that all anger should be ventilated or verbalized. They say
      it is emotionally and physically harmful to repress or withhold any intense feeling. Can you
      harmonize this scientific understanding with the scriptural commandment that “everyone
      should be quick to listen, slow to speak and slow to become angry” (Jas. 1:19).
              Let me state the one thing of which I am absolutely certain: Truth is unity. In other words, when
      complete understanding is known about a given topic, then there will be no disagreement between
      science and the Bible. Therefore, when these two sources of knowledge appear to be in direct
      contradiction—as in the matter of anger—then there is either something wrong with our interpretation
      of Scripture or else the scientific premise is false. Under no circumstance, however, will the Bible be
      found to err. It was inspired by the Creator of the universe, and He does not make mistakes!
              In regard to the psychological issues involved in the question, there is undoubtedly some validity
      to the current view that feelings of anger should not be encapsulated and internalized. When any
      powerful, negative emotion is forced from conscious thought while it is raging full strength, it has the
      potential of ripping and tearing us from within. The process by which we cram a strong feeling into
      the unconscious mind is called “repression,” and it is psychologically hazardous. The pressure that it
      generates will usually appear elsewhere in the form of depression, anxiety, tension, or in an entire
      range of physical disorders.
              On the other hand, it is my view that mental health workers have taken the above observation
      and carried it to ridiculous lengths. Professions of medicine, psychiatry, psychology, law, etc., go
      through fads and trends just like everything else involving human behavior. And for the past 10 years
      people working in the “helping sciences” have been obsessed by the need to express anger and
      resentment. It has almost become the all-time bogeyman of emotional illness, producing some strange
      recommendations for patients....
              In specific response to the question, we must harmonize the psychological finding that anger
      should be ventilated with the biblical commandment that we be “slow to become angry.” Personally,
      I do not find these objectives to be in contradiction. God does not want us to repress our anger—
      sending it unresolved into the memory bank. Why else did the apostle Paul tell us to settle our
      irritations before sundown each day (see Eph. 4:26), effectively preventing an accumulation of
      seething hostility with the passage of time?
              But how can intense negative feelings be resolved or ventilated without blasting away at the
      offender—an act which is specifically prohibited by the Scripture? Are there other ways of releasing
      pent-up emotions? Yes, including the following:
         • by making the irritation a matter of prayer;
         • by explaining our negative feelings to a mature and understanding third party who can advise and

to, she miscalculated the amount of control that her letter would have in determining the course of the counsel. For
my background reading in counseling warns against allowing counselees to control the situation. As noted herein,
regarding the professional weaker sister, Aldrich says, “More than anything else, they want to control. Don’t let
them! They must be confronted in love.” And from Dobson’s counseling experience, he likewise warns, “The child
had become a tyrant and a dictator.” “To a power-hungry tyrant of any age, appeasement only inflames his lust for
more power.”
