       Writing one’s thoughts out and analyzing them might also be added to this helpful list as a healthy way of
releasing one’s pent up emotions and thereby avoid internalizing them. Writing this book, for instance, allows me
to take constructive action and diffuse any destructive emotions I might have. It also provides a medium by which
to control emotions when dialoguing with an emotional person who has an offense against me, allowing me to act
in the wisest manner possible, under such circumstances, to fulfill the injunctions of Mt 5:23-24 and Mt 18:15. I get
to practice what I preach, so to speak. Emotions follow behavior. My behavior is to analyze my behavior and my
emotions through a biblical and logical grid. By writing analytically, I am disciplining my emotions to follow
biblical and logical thought.
                                            Confirmatory Emotions
       At times, we feel that God is leading us to take a certain course of action. Do we uncritically accept such
feelings as valid? No! Again, such feelings must be evaluated. Dodson suggests four popular tests:

         1.   Scripturally, is the course of action in harmony with what the entire Bible says?
         2.   Morally, is the course of action right?
         3.   Providentially, is the Lord opening a door for this course of action, or is He closing the door?
         4.   Logically, is the course of action reasonable and rationale?

However, as Dobson goes on to explain, not even these tests necessarily lead us to an infallible conclusion every
time. Indeed, in harmony with what Dobson said about erring, we may affirm that God can give us room to err
without our sinning. Moreover, as discussed in POV, at times God may give an area of freedom to choose between
a range of options which are not sinful nor in error.
                                                       Conclusion
       While at work this week, an employee mentioned that she and her husband had two different ideas about
discipline. She said that she was more nurturing, while he was a more-tell-them-what-to-do-and-expect-them-to-
do-it type of guy. Perhaps this tendency is prevalent between females and males in handling discipline. Perhaps not.
Perhaps it is just attributable to personality types, independent of gender, or maybe it is traceable to other factors.
The way we were disciplined as children certainly plays a role in the way we tend to discipline as adults, for
example. In any case, her comments reminded me of the difference between me and Dianne in disciplining our
children when they were young and even our tendency now. Frankly, even though our disciplinary differences
occasionally caused tensions at the time, I am thankful that Dianne did not share the exact same temperament
regarding disciplining our children as I did. For one thing, I certainly wouldn’t want to be married to another me!
We make a good team. For another thing, I believe that our sons, having been exposed to both forms of discipline
in a healthy marriage, will probably be able to express both forms of discipline themselves as the need may arise.
They probably have a tremendous potential to excel beyond me and Dianne as disciplinarians. We will see.
Regardless, God did not make us cookie cutouts of one another, and we all have the potential to change from the
way we were programmed by our upbringing. We can take an active role in reprogramming our brains.
       My sons had a healthy, loving environment in which my tough-love approach was expressed. I get that. Sue
did not have that type of loving atmosphere. I get that too. Sam wisely pointed out this contextual difference in
which Sue experienced her dad’s tough love versus the environment in which my sons experienced my tough love.
Consequently and subsequently, Sam sent me a sixteen-page letter in which he respectfully advocated on Sue’s
behalf for a tender-love approach as being better suited for her than a tough-love approach.141
       On Sam’s behalf, I would like to strengthen his line of reasoning by mentioning that decades ago, when I

141
      Personal correspondence, 2/28/2019.
Chapter 5. Dobson’s Tough Love                                                                                  Page 87
was taking classes to be a high school teacher, I vaguely remember the instructor saying something to the effect
that discipline would not work on abused children. Since they already had been abused by discipline, discipline
would not be effective on them. I am not acquainted with literature addressing that issue. I probably have a blind
spot in my counseling perspective in that area, as well as in other areas also. For that matter, I probably had a lot of
blind spots about being a good parent too. Still, my sons turned out well. Certainly, I cannot take full credit for them
doing so. I have a great wife and a great Lord, plus family support, and community support. As the adage goes, it
takes a village to raise a child. Nonetheless, just as the Lord could use me, an imperfect father, to assist successfully
in raising two fine sons, I believe that He can use me to assist, even with my limited counseling perspective, to
assist with providing a corrective recourse for Sue.
       Therefore, given the vantage point which I have from my understanding of Scripture, from my experiences,
and from my reading in this area, I will close this chapter in defense of Dobson’s tough-love approach with a couple
of observations regarding potential blind spots that Sam may have in his own vantage point regarding this particular
aspect. The anti-tough-love counter perspective, offered by Sam and that instructor I had years ago, seems to go
something like this:
                                      Illustration 23. No-Discipline Syllogism
      1. Discipline should not be applied to those who have been abused by discipline.
      2. Sue’s father used an abusive form of discipline on her.
      3. Therefore, discipline should not be applied to Sue.

Perhaps that is a strawman caricature. Perhaps Sam and that instructor did not mean to rule out all forms of
discipline. Maybe they would allow some form of wimpy discipline, just not tough-love discipline, in Sue’s case.
Possibly, if I were more extensively read in tough-love literature, I would be aware of exceptions being made, even
in the tough-love literature, to children who have been abused by their parents.142 Perhaps those advocating for
tender love rather than tough love in such cases would mean something more like the following:
                                     Illustration 24. No-Tough-Love Syllogism
      1. Tough love should not be applied to those who have been abused by tough love.
      2. Sue’s father used an abusive form of tough love on her.
      3. Therefore, tough love should not be applied to Sue.

This modified syllogism only rules out tough-love discipline, not all forms of discipline for those who have been
abused by tough love. Even so, for reasons provided by Dobson in the present chapter and by Adams in the following
chapter, I disagree with the no-tough-love syllogism, even in Sue’s case. Rather, a better syllogism is available:
                                Illustration 25. No-Abusive-Tough-Love Syllogism
      1. Abusive tough love should not be applied to those who have been abused by abusive tough love.
      2. Sue’s father used an abusive form of tough-love discipline on her.
      3. Therefore, abusive tough-love discipline should not be applied to Sue.

This syllogism just rules out abusive tough love for those who have been abused by tough love. This syllogism is
good, but an even better line of reasoning would be as follows:
                                 Illustration 26. No Abusive Tough-Love Syllogism
      1. Abusive tough love should not be applied to anyone.
      2. Therefore, abusive tough love discipline should not be applied to Sue.

What is objectionable in all cases is abusive tough love, not tough love itself. Abusive discipline of any type is
never justified, but it cannot be used to rule out a justifiable discipline. Using unjustifiable discipline to rule out
justifiable discipline is not justifiable. It mixes the proverbial apples and oranges. This conclusion is confirmed by
the twin argument Sue employed. She not only wanted to rule out my applying tough love to her, but she also

142
   A search on the expression, “tough love,” at Focus on the Family turns up quite a few entries and might be a
place to start: www.focusonthefamily.com.
Page 88                                                                            Degrees of Love and Forgiveness
objected to my seeking to apply Scripture to the situation. She objected to my wanting to appeal to Scripture. Since
her dad had used Scripture abusively, her objection was that I could not appeal to Scripture either. How would this
argument look?
                                      Illustration 27. No Scripture Syllogism
      1. Scripture should not be applied to those who have been abused by Scripture.
      2. Sue’s father abusively used Scripture to correct her.
      3. Therefore, Scripture should not be applied to Sue.

The conclusion is false because the major premise is false. What should be ruled out is the abusive use of Scripture,
not the use of Scripture itself. Yet, if the Scripture is used for correction in this situation, then my contention is
that it would advocate a tough-love discipline. My perception is admittedly influenced considerably by the primary
disciplinarian counselors I have read over the years: Dobson and Adams. We will consult Adams in the next chapter.
                  Chapter 6. Adams’ Nouthetic Counseling
                                                     Introduction
        Jay Adams is a Christian counselor who, following the lead of Mower’s secular book, The Crisis in
Psychiatry and Religion, concludes that psychiatry is not the solution for people’s problems. Patients must take
responsibility for their problems, seeing their problems as moral rather than medical in nature. Both secular and
Christian counselors can agree that taking personal responsibility is key: “He must stop blaming others and accept
responsibility for his own poor behavior. Problems may be solved, not by ventilation of feelings, but rather by
confession of sin.”143 The Freudian etic of non-responsibility should be rejected. “The idea of [mental] sickness as
the cause of personal problems vitiates all notions of human responsibility. This is the crux of the matter. People no
longer consider themselves responsible for what they do wrong” (p. 5). If the so-called mental illness is not
genuinely organic, then the issue is actually unresolved personal problems (p. 29). The personal accountability
approach is much more effective in requiring people to take responsibility for their actions. “The thesis of this book
is that qualified Christian counselors properly trained in the Scriptures are competent to counsel—more competent
than psychiatrists or anyone else” (p. 18). “Clients are encouraged to focus not on feelings (symptoms) but on
behavior” (p. 201).
                                           Solomon Endorses Adams
       The present chapter will focus on Adams’ approach to counseling. Solomon highly recommends Adams for
collateral reading:

      Another approach to Christian counseling is Nouthetic Counseling developed by Dr. Jay Adams and
      documented in his book Competent to Counsel. In this valuable book he repudiates all forms of
      psychotherapy and uses Scripture to buttress his positions and in his counseling, and yet he seems to
      rely heavily upon the approach of Dr. Glasser in Reality Therapy. The book is highly recommended
      collateral reading for those who would do Christian counseling, though it has a differing theological
      basis.144

While Solomon is correct that Adams relies heavily on the approach of Glasser, Adams clarifies a critical
distinction between his approach and that of Glasser:

      I want to say clearly, once and for all, that I am not a disciple of Mowrer or William Glasser (a writer
      in the Mowrer tradition who has become popular recently through the publication of Reality Therapy,
      a book that has confirmed Mowrer’s contentions in a different context). I stand far off from them.
      Their systems begin and end with man. Mowrer and Glasser fail to take into consideration man’s basic
      relationship to God through Christ, neglect God’s law, and know nothing of the power of the Holy
      Spirit in regeneration and sanctification. Their presuppositional stance must be rejected totally.
      Christians may thank God that in his providence he has used Mowrer and others to awaken us to the
      fact that the “mentally ill” can be helped. But Christians must turn to the Scriptures to discover how
      God (not Mowrer) says to do it.
             All concepts, terms and methods used in counseling need to be re-examined biblically. Not one
      thing can be accepted from the past (or the present) without biblical warrant. Biblical counseling
      cannot be an imposition of Mowrer’s or Glasser’s views (or mine) upon Scripture. Mowrer and Glasser
      have shown us that many of the old views were wrong. They have exposed Freud’s opposition to
      responsibility and have challenged us (if we read their message with Christian eyes) to return to the
      Bible for our answers. But neither Mowrer nor Glasser has solved the problem of responsibility. The
      responsibility they advocate is a relative, changing human responsibility; it is a non-Christian
      responsibility which must be rejected as fully as the irresponsibility of Freud and Rogers. At best,
      Mowrer’s idea of responsibility is doing what is best for the most. But social mores change; and when

143
    Jay E. Adams, Competent to Counsel: Introduction to Nouthetic Counseling, Kindle Edition (Zondervan, MI:
Grand Rapids, 2009), KL 142.
144
    Solomon, Rejection, 248.
Page 90                                                                              Degrees of Love and Forgiveness
      pressed as to who is to say what is best, Mowrer falls into a subjectivism which in the end amounts to
      saying that each individual is his own standard.145 (Emphasis his.)

       My personal approach is to combine the nikology of writers such as Solomon and Adams with my own field
of study—misthology. I was taught exchange-life nikology since my high school days by my father, a Southern
Baptist minister. This was the focus of his ministry. I followed in his footsteps, not only reading many of the same
exchanged-life books and going to the same seminary, but I also adopted the exchange-life perspective as my means
of self-discipline. At the same time, my approach toward church discipline throughout the years has been basically
that of Adams, while my approach toward family discipline has been primarily that of Dobson.
       Like Solomon, I find Adams’ material to be “highly recommended collateral reading.” Solomon focuses on
the ministry of the Holy Spirit and the internal application of identity truths to take care of the problem of self.
Counselors from that school of thought focus on assisting in the internal application of identity truths. Adams
apparently has much the same views regarding the role of the Spirit and the necessity of applying identity truth;
however, his emphasis is on counselors externally confronting those who refuse to take personal responsibility. The
two approaches need each other and complement each other very well. Both approaches agree on the superior
competency of Spirit-led Christians over psychiatrists in ministering to the needs of the human soul.
       Like Adams, as already noted, Solomon stresses, in the case of someone like Sue who is caught in a rejection
syndrome, that the counselee must take personal responsibility.

             The next pattern we consider is the person who has come for counseling (or discipleship)
      because he has encountered severe problems. This person may have been away from the Lord, living
      in sin, and having deep psychological problems. From the time of his salvation things have gone
      progressively downward, compelling him to seek counseling. Although he may have minor victories
      in some areas, the overall pattern is one of defeat.
             ....During the counseling, the first thing he must do is take the personal responsibility for
      what is transpiring in his life. Many persons come for counseling who place the blame for their
      failures or troubles on their parents, mates, their jobs or lack of jobs, circumstances, or others
      generally. After he realizes he must take the responsibility for his life and his failures, he can
      begin to look at them objectively and see how God can meet his need.146

Solomon notes that, even in the case of those very active in serving the Lord, they must be confronted with the
reality of their carnality. Sue was upset by my pointing out her carnal immaturity, but it was necessary:

      They refuse to accept the fact that they are carnal Christians—that self still controls the life.
             Many times, there is resistance to the point of open antagonism and hostility as a person begins
      to struggle with the fact that, indeed, he is not a mature Christian. He must then deal with this hostility
      and be able to see and admit that this is his situation as God sees it. God will even use this frustration
      and hostility to bring the counselee to surrender the way of the flesh and experience the liberty and life
      in Christ.147
                                                Spirit and Scripture
       One reason that Christians are more competent than psychiatrists is that Christians rely on the Holy Spirit.
Like Solomon, Adams stresses the need of depending on the Holy Spirit: “Counseling is the work of the Holy Spirit.
Effective counseling cannot be done apart from him” (p. 20). Biblically, having begun by the Spirit, we must be
brought to completion by the Spirit rather than allowing ourselves to be bewitched by fleshly counseling
(cp. Gal 3:1-3). Naturally, this means that the Scripture will be our primary means of therapy: “The Holy Spirit
ordinarily effects his characterological work in the lives of believers through the means of grace. He uses the
ministry of the Word” (p. 21). Of course, the Holy Spirit has given believers gifts and will work through these gifted
individuals as counselors in their application of the Scripture to the situation. To be led by the Spirit is to be led by

    Adams, Competent, KL 160.

    Solomon, Rejection, 190. See Being Neurotic is a Choice, 69.

Chapter 6. Adams’ Nouthetic Counseling                                                                        Page 91
                                                        148
the Scripture (Gal 5:18) rather than by feelings (p. 23).
                                               Nouthetic Counseling
       Adams terms his approach as nouthetic confrontation, based on the Greek words noutheteo/neuthesia,
meaning to counsel to stop an improper course of action via warning, admonishing, instructing (Rom 5:14;
1Cor 4:14; 10:11; Eph 6:4; Col 1:28; Col 3:16; 1Thess 5:12,14; 2Thess 3:15; Tit 3:10). The need for confrontation
in dealing with a problem is presupposed. “In short, nouthetic confrontation arises out of a condition in the counselee
that God wants changed. The fundamental purpose of nouthetic confrontation, then, is to effect personality and
behavioral change” (emphasis his, Competent, 45).
                                              Camouflage of Tears
      Adams describes the efforts of Mary, who had been diagnosed by psychiatrists as manic-depressive. She
would typically burst into tears when they or her parents attempted to discover the issue that was distressing her.
This ploy did not work with nouthetic counselors:

             Instead of showing her sympathy, instead of responding to her tears, instead of being taken
      in by Mary’s tactics, the counselors brushed aside the camouflage and pursued a straight course
      directly to the heart of the matter. At this, Mary turned off her antics almost as automatically as if
      she had pushed a button. She told the story, a miserable story, which had been so hard to tell she had
      never told it to another person before.
             Mary was helped only because her counselors were not shaken by Mary’s screams and
      tears. They dealt appropriately with her feelings and demanded more content. They insisted on
      working with data. They called a spade a spade. (P. 34)
                                            Sympathy Versus Empathy
      Failing to call a spade a spade is the chief reason why nouthetic counseling might fail:

             When nouthetic counseling fails, what are some of the reasons? First, consider failures by
      counselors. Probably the chief reason why nouthetic counselors fail is because they sometimes
      become too sympathetic to the complaints and excuses of the counselee. Frequently when a
      counselee tells a very pitiful tale, there is the temptation for a counselor to decide that this indeed
      is a special case. If the counselor agrees that under the circumstances the counselee was not
      responsible for his action, there is little more than the counselor can do. The counselor has placed
      himself in an impossible position. He has, in effect, denied the promises and the assurances of
      God in I Corinthians 10:13 which will be discussed fully elsewhere. There is only one thing for him
      to do if the counselor comes to his senses in time: admit his mistake to the counselee and continue
      properly from there.
             Counselors fail when they become too sympathetic toward excuses and do not hold
      counselees responsible for their behavior, but they can never fail when they become truly
      sympathetic toward them. Perhaps the first attitude may be called sympathy and the latter
      empathy. When counselors simply become softhearted, they are most unmerciful toward their
      counselees. The most kindly (empathetic) stance is to tell the truth, help the counselee to face up
      to his own sin, and encourage him to make the changes necessary to rectify the situation. (P. 58)

Empathy for self-pity is nothing more than pathetic sympathy. Because such sympathy allows softheartedness to
override what is needed, it fails to offer real help and thus is unmerciful. Biblical empathy, in contrast, refuses to
put up with excuses; rather, it holds the counselee accountable:



148
    One point where Solomon and Adams differ, however, is that Solomon is a trichotomist and Adams is a
dichotomist, though he stresses holistic unity. See Adams, Theology, 108-118. My series of books on exchanged
life trichotomy, when released, will advocate a holistic trichotomous dichotomy. Believing that the Scripture speaks
in terms of multiple vantage points, I seek to integrate the best of all three vantage points. At times, contrary to
Adams, trichotomy is a necessary deduction to handle the biblical data.
Page 92                                                                              Degrees of Love and Forgiveness
         Counselors may fail by becoming too involved emotionally. They can never become too involved in
         the proper sense, but they may get too involved in the sense that they may allow their own emotions
         to cloud their judgment. When this happens, counselors have been caught by the same snare as their
         counselees, who are so emotionally involved in their problems that they no longer think straight, but
         instead allow feelings to govern actions. (P. 59)

LaHaye has a title for this imprudent sympathy: “Sympathism connotes avoiding a problem by obtaining the
sympathy of others. This mechanism enables the individual to evade his problems and troubles through being
babied.”149 Counselors who give into this evasive maneuvering on the part of counselees are babying them, thereby
setting the counselee up to fail and not grow up. Sure, there is a time “to weep with those who weep” (Rom 12:15).
But Paul was not expecting believers to weep with the professional weepers of his day nor the chronic weepers of
our day.
                                                Competent to Counsel
         In accord with his premise that the most effective counseling relies on the Spirit and Scripture, Adams states:

         Preeminently, a nouthetic counselor must be conversant with the Scriptures. This is one reason why
         properly equipped ministers may make excellent counselors. A good seminary education rather than
         medical school or a degree in clinical psychology, is the most fitting background for a counselor.
         Real counseling involves the imparting of information. Counseling means, among other things,
         giving advice. The Holy Spirit uses counselors to right wrongs by the application of God’s Word
         to human problems. Knowledge of Scripture does not mean merely the memorizing and cataloging
         of facts. One in whom the “word of Christ dwells richly” (Colossians 3:16), is one who knows the
         meaning of Scripture for his own life. Because he is capable of solving his own problems scripturally,
         he is qualified to help others do so. (P. 61)
                                             Rejection of Rogerian
       Adams rejects the popular Rogerian, non-judgmental, client-centered, permissive-acceptance counseling.
Instead, he poses a confrontational, Bible-centered, accountability-acceptance model. The Bible commands us to
judge (Jn 7:24), even in the very passage that is thought to teach otherwise (Mt 7:1-6). Neutrally accepting sinful
behavior implicitly condones it. Don’t condone it; confront it. Unfortunately, the Rogerian counselor focuses on
recognizing and interpreting emotions. Contrastively, the nouthetic counselor asks specific questions, provides
specific information, and demands change in one’s actions.

         Nouthetic counseling assumes that the feelings are not the most profound level of human relationship
         with which one must be concerned in counseling. God speaks of love in attitudinal and behavioral
         forms when he defines it as keeping his commandments. Moreover, feelings cannot be altered directly
         in the same way that one can change behavior. Consequently, in accordance with Scripture, nouthetic
         counselors spend less time finding out how people feel. They are more interested in discovering
         how clients behave. They have learned also that this approach much more successfully cuts through
         what Hiltner calls the “emotional underbrush.” (P. 92)

If you want to change the way you feel, then change the way you behave. God said as much from the very beginning
in Gen 4:3-7.

         God then rhetorically asked Cain, “Why are you depressed?” and pointed the way toward overcoming
         it: “If you do right, will it [your face] not be lifted up?” Here, God sets forth the important principle
         that behavior determines feelings.
                 Sometimes instead of speaking of mental illness, people talk about “emotional problems.”
         But this language is as confusing as the former. When a client feels depressed or high, or anxious, or
         hostile, there really is no problem with his emotions. His emotions are working only too well. It is true
         that his emotions are not pleasant, but the real problem is not emotional, it is behavioral. Solutions
         aimed at relieving the emotions directly (as, e.g., chemical methods like pills or alcohol), therefore,

149
      LaHaye, 56.
Chapter 6. Adams’ Nouthetic Counseling                                                                        Page 93
      must be considered to be nothing more than the relief of symptoms.
             People feel bad because of bad behavior; feelings flow from actions. This relationship
      between feelings and behavior is set forth very clearly in Scripture. For example, Peter often pointed
      out that good living produces good feelings. In his first letter ([1Pet] 3:10), he quoted Psalm 34:12, 13:
                  Let him who means to love life, and see good days, refrain his tongue from evil,
            and his lips from speaking guile: and let him turn away from evil, and do good; let him
            seek peace, and pursue it.150 (Insert his, 93-94.)

For those who prefer a more scientific explanation, Adams provides it:

             While there is no direct voluntary access to the emotions, the emotions can be reached indirectly
      through the voluntary system, because extensive fiber overlappings in the cortex allow unified
      correlation of the two systems. Thus actions affect emotions. Voluntary behavioral alterations will
      lead to involuntary emotional changes. It is important to understand, therefore, that feelings flow from
      actions. (P. 97)
                                      Rejection of Emotional Transference
      In contrast to Adams, some counselors, like Wright, permit emotional transference in which counselees are
allowed to transfer their negative emotions to the counselor:

             Sometimes the person who seeks your help is the one experiencing a crisis, or it may be someone
      who is trying to help a person in crisis and is not succeeding. Either one may have the same struggle
      with self-esteem. Be aware of the tendency to blame in order to protect the self-image. The blame will
      be coupled with anger. Anger sometimes gives a person a sense of control or of being in charge again.
      And even if it is irrational, it feels better than the state of hopelessness.
             Some ministers and students have told me of their shock at the extent of the antagonism
      and belligerence that has been shown toward them as they tried to help. We don’t have to be
      thrown by this if, in our own minds we can say, “It is all right for them to feel this way, and I do
      not have to take it personally. It is their protection against their feelings of failure and
      helplessness.”
             Expect negative feelings, and see them for what they are, camouflage against the pain of
      feeling bad about the situation and not too good about oneself either.151
                                        Illustration 28. Carnivorous Sheep

        In WWWS, the above picture was used to describe the reality of carnivorous sheep who had “fallen from
grace” into legalistic carnality and consequently “bite and devour one another” (Gal 1:6-8; 5:4,15). According to
Paul, wolves are not the only creatures capable of biting the sheep. In functional terms, believers are quite capable
of becoming wolves either morally, theologically, or both. What should a counselor do when dealing with biting
sheep? Wright seems to be of the opinion that the counselor should just take it in stride. Just sit there and take it,
just do not take it personally. I am of a different opinion, as was Paul. He strongly rebuked these biting sheep in
Galatia.
        Sue’s husband, Sam, was shocked at the anger she had expressed toward him when he has tried to counsel
her. As mentioned at the end of the previous chapter, Sam wrote a well-reasoned, sixteen-page letter to me
explaining why he feels that a tough-love approach is not appropriate for Sue. I commended him on the letter and
acknowledged that I respected the position he had taken and the articulation and spirit in which he had offered it.
His letter showed depth, respect, and love. Further, I told him that there were counselors who would share his
perspective. Accordingly, I have included Wright’s quote above as an apparent representative of that mentality.
Furthermore, given the position that Sam was in, as Sue’s husband, I told him that the empathetic recourse he was
seeking might be the best course of action he could take. Also, I informed him that I already had acknowledged in
this book, which I was already writing at the time, that the Lord might use one counselor to take one course of
action and another to take another course of action.
        Is it not interesting that, in seeking our reconciliation, the members of the Trinity took different roles? For
example, the Son propitiated the Father on our behalf. The Son had to appease the Father’s wrath, making it possible
for the Father to accept our repentance. If we do not repent, then Jesus’ efforts will not be successful. Believers will
not experience the familial reconciliation to which they are otherwise entitled (2Cor 5:20). Nonetheless, even though
both the Son and the Father love us, the demands of the Father’s righteous anger must be satisfied by the Son for
reconciliation to take place. Accordingly, Jesus died to propitiate the Father’s wrath (1Jn 2:2). Amazingly, the
Father sent Jesus to propitiate His own fatherly wrath. The Father “sent His Son to be the propitiation for our sins”
(1Jn 4:10). The Father was reconciling us to Himself through the sacrifice of the Son (2Cor 5:18-19; Col 19-22).
The Son was acting in the role of reconciler. The Father was acting as both reconciler and reconciled. Therefore,
especially when seen from this vantage point, I could appreciate Sam’s position.
        Just to follow through on this analogy, Dianne is evidently playing the role of the Holy Spirit. She is grieved
by what is happening, but she never had much inclination toward disciplining our children. She played a more
affectionate nurturing role, simply telling them what to do. Still, this does not mean that I was going to set back and
let Dianne be an emotional punching bag for our children. The bulk of the discipline fell upon me, as the fatherly
figure. No one with an inkling of biblical understanding would think that you can get away with blaspheming or
mistreating the Holy Spirit (Mk 3:29). It is not that the Holy Spirit necessarily gets mad.152 The Spirit may get sad
(Eph 4:30). But watch out for Dad! As explained in BRH, your obedience or lack of it does not throw your birth
into question, but it does raise the issue of discipline. The Holy Spirit has given us new birth. If you fail to obey
your mother who gave you birth after your father explicitly has told you to obey her, then you can expect to suffer
the paternal consequences. Young children can understand this principle very well: If you don’t obey mom, then
look out for dad! I suspect that the Lord is using these circumstances regarding Sue for our good to teach each of
us something about how the Godhead operates in various roles. The Godhead may be using me, Dianne, and Sam
to flesh out the roles that each member of the Trinity plays in reconciliation. In any case, Dianne’s response
(representing the Spirit) would be more suited to Sam’s non-disciplinary approach (representing the Son). Still, I
(acting in the capacity of the Father) am not going to let Dianne be Sue’s punching bag.
        Sam, on the other hand, is assuming the role of the Son in this reconciliation, which is fine and good.
Husbands are told to love their wives just as Christ sacrificially loved the church (Eph 5:25). Wives are told to see
to it that they respectfully submit themselves to their husbands (Eph 5:33; Col 3:18). The respectful discipline of a
wife to her husband is primarily a form of self-discipline: the wife disciplining herself, not a husband-imposed
152
   This is not to deny that the Spirit can get mad and infuse us with His righteous anger (cp. Jdg 14:19; 1Sam 11:6).

Passover. See Heiser, Angels. Jesus is both a fierce Lion and gentle Lamb.
Chapter 6. Adams’ Nouthetic Counseling                                                                         Page 95
discipline. Therefore, even though I do not see such passages as being grounds for either husbands or wives allowing
themselves to be treated as doormats or punching bags, plausible allowance can be made that my responsibility to
act in a disciplinary role as a fatherly figure could be considered much greater than Sam’s responsibility to act in a
disciplinary role as a husband figure. A husband’s way of dealing with his wife is necessarily different from a
father’s way of dealing with his daughter in that the husband does not have the authority to discipline his wife
whereas the father has both the authority and responsibility to discipline his daughter. The propensity of Sam and I
toward different relative degrees of discipline might be attributed, in part, to the respective roles we must take in
the course of counseling. The necessity of outside help, which already has been affirmed herein, suggests the need
for a trinitarian team effort.
        Sam asked that I consider imitating the role of the good shepherd who sought out the wandering sheep. Yet,
even in the parables of the lost sheep, coin, and son (Lk 15:1-31), we see different roles being played by the parabolic
seeker (as a shepherd, sweeper, and father). The good shepherd seeks out the wayward sheep. The father does not
seek out the wayward son; rather, the father waits at home for his son, seeking him by looking for him to repent and
return home. Sam was hopeful that Dianne and I would pursue Sue like the good shepherd. However, on several
previous occasions, when Dianne and I were out in the vicinity of Sue’s home, Dianne had entertained the thought
of us dropping by and seeing Sue. However, because Sue had displayed a strong tendency to be very negative
toward Dianne, when Dianne had tried to interact with Sue via texting, we did not pursue the drop-by possibility.
One becomes wary of reaching out when one’s hand is bitten when doing so. Some sheep bite. And Sue’s biting
responses discouraged further attempts to reach out. Plus, I prefer to avoid emotional confrontations if possible,
preferring logic over emotions in confrontations. Granted, we are to “pursue peace with all” (Heb 12:14). Thus,
pursuing peace with Sue was still desirable. Yet, the Bible acknowledges that being at peace with all may not be
possible (Rom 12:18). Even so, believing that peace is possible once Sue overcomes her rejection syndrome, my
way of pursuing peace was to recommend exchanged-life books and exchanged-life counseling to her. Plus, writing
this book for her—not exclusively for her, to be sure, but also to help those like her who are caught in the same
syndrome—is an additional means of pursuing peace. In the meantime, Sam may find it necessary to play the
counseling role of the good shepherd while I find it necessary to play the counseling role of the loving father, who
stayed at home waiting until his wayward son “came to his senses” (Lk 15:17) and repentantly returned home.
Regardless of the counseling role employed (whether it be as a parabolic shepherd, sweeper, or father), repentance
on the part of the counselee is necessarily the goal. Repentance was necessary, not only on the part of the wayward
son but also on the part of the lost sheep and coin, so that restoration could be accomplished (cp. Lk 15:7,9).
Repentance entails taking responsibility. Regardless of the counseling technique employed, Sue should not be
allowed to avoid taking responsibility by attributing her lack of repentance to the particular counseling technique
implemented by any given counselor. The time has come for her to take responsibility for her own behavior and
repent. She needs to stop blaming others for her behavior.
        Counseling as a father, I will not allow sinful accusations to go unchallenged or undisciplined. Nor will I
submit me or Dianne to being unfairly criticized as being uncaring and unloving in this counseling experience.
When my boys disobeyed me or Dianne, I would not tolerate it. I met it with tough love. I will do the same with
Sue when she disrespects me or Dianne and calls us unloving and uncaring. I am not going to sit there and allow us
to be Sue’s punching bag or chew toy. Reflecting my perspective, in conjunction with rejecting the Rogerian focus
on feelings, Adams rejects the popular counseling usage of emotional transference:

             Consider another example, the use of transference. By “transference” Rogerians and other
      Freudians mean that clients frequently redirect their feelings (often negative) for one person to another
      (in this case to the counselor). Hatred for a father may be transferred to the counselor.
      Transference is encouraged by many as a useful technique in counseling. But is transference a
      technique that Christians may use? No. When counselors encourage or allow clients to transfer
      feelings of hatred and resentment to themselves, they thereby encourage clients to perpetuate
      and multiply their sin and guilt. In contrast, nouthetic techniques call for the loving rebuke of
      sinful attitudes and actions, even when directed toward the counselor. Instead of adopting
      reflective methods which encourage transference, the nouthetic counselor considers incidents of
      transference a nouthetic opportunity to put an end to such transference. He will therefore employ
      methods appropriate to bringing about repentance and reconciliation rather than the acceptance of
      sinful feelings. He points out to the client that his negative transference is one evidence that he has
      been using wrong methods of handling his problems. Whenever a client transfers strong feelings of
Page 96                                                                             Degrees of Love and Forgiveness
      antipathy toward his counselor, for instance, the counselor should seize upon this opportunity
      to observe that such behavior may be one instance of underlying patterns which in the past have
      gotten the client into difficulty. The nouthetic counselor will probably say something like this:
                     Jim, so far as I can see, this attitude toward me is totally unwarranted. If I’ve
             wronged you, tell me how, and I’ll rectify the situation; if I haven’t, you’d better take
             note of this pattern. In either case, you have failed to handle the problem biblically, and
             this is precisely the sort of thing which has been getting you into trouble.
            The counselor, by capitalizing upon this instance of the client’s sinful behavior, already has
      begun to help him by not allowing sin to go unchallenged. (Pp. 100-102.)

        As Adams goes on to explain, for counselors to do otherwise and allow emotional transference would make
the counselors “a party to the client’s sin” (p. 102). In other words, to be a party to Sue’s pity-party would make
one a party to her sin; it would also condone her sin. To his credit, Sam rejected her projecting her negative emotions
toward her father onto me. Nevertheless, Sam and I do not see eye-to-eye on what my response should be. If he
needs to be her punching bag, so be it. I will not. Nouthetically, I punch back.
        During His ministry, Jesus punched back as well.153 Some mistakenly think that the injunction in Mt 5:39 to
turn the other cheek applies in full force to the present age. It does not. As Elkins explains in his commentary on
this passage: “In this Age there are times we shouldn’t turn the other cheek.”154 Nouthetic counseling is one such
time. Self-defense would likewise be another. Jesus allows for reasonable self-defense.155 Dobson likewise instructs
parents to teach their children “to fight back when attacked” (Dare, 187). We are not to encourage our children to
be punching bags but to fight back in self-defense.
        Sometimes one must act in self-defense, not only for one’s own sake but for the sake of the one doing the
attacking. As Dobson illustrates, acting in self-defense can, at times, help both the victim and the victimizer. It
teaches the attacker to stop attacking. Being a pacifist would make one an enabler, allowing the attacker to keep
attacking. Writing this book to document and demonstrate that Sue is using wrong methods in dealing with her
emotions is a nouthetic means of rebuking her sin and calling a spade a spade. Pointing out her sinful behavior and
not allowing it to go unchallenged is the appropriate means to seek her repentance and thus achieve reconciliation.
Condoning her sin by allowing her to continue in it would make one an enabler rather than a counselor. Granted,
this book is strong medicine, but weak medicine will not suffice.
        On the other hand, on Sam’s behalf, I would like to point out that, at the time of His crucifixion, Jesus did
not punch back. The Lord chose to be a punching bag on that occasion. The Lord showed that He knows how to
take a punch when it is necessary. Doing so was the only way He could win our redemption. Losing was the only
way He could win. His role model in this regard is certainly worthy of imitating as well. Peter uses it to exhort
servants to be submissive to their masters, even if servants must be a punching bag in doing so (1Pet 2:18-24).
Pragmatically, doing so is certainly understandable since servants are not in a role where punching back is advisable.
Likewise, husbands are not in a role where punching back is necessarily or always advisable. Sometimes being a
punching bag might be the only way that they can win their wives. Being a punching bag to avoid his wife’s anger
does not necessarily make a man a coward.
        However, I am not in Sam’s shoes. For me to allow his wife to attack me and my wife would be to cower
before his wife’s anger. I will not do it. Instead, as Sue was sitting on our couch, after having written me and my
wife that six-page accusatory letter, I decided that it was time to confront Sue’s anger head-on. The mental imagery
that I had while Sue was sitting there was that of a festering sore that needed to be brought to a head so that it could
be lanced and allowed to drain. Many years before I had read about this recourse as being a recommended
counseling technique, and it was the course of action I chose.

Publications, 2014), 236.

    See Geisler’s discussion on Lk 22:36, for example. Norman L. Geisler, Christian Ethics: Contemporary Issues

Chapter 6. Adams’ Nouthetic Counseling                                                                        Page 97
                                                 Responsibility Crisis
       In various cases, counselors need to invoke some sort of crisis that confronts the counselee with the necessity
of taking responsibility for his or her actions. In a footnote to the following material, Adams likens it to lancing a
boil that needs to be drained:

             Many clients do not come to counseling in a state of despair. Frequently clients are discouraged
      but also somewhat cocky about the way that they are handling life’s problems. In such cases, it may
      be necessary to precipitate some sort of responsibility crisis which has been pending but which
      the client has avoided until now. As an example, if a Matthew 5:23-24 type confrontation between
      the client and someone whom he has wronged is long overdue, the confrontation might be set up.
      Not only is the client thereby encouraged to right a wrong, but he also breaks up an old sinful
      relationship pattern and in its place begins to establish a new biblical one. The client often needs God’s
      law applied to his life with power in order to blast the old patterns loose. Nouthetic counselors do not
      think that clients need to be led along gently over months or years. Instead, they sometimes find
      that it is necessary to use God’s truth shatteringly to show the person the inevitable hopelessness
      of his present way of life. They may need to warn him and show him how his present sinful courses
      of action will lead to nothing but greater discouragement and ultimate defeat. Nonetheless, as Adams
      acknowledges, this confrontational approach has been vigorously attacked. Note the following
      objection followed by Adams’ response:
                    There are counselors who feel that a quick and complete emptying of sinful facts
             helps the client to get them out in the open where he can ask for forgiveness. But
             “yanking” is dangerous and unwise. If you are to be your best in counseling you must let
             information and thoughts “ooze” out. Why shouldn’t counselors yank things out of
             people? Because it hurts. It hurts both you and the one with whom you are counseling.
             And the professional counselor is always sensitive to “readiness” when counseling with
             others.
             No one wants to yank teeth unless it is necessary. But in some cases, yanking is not only
      essential, but it is also the only thing to do. After all, when a tooth has been bad for a long while, it
      abscesses and poison run through one’s system. It is more merciful to yank that tooth. Who takes out
      teeth by stages over a long time? It is far less painful to suffer one bad hurt during the yanking. Then
      the pain is over and healing can begin immediately. As a matter of fact, the agony of a long drawn out
      series of sessions is far worse than getting the whole problem out as quickly as possible. It hurts less
      to cut off the dog’s tail with one deft blow than to slowly slice away from the tip end. God sent Nathan
      the prophet to yank a confession out of David. But Narramore protests, “yanking hurts.” Of course,
      yanking hurts; nobody denies that, but abscessed teeth hurt too, and in the long run do far more damage.
      The point of the psychosomatic Psalms (51, 32, 38), as David clearly indicated, is that God makes us
      miserable when we hold within the guilt of unforgiven sin. He therefore urged his readers not to be
      like the stubborn mule that has to be dragged to confession. What hurts most is not the yanking, but
      the sin which caused the condition in the first place. Wherever sin exists there will be pain. Confessing
      sin hurts one’s pride. It is vain to attempt to avoid this fact, or try to devise some painless method of
      extracting confessions. Moreover, the way to handle personal problems is not to determine whether
      one method may be more or less painful than another. In the long run, the “yanking” method must
      be adopted simply because God calls us to an immediate confession of sin. It is wrong to advise
      anyone to postpone the confession of sin. Under no circumstances can such advice be justified
      biblically. (Pp. 173-175.)

      Sam prefers to let things ooze. I prefer to yank. In some cases, such differences in personal preference are
permissible, but not in all cases. For example, personal counseling preference would not be a viable option for
allowing sin to remain unconfessed in certain cases. Namely, if sin against someone else is involved so as to warrant
a Mt 5:23-24 type confrontation, then repentance and confession are requisite. If Sue has sinned against me and
Dianne by repeatedly calling us unloving and uncaring, which I believe to be the case, then Dianne and I are due a
formal apology. Sue needs to repent and confess her sin. Postponing confession cannot be justified. Differences of
opinion regarding the advantages of tender love versus tough love are permissible. A sin coverup is not permissible.
Page 98                                                                               Degrees of Love and Forgiveness
                                                Covered or Cover Up
       When should discipline be used? Basically, when the issue cannot be simply covered in love. Proverbs says,
“Hatred stirs up strife, but love covers all transgressions” (Prov 10:12). Does this mean that love covers all sins
regardless of repentance or confession? Obviously not. Peter provides a limitation: “Love covers a multitude of
sins” (1Pet 4:8). In comparison to what is said in Proverbs, Peter is saying that love covers a multitude of sins rather
than all sins. Some sins are not automatically covered by love. When sins cannot be simply covered automatically,
they must be dealt with directly before love can cover them. The same principle applies to our walking in fellowship
with God in the light.
       6
         If we say that we have fellowship with Him and yet walk in the darkness, we lie and do not practice
       the truth; 7 but if we walk in the light as He Himself is in the light, we have fellowship with one another,
       and the blood of Jesus His Son cleanses us from all sin. 8 If we say that we have no sin, we are deceiving
       ourselves, and the truth is not in us. 9 If we confess our sins, He is faithful and righteous to forgive us
       our sins and to cleanse us from all unrighteousness. (1Jn 1:6-9)

       As we walk in the light, we simply confess the sins of which we become aware. If we are unaware of them,
they are automatically covered by the blood if we are walking in the light. We do not need to be introverted or
hypersensitive in seeking for sin in our lives. Simply, if we become aware of sin, we just need to confess it so that
our fellowship with God remains unbroken. Mature believers can go for days without needing to confess anything.
Similarly, in our marriage, Dianne and I very rarely need to tell one another that we are sorry for something or ask
for forgiveness. As I write this note, we are getting ready to celebrate our 36th wedding anniversary next week.
Earlier in our marriage, we had to ask each other for forgiveness with some regularity. Nowadays, not nearly so
much. In fact, hardly ever do we need to offer a formal apology (i.e., confession), saying, “I’m sorry. I was wrong.
Please forgive me.”156 This is not to say that we do not do or say anything that might get on each other nerves if we
let them; rather, the infractions are so small, relatively speaking, that we can normally just cover them in love and
move on and remain in fellowship, practicing “forbearance to one another in love” (Eph 4:2). Most of the time we
just ignore these quirks because they are not worth mentioning in the context of our loving fellowship.
       Sometimes we can even tease each other about our shortcomings. Does this mean that we tease about sin?
No. But not every shortcoming is a sin. All sin is a shortcoming, but not all shortcoming is a sin. As has been stated
already, to err is human; to err willfully is sin. Being fallible is not a sin. To further illustrate, I am very forgetful.
My memory is very fallible. Sometimes, Dianne will tease me about it. I generally don’t even say that I’m sorry on
those occasions. Sometimes, she gets mildly frustrated about it, depending on how big a deal the item was that I
forgot. On those occasions, sometimes I will say, “I’m sorry.” And she forbears with my absentmindedness. But
even then, I don’t offer the full-blown, formal apology, saying, “I’m sorry. I was wrong. Please forgive me.” Why?
Because I did not intentionally forget. It was not willful, so I don’t ask, “Will you forgive me?”
       In From Forgiven to Forgiving, Adams puts it this way:

       In 1 Peter 4:8, quoting Proverbs 17:9, Peter points out that those who love one another “cover a
       multitude of sins” in love. It is only those sins which throw the covers off that must be dealt with by
       the Luke 17 and Matthew 18 processes: those offenses that break fellowship and lead to an
       unreconciled condition require forgiveness. Otherwise, we simply learn to overlook a multitude of
       offenses against ourselves, recognizing that we are all sinners and that we must gratefully thank others
       for covering our sins as well.157

        Ok, so, when is a formal apology necessary? In his Handbook of Church Discipline, Adams again addresses
this issue potently:

              Jesus leaves no options whenever sin separates brothers; He commands informal discipline to
156
   Actually, Adams is opposed to apology and demands confession instead. See Apology Versus Confession, 155.
157
   Jay E. Adams, From Forgiven to Forgiving (Victor Books, WI: Victor Books, 1989), 39. See his discussion
pp. 63-65 for the difference between apologizing (say, “I’m sorry,” which is merely a statement of how you feel)
and asking for forgiveness (which is an admission that you did something morally wrong). The former does not
suffice for the latter.
Chapter 6. Adams’ Nouthetic Counseling                                                                        Page 99
      bring about reconciliation. He does not say that it would be nice if the offended party were to go and
      seek reconciliation; He requires him to do so. Whenever an unreconciled condition exists between two
      believers, there is no option left; discipline must be pursued.
             All the reservations, objections, and hesitations that people have about discipline are irrelevant.
      Jesus does not leave the matter up to us: He tells us what to do.158 (Emphasis his.)

Returning now to his discussion in Counsel, Adams gives prudent counsel:

      The idea of love covering a multitude of sins means that, whenever possible, love lets offenses go by.
      Whenever love can cover sins, there is no need for nouthetic confrontation. If it were necessary to
      confront one another about every problem that occurred (especially in a home), there would hardly be
      time for anything else. When we learn to love, we also learn to cover, to forget, and to overlook many
      faults in others. But whenever something cannot be overlooked, whenever something rankles inside,
      and it is obvious that it will be carried over to the next day, the problem must be dealt with and not
      covered up. Only love truly covers; anything else is but a cover up. (P. 228.)
                                        Confess Only If Morally Wrong
      Since the issues between Sue and I cause offense and break fellowship, they cannot be covered apart from
confession. On the other hand, the bar for confession is not so low that either she or I can demand forgiveness just
because we have been offended.

      In the final analysis true confession is agreement with another that is in agreement with God’s Word.
      One must never confess as sin what he is not sure, biblically, is sin. Nor should he confess to sins that
      he does not believe he has committed merely in order to appease another who has charged him with
      such wrongdoings. Confession must be the genuine, heartfelt conviction of the repentant confessor.159

      In the following illustration, Lois reads something into the situation that is not supported by the facts and
then makes false accusations. Adams gives these instructions:

             At this point, many persons would approach Lois in a weak, unbiblical way, saying something
      like, “I’m sorry if I said or did anything to give you the idea that I was interested in you, Lois. Will
      you forgive me?”
             But that is all wrong. You did nothing wrong. To ask Lois for forgiveness when you have done
      nothing wrong (with that all-too-frequent appendage, "If I did or said anything”) is an abuse of the
      process of forgiveness. When you ask for forgiveness, be very sure that it is out of repentance over
      sin. To ask for forgiveness is always an admission of sin! Lois has wronged you....
             ....You must never ask forgiveness for error or wrongdoing on the part of another in order to
      appease her. All too often this ploy is used to relieve the tension, but it is a pretense and a sham. If
      you know you are not guilty, in effect you are willingly misrepresenting the situation in order to
      appease Lois....
             Together, you [and your wife] will confront Lois telling her that her suspicions are wrong. She
      must be rebuked for entertaining them on such a fanciful basis. 160 (Emphasis his.)

I cannot expect Sue to confess to doing wrong if she does not believe she has done wrong. Nor can I be expected to
confess to having done wrong if I do not believe that I have done wrong. Other counselors may take different
approaches in their counseling than I do. A reasonable allowance has been made for that possibility. My approach
may not have been executed flawlessly. Allowance for that possibility also has been made. Nevertheless, neither of
these objections to my counseling warrant my confession that I have done something morally wrong. By no means
do I claim to be an infallible counselor. Far to the contrary. On the other hand, a biblical framework allows for
errors to be made without requiring that confession be made. To say that I could do something better is not the

158
    Jay E. Adams, Handbook of Church Discipline (Grand Rapids, MI: Zondervan, 1986), 47-48.
159
    Idem., Forgiven, 101.
160
    Ibid., 109.
Page 100                                                                            Degrees of Love and Forgiveness
point. To the point, Sue has said at various points that I could do something better. Probably. But to say that my not
doing something to her satisfaction requires that I acknowledge wrongdoing on my part is not an automatically
conceded point. Not by a long shot!
       Did I offend Sue’s feelings? Definitely. Am I necessarily guilty of wrongdoing in doing so? No. To the
contrary, for reasons expressed herein, I believe that I would have been sinning if I had refused to hurt her feelings.
Avoiding hurting her feelings was not my objective. A collision with her feelings was necessary if confession was
to be achieved. Conversely, given Sue’s perspective, I can see why she believed I was in the wrong for hurting her
feelings. If love is necessarily affectional rather than volitional, then she has a valid point. If, on the other hand,
volitional forms of love necessarily suppress affectional forms of love when the occasion demands it, then my
approach is a reasonable biblical recourse. As to which perspective is biblical, the answer to that question has
required this book-long explanation.
                                                 Nouthetic Mocking
       Sue also said that I mocked her and laughed at her. My initial impression, as I shared earlier, was that this
was not true because she had distorted my attempt to get her to face her issues scripturally as being something
negative by claiming that I was mocking her. At the time, I did not regard this accusation as being true because I
was seeking a positive outcome. Mocking, in my opinion at that time, would be for the purpose of harming rather
than healing. Thus, when she ranted the next day about how uncaring I am, I was unphased because my intent was
to heal rather than harm via nouthetic confrontation. Upon further analytical reflection, however, as this study has
progressed, my assessment has become more malleable in light of the biblical data. If my limited initial vantage
point is maintained, that the word mocking is used exclusively with the negative connotation of simply seeking to
do harm, then I am acquitted of the accusation since I was seeking to be helpful. If, however, mocking can be used
in the sense of doing harm in order do good, then the accusation may be applicable.
       For sake of argument, let us presume that I used mockery while Sue was throwing a temper tantrum that
Saturday night. From my perspective, she was using her tears to avoid dealing with the real issue. Given my
personality and my perspective and my views on discipline, I would not rule out my having mocked or laughed to
some extent at what I considered a ridiculous reaction on her part. Even so, I would have done so in order to get her
to move beyond that point to something more positive. Quite possibly, I might have chosen mocking humor to show
that her tears were not having her intended effect on me. Counseling books acknowledge that you have a choice as
to your emotional response when faced by another person’s anger.161 Plus, Jesus responded variously when faced
with adversaries: sometimes with mocking humor, sometimes with anger, sometimes with some other emotional or
unemotional reaction.
       In his book, The Humor of Christ, Trueblood shows that the meek-and-mild stereotype of Jesus as being
wimpy is simply false. Jesus punched back. He counter jabbed the Pharisees. He counterattacked them (p. 16).
When they were trying to find a fault in Him, Jesus responded with anger (Mk 3:1-6). When they personally attacked
Him in a heated exchange, He responded scathingly: “You are of your father the devil, and you want to do the
desires of your father” (John 8:44). He rebuked His own disciple, Peter, very harshly: “He turned and said to Peter,
‘Get behind Me, Satan! You are a stumbling block to Me; for you are not setting your mind on God’s interests, but
mans’” (Mt 16:23). Jesus got angry with His disciples. When He saw how they were treating the little children, “He
was indignant and said to them, ‘Permit the children to come to Me; do not hinder them; for the kingdom of God
belongs to such as these’” (Mk 10:14).
       There is a limit to patience being a virtue. Taken to the excess, patient toleration can be a vice:

                The fiction that Christ was endlessly patient will not bear examination. In His impatience with
         His inept disciples He is reported as saying, “How much longer must I endure you?” (Matt. 17:17,
         N.E.B.). In the parable of the Barren Fig Tree (Luke 13:6-9) we have a vivid account of the divine
         impatience. The clear teaching is that it is reasonable to be patient for a while, in order to give the
         unfruitful person or unproductive organization a chance, but that there is a limit to the required
         patience, and that, when we go beyond this limit, tolerance ceases to be a virtue. It is all right, we
         are told, to put on manure for one more year, but no longer.162


161
      See Excursus: The Choice of Anger, 167.
162
      Elton Trueblood, The Humor of Christ (San Francisco: Harper & Row, 1964), 16-17.
Chapter 6. Adams’ Nouthetic Counseling                                                                         Page 101
       Jesus indicated that there were limits to His patience. When the epileptic boy’s father complained about His
disciples not being able to free him from the demon, he complained to Jesus and got a rebuke from Jesus in the
process: “‘And I begged Your disciples to cast it out, and they could not.’ And Jesus answered and said, ‘O
unbelieving and perverted generation, how long shall I be with you, and put up with you? Bring your son here’”
(Lk 9:40-41).
       The time allowed for a person to grow is limited. Beyond that time, tolerance for that person’s lack of growth
“ceases to be virtue,” as Trueblood has demonstrated. In certain cases, forgiveness can be sin.163 Lovingly tolerating
willful lack of growth becomes a vice. Paul had enough of the Corinthians lack of growth, and he clearly let them
know it, calling them immature babies. Paul used mocking at times to make his point. The writer of Hebrews also
did not mince words in confronting carnal believers for their lack of growth:
         11
           Concerning him we have much to say, and it is hard to explain, since you have become dull of
         hearing. 12 For though by this time you ought to be teachers, you have need again for someone to teach
         you the elementary principles of the oracles of God, and you have come to need milk and not solid
         food. 13 For everyone who partakes only of milk is not accustomed to the word of righteousness, for
         he is a babe. 14 But solid food is for the mature, who because of practice have their senses trained to
         discern good and evil. (Heb 5:11 - 6:1)

       We might call this nouthetic sarcasm. The biblical writer is telling these believers to stop being little, milk-
sucking babies and grow up! Is the use of such nouthetic sarcasm permissible today? Trueblood acknowledges that
laughter can sometimes be used as a cruel weapon used to hurt others. Yet he defends Jesus’ use of laughter/humor,
noting that used rightly it can “have a purgative effect” (p. 54). The most common type of humor Christ used to
chastise was irony:

                 Though Christ employed several types of humor, the most common type which He used is irony,
         i.e., a holding up to public view of either vice or folly, but without a note of bitterness or the attempt
         to harm. The ironical is always marked with a subtle sharpness of insight, free from the desire to
         wound. Ibis is what distinguishes it most clearly from sarcasm, A number of examples can be
         assembled to illustrate this light. (Italics his, 55.)

        Using this differentiation as a possible starting point, as it applies to mocking, we could surmise that the
difference between non-nouthetic mocking and nouthetic mocking comes down to ultimate intent. Ultimately, are
you seeking to harm or to help by using mocking? Mocking hurts. Mocking is intended to hurt. Nouthetic mocking,
though, is intended to help by hurting. Helping is the intention. Hurting is a means to that intended end. The same
is true of discipline. Discipline hurts. Nouthetic discipline is intended to hurt in order to help. A hurt-help-intention
would be the key distinguishing trait of such nouthetic tools.
        Notwithstanding, we must not conclude that Jesus limited His toolbox to tools of the of hurt-help-intention
type. As a case in point, Jesus also used sarcasm, both in mixed and pure forms:

                The dividing line which separates irony and sarcasm is sometimes a narrow one and is easily
         crossed. Truth compels us to say that it is sometimes crossed in the words recorded in the Gospels. In
         a few instances the irony becomes so exaggerated that it is really sarcastic. We do well to note carefully
         the instances of this, inasmuch as Christ must have felt that the gravity of some problems required a
         sterner treatment. (P. 64).

       As Trueblood demonstrates, the Lord sometimes mixed sarcasm with some of His more pungent sayings
(p. 65). Sometimes Jesus’ barbs were very sarcastic (p. 66). “Christ’s major weapon against the Pharisaic attack
was laughter, and He used it fully” (p. 74). At times, He employed “mock admiration” and “mock respect” as
humorous forms of sarcasm (pp. 81,83). Sarcasm certainly had its rightful place on the loving lips of our Savior.
       A loving person can use sarcasm. Jesus did. Paul did. Some say, however, that sarcasm should have no part
in our lives. Therefore, we should not be imitators of Paul or Christ. Obviously, I am being sarcastic of the no-
sarcasm position with that latter statement. Paul says, “Be imitators of me, just as I also am of Christ” (1Cor 11:1).
163
      See Sinful Forgiveness, 35.
Page 102                                                                              Degrees of Love and Forgiveness
In that same chapter, Paul immediately will go on to tell them twice, rather sarcastically, “I do not praise you”
(1Cor 11:17); “I will not praise you” (1Cor 11:22). He reproves them rather than praises them. If we are to be
imitators of Paul and Christ, sarcasm has a proper place in dealing with some people, even when dealing with carnal
believers like the Corinthians. This is the same epistle where Paul called them carnal, milk-sucking, babies.
         1
          And I, brethren, could not speak to you as to spiritual people but as to carnal, as to babes in Christ.
         2
          I fed you with milk and not with solid food; for until now you were not able to receive it, and even
         now you are still not able; 3 for you are still carnal. For where there are envy, strife, and divisions
         among you, are you not carnal and behaving like mere men? (1Cor 3:1-3; NKJ)

       Nouthetically confronting immature, carnal, milk-sucking, spiritual babies with their spiritual state certainly
has biblical precedence when they have persisted in that state beyond a reasonable period of time. Any supposed
line between mocking and sarcasm might be a matter of degree. Even the line between irony and sarcasm is not
always clear. Nouthetic mocking would generally suffice in working with counselees seeking help. Because they
are seeking help, this form of humorous discipline should generally suffice. The potential difference between
nouthetic mocking and nouthetic sarcasm might be reduced to a matter of emphasis:

              •   Nouthetic mocking: hurt-help-intention.
              •   Nouthetic sarcasm: hurt-help-intention.

        In response to Sue’s anger, I used a variety of responses. Let us presume that nouthetic mocking humor was
one of those responses, since she said that I laughed at her and mocked her. Taking her assessment at face value,
let us further evaluate such mocking humor from a biblical perspective.
        As noted, I loved Spock in Star Trek, my kind of guy, for the most part. One of his sayings, when faced with
a decision, was something to the effect, “Logic would dictate....” He would then proceed with pronouncing a logical
course of action. I typically amend his saying with this, “Wisdom would dictate....” And then I suggest what I
consider a wise course of action. The course of action I suggested to Sue was to focus on Scripture rather than on
her emotions. She refused. Well, let us ask, “What would Wisdom dictate in the face of someone who rejects wise
counsel?” Wisdom can answer that question for herself:
         20
            Wisdom shouts in the street, she lifts her voice in the square; 21 At the head of the noisy streets she
         cries out; at the entrance of the gates in the city, she utters her sayings: 22 "How long, O naive ones,
         will you love simplicity? And scoffers delight themselves in scoffing, and fools hate knowledge?
         23
            “Turn to my reproof, Behold, I will pour out my spirit on you; I will make my words known to you.
         24
            “Because I called, and you refused; I stretched out my hand, and no one paid attention; 25 And
         you neglected all my counsel, and did not want my reproof; 26 I will even laugh at your calamity;
         I will mock when your dread comes, 27 when your dread comes like a storm, and your calamity
         comes on like a whirlwind, when distress and anguish come on you. 28 “Then they will call on me, but
         I will not answer; They will seek me diligently, but they shall not find me, 29 because they hated
         knowledge, and did not choose the fear of the Lord. 30 “They would not accept my counsel, they
         spurned all my reproof. 31 “So they shall eat of the fruit of their own way, and be satiated with
         their own devices. 32 “For the waywardness of the naive shall kill them, and the complacency of fools
         shall destroy them. 33 “But he who listens to me shall live securely, and shall be at ease from the dread
         of evil.” (Prov 1:20-33)

       Wisdom says that she will mock and laugh at those who refuse her wise counsel when their calamity comes.
If we follow Wisdom’s example, mocking laughter at the tears of those who reject wise counsel certainly seems to
be an appropriate response. Dare we even say, “A wise response?” Yes, I dare.
       As already noted, in his book, Dare to Discipline, Dobson recommends disciplinary extinction be used when
facing someone who is using emotional blackmail in throwing manipulative temper tantrums.164 As noted, my
perception was that Sue was doing just that, throwing a temper tantrum. In the example I cited from Dobson, he

164
      See Disciplinary Extinction, 75.
Chapter 6. Adams’ Nouthetic Counseling                                                                         Page 103
recommended ignoring the emotional blackmail attempts. He cites other examples where he took a more proactive
response in dealing with those manifesting such behavior. For our purposes, though, it will suffice to think back to
Dobson’s example cited earlier, in which Mark’s parents ignored his screaming, kicking, flailing, temper tantrum.
Since they did not know what more to do, his parents did nothing and continued reading their newspapers. Mark
apparently came to see that his behavior was so silly that he eventually stopped doing it.
       What if, rather than be appalled by Mark’s behavior and eventually ignoring it, his parents had used humorous
mocking instead? Suppose that they had applauded and smiled, giving a little clap of their hands and saying, “Come
on Mark, you can put on a better show than that. Scream louder, or at least kick harder.” Would Dobson approve?
I think so. After all, Dobson cites other examples of more proactive extinction responses and then goes on to
conclude, “The uses of extinction are limited only by the imagination and creativity of the parent or teacher.”165
Mark might have caught on to the silliness of his behavior sooner if his parents had dared to use mocking discipline
to demonstrate the silliness of his behavior. The ineffectiveness of Mark’s behavior to manipulate them into feeling
distraught with his emotional blackmail would have been perceived quicker, perhaps extinguishing his silly
behavior sooner, if his parents had used nouthetic mocking. Rather than ignore the emotional blackmails, one could
appropriately use mocking humor to make fun of any such emotional blackmail attempts. One might achieve
accelerated results with such an approach.
       Perhaps the most vivid form of humorous mocking in Scripture was a very literal discipline extinction
employed by Elijah:
               25
                   So Elijah said to the prophets of Baal, “Choose one ox for yourselves and prepare it first for
         you are many, and call on the name of your god, but put no fire under it.” 26 Then they took the ox
         which was given them and they prepared it and called on the name of Baal from morning until noon
         saying, “O Baal, answer us.” But there was no voice and no one answered. And they leaped about
         the altar which they made. 27 And it came about at noon, that Elijah mocked them and said,
         “Call out with a loud voice, for he is a god; either he is occupied or gone aside, or is on a journey,

         themselves according to their custom with swords and lances until the blood gushed out on them.
