         according to the number of the tribes of the sons of Jacob, to whom the word of the Lord had come,

         there. (1Kgs 18:25-40)

       Elijah was humorously mocking the prophets of Baal, taunting them to cry louder, just as Mark’s parents
could have done, if they had used Elijah’s technique. In the end, the crying and flailing were extinguished. Was
Elijah acting wisely? God seemed to think so. Would biblical Wisdom dare to use humorous mocking as sarcasm
165
      Dobson, Dare, 86.
Page 104                                                                            Degrees of Love and Forgiveness
for those who spurn her wise counsel? We know so. My counsel to Sue was to use Scripture rather than tears. When
Sue rejected my wise counsel, I viewed her response as being foolish. Wisdom would dictate that humorous
mocking would be an appropriate response for such occasions.166 The prophets of Baal were opposed to the
proclamation of the word of God, and Sue was opposed to the application of the word of God. Humorous mocking
would be a viable response to such opposition.
       Biblical confrontational counseling can provide real help and faster results in contrast to the approach taken
by psychiatrists. By confronting sin as sin and dealing with it as sin, we get superior results. The therapeutic results
can be much quicker and more successful than those found in secular nonconfrontational approaches.
Therapeutically, the difference in the success of these two competing approaches can be reminiscent of the Elijah
and Baal showdown.
                                                  Hope Affirmed
       If, as Adams explains, Sue were to confess her sin (Jam 5:16) and repent through Christ’s enabling grace
(Phil 4:13),167 then she would have real hope of recovery:

             The Medical Model destroys hope. Discouragement and despair permeate the concept of
      “mental illness.” Most people are aware that the mental institutions are not helping many people. They
      also know that psychiatrists characteristically say, “You must expect therapy to take a long time,
      and then we can promise you nothing.” So to inform a Christian client in an early interview, “Your
      problem seems basically to be the result of sin,” does not discourage him, but rather gives him hope.
      Christians know that sin and its effects can be dealt with because God has said so in the Scriptures and
      Christ died to overcome sin. So when sin is mentioned, there is real hope. (P. 138-139)

Adams provides an example of what real hope looks like when people submit to nouthetic counseling. The
contrasting results are so stark that it reminds one of the effectiveness of Elijah’s approach versus that of the
prophets of Baal:

             Millie, a Christian, had been in and out of mental institutions for thirteen years. No one seemed
      to be able to help her. She was lying around the house unable to do her housework, not caring for her
      children. Her husband was in complete despair and Millie, herself, was totally depressed. She was
      brought for counseling by friends from another state. Millie’s first visit made a remarkable change in
      her life. When a nouthetic counselor confronted her strongly on that first day about her lazy,
      undisciplined, irresponsible behavior and told her to go back to church, to get to work at home,
      to do her ironing and cleaning, everyone was shocked. That is, everyone but Millie, who
      responded with hope. Her husband was dumbfounded. Millie had been coddled by a psychiatrist
      for nearly a year. The psychiatrist listened to her sympathetically and sold her tranquilizers, yet
      there was no improvement. After her first week of nouthetic counseling, on her own Millie laid
      her pills aside. (Pp. 141-142)

    If I did not use humorous mocking on that occasion, hopefully a comment I made later would qualify, when I
told her if she wanted to hurt my feelings, she should try harder—by at least speaking the truth. See Sunday School
Car Trip, 109. Wisdom dictates that humorous mocking is prudent for disciplinary extinction. Dobson provides
graphs which show that it normally takes much longer for disciplinary extinction to be completely effective. Perhaps
if disciplinary mocking is used more frequently and consistently, Sue will learn more quickly.

     To repent means to stop sinning through Christ’s enabling grace. Sinless perfection is not envisioned
(1Jn 1:8,10). However, walking in the light and in fellowship with no known sin is possible (1Jn 1:7,9) so that we
do not sin relative to that degree of expectation (1Jn 2:1). Thus, considering what Paul will say in Rom 8:1 about
the victory available to those believers who walk according to the Spirit, he can confidently command believers,
“Stop letting sin reign in your mortal body” (Rom 6:12; TM). Yielding to the mastery of sin is a choice (Rom 6:14).
Believers can yield to the mastery of Christ rather than the mastery of sin. To repent means to choose Christ, rather
than sin, as your master. For a discussion of Rom 8:1, see Bob Wilkin, “Romans 8:1 May Not Mean What You
Think It Means, Part 2.” Available at: https://faithalone.org/blog/romans-81-may-not-mean-what-you-think-it-

                                                   Foolish Venting
       Yet, to have biblical results, one must use biblical means. Ventilation is not confession. Prov 29:11 says, “A
fool gives vent to all his feelings” (CJB). “A fool gives full vent to his anger, but a wise man holds it in check”
(CSB). Other verses cautioning against being prone to anger include Prov 14:29; 22:24-25 29:20,22. Adams
expounds upon such passages:

             The idea of allowing anger to break out in an undisciplined manner by saying or doing whatever
      comes into mind without weighing the consequences, without counting to ten, without holding it back
      and quieting it, without hearing the whole story, is totally wrong. Group therapy that is predicated
      upon the principle of ventilating anger in order to get something off one’s chest, is totally out of
      accord with the verses just cited. Ventilating sinful feelings is simply unbiblical. The words “full
      vent” (29:11) mean literally “to send forth all of one’s spirit.” The wise man, according to Proverbs,
      knows how to check his feelings. Feelings do not have to rule and run a person’s life. They can
      be checked and quieted. Clients should practice holding back and quieting anger rather than
      ventilating it. Checking anger is neither harmful nor impossible since the Bible commands it. (P. 221)
                                              Nouthetic Confession
       Nouthetic counselors contend that ventilation should be replaced with confession: “They insist that
ventilation of feelings must be replaced by confession of Adams’. No longer will they speak of emotional
problems, but rather of behavioral problems. They say the term ‘mental illness’ must be replaced by words that
indicate irresponsible behavior” (p. 14). Confrontation leading to confession is the methodology employed. Both
the confession and forgiveness need to be formally and explicitly stated:

      One of the problems that has already been mentioned in another context is that people sometimes
      assume that they have secured forgiveness without specifically asking to be forgiven. Nouthetic
      counselors stress the importance of saying specifically, “Will you forgive me?” When attempting
      reconciliation, not only is it important to ask specifically for forgiveness, but it is also useful to attempt
      to get a clear statement of forgiveness like, “Yes, I will forgive you.” (P. 227)
                                         Nouthetic Tough Love
      Like Dobson, Adams affirms the importance of discipline:

             Clients frequently comment about the important part that insistence upon discipline
      played in the solution to their problems. At the end of counseling, when the six-week checkup
      comes around or during the debriefing session which closes regular counseling sessions, they
      often say something like, “We appreciate the fact that you were rough on us, that you were hard
      on us, that you did not let us get away with anything.” Most people appreciate this because for
      the first time someone has held them to the commands and the commitments of Scripture. For
      the first time, their lives have begun to be structured biblically. For the first time the gimmicks,
      tricks and ruses that they developed to make others pity and coddle them have been penetrated.
      Clients themselves recognize that this is why they have been helped; and they say “thanks” for
      it, because they have begun to taste the fruit of holiness. (Pp. 167-168)

      Bob told his counselors at the final briefing session: “What you said to us at the sixth session was just
      what we needed. I don’t think we would have made it if you hadn’t been rough on us and knocked
      some sense into our heads. Thanks!” (Pp. 169-170)

       Not all clients are going to respond positively to discipline. Adams acknowledges that most people appreciate
it, thus implicitly admitting that all do not. Understandably, those who are helped by nouthetic counseling will
appreciate it. Those who reject it will not. Dobson, Adams, nor God for that matter, pretend that discipline works
every time. Even God’s discipline fails to achieve its objective at times, which is why the author of Hebrews warns:
      5
        “My son, do not regard lightly the discipline of the Lord, nor faint when you are reproved by Him;
      6
        For those whom the Lord loves He disciplines, And He scourges every son whom He receives.” 7 It
      is for discipline that you endure; God deals with you as with sons; for what son is there whom his
Page 106                                                                          Degrees of Love and Forgiveness
                                   8
      father does not discipline? But if you are without discipline, of which all have become partakers,
      then you are illegitimate children and not sons. (Heb 12:5-8)

       Discipline is a form of love. Some believers will resist God’s loving discipline to such an extent that they
become undisciplined. Likewise, in the natural realm, those who reject discipline long enough eventually become
undisciplined. Nothing is left but punishment. God’s love for us as legitimate children is conditioned on our
submitting to His discipline. Believers who reject God’s discipline long enough are no longer loved as legitimate
children. The Lord no longer disciplines (paideuo) them. Instead, they will receive severe punishment (timoria) at
the Bema (Heb 10:29). They are cut off from God’s misthological kindness and dealt with severely instead (Rom
11:22). God will love them as illegitimate children rather than as mature, heir-entitled sons.
                                           Team-Multiple Counseling
       Adams recommends team counseling if counselors can work together in harmony: “Not every counselor
approaches clients in precisely the same way, and this may be good. Individuality has its place in counseling as
elsewhere. Yet, unless counselors learn to work together in harmony as a team, confusion results” (emphasis his,
206). “Can two walk together, unless they are agreed?” (Amos 3:3) Basic harmony is necessary for fellowship and
working together. Counseling all parties involved in the controversy at one time is also advantageous: “There are
many implications of multiple counseling. Solutions to difficulties often are reached much more effectively, much
more permanently, and much more rapidly when all of the parties of a controversy are included in counseling”
(p. 237). “When the other parties know what is going on in counseling, they potentially become assistant counselors,
who day by day may help carry the counseling into the rest of the week” (p. 241). Another reason for my putting
my counseling strategy in this book format is so that others involved in this counseling might know what is going
on and why I have chosen the confrontational recourse. Most of the books I have read on counseling recommend
this confrontational course of action. Therefore, condensing the statements of such books concisely in the present
book allows others, such as Dianne and Sam, an opportunity to more effectively counsel as part of a team effort.
       Nevertheless, my personal preference to defer the nikological aspects of the counseling to others, such as
Solomon, is certainly reasonable: “There may be times when cases should be transferred from one counselor to
another. If one counselor is getting nowhere, he should be frank enough to admit this and hand over the case to
someone who may be able to do a more adequate job” (pp. 206-207). I would include writers such as Solomon,
Dobson, and Adams as part of this team effort since I have employed their basic methodology. After all, Christians
have varying degrees of competency in various fields:

             In this volume, I have tried to show that the minister, Christian workers, and indeed every
      Christian, may consider himself at least potentially competent to counsel. I freely acknowledge that
      some counselors are more effective than others, and I fully recognize that many ministers have not
      been trained to counsel biblically. But to agree that for one reason or another (mistraining, lack of
      training, etc.) Christian workers are not now competent to counsel, is not the same as saying that they
      cannot become so. Given the qualities mentioned in Romans 15:14 and Colossians 3:16, plus the
      proper convictions about counseling, any Christian worker may become a helpful counselor in the
      place where God has called him to serve. That the work of counseling should be carried on
      preeminently by ministers and other Christians whose gifts, training and calling especially qualify and
      require them to pursue the work, I do not doubt. Moreover, in the course of counseling, every counselor
      will encounter difficult cases and special problems which go beyond his present competency and which
      therefore indicate the need for referral to some other Christian counselor. Medical problems demand
      close cooperation with a physician (preferably a Christian). Yet, to say all this in no way denies or
      detracts from the competency of the Christian counselor to counsel, so far as his ability and training
      legitimately allow him to do so. He should see no need, therefore, to defer to a self-appointed caste of
      men called psychiatrists. (Emphasis his, 268.)
                                          Forgive Then Maybe Forget
      Once forgiveness is granted, forgetting the offense is natural but not equatable:

            Forgiving is not the same as forgetting. Nor does the Bible command us to “forgive and forget.”
      What it tells us is that our model of forgiveness is God’s forgiveness of us (Ephesians 4:32). How does
      God forgive? He promises us. “Your sins and iniquities will I remember against you no more”
Chapter 6. Adams’ Nouthetic Counseling                                                                       Page 107
      (Jeremiah 31:34c).
             “To remember no more” is not the same as forgetting. It is to work actively at not raising
      a matter: to forget is to have a matter passively lade from memory. The first is forgiveness; the
      latter is the result of forgiveness. One can promise not to raise a matter again and he can keep
      that promise, whether he feels like it or not. He cannot promise to forget. Forgiving leads to
      forgetting, because if a matter is never raised to anyone else and if it is not brooded upon by the one
      who granted forgiveness, it will soon fade from memory. I have seen even sins with the most serious
      social consequences, the grossest sorts of offenses, forgotten in shorter time than one would think
      possible when the promise of forgiveness is kept.168

LaHaye gives much the same counsel with some historical background:

              Henry Ward Beecher (1813—1887), an American preacher, once said, “‘I can forgive, but I
      cannot forget’ is only another way of saying, ‘I will not forgive.’ Forgiveness ought to be like a
      canceled note—torn in two, and burned up, so it never can be shown against one.” Beecher’s thoughts
      were later modified by others to express, “If you haven’t really forgotten, then you haven’t truly
      forgiven.” I don’t believe that is true at all. I don’t think we ever truly forget the offenses we’ve
      experienced in life. I believe we remember them. In fact, I don’t think God forgets our offenses.
      “Wait a minute,” you say. “What about the verse in Jeremiah (31:34) that says, ‘For I will forgive their
      wickedness and will remember their sins no more’?” The phrase “remember…no more” does not mean
      it is out of God’s memory banks. How can an omniscient God—a God who knows everything—forget?
      Moreover, in other passages of Scripture God says He will remember their wickedness (see Hosea 7:2;
      8:13; 9:9). I believe the concept God wants to convey is not that the event or sin itself is forgotten, but
      that judgment for the offense is removed. In other words, “I will not hold it against them in judgment
      anymore.....
              The plain truth of the matter is that I do remember. I don’t forget! But I have a choice with
      regard to how I will respond to my memories. I can let my memories lie and move on in life, or I can
      let my memories overpower me. (Emphasis his, 108).

      For those who need a simplistic, pragmatic way of dealing with forgiveness and forgetting, the statements
provided above by Adams and LaHaye should serve that purpose quite well. For those who would prefer something
much more in-depth, my 4DF can be consulted. Therein, the possibility of God forgetting is entertained as a realistic
possibility. God has perfect mental control and can forget if He chooses. Even then, however, God strategically
applies His ability to forget to various strata. Forgiving and not remembering are not the same thing; nor are they
necessarily free. At upper strata, they are offered conditionally. Nonetheless, to prevent a root of bitterness and
promote a forgiving spirit, one should relinquish the right to remain angry even if the offending party does not
repent. If Sue does not repent, then I will continue to pray for her. Relinquishing anger via prayer is much more
prudent than relishing anger through an unforgiving spirit (Mt 5:44; Mk 11:25; Eph 4:32; Heb 12:15).169
                                                  Conclusion
      “Are Christians competent to counsel? Of course they are,” though, as Adams acknowledges, “but not enough

                                   Chapter 7. Reoccurrence
                                                     Introduction
       In the previous chapters, the occasion for writing this book was given. A counselee, whom I am referring to
as Sue, had sent me and my wife, Dianne, a six-page letter accusing us of being cold, unloving, and uncaring. This
counselee distorts reality due to her rejection syndrome. One way I attempted to correct this distortion was to
differentiate between volitional and emotional love, pointing out, for example, that the lack of the latter does not
disprove the lack of the former. When Sue came to our house after writing that letter, I presented her with the rough
draft of that volitional-emotional differentiation to demonstrate that her emotions were not in accord with factual
reality since we really cared about her regardless of what she was allowing her emotions to tell her. That material
was to become the foundation for the material that went into chapter one of the present book. The second chapter
of the present book documents some of our interaction with Sue when she came to our house. I used a tough love
approach. Sue did not receive it well, which was not surprising. She sent a series of texts in response. However, we
were eventually able to move beyond this encounter to some extent, but the underlying issues had not been resolved.
I proceeded with writing this book to address the underlying issues, with the anticipation that these problems would
resurface. The wait was not long.
                                               Sunday School Car Trip
       As discussed previously, Sue had come over to our house on Monday evening (1/21/2019) to demand that I
apologize for hurting her feelings. I had refused. But things were better after that encounter for a few weeks. Even
so, I continued working on this book, anticipating that the cease-fire would not last. Since neither she nor I had
acknowledged any Adams’, no repentance or confession had taken place. Sill, we ended that encounter on relatively
good terms for the time being. In fact, we invited Sue and Sam to ride with us to Sunday School.
       Dianne and I attend Sunday School at a church forty-five minutes away. Sue had not attended this class
before. She and Sam rode with me and Dianne to that class for a couple of weeks. Things were going rather well.
Even so, I was still working on this book since the underlying tensions had not been resolved. Our Sunday School
class had been studying through Revelation and were now at the end of Revelation. Sue kept asking various
questions during the class. Many of the questions she was asking pertained to material that already had been
answered and discussed in detail in the class. Some of her questions would require very extensive explanation. The
teacher paused to answer some of Sue’s questions, at least at a basic level, trying to proceed through the material
so that we could see the point being made from the big picture. The teacher was trying to avoid the problem of not
being able to see the forest for the trees, so to speak. Trying to examine the details without understanding the
situation from its broader perspective would be counterproductive to understanding the passage. But Sue responded
to the teacher in front of the class, “You’re not explaining anything at all!”
       After church that Sunday morning (2/10/2019), the four of us (Sue and Sam, as well as Dianne and I) went
to a restaurant for lunch. On the way, Sue asked what we thought about her questions during Sunday School. I
responded that many of her questions had been explained previously in the class and would call for a more thorough
explanation than what could be attempted in the class. The illustration I used was that if one walked into an advanced
calculus class and did not understand algebra, the class could not pull over to teach that student algebra. However,
I added, we could discuss the questions in more depth over lunch. Sue took offense at my statement. So, when we
ordered lunch, she refused to eat, preferring to pout instead. Then, on the forty-five-minute drive back home from
church, she was extremely upset with me for upsetting her and vented her anger. Such behavior is not that surprising
given her rejection syndrome, as Solomon explains:

                The person who has had a pattern of rejection from childhood will often read rejection
         into a situation or conversation where none exists. And, whether or not the rejection is actual, if he
         reads rejection into the conversation or circumstances, it opens up old rejection wounds which amplify
         many times the supposed or actual rejection. The same statements or set of conditions where
         rejection actually existed might go completely unnoticed by a person not previously programmed
         for rejection. The person with old rejection wounds, on the other hand, may be completely
         devastated by merely being slighted in some particular.171

171
      Solomon, Rejection, 38.
Page 110                                                                            Degrees of Love and Forgiveness

        Once again, Sue was demanding an apology from me for hurting her feelings, and once again I refused. She
was very upset; I was not. My not being upset evidently upset her even more. In any event, I explained, yet again,
that I was not sorry for hurting her feelings; therefore, I could not apologize for hurting her feelings or say that I
was sorry for hurting her feelings. Rather, I explained that I would prefer for us to conduct ourselves rationally
rather than emotionally. She criticized me as being Stoic. Fine. After all, I was being rather emotionally unaffected
by her emotional outburst.
        She also repeatedly criticized me as being uncaring. I responded with logic rather than with emotion by
explaining the fallacy of her statement. I did care about her, as is demonstrated in part by my taking the time to
write this book in hopes that it might help. I also pointed out that trying to be rational with someone who is being
emotional is largely doomed to failure, reminding her that Solomon explains why this is so in Rejection. Thus, I
told her that our situation would not be resolved with a band-aid but would require something much more extensive.
Thus, I encouraged her to continue with rereading Rejection. In the meantime, I told her that I would continue
working on this book. She continued in her verbal attacks, demanding that the matter be resolved then and there
with dialogue. As I pointed out to her via the material she was reading in Rejection, her demand was not logically
possible.172
        Seeing that my rational response was not working and could not work with her in her current emotional state,
I switched gears. At the end of the ride back home, after having endured her forty-five-minute verbal assault, I told
her that if she were going to try to hurt my feelings, she should try harder.173 The shocked response she gave was in
part what was I was going for, hoping that the truth of what I had to say next might at least make a dent. I told her
that if she could not at least speak the truth in love, at least speak the truth. The truth was that I care about her.
Therefore, for her to say that I did not care did not phase me because it was untrue. If she wanted to phase me, she
would at least have to speak the truth. My hope is that taking this approach would at least cause her to pause and
consider the points being made in Rejection and think through the difference between truth and emotion. I concluded
by encouraging her to continue in her reading of Solomon’s books and by saying, “May the Lord bless you. Have
a nice day.”
        The next morning, Sue proceeded yet again by sending a text saying that she wants nothing to do with our
family since I had rebuked her and hurt her feelings.174 The last time that she had started a day off with such a text,
I had told her that I would respond in writing via this book. On that previous occasion, she then proceeded to text
me throughout the day, telling me how cruel, uncaring, and unloving I am. So, this time, I responded that I would
be turning off her texts until she calmed down. However, given her perspective, I am not even sure that she can
calm down or discuss the issue calmly. A person must be able to see the distinction between volitional and affectual
love to be able to respond to this situation rationally. Without this distinction, her irrational assessment is
understandable to some extent. For instance, if she had simply criticized me as being unaffectionate to her on such
occasions, then this would have been an accurate statement. However, I do not equate affection with love. Affection
is a form of love, but it is not the only form of love. Disciplinary love need not be affectionate in order to be worthy
of the name love. Therefore, for her to say that I was being cruel, uncaring, and unloving because I was not being
affectionate is a slanderous accusation out of accord with reality. Consequently, the present book provides a
framework for laying a foundation that will hopefully result in a resolution by providing some rather basic
distinctions.



172
    The Bible tells us, “If possible, so far as it depends on you, be at peace with all men” (Rom 12:18). At that point
in time, while riding in the car from Sunday School, peace with Sue was impossible. The Bible makes allowances
for lack of peace when it is impossible. Since it was impossible at the time, there was no need for me to pretend that
it was possible or to beat my head against the windshield in frustration. Rather, I largely put my emotions in neutral
by logically accepting the current impossibility, knowing that I would resume work on this book when I got home,
in hopes of moving reconciliation from the realm of impossibility to possibility.

                                              Sunday School Follow-up
       As I was wrapping up the initial rough draft of this book, my youngest sister, Janina, shared her testimony
the following Sunday morning (2/17/2019) at the nearby church where she attends. Janina attested that she had
learned to overcome some very negative emotional traumas: including inferiority complex at a young age, followed
by subsequent rapes. The video was posted on the church’s Facebook site.175 After listening to the video that
evening, I sent Janina an email, commending her for a powerful and well done presentation of her testimony, noting
that I was sorry for what she had been through but was thankful that she was allowing the Lord to use it to make
her even more beautiful than she would be otherwise.176 Her describing herself as a warrior princess was especially
impressive. To assist in her ministry, I offered her a copy of my book, 4DF, and copies of Charles Solomon’s two
books, Handbook to Happiness and The Ins and Out of Rejection. Additionally, I sent her a link to his website:
https://gracefellowshipinternational.com. I explained that she and Solomon had several things in common. They
both have secular degrees in counseling, and they both stress identity truth, trichotomy, and spiritual warfare (thus
polemology). As a bonus, his two books tend toward Free Grace soteriology. Plus, Charles Stanley recommends
Solomon’s ministry. Hopefully, Janina will be able to incorporate his material into her local counseling and cover
all the bases: Free Grace soteriology, exchanged life nikology combined with warfare demonology, and kingdom
misthology. In any case, I recommended that Sue and Sam consult with Janina. About this same time, Sue contacted
our Sunday School teacher asking if he would be willing to facilitate a reconciliation between me and her. He asked
if I would be willing to do so. I told him that I would be very much interested in doing so, in the spirit of Mt 18:15-
17. After all, I was writing this book with that very objective in view. To the extent she is really interested in biblical
counsel, I am all for it.
                                                   Mixed Motives
       Frankly, though, I wonder if Sue’s expressed interest in receiving counsel from me and Dianne was her
primary reason for seeking our counsel when she gave us that six-page letter. No doubt Sue is interested in learning
biblical truth. Yet, when it comes to applying our recommendations of that biblical truth to her situation, she has
shown a history of resistance. Why is she so supposedly interested in our counsel now that she and Sam are married,
when she spurned our counsel before their marriage? Dianne and I had counseled them that, given Sue’s issues with
the rejection syndrome, they would be better waiting to get married until after stability regarding the rejection
syndrome had been achieved.
       Months before her marriage, when Sue was expressing her negative emotions regarding the rejection that she
had experienced from her father, she also expressed disdain for the help we attempted to offer. For instance, I had
suggested that she and Sam read Solomon’s two books. She said that she found my advice and exchanged-life
recommendations disappointing because she did not see how this was supposed to make her feel better. She was
reading Solomon’s book, Happiness, and was unhappy with it because she did not see how it was going to make
her feel happy. She just regarded its exchanged-life teaching as one more thing that she was supposed to learn how
to do. She did not see any healing balm in it. I told her that I thought exchanged-life teaching is a good place to
begin because, for one thing, you learn to give thanks for everything (Eph 5:20), even for the pain. My father was
an exchanged life minister, and he instilled this foundational truth into me at the very conception of my learning
exchanged life truth. And I have found it very helpful over the years. For example, when Dianne got cancer, we
gave thanks for it. We did not like it, but we gave thanks for it. When I get negative responses from Sue, I give
thanks for them. I do not like them, but I give thanks for them. For I believe that God works all these things for my
good (Rom 8:28). One good thing that could come out of her negativity, for example, is this book.
       As to Solomon’s other book, Rejection, I told her that the feelings of rejection she had expressed regarding
her dad sounded very much like what Solomon described in that second book. I suggested that she especially read
his sections entitled “Rejection in Marriage” and “Rejection from Infancy to Adulthood” (pp. 37-42). Excerpts from
that material have been quoted herein. Statistically, the bottom line is that Solomon said, “Marriages established
under such conditions seldom endure.” Methodically, the bottom line is that those who are caught in a rejection
syndrome know how to respond to rejection, not to acceptance. Thus, when they are given acceptance, they will
find a way to turn it into rejection because they know how to handle the rejection rather than accepting. They will
sabotage acceptance with self-perpetuating rejection:

        I warned Sue that if these issues were not addressed, then, statistically, they would probably destroy her

getting married would be the wiser recourse, I advised. Nevertheless, they were determined to proceed anyway. So
Dianne and I, having given them our warnings, gave them our support. They are adults. They are going to have to
take responsibility for their actions—and for their emotions. Given such instances, in which Sue’s interest in taking
our advice has been less than stellar, my impression is that her seeking our advice is, at times, mixed with other
motives, such as wanting our never-ending sympathy for her self-pity. When our sympathy for her self-pity
dwindles, her desire for counsel seems to dwindle as well. To some degree, she seems to condition her willingness
to listen to our counsel to our willingness to pity her self-pity. Her mixed motives may give her mixed emotions.
Mixed emotions are not necessarily bad in and of themselves, of course. It depends on what is causing the mixture.
But a never-ending thirst for self-pity puts Self on the throne and can be used by those desiring self-pity to ward off
acceptance from those who are only willing to give acceptance on other bases. Hopefully, meeting with the Sunday
School teacher will not prove to be a desire on her part to indulge her self-pity but resolve our issues biblically.
                                                  Mixed Emotions
        The intimacy between Sue and I, as well as others involved, will not reach maturity or consistency until we
are walking in harmony. Intimacy and affection are directly proportional to harmony. My hope is that this book will
result in increasing harmony between us rather than be a source of disharmony. My emotions while writing it have
been mixed. Performing the biblical analysis has been enjoyable and informative, as usual. Nonetheless, writing a
rebuttal that I must give to Sue in hopes that she will be mature enough to handle it has been unpleasant and is
certainly risky. The experience reminds me of raising my children. I did not enjoy disciplining them, and I risked
their displeasure (cp. Eph 6:4), but I disciplined them anyway because it was necessary. Love made it necessary.
From my fatherly experience, I take it that God, as our Heavenly Father, does not necessarily enjoy disciplining us
either; nevertheless, He does it because He loves us. After all, the Bible expects us to see the correlation between
our discipline and God’s disciple (Heb 12:6-8). “All discipline for the moment seems not to be joyful, but sorrowful
yet to those who have been trained by it, afterwards it yields the peaceful fruit of righteousness” (Heb 12:11). All

was true to form from Solomon’s predictions. Sue would provoke rejection in order to be rejected so that she could

forms of discipline (including this book) are unpleasant—both to the parent and the child; both to God and us; both
to the writer and the recipient—but the intent of discipline is to lead to peaceful righteousness. I want peace, not a
not momentary cease-fire that will erupt again later.
       Expecting emotional stability from Sue at this point would be premature because she is too immature to be
able to offer that, especially when emotional crises come, as they inevitably do. Still, I do expect her repentance
and confession on such occasions. Expecting such a response from her is reasonable and biblical, even at her level
of spiritual development. Plus, holding her accountable will help her mature, reaching higher levels of spiritual
development, teaching her not to let her emotions rule her. She may not be able to control her emotions directly,
but she can control her behavior immediately. To the extent I interact with her, I expect Spirit-enabled self-control.
To that end, writing this book is a labor of love, a love that is accompanied by mixed emotions.
       As has been noted herein, mixed emotions are understandable. Even God has mixed emotions. Consequently,
biblical writers had mixed emotions. As he writes, Paul says that he is weeping: “For many walk, of whom I often
told you, and now tell you even weeping, that they are enemies of the cross of Christ” (Phil 3:18). But just a few
moments later he tells them: “Rejoice in the Lord always; again I will say, rejoice!” (Phil 4:4). Earlier in the letter,
he said, “Therefore I sent him the more eagerly, that when you see him again you may rejoice, and I may be less
sorrowful” (Phil 2:28; NKJ). How could Paul exhort them to rejoice always while admitting that he himself was
sorrowful? Was he being hypocritical? No. Logical explanations are possible. This word for sorrowful can also be
translated as anxious. Even so, Paul tells them that he writes so that he may be “less anxious about you” (TM). He
is anxious yet later he tells them, “Be anxious for nothing, but in everything by prayer and supplication with
thanksgiving let your requests be made known to God” (Phil 4:6). How could Paul exhort them to be anxious for
nothing while admitting that he himself was anxious about them? Was he being hypocritical? No. Mixed emotions
are a fact of life. Logical explanations as to how this can be the case have been provided herein. Nevertheless, it is
a natural phenomenon with which we are all familiar. Being made in God’s image, we share this attribute with Him.
Thankfully, God is not ruled by His emotions, however. Nor does He want us to allow our emotions to rule us. He
gives us a personal example to follow—His own!
       At the rebellion of Korah, LaHaye relates how Moses used a logical plea so that God would not give full vent
to His anger:

      Some of the men accused Moses, saying, ‘You brought us out of lovely Egypt to kill us here in this
      terrible wilderness’ (Numbers 16:13). Keeping in mind that in ‘lovely Egypt’ they had been slaves of
      the king, beaten and mistreated, it is no wonder Moses responded in anger: ‘Then Moses was very
      angry and said to the Lord, “Do not accept their sacrifices!’” (Numbers 16:15).
             “To Moses’ surprise, God’s anger outdid his: ‘Get away from these people so that I may
      instantly destroy them’ (Numbers 16:21).
             “At that point Moses pleaded to God: ‘Must you be angry with all the people when one man
      sins?’ (Numbers 16:22). And God relented.
             “None of this anger was sin; yet both Moses and God were angry and free in expressing that
      anger. (LaHaye, 99-100.)

Likewise, in Ex 32:9-14, we read where Moses used reason to overcome the Lord’s anger:
      9
         And the Lord said to Moses, “I have seen this people, and behold, they are an obstinate people.

         “Now then let Me alone, that My anger may burn against them, and that I may destroy them; and I
      will make of you a great nation.” 11 Then Moses entreated the Lord his God, and said, “O Lord, why
      doth Thine anger burn against Thy people whom Thou hast brought out from the land of Egypt with
      great power and with a mighty hand? 12 “Why should the Egyptians speak, saying, ‘With evil intent
      He brought them out to kill them in the mountains and to destroy them from the face of the earth”?
      Turn from Thy burning anger and change Thy mind about doing harm to Thy people. 13 “Remember
      Abraham, Isaac, and Israel, Thy servants to whom Thou didst swear by Thyself, and didst say to them,

      I will give to your descendants, and they shall inherit it forever.’” 14 So the Lord changed His mind
      about the harm which He said He would do to His people.

Such passages demonstrate that the Lord is quite serious in His injunction for us to use reason even in dealing with
Page 114                                                                              Degrees of Love and Forgiveness
Him: “‘Come now, and let us reason together,’ Says the Lord” (Is 1:18). Reason can trump over emotions, even
over God’s emotions. Emotions certainly have their place. Even the anger of emotion has its proper place. But even
justified anger is made subject to reason. The Lord has given us Himself as an example to follow in subjecting
emotion to reason.
                                                 Affection and Maturity
        As we also know from experience, people who live by their unbridled emotions are going to be mixed up.
Sue’s issues cannot be cured by a simple hug and pat on the cheek, which Dianne and I gave her previously.
Affection is not the solution. Until Sue achieves some emotional stability, disciplinary rebukes are necessary. Sue
was concerned that God did not love her very much at her level of maturity and questioned how God’s love for us
can be conditional because, when she was holding a little infant, she felt great love for the little infant. I pointed out
that if the infant becomes a two-year-old and then says, “I hate you,” then the affection she had for that child would
not be at the same level it had been while the child was an infant. She said that she understood that explanation. I
respond, “Yes, it’s not exactly rocket science.” When someone is responding negatively toward you, the affection
you feel toward that person naturally tends to diminish. Sometimes, the affection may disappear altogether. When
my brother told our dad, “I wish you were not my father,” our dad’s love for him was not expressed for him in an
affectionate manner, nevertheless, in an appropriate manner, in the form of discipline. I do not believe our dad was
feeling very affectionate toward my brother at that moment, and I am certain that my brother would concur!
Nevertheless, I do not doubt that my dad loved my brother during that moment either.
        God’s love for us is appropriate at the level at which we are developmentally and functionally. Parents love
their toddlers very much, and parents are not disappointed with toddlers who are developing naturally and
functioning lovingly. As my brother and I grew older, we enjoyed playing basketball with our dad. But our dad was
not disappointed with us when we were only knee high because we could not play basketball with him. Likewise, I
am finally learning to enjoy trout fishing because it is something that my sons and I can do together. I certainly was
not disappointed with them when they were toddlers because they were not performing as teenagers or not trout
fishing with me. Likewise, God is not disappointed with us when we are growing spiritually, even though He looks
forward to the day when we can do things with Him at a more mature level. He might have a greater degree of
affection with those believers who are more mature, but this does not mean that He is disappointed with us where
we are at in our path toward maturity. Simply, higher degrees of maturity naturally result in higher degrees of
intimacy.
        Instead of worrying about not growing fast enough or being loved enough, what I did was tantamount to
encouraging Sue to cross the Jordan and start fighting (see TOD for explanation and illustration). One tends to grow
rather quickly at that point. Instead of living in fear of not growing or being loved, we should live in faith by fighting
the good fight of faith, knowing that we are loved as a fighter rather than a whiner (1Tim 6:12; 2Tim 4:7). Accept
the fact that you are loved at the level appropriate to your maturity and then press on to greater maturity, and you
will be well on your way to being loved as a winner.
                                               Worthy Love is Earned
        Jesus did not entrust Himself to new believers (Jn 2:23). Such trust is earned, conditioned on spiritual growth.
Accordingly, higher degrees of affection with the Lord are conditioned on our spiritual growth and maturity. We
must earn the right to be entrusted in emotional intimacy. At these higher levels, the Bible affirms both misthological
forgiveness and love. Forgiveness and love at these higher levels are conditional. They are earned. Walking with
the Lord in harmony results in greater intimacy with the Lord. Some Christians would balk at the idea of earned
love. Too bad. The Bible teaches it. Therefore, it is true. It corresponds to reality. Love works this way in the real
world. Jesus earned our love. “We love Him because He first loved us” (1Jn 4:19). Jesus is worthy of our love, not
only because of who He is but also because of what He has done. Did Jesus bribe our love with His love? No! Love
is the natural reward for love. C. S. Lewis is well-known for having expressed some astute comments in this regard:

             We are afraid that Heaven is a bribe, and that if we make it our goal we shall no longer be
      disinterested. It is not so. Heaven offers nothing that the mercenary soul can desire. It is safe to tell the
      pure in heart that they shall see God, for only the pure in heart want to. There are rewards that do not
      sully motives. A man’s love for a woman is not mercenary because he wants to marry her, nor his
      love for poetry mercenary because he wants to read it, nor his love of exercise less disinterested
      because he wants to run and leap and walk. Love, by definition, seeks to enjoy its object.”
Chapter 7. Reoccurrence                                                                                       Page 115
As I share in more detail in TOD:
            Betz relates this quote from C. S. Lewis: “Money is not the natural reward of love; that is why
      we call a man a mercenary if he married a woman for the sake of her money. But marriage is the
      proper reward for a real lover, and he is not a mercenary for desiring it.” We are not
      mercenaries seeking rewards when we see that the natural connection between them and our
      enjoyment of them as consummation with Christ. We get to rule with Him in His kingdom, dine with
      Him at His table, sit with Him on His throne, and walk with Him in white.
       Desiring my wife’s love does not make me a mercenary. Likewise, Dianne is worthy of my love. She has
earned my love. With her love, she has earned my love. My love was her reward for her loving me. I love her
because she first loved me. She acknowledged her love for me long before I acknowledged my love for her. In fact,
it took me quite a while to realize that I already had fallen in love with her in response to her love for me, and even
then, I did not tell her that I loved her until I proposed to her. Fittingly, we had, Just You and I, by Eddie Rabbit and
Crystal Galye sung at our wedding:
                                             And I remember our first embrace
                                              That smile that was on your face
                                                The promises that we made
                                             And now your love is my reward
                                                And I love you even more

Initially, the refrain, “Your love is my reward,” could only be sung as representing Dianne’s perspective. However,
by the time of our wedding, it was true of my perspective as well. My professed love for her and my marriage
proposal was her reward. Her deepening love for me was my reward. Over the past 36 years since then, our growing
reciprocal love for each other is our mutual reward.
        In the same fashion, I love Jesus because He first loved me. Love earns love. The natural reward for love is
love. Love is the appropriate reward for love. Repaying love with love is natural, not mercenary. Jesus is worthy of
our love, of course, but we are also worthy of His love. Consider the alternative. What groom would tell his bride:
“You are unworthy of my love, but I’m going to love you anyway”? Why do we picture Jesus as saying some sort
similar nonsense to His Bride: “You are nothing but an ugly, unworthy, wretch saved by My grace, but I am going
to love you anyway”? Far to the contrary, the Holy Spirit and Jesus are working diligently to make us a worthy
Bride. As stated previously regarding Illustration 17. The Pearl of Great Price, sin was the necessary price, which
cost His blood, to produce a Queen worthy of Himself. This was not a slip of the pin. The Queen is worthy of
the King! The Bride of Christ is worthy of Christ! How can this be? How can we be worthy of Christ’s love?
Because love not only enjoys the value of its object, love creates value.
                                                   Love Creates Value
       Lazar writes: “Love finds value in the beloved for their own sake. But unconditional love creates value in
the beloved, whether they are loveable or not. We’re so used to conditional ‘love’ that depends on the other person
meeting our expectations. We’re not used to God’s unconditional ‘one-way love.’ That is, we’re not used to grace.”
(emphasis his).179 Is Lazar’s statement true? Well, as an act of grace, God’s unconditional love for humanity
certainly created value in us by creating us in His image. Humanity is loved for that reason. We did not earn the
right to be created in God’s image and be loved accordingly. Such unconditional love is a display of God’s
unconditional grace. This grace created us as valuable creatures, made in the image of God. Yes, Lazar has a point.
What Lazar failed to consider, though, is that God’s conditional love creates even greater value in the beloved.180
       A fuller articulation of the point Lazar is making would add that conditional love creates greater value in the
beloved. God’s love for us as His children is conditioned on our becoming His children through faith alone in Christ
alone for eternal life. We become soteriologically valuable at that point, and God loves us as such—as children who
have become soteriologically valuable because of the value His soteriological love has created in us by imparting
soteriological life within us, as an act of God’s undeserved grace. Similarly, the Lord’s love for us as His Bride is

179
    Shawn Lazar, “Why Do Good If Salvation Is Not By Works?” Available at: https://faithalone.org/blog/why-do-
good-if-salvation-is-not-by-works. Accessed 3/1/2019.
180
    See Love Because of Future Value, 14; Love for the Bride, 52.
Page 116                                                                           Degrees of Love and Forgiveness
conditioned on our becoming His Bride through walking by faith in the Spirit. We become increasingly valuable as
we mature in our walk with Christ, and God loves us as such—as mature believers who have become
misthologically valuable because of the value His misthological love is creating within us in imparting misthological
life within us and creating misthological value within us in the form of godly character. Misthological love for the
Bride creates misthological value in the Bride (Eph 5:25-27). Even when we love those who refuse to love us in
return, our love still creates value—in us. Love creates value boomerang effect in the one doing the loving. When
we love our enemies, it stretches our capacity to love, not only laying up for us a treasure in Heaven but perfecting
a loving spirit within us as an internal treasure (Mt 5:46-48; Lk 6:32-35; cp. 1Pet 3:4).
       As for love finding value, the inconsistency of Wuest’s comments has been pointed out already. He spoke of
agape love as an unconditional love that finds a precious value in the object loved that is not conditioned on the
precious value of the object. Right. If you can believe that, you should have no trouble believing in round squares.
Wuest was more accurate when he acknowledged that love values the precious worthiness of the object loved.
Love finds value because of the value of the object loved. More comprehensively, we love someone because of the
actual value or potential value we see in that person. As a corollary, once there is no longer any potential value,
there is no longer any potential for love. Therefore, God does not love people in Hell because they no longer have
any value or potential value. God loves you because He sees value in your actual value and even more in your
potential value.
       Now, as to the Bride. God’s love for Her is infinite because Her potential value is infinite, not only
horizontally but vertically. Why did God allow sin and suffering? What was the greater good that could possibly
justify so much evil? As further noted in MSIH:

             Carmical allows a qualified-end-justifies-the-means (QEJTM) as an explanation for the
      problem of evil—a solution that is not uncommon in Christian circles. Why did a good God create a
      world in which evil would come to exist? Why does God not remove evil? Writing at a philosophical
      level, Plantinga considers various answers, some of which sound like QEJTM.181 Did God cause the
      evil? Of course, not. But He did create a world in which He knew that evil would come to exist. Then
      why did He do so?

       Carmical answers: “An infinite, eternal good justifies the use of finite, temporal evils.”182 God allowed evil
for the greater good: “The end that justified the means of creating a world in which a finite amount of sin would
occur for a finite amount of time” (MSIH). Ok, but what is this infinite good that could justify so much evil? As
discussed in MMP3: “Some of the videos on YouTube about the Bride picture Christ as saying that the Bride’s
beauty proves that she was worth His life. (Of course, this beauty is not merely the beauty of her appearance but
the beauty of her character since the latter will result in the former.)” The Bride was worth Christ’s life!
       Some may object. But think through the implications of such an objection. To resume our earlier
discussion,183 can you really imagine Christ telling His Bride, “I died for you, but You are not worth it”? What
groom would tell his bride that she was not worth the sacrifice he made for her? Think through the implications of
Eph 5:25-27. To claim that Christ could not create a Bride worthy of Himself would be to dispute the implication
of the text and impinge His power. Surely, we must conclude that Christ can create a Bride worthy of Himself. He
can, and He will. He will succeed. He will not fail. He wants Her to feel beautiful because She is beautiful. She is
flawless in beauty, worth every bit of His desire for her beauty, worth every ounce of His affection for Her (Ps
45:11). Her infinite value more than offsets the finite sin that was necessary to create the beauty of Her character.
Was Eve a fit/suitable/comparable companion for Adam (Gen 2:20)? Yes! Eve typologically represents the Bride
of Christ. The Lord’s Bride will likewise be a fit/suitable/comparable companion for Him. Eve was worthy of
Adam’s affection because God created her to be worthy of his affection. The Bride likewise will be worthy of
Christ’s affection. His love infuses Her with His nature. His loving discipline infuses Her with His character. She
will ontologically participate in His nature and experientially participate in His character. Their shared experiences
in fighting and suffering now and their shared experiences in the future will make Her infinitely beautiful and
valuable. She will become even more beautiful throughout eternity future as Her intrinsic ontological misthological
beauty becomes increasingly realized functionally. What a return on His investment!

       If you want to contemplate just how beautiful the Bride is, you must not only look at the Cross but at Hell.
She must not only be beautiful enough to justify Jesus’ death but all the sorrows and sufferings of Hell and the
present life! God could have chosen to create freewill creatures that would never sin and yet rule. Moreover, they
could learn and grow from their ruling experience through all eternity. But such sinless creatures could never ever
attain to the beauty of the Bride. Why? For the Bride to become as beautiful as She will be, She had to suffer sin
and fight sin. In doing so, She becomes more like Her Groom. He had to suffer sin and fight sin in order for the
full beauty of His character to be manifested. Jesus is both suffering servant and conquering hero. He is looking for
a Bride who shares this same character. The Lord had to allow sin and suffering so that we might better understand
His nature and experientially share in it. Although holy angels have fought sin, they have not fought it as the Bride
has. Nor have they suffered as She has. Nor do they partake of God’s nature as fully as She does. Therefore, they
are not as beautiful as She is. She not only had to fight sin without; she has had to suffer and fight sin within.
       Presently, not only does the Bride have to live in a hostile, visible, sinful, worldly environment, but She must
defeat invisible spiritual forces while doing so. Moreover, not all these spiritual forces are external. Sin, as a spiritual
entity, still resides in Her flesh. Talk about a pressure cooker! God is using all this sin to turn us into an exquisite
diamond that has been formed under heat and pressure, a priceless pearl formed from the internal pain we have
endured. We must cherish these opportunities to suffer and fight. We only have a very brief window of opportunity
to develop a beautiful godly character through suffering and fighting. If we are victorious, we will have tremendous
internal glory: “For I consider that the sufferings of this present time are not worthy to be compared with the glory
which shall be revealed in (eis) us” (Rom 8:18; cp. vv. 8:17b,21). This glory to be revealed in us is the revealing of
the glory of the beauty of faithful believers (GNTC).
                                     He Loves Her Because She is Beautiful
       In his song, The Beauty of the Bride, Steve Green says that the Lord will be captivated by the hidden beauty
of the Bride:
                                          I'm a part of the beauty of the bride
                                                    My belongingness
                                              Was bought by His faithfulness
                                              Was purchased with His blood
                                                 We will see Him coming
                                               With His arms held open wide
                                      Captivated by the hidden beauty of the bride
Green’s emphasis on inner beauty is biblical (cp. 1Pet 3:4). All the sin within us will be removed. Yippie! Only a
godly character will remain. I can hardly wait! Still, Christ will be captivated by more than just our inner beauty.
Other songwriters do better in capturing the jest of the principal passage (SSol 4:9): “You have captivated my heart,
my sister, my bride; you have captivated my heart with one glance of your eyes, with one jewel of your necklace”
(ESV). “You have ravished my heart, my sister, my spouse; you have ravished my heart with one look of your eyes,
with one link of your necklace” (NKJ). The Bride captures the Lord’s heart with her ravishing beauty. King Jesus
will greatly desire Her beauty (Ps 45:11). This beauty is not hidden; it can be seen! It is not merely internal; it is
external as well. She will be beautiful through and through and dressed in beauty: “Let us rejoice and be glad and
give the glory to Him, for the marriage of the Lamb has come and His bride has made herself ready. And it was
given to her to clothe herself in fine linen, bright and clean; for the fine linen is the righteous acts of the saints”
(Rev 19:7-8). Her skin will be without spot or wrinkle or any such thing; rather, She will be holy and blameless
(Eph 5:27). Based on such passages, we can confidently say to the Lord,
                                                  I am beautiful to You,
                                           For You see me not only for who I am,
                                                But also for who I will be.
                                               You love me for my beauty,
                                  Not only for the inner beauty You see hidden in me,
                                 But also for the ravishing beauty I will be for all to see.
                                  Yet You love me not only for my beauty which all see;
                                       You love me because You know the real me.

       The beauty of the Bride’s hidden internal beauty will shine through Her externally so that She will shine like
Her Husband “brighter than the sun (Acts 26:13), like “the stars for ever and ever” (Dan 12:3). The more She
suffers, the more beautiful She becomes: “Beloved, do not be surprised at the fiery ordeal among you, which comes
upon you for your testing, as though some strange thing were happening to you; but to the degree that you share
the sufferings of Christ, keep on rejoicing; so that also at the revelation of His glory, you may rejoice with
exultation” (1Pet 4:12-13). A direct correlation is expected between Her degree of suffering and Her degree of
exultation. Those who have experienced rejection syndrome have tremendous potential for bridal beauty. Yet they
must avail themselves of the opportunity. We must “exult in our tribulations, knowing that tribulation brings about
perseverance; and perseverance, proven character, and proven character, hope” (Rom 5:3-4). Overcoming the
tribulation of rejection gives us proven character (i.e., internal beauty) and hope (i.e., confident expectation) of
misthological beauty. This beauty is more than sentimental value. It is not fluff; it is real stuff: “For momentary,
light affliction is producing for us an eternal weight of glory far beyond all comparison” (2Cor 4:17).

       This beauty is tangible, measurable, so real you can weigh it. Instead of being crushed by its weight, you will
be blown away by its power, however. The force of this beauty blows like the overpowering solar winds of a solar
hurricane. This solar-force beauty can be measured: “There is one glory of the sun, and another glory of the moon,
and another glory of the stars; for star differs from star in glory” (1Cor 15:41). Bridal believers will shine like stars
in the glory of their beauty, but not all stars shine with equal luminosity. You can measure a star’s luminosity by
measuring the amount of total energy output (light/power) it emits in watts. The glory of the Bride will be just as
real as the glory of the Groom. The glory of the Goddess will be just as real as the glory of God, which makes sense
since the Lord shares His glory with the Bride in the form of co-glory (Rom 8:17b).
       This affirmation of measurable objective value is not meant to devalue subjective sentimental value, though.
The Bride obviously has value because of Her Lord’s affectionate love for Her. His affectionate love for Her also
gives Her infinite value. He values not just Her radiant objective beauty but also the subjective beauty of Her heart—
a heart affectionately in love with Him. Emotions have value. We can easily relate to the multifaceted nature of
such love. As a husband, I love my wife’s love. I love not only her physical beauty but also her laugh, positive
outlook, diligent work ethic, faith, loving nature, etc., but perhaps most of all, her love for me. I love the way she
makes me feel—loved. Similarly, the Lord will love everything about His Bride, including how She feels about
Him and the way She makes Him feel. His love for Her makes Her valuable. Her love for Him makes Her valuable.
Her character makes Her valuable. Her ontological and experiential participation in His divine nature makes Her
valuable. Her capabilities make Her valuable. Her tender loving nature makes Her valuable (2Pet 1:4). Her moral
strength makes Her valuable. Her wisdom makes her valuable. The bottom line is that She is loved because She is
valuable, and, conversely, She is valuable because She is loved. An ever-deepening, perpetual, spiraling, falling in
love and increasing in value takes place as a result of their mutual reciprocal loving affection, making Her
invaluable.185
       Trichotomy offers a plausible model as to how to explain the correlation between the Bride’s inner and outer
beauty. We know that some believers will be more glorious/luminous than others on the outside. We also know that
those believers who are more glorious/luminous on the outside have a godlier character on the inside. We can also
infer that believers who are cast into the outer darkness are not luminous while those who compose the Bride are
luminous, though not equally luminous. Not all members of the Bride are equally luminous. Therefore, we can infer
that they are not equally godly on the inside. Since Scripture uses the analogy of the stars to illustrate the
phenomenon, we can formulate a hypothesis using stars to explain the difference.
       My theory is that the trichotomous spirits of believers develop to various stages of godliness. The spirit of a
believer cast into the outer darkness does not achieve sufficient godliness to burst forth misthologically in spiritual
nuclear fusion. Nuclear fusion is the type of energy used to fuel stars. Angels are spirit beings. Yet not all these
spirit beings are equally glorious. Just as God created stars with various degrees of internal nuclear fusion, He
created angelic spirits with various degrees of glory. It stands to reason that the luminous capacity of angels varies
according to the luminous capacity of their individual spiritual composition. Just as the inner composition of one
star varies from another star so that this inner variation explains their different degrees of luminous glory, so the
inner composition of one believer can vary significantly from the inner composition of another believer. I would
further pose that the inner composition for each believer is unique, yet not so unique so as to prevent classification
and stratification. For example, some angels are archangels. Presumably, archangels are more glorious than lower
class angels and have a corresponding more glorious composition. Yet we need not pose that each archangel has
the same internal composition. The internal composition of one archangel could vary from another archangel much
like no two snowflakes are identical. The internal composition of each spirit being would be the unique spiritual
fingerprint of that spirit being. The same is true of the trichotomous spirit of believers.
       Speaking in terms of the trichotomous distinction between the soul and spirit, Austin-Sparks urges believers
to “be strong in spirit.”186 Paul spoke of the trichotomous spirit (i.e., the inner man) being renewed and strengthened
by the Holy Spirit (2Cor 4:16; Eph 3:16). Otherwise, the believer will experience defeat if the inner man is weak
(Rom 7:22). Regarding the childhood of John the Baptist, Luke summarizes, “And the child continued to grow, and
to become strong in spirit, and he lived in the deserts until the day of his public appearance to Israel” (Lk 1:80).
Jesus taught “that which is born of the Spirit is spirit” (Jn 3:6). This passage should be understood trichotomously.
Certainly, it makes sense that a believer’s trichotomous spirit, although weak initially at the point of new birth, may
grow strong. Speaking trichotomously, Paul says, “The Spirit Himself bears witness with our spirit that we are
185
      See Illustration 2. 4D Love Spiral, 4.

      T. Austin-Sparks, What is Man? Kindle Edition (n.p.; n. d.), KL 536.
Page 120                                                                            Degrees of Love and Forgiveness
children of God” (Rom 8:16). Then, in this same epistle, he proceeds to urge believers to be “fervent in spirit,
serving the Lord” (Rom 12:11). Elsewhere, he talks about the potential misthological salvation of the believer’s
spirit: “Now may the God of peace Himself sanctify you holistically; and holistically may your spirit, and your soul,
and your body be preserved blamelessly at the coming of our Lord Jesus Christ” (1Thess 5:23; TM; cp. 1Cor 5:5).
        Thinking along similar trichotomous lines with misthological overtones, Peter encourages suffering believers
with these words:
             3
              Blessed be the God and Father of our Lord Jesus Christ, who according to His great mercy has
      caused us to be born again for a living hope through the resurrection of Jesus Christ from the dead,
      4
        for an inheritance which is imperishable and undefiled and will not fade away, reserved in Heaven
      for you, 5 who are protected by the power of God through faith for a salvation ready to be revealed in
      the last time. 6 In this you greatly rejoice, even though now for a little while, if necessary, you have
      been distressed by various trials, 7 in order that the testing of your faith, being more precious than
      gold which is perishable, even though tested by fire, may be found to result in praise and glory
