       The misthological salvation of their souls and a misthological inheritance are attainable, if believers
successfully undergo the fiery testing of their faith. Passing these tests results in something more misthologically
precious than gold. Peter resumes this theme when addressing women: “And let not your adornment be merely
external—braiding the hair, and wearing gold jewelry, or putting on dresses; but let it be the hidden person of the
heart, with the imperishable quality of a gentle and quiet spirit, which is precious in the sight of God” (1Pet 3:3-
4). The precious, imperishable, misthological result is a gentle and quiet spirit. If you have this type of spirit, your
soul will be saved misthologically. Misthologically, you will be saved holistically.
                       Illustration 29. Misthologically Precious Gentle Trichotomous Spirit
Chapter 7. Reoccurrence                                                                                         Page 121
        As a believer walks in dependency upon the Spirit and is strengthened by the Spirit, his or her spirit becomes
strong, yet gentle and humble. Understandably, therefore, Peter sums up by exhorting believers to be “humble in
spirit...for you were called for this very purpose that you might inherit a blessing” (1Pet 3:8-9). This misthological
blessing is reminiscent of Jesus’ blessing: “Blessed are the poor in spirit, for theirs is the kingdom of heaven”
(Mt 5:3). The poor in spirit are the humble in spirit who have become strong in spirit by the power of the Spirit.
Their regenerate trichotomous spirit has undergone a transformation beyond the original, immature, new-birth state
which their spirit originally had at regeneration. At the point of new birth, they were spiritually immature. Their
trichotomous spirit needed to grow, to mature, to develop its full potential. Undergoing fiery trials is the means to
an end—to produce a misthologically precious trichotomous spirit within.
        Paul concurs that believers can experience dynamic, ongoing, present transformation: “And do not be
conformed to this world, but be transformed by the renewing of your mind” (Rom 12:2). “But we all, with unveiled
face beholding as in a mirror the glory of the Lord, are being transformed into the same image from glory to glory,
just as from the Lord, the Spirit” (2Cor 3:18). My theory is that this transformation goes all the way to the believer’s
trichotomous core. Their immature regenerate spirit is being transformed into greater Christlikeness, maturity, and
glory. As a result, the spirit of mature believers will shine greater in glory than that of a carnal believer. Both the
spirit of a carnal believer and the spirit of a mature believer will be sinless. But the spirit of the carnal believer will
be shineless, or at least shine less. The beauty of the bridal believer is more than skin deep, more than soul deep; it
is spirit deep. At the transformation of their physical bodies into their misthologically glorified bodies, the
misthological mature spirits of bridal believers will shine forth in radiant beauty. Carnal believers will likewise
have transformed bodies, but their spirits will not be transformed because their spirits are not being transformed
now. The spirit of a carnal believer will only have undergone the soteriological baseline transformation that it
experienced at regeneration. The time to transform your spirit is now. If you want a radiant beautiful spirit in eternity
future, you must allow the Spirit to strengthen your humble spirit now. The developing time for your spirit is in the
here-and-now, not in the hereafter. The hereafter will primarily reveal the degree of development you achieved in
the here-and-now. If you want a bridal spirit then, you must develop a bridal spirit now.
        The Bride is the pearl of great price. The core of Her beauty is Her trichotomous spirit. Having a sinless
trichotomous spirit is not our goal. That is a free gift. Every believer has a born again trichotomous spirit which
cannot sin (1Jn 3:9). God could have created humanity with such a nature at the very beginning. But He did not. He
needed to allow sin so that our regenerate sin nature might grow strong in its opposition to sin. Although this sinless
nature cannot sin, it can receive battle scars in its fight against sin. Just as Jesus did not sin and could not sin, yet
He still bears the scars of His fight against sin, so too the Bride will wear Her internal scars against sin. These scars
create a unique signature, a unique spiritual fingerprint so to speak, within the spirit of each bridal believer. Yet
these battle scars make the Bride all the more beautiful.
        Royce Powell correctly recognizes that “collectively, overcomers are the Bride of Christ” (p. 40). “Through
the power of the Holy Spirit,” Christ is presently “locating and beautifying a bride from among the body of the
church” (p. 52). Powell uses an OT analogy to explain how the scars of present sufferings can be used by the Lord
to make us internally beautiful:

       Every sacrifice has cedar wood. Solomon is told that no stone would be seen in the temple, all covered
       with wood. These boards are carved with the shapes of flowers and cherubim and palms trees, speaking
       of the cutting edge of God’s Word. The carving hurts. It is the pruning that hurts us. This is suffering,
       but when we are in difficulty, the carving is done by the great artisan, tracing a pattern for our life. The
       deeper the cut, the deeper the gold overlay. The more difficult our life, the more gold we shall receive
       at the Judgment Seat of Christ. “For I reckon that the sufferings of this present time are not worthy to
       be compared with the glory which shall be revealed in us” (Romans 8:18).187

       Each bridal believer will have a unique pattern cut into his or her spirit but filled with gold. If we overcome
our sufferings by the power of the Holy Spirit and allow Him to fill our spirit with His beauty, then the deeper the
cut, the more gold He uses to fill it. Suffering is not enough, though. Everybody suffers. Overcoming the difficulties
in our lives by the power of the Holy Spirit is absolutely essential if we are to fill our suffering with beauty. Sue
has great potential for eternal internal beauty because of what she has suffered. Nevertheless, she will fail to
187
   Royce Powell, Rule and Reign: Kingdom Studies in Genesis, Second Edition (Sylva, NC: Millennial Kingdom
Press, 2018), 93-94.
Page 122                                                                             Degrees of Love and Forgiveness
actualize that beauty if she fails to overcome her self-pity. By the power of the Spirit, she must allow her scars to
be filled with the Spirit’s golden beauty so that she can become the raving beauty that God has designed for her to
be. Yes, she has been hurt. Carving and pruning are intended to hurt. Yet God’s intended purpose for the hurt is to
make her more beautiful than she can possibly imagine. Each bridal believer will have a trichotomous spirit of
infinite value and beauty.
                                                    Infinite Beauty
       Is the beauty of the Groom finite or infinite? Infinite, obviously. Consequently, we must deduce that the
beauty of the Bride is also infinite, not only in duration but in value, thus making Her invaluable. As was noted in
TOD: “‘Haller correctly notes that “the duration and severity of hell are congruous with sin against the infinite holy
God” (Matthew, GNTC, 121). The quality and quantity of punishment are congruent with the nature of the Person
against whom the sin is committed and thus eternal.’” Sin against the infinitely holy God deserves an infinite
punishment because of the infinite nature of the Person against whom the sin was committed. How about sin against
the infinitely holy and beautiful Goddess? Such sin is necessary, but sin is still equally hideous. She must finitely
endure such finite sin in order to become infinitely beautiful. The beauty of Her holiness would be impossible to
create apart from the sin that She endures. Her resulting holiness makes Her beautiful. Adam was merely created
innocent in a neutral state. She has been made holy, a positive attribute, by Her positive fight against sin:

                Holiness is positive, and Adam’s innocence was accompanied by a capacity for holiness.
         Holiness is the result of faithfulness under testing, in man’s case. He may go into testing innocent, but
         the very essence of testing is a capacity to choose between two courses, his own and God’s.
                Faith, obedience, loyalty to God, resisting evil by resort to God, issue in a positive state which
         is something more than innocence, i.e. more than the fact of not yet having sinned.188

        The Bride’s resulting holiness is beautiful. The fact that we are to worship God in the beauty of holiness
(Ps 96:10) certainly indicates that holiness is beautiful. Whereas the beauty of God’s holiness is intrinsic to His
eternal nature, the beauty of the Bride is intrinsic to Her new nature, which is derived from His nature. Amazingly,
though, the Bride can grow in beauty. Her beauty is not static but can grow dynamically and dramatically, especially
as She grows in holiness. Even so, Her opportunity to increase the rate at which this growth can occur is set in this
life. We all know about our IQ (intelligence quotient). Early in our life, we can increase our IQ. As we get older,
our IQ stabilizes. The Bride likewise has a BQ (beauty quotient). The time for Her to increase Her BQ is early in
Her life, that is, in the present life. Sin and suffering give Her the opportunity to increase Her BQ dramatically. But
sin and suffering will be removed from Her in the hereafter. Thus, She must do all that She can to increase Her BQ
right now in the here-and-now, even to the point of shedding Her own blood if necessary.
       The Bride has been cleansed soteriologically and misthologically by the blood of the Lamb. Nevertheless,
the latter cleansing frequently requires Her own blood as well. The blood of the Bride has been shed since the
beginning, when Cain killed Able. From Genesis to Revelation, the blood of the Bride cries out for vengeance. “The
voice of your brother’s blood is crying to Me from the ground” (Gen 4:10). “They cried out with a loud voice,
saying, ‘How long, O Lord, holy and true, wilt Thou refrain from judging and avenging our blood on those who
dwell on the earth?’” (Rev 6:10). We were “redeemed with precious [timios] blood, as of a lamb unblemished and
spotless, the blood of Christ” (1Pet 1:18). Timios means valuable, precious, costly, of great worth or value.
Naturally, we affirm these attributes for Christ’s blood. Yet we should also affirm these attributes regarding the
Bride’s blood. After all, the same word is used to describe the death of those who comprise the Bride: “Precious
[timios] in the sight of the Lord is the death of His godly ones” (Ps 116:15; LXX). The godly compose the Goddess.
Despite Her present circumstances, by faith the Bride must affirm Her self-worth, Her own personal worthiness.
God’s appraisal of a godly wife is well known: “An excellent wife, who can find? For her worth is far above jewels”
(Prov 31:10). Imagine how much greater will be His appraisal of His own godly Bride!
                                          We Must Perceive our Worth
       By faith believers need to be able to say: “I am worthy of being loved.” “I am worthy of Christ’s love.” In
faith, with an eye to our future, we can say that we are worthy of the sacrifice that Jesus made for us and the
sanctification He is working in us. Perceiving self-worth is essential for those who are caught up in the rejection
syndrome. Consider the alternative, as Solomon explains:

188
      Austin-Sparks, KL 321-328.
Chapter 7. Reoccurrence                                                                                       Page 123

      His lack of worthiness makes him feel unacceptable to God as well as to men, and he frequently
      casts God in the role of a rejecting father. Having difficulty attaining acceptance on a human level,
      it is only natural that he would feel unacceptable to God. Such persons who have received Christ as
      Lord and Savior still find it difficult to believe that God loves them unconditionally. They believe that
      the Bible is God’s Word and that it expresses His love for them, and yet they can’t relax from their
      own works or efforts and realize His love and acceptance at an experiential level. It seems to them
      that prayers of others are answered but not their own. The lack of acceptance on a human or
      psychological basis carries over into the spiritual realm. Not feeling accepted by others, they do not
      feel accepted by God. Their emotions are almost always at variance with reality. As a result, they
      feel unworthy and unsaved even though they know their salvation is assured biblically.189

        If we believe that we are unworthy of Christ’s love, then we will feel that we are unworthy of His love. The
first thing that needs to be corrected is the way we think. Once we volitionally correct what we believe intellectually,
then what we feel emotionally can start falling in line eventually. In Mark Minervini’s interview with Jairek
Robbins, Minervini begins by stressing neuro-linguistic programming (NLP), as having made a huge difference not
only in his stock trading, but his life.190 As to what it entails, an excerpt will suffice:
             Mark: What supports a positive mental attitude or state?
             Jairek: It’s built every day, based on what you feed your mind, what you choose to focus on,
      what you say to yourself, and how determined you are. Here’s an example: Something happens—you
      make an error in your trading. The question you ask yourself (what you focus on in that moment)
      is: “What’s wrong with me?” Your brain will start analyzing all the data and inputs to tell you
      what’s “wrong” with you. Pretty soon, you will feel terrible in general and bad about yourself.
      Similarly, a question such as, “Why am I such an idiot?” will direct your brain to give you
      answers that make you feel you are an idiot. Instead, what if in response to making a mistake or
      failing to follow your rules, you asked yourself a different question. What would happen if you asked:
      “Why do I always find a way to make things work?” That would really change things. You’d see
      yourself as creative and resilient. If you find yourself getting emotionally hijacked, ask yourself
      questions that will get you back on track. Instead of, “Why did I do that?” you ask, “How can I
      improve?” Not only do you avoid wasting time moping with the first question, with the second
      question you set about to learn and improve. (KL 3290-3299.)

        Minervini stresses that, if you are going to be a winner, you must believe that you can win, that you can
choose to win, that the choice is yours. To win you must believe that you are a winner. This is nothing but soul
power, learning how to use the power of the human mind. Spirit power is superior, relying on the power of the Holy
Spirit. Nevertheless, proverbially, the Bible concurs with the basic premise: As a man thinks in his heart, so is he
(Prov 23:7). Volitionally, what your spirit believes becomes what your soul believes. Your spirit knows you are a
winner (Phil 4:13; 1Jn 4:4). Accept that truth and meditate on it. Otherwise, if you think you are a loser, then you
will think like a loser and lose. If you think you are a champion, then you will think like a champion and win. Stop
setting your mind on negative computations to answer such questions as, “What’s wrong with me?” Instead, set
your mind on things above, to make positive computations by answering such questions as, “Why does the Lord
love me so much?” “What does the Bible have to say about my true identity?”
        TOD provides a list of forty-six Be-What-You-Are truths. For example, you are light so shine. To this list,
others could be added: You are loved so love. You are a winner so win. This biblical thinking is more than mere
positive thinking. Volitionally, you are choosing to appropriate intellectually the spiritual reality that you are
worthy, thereby forcing your soulish emotions to comply with spiritual reality rather than allowing your soulish
emotions to deny and determine your spiritual and soulish reality. You are worthy so feel worthy. You are loved so
feel loved. You are accepted so feel accepted. Walk in the Spirit by faith, and by faith appropriate these statements
as being appropriate, thereby forcing your emotions to get in line and follow as an obedient caboose.

        We are not the helpless pawns of our feelings. We have a volitional choice as to whether, intellectually, to

intelligent thing to do, when there is a conflict between your gut and what the Bible says, is to believe the Bible. If

        How about those who are presently struggling with believing that they are worthy because they know that
being worthy is a misthological issue tied to their performance and maturity, but their behavior and maturity are
subpar rather than super? First, affirm your soteriological worth. You are beloved as a child of God because you
are in fact a child of God (1Jn 3:1-2). You have a new nature from God and a new relationship with God that makes
you very valuable soteriologically. He justified you legally and sanctified you positionally, making you loveable
relationally as His child. Your having believed in Him for the free gift of eternal life allowed His love to make you
immediately loveable and invaluable soteriologically. Second, acknowledge that you have the ability and
opportunity to start behaving and maturing immediately. You are justified and sanctified so act like you are justified
and sanctified. Be what you are. As a result, become sanctified experientially, resulting in being justified
misthologically. Decide to allow His love to make you loveable and invaluable misthologically. The combination
of Spirituotherapy and Noutherapy gives you no excuse for waiting or for making this a long, drawn-out process.
Immediate results are possible. Maturity takes time, of course, but nikology does not. Steep gains in victory are
immediately available.
                                               Stock Trading Analogy
       Look at Solomon’s growth charts and compare them to Minervini’s stock charts. Both charts have a pivot
point. On Solomon’s charts, it is called the identification point: the point at which you appropriate identity truth and
start experiencing its reality. You are an investment, entrusted with great opportunities and abilities, ready to soar
immediately upwards in accumulating value if you make that pivot. The choice is yours.
                                     Illustration 30. Identification Pivot Point




       Accordingly, for the sake of analogy, let us liken God to a stock trader. His currency is love. He has invested
large amounts of love into humanity. In creating us in His image and dying for us so that we could not only have
the opportunity to live with Him but also to rule with Him, He has invested us with unimaginable value. His portfolio
is very diversified initially. Yet, in time, some stocks perform better than others. For those that respond particularly
well, He invests more of His portfolio. For those that respond less favorable, He invests less of His portfolio. For
some stock, that respond particularly poorly, He must eventually remove His investment entirely.
       For instance, at the beginning of his life, initially Hitler would have been the object of both God’s volitional
and affectual love, as befits the Lord’s general benevolence for the lost. Over the course of Hitler’s life, however,
Chapter 7. Reoccurrence                                                                                         Page 125
the Lord’s affectual love for Hitler would eventually reach zero, leaving only volitional love, which still allowed
the possibility of salvation. At the end of Hitler’s life, though, God’s volitional love for Hitler would also reach
zero. At that point, any potential for value no longer remained for Hitler. The value of his stock could not be
salvaged. The value of his company’s stock, so to speak, had reached zero. The Hitler company had become
bankrupt and gone out of business. Investment in that company’s stock was no longer possible.
       God is looking to maximize His return. Nevertheless, He has not rigged the market. He allows the stock
freedom to perform in accord with the market. Allowing some stock to fail was the only way to allow other stock
to excel. As a result, the value of His portfolio varies in accordance with the performance of His various stocks.
Paul uses the analogy of his day to express this truth: “Now in a large house there are not only gold and silver
vessels, but also vessels of wood and of earthenware, and some to honor and some to dishonor. Therefore, if a man
cleanses himself from these things, he will be a vessel for honor, sanctified, useful to the Master, prepared for every
good work” (2Tim 2:20-21). The performance of the various stock determines their value. Naturally, they must
perform according to the rules of the market. However, stock can start moving upward very rapidly in the
accumulation phase once big institutions start investing, and God is looking to make some very big investments
into stocks that reach the pivot point. Spirituotherapy and Noutherapy can put you at the pivot point right now.
Become a valuable investment right now. The choice is yours. Because of your potential value, the Heavenly Father
is eagerly waiting for you to reach that pivot point so that He can invest heavily in you, with dramatic results.
                                                     God’s Care
       The general benevolent care of God can be presumed for all of God’s children to some level, even in texts
like Mt 6:25-34; 7:11; Lk 12:22-32; Phil 4:6; 1Pet 5:7. Nevertheless, even these texts suggest that God’s care is
especially directed toward those believers who are disciples, seeking His kingdom treasure, trusting Him with their
present needs, sharing in the struggle for the cause of the gospel, and humbling themselves before Him. Even the
affirmation in 1Pet 5:7 is primarily misthological: “Casting all your anxiety upon Him, because He cares (melo)
for you.” As is widely acknowledged by the commentators, Peter draws upon the OT background in making this
statement: “Cast your burden upon the Lord, and He will sustain you; He will never allow the righteous to be
shaken” (Ps 55:22). God is particularly concerned about the righteous in this OT passage. Likewise, in the broad
context and immediate context in which Peter encapsulates this promise, the focus is on God’s care for those
believers who are suffering righteously and living obediently. As to the immediate context, GNTC puts it succinctly:
“God cares for believers in response to their submission to others (v 5) and maintain [sic.] a mindset of humble
service (v 6)” (bold original). God’s care for these believers in this passage is attributed to their submission and
performance. This passage is thus focused on God’s misthological care.
       The flip side is that God does not care, in this misthological sense, for those believers who are unrighteous
and disobedient. Misthologically, God does not care (ameleo) for believers who do not care (ameleo) about
misthology. Speaking of the carnal believers, the Lord says, “They did not continue in My covenant, and I did not
care [ameleo] for them” (Heb 8:9). “How will we escape if we do not care [ameleo] about so great a [misthological]
salvation?” (Heb 2:3; TM). God’s misthological care for us is conditioned on our care about misthology.
         To be sure, at a basic level, God cares about all His children, as is indicated in the parables of the lost sheep,
coin, and son (Lk 15:1-32).191 The good shepherd was even more concerned, in one sense, about the lost sheep than
the righteous sheep. And the prodigal’s father was more concerned, in a sense, about the wayward son than the
faithful son. Still, you reap what you sow. At a higher level of concern, if you do not care about rewards, God will
not care about you not reaping rewards. Even in the story of the prodigal son, you must come back home before
God will care about meeting your day-to-day needs in any special way that will help you qualify for heavenly
rewards (cp. Rom 8:28). Although the Father is concerned about you and cares about you at the basic level
regardless of how you perform, He will not care about you, that is, care for you, in a misthological sense, if you
refuse to live in fellowship with Him and despise seeking His kingdom rulership. The Father will let you dine with
the swine and miss out on the robe and the ring, if you so choose. You will miss out on His tender loving care
(TLC) in that case. His TLC is misthological. Choose to have God care for you, not only about you. You have a
choice. If you do not care about fellowship with God or rewards from God, He will not care for you in the
misthological sense, even though He deeply cares about you. He cannot misthologically care for you if you are

   Although the background and context of 1Pet 5:7 are principally concerned about God’s care for the righteous
who submit to His will, a baseline level of concern can be reasonably inferred for all God’s children so that the
passage is best perceived as suggesting two strata to God’s care: one unconditional, the other conditional.

rejecting His misthological care because, by the very nature of the case, misthological care is necessarily a reward

                                           Tough Love as Self-Discipline
        One reason I insist so strongly on tough love toward others is because it is the same type of love that God
practices on me and that I practice on myself. We are to love others as God loves us and as we love ourselves. Well,
this is the way that, when necessary, God loves me. So, if I am to love others as God loves me, I must love them
with tough love if the occasion demands it. Plus, we are to love others as we love ourselves, and tough love is
certainly one way I love myself. Sure, it is not the only way I love myself; sometimes, I will pamper myself. As I
add this note, I am reminded of my 36th anniversary that my wife and I celebrated yesterday. For the first time in
about ten months, I ate Dunkin Doughnuts. That was the exception rather than the rule, though.
        Last year I lost sixty pounds doing the keto diet. One verse that helped me maintain the level of discipline
for all those months was Paul’s statement: “I buffet my body and make it my slave, lest possibly, after I have
preached to others, I myself should be disqualified” (1Cor 9:27). I do not want to be disqualified for the prize of the
up-calling at the Bema because I have been spending too much time at buffets or eating doughnuts. So I buffeted
my body with keto. Likewise, if you love yourself, you will buffet yourself, practice self-discipline upon yourself,
so that you are not disqualified from the misthological reward. If you believe in tough love, the first person you
should practice it upon is yourself. And I do. I tough love myself, not only by practicing dietary discipline in the
form of keto but by practicing intellectual discipline in denying myself the luxury of doubt and emotional discipline
in denying myself the luxury of self-pity.

       In SAS, the Choke Doubt illustration was used to show how we must combat doubts pertaining to our
assurance of baseline salvation—salvation from Hell. We must use the biblical affirmations of OSAS (Once Saved
Always Saved) to choke out NOSAS (Not Once Saved Always Saved) doubt. One such assurance is the doctrine of
our sealing. Our soteriological sealing gives us unconditional soteriological security. If we will study out the
implications of that sealing, accept them as true, and meditate on them, then our feelings can be expected to comply
so that we feel secure. Accepting the truth of our soteriological sealing leads to appropriate soteriological feelings.
The same principle applies to self-pity. We should stop entertaining thoughts of self-pity so that we can extinguish
feelings of self-pity. The place to start is by changing our behavior with ELTL.
                                      Illustration 32. ETTL Versus Self-Pity
Page 128                                                                            Degrees of Love and Forgiveness
        To make the choking illustration more pertinent to the present study, it has been modified to focus on the
need self-pity. A time comes when you simply must grab your self-pity exegetically by the throat and deny its right
to exist. Doing so is a matter of life and death. I know from firsthand experience what it is to experience an extremely
painful rejection. Many years ago, the Sunday before our youngest son was born, I was terminated as the pastor of
my home church where my dad pastored and where I grew up. The basic reasons for my termination were because
I preached against (1) homosexuality and abortion, and for (2) free grace soteriology and misthology. (See ATTG.)
From this and other experiences, I know firsthand what it is like to have to fight the rejection syndrome. I had to
fight it myself. But I also know how to fight and win. You win it by fighting, not by whining. Through exchanged
life (EL) teaching and tough love (TL) discipline, “I have set My face like flint, and I know that I shall not be
ashamed” (Is 50:7; cp. Lk 9:51). The exchanged-life-tough-love (ELTL) approach I am taking with Sue in seeking
to help her overcome her rejection syndrome is the same approach I took on myself to overcome my rejection
syndrome.
        We are told that God “comforts [parakaleo] us in all our affliction so that we may be able to comfort those
who are in any affliction with the comfort with which we ourselves are comforted by God” (2Cor 1:4). Well, ELTL
is the comfort that God gave me to enable me to overcome my own personal rejection syndrome. Yes, there is a
time for tender, affectionate, lovey-dovey stuff, just as there is a time for Dunkin Doughnuts. There is a time to
pamper yourself, and a time to be pampered by God. I know what it is like to be held tenderly in God’s arms during
those times. But I also know that God is no fluff. God does not expect us to be pampered or to wear pampers
indefinitely. God “encourages [parakaleo; imparts courage to] us in all our affliction so that we may be able to
encourage [parakaleo; impart courage to] those who are in any affliction with the courage with which we ourselves
are encouraged [parakaleo; imparted courage to] by God” (2Cor 1:4; TM).192 God said, “Charge Joshua, and
strengthen him, and encourage [parakaleo; impart courage to] him; for he shall go before the face of this people,
and he shall give them the inheritance of all the land which thou hast seen” (Dt 3:28). The Lord was not telling them
to comfort Joshua by offering him a Kleenex but by exhorting him to have the courage to take up a sword and fight.
Frequently, we are exhorted to have courage, to be strong and courageous (Dt 31:6-7,23; Josh 1:6-9, etc.). God not
only comforts us with a tissue but by encouraging us to take up a sword and fight. God comforts-encourages with
tissue-and-sword. Blow your nose, then get up and fight.
        The Lord was not impressed with Joshua’s pity party. Rather, the Lord told him to get up and take care of
business, to remove the sin that was causing their defeat:
      6
        Then Joshua tore his clothes and fell to the earth on his face before the ark of the LORD until the

      The Lord has a limit on self-pity, so much so that He told Joshua to stop whining to Him in prayer! Whining
prayer is not winning. Nor did the Lord allow me to keep whining in prayer after my termination. A little time for
whining is permitted, perhaps. But the Lord soon says, “Get up and start winning.” He does not crown whinners as
winners. So He did not allow me to wallow in self-pity when I underwent termination and rejection. Nor could I
allow myself to wallow in it. I had to fight it and overcome it. I had no pity on my self-pity. I showed no mercy,

       I recommended EL material and counseling to Sue and took a TL approach with her because ELTL is the
way both my dad and I overcame our own battle with rejection syndrome. This struggle can make her strong. But
you must be tough to be strong. My dad was also terminated as a pastor for unjust reasons. He practiced ELTL on
himself and me. Did he make some mistakes in doing so? Sure. I practice ELTL on myself and others. Will I make
some mistakes in doing so? Sure. Still, neither my dad nor I had to achieve infallibility to achieve victory. I write
this book in hopes that it will likewise give Sue and others like her the courage to practice ELTL on themselves and
others. It works. And you don’t have to be infallible for it to work.
                                               Heavenly Father Model
       Some parents seem to have the attitude, “Do as I say, not as I do.” That was not my attitude when I was
raising my sons. My attitude was, “Do as I do.” My belief was that my beliefs would be more effectively caught
than taught, though I did my fair share of teaching. I wanted my boys to memorize Scripture, read their Bibles, pray,
respect their mother, etc., because I did those things. The same goes for tough love. I expect my boys to practice
tough love because that is what I practiced before them and on them. In all likelihood, they will be better at practicing
ELTL both on themselves and others than I was on them. That was my intent in any event. I laid a good foundation
for them to build upon. My dad built the best foundation he could for me to build upon, and I built upon it, excelling
because of it. And I have laid the best foundation I could for my sons to build upon, and I believe that they will
excel because of it. Will they make mistakes? Sure. But probably less than I have.193
       I fondly remember the song by Phillips, Craig, and Dean, I Want to Be Just Like You. When my children
were young, we sang this song together in church. It goes like this:

do your best to add moral virtue to your faith. This virtue is added by the believer’s hard work, by the believer’s
effort. To be sure, it is God-enabled effort. Nevertheless, it is the believer’s effort (i.e., the believer’s hard work)
that goes into adding these virtues to the believer’s faith. Rewards are based on the believer’s works.

       The role model I have adopted for my role as a father is the Heavenly Father. To be certain, I am a fallible
follower of His infallible lead. Like the father in this song, I make mistakes. Nevertheless, I take comfort in knowing
that my Heavenly Father does not expect me to be infallible or free of mistakes. I can live up to His expectations
by seeking to follow His fatherly example through the Spirit and Scripture. I will err, naturally, but to err is not
necessarily sin. Others may have other roles to play in counseling those suffering from rejection syndrome, but the
role I have been assigned in this situation is that of a father, and I apply the same Heavenly Father role model as
my standard. A natural correlation between the discipline by earthly fathers and the Heavenly Father is generally
expected:
       9
         Furthermore, we had earthly fathers to discipline us, and we respected them; shall we not much
      rather be subject to the Father of spirits, and live? 10 For they disciplined us for a short time as seemed
      best [dokeo] to them, but He disciplines us for our good, that we may share His holiness. 11 All
      discipline for the moment seems not to be joyful, but sorrowful; yet to those who have been trained by
      it, afterwards it yields the peaceful fruit of righteousness. (Heb 12:9-11)

       Earthly fathers can only do the best they can. What they deem best may not always be the best, but it typically
will be what they deem best. Commentators on this passage explain it as follows: “That is, human fathers discipline
their sons ‘at their discretion,’ which is fallible” (NIGTC). “However high the principles on which an earthly father
acts he is not infallible” (TNTC). Such training is imperfect. “There is a margin of uncertainty in all human training”
(WBC).

      We accepted the discipline because it was their province to impose it and our duty to submit to it; they
      knew, or thought they knew, what was best for us and subjected us to the discipline which commended
      itself to them. If our heavenly Father also imposes discipline on us, shall we not accept it willingly
      from him? Our earthly fathers may sometimes have been mistaken in their estimate of the discipline
Chapter 7. Reoccurrence                                                                                        Page 131
        we needed; our heavenly Father, in the perfection of his wisdom and love, can be relied upon never to
        impose any discipline on us which is not for our good. The supreme good he has in view for his children
        is this, that they should share his holiness. (NICNT)

       Neither as a father nor as a counselor adopting a fatherly role model do I perceive myself to be infallible. To
think otherwise would be laughable. However, neither the Heavenly Father nor Scripture expects earthly fathers to
be infallible. Like any other fallible creature, they can only do the best they can. On the other hand, as the writer
of Hebrews indicates, children are expected to obey and respect those acting in this capacity when those acting in
this role are doing the best they can. Earthly fathers would be failing in their responsibilities to do their best if they
accepted less from those placed in their disciplinary care.
                                                    Warfare Prayer
       Just as I get dressed each morning, I start off by getting dressed for spiritual warfare (polemology) with some
version of the following prayer, using the armor passage in Eph 6:10-18 as my template. This is a spiritual discipline.
For the most part, I am praying Scripture back to God in this prayer. I have various passages in mind as I pray. But
I do not cite them. I shared this prayer with Sam. He shared it with Sue. She has read it. This prayer has grown and
adapted over the years, as my understanding has grown. Its current form is as follows:
Armor
      Lord Jesus, I appropriate You as my Armor of Light so that I may stand firm against the schemes of the devil
and the forces of darkness throughout this day and night and be the warrior of light You desire for me to be.
Belt
       I appropriate Your Word of truth as my belt of truth by appropriating the truth of Your Word concerning my
death, burial, resurrection, and accession with You, Lord Jesus, to the Father's right hand. Thank You for having
crucified me with You to the law, sin, self, and the world, and thereby delivering me from their power and control
and deadening me to their influence.
                                                         Law
       Thank You that I am no longer under the Law and thus no longer walk with my focus on Your legal demands
but rather by dependency on Your Spirit, intimacy with Your Spirit, and fellowship with Your Spirit. Thank You,
Holy Spirit, for setting my heart and mind on Jesus, making Him my focus rather than rules and laws, so that I may
experience intimacy with You and with Him throughout the day, making this intimacy my internal guiding light.
Thank You, Holy Spirit, for strengthening me and encouraging me throughout this day as You enable me to be a
victorious warrior and beautiful Bride for my Lord Jesus, infusing me with Your beauty as you beautify my spirit.
I love You, Holy Spirit.
                                                          Sin
       Thank You, Lord Jesus, for Your death for me and having delivered me from Hell. I love You, Lord Jesus.

       Thankfulness is vital. Paul says, “In everything give thanks; for this is God’s will for you in Christ Jesus”
(1Thess 5:18). He goes beyond this to instruct us: “Always giving thanks for all things in the name of our Lord
Jesus Christ to God, even the Father” (Eph 5:20). LaHaye notes the therapeutic benefits: “Formally give thanks for
anything that bothers you....Thanksgiving is therapeutic and helpful, particularly in reducing anger. You will not be
angry or depressed if in every insult, rejection, or injury you give thanks. Admittedly this is difficult at times, but it
is possible” (pp. 153-154).
                                          Depth of Four-Dimensional Love
       In his chapter, “Grasping Four-Dimensional Love,” Hollandsworth begins with a discussion of the triplet of
faith, hope, and love found in 1Cor 13:13. Faith and hope are progressive steps to love and are included in love:
“Love is dependent on hope, and hope is dependent on faith. Where there is no faith or hope, then can be no real
love.” Christ’s love is four-dimensional, having “breadth and length and height and depth” (Eph 3:18). Our bodies
are three dimensional, having breadth and length and height, but depth goes deeper than these three dimensions.
Paul’s prayer for these believers is that Christ’s love will go experientially all the way down into the depths of their
inner man (Eph 3:16), being experienced via their trichotomous spirit, so that spiritually they “can comprehend the
incomprehensible,” as they perceive Christ’s love at a subatomic level that is deeper than their physical senses. This
love is “radically intimate,” penetrating into our “innermost being.” As a result, we can experience Christ’s four-
dimensional love in our three-dimensional bodies. Yet, as Hollandsworth adds, our fullest experience of this love
is expected to be experienced in harmonic “symphony with other Spirit-filled saints.”195 The fellowship of the Spirit
is only surpassed by the fellowship of the Spirit being shared in the fellowship of Bridal saints.
                                             Illustration 33. Steps to Love




                                                        Conclusion
       We are to forgive and love one another just as God loves and forgives us. But love and forgiveness are very
complex subjects when studied in the light of Scripture. If I want to be a vehicle through whom the Heavenly Father
can express His love and forgiveness for Sue, then I must follow His fatherly example by loving Sue and forgiving
her in the way that He does so in Scripture. God is love, but He is not lovey-dovey fluff. He not only cuddles us
and wraps His arms around us, but He also disciplines us so that we might share His holiness and thus ultimately
be a fitting Bride. The Father does not adopt a-peace-at-any-cost mentality, and neither do I. And He has limited
toleration for self-pity, as do I. Life is too valuable to waste on self-pity. We want to increase our value so that we
might become a beautiful Bride for Christ, and He is not looking for a Bride who is wallowing in self-pity. Do not
adopt a victim mentality but a warrior mentality. Christ is looking for a warrior Bride, not a self-pitying Bride. We
can be victims or victors. But we cannot be both. Choose one or the other. You cannot choose both. We can choose
to allow our emotions to traumatize us, or we can choose to take charge of our emotions. God has given us that
choice. Choose wisely.

                                                     Introduction
       Parts of this book have the makings of a diary; this chapter will be no exception. I had just finished my initial
proofreading of this manuscript yesterday afternoon when Sam dropped by (3/20/2019). He related that in their
Grace Life International (GLI) counseling session this week, he and Sue had to instruct their new instructor on
some basic nikological and misthological truths. As to nikology, Sam and Sue told him that, contrary to his claims,
1Jn 1:9 has nothing to do with soteriology. As to misthology, they pointed out that the NT teaches that rewards are
conditional. Even popular nikological illustrations such as the crossing of the Jordan River have misthological
implications. Sam said that Sue was being somewhat of a tigress in pointing out some of the misthological
shortcomings of the GLI instructions. If I had been there, I might have been a tiger. She and I seem to have somewhat
of the same temperament in that regard. As pointed out herein, controlled temper can be a very useful tool when
used rightly in a counseling situation. I look forward to her learning exchanged-life nikology so that she can hone
her temper into a soteriological-misthological weapon. That is what I have done with mine. Like Paul, on the one
hand, I fight legalism; on the other hand, I fight antinomianism.
       When Sam came over, I gave him a copy of two more Grace Fellowship International (GFI) books by
Solomon: The Rejection Syndrome and Handbook to Happiness and You. I also gave copies of these two books to
Janina last Sunday. Since I am exhorting Sam, Sue, and Janina to read Solomon’s material, my taking a moment
herein to make up for Solomon’s misthological deficiencies is prudent. His lack of misthological integration is
posing a serious roadblock to Sue’s ability to assimilate his nikological teaching. Since I highly value Solomon’s
nikology and want Sue to be able to experience its power for herself, I will assist by including a few basic integrated
models in this chapter.
                                                Be What You Are
        As noted in TOD, exhortations in the NT are frequently based on what God has done, is doing, or will do.
Many of these implicit and explicit exhortations fall within the first category; that is, they are addressed to believers
to be or do something based on what God already has made them or has done for them or has unconditionally
promised to them. As in TOD, the following list is confined to examples derived from single passages. Otherwise,
the list could be greatly increased with composite passages which teach, for example, that you are a winner so win.

      Misthological Motivation for Nikology
        The crossing of the Jordan River is a popular nikological teaching tool. In Rejection, Solomon uses a railway
illustration to commence his explanation of the analogy (pp. 61-72). Crossing the Red Sea represents regeneration.
The wilderness experience represents self-effort, which he attributes (1) firstly to legalism with its overemphasis
on rules at the expense of grace (p. 55) and (2) secondly to a failure to depend on the Spirit. The crossing of the
Jordan represents the appropriation of exchanged-life-identity truths encapsulated in Gal 2:20. Victory is
conditioned on Spirit-enabled performance. Solomon says, “The terminal point is glorification, which comes at
physical death, when the believer enters God’s sinless presence. Then the believer is delivered from even the
presence of sin.” For the most part, his discussion and illustration are good, insofar as they go, and are basically
what one would expect from nikological teachers. However, Solomon proceeds to say that there is no “possibility
of being rejected by God” (p. 70). This statement is only true of soteriological glorification, though; it fails to take
misthological glorification into account. Granted, Solomon is limiting the scope of his discussion to soteriology and
nikology, but this limited scope presents a problem. Not factoring in misthology leads to several errors.
        What happens to believers who die in the wilderness, that is, in a carnal state? They reach soteriological
glorification just the same. What about believers who cross the Jordan but then stop obeying and stop experiencing
victory? They reach soteriological glorification just the same. What is the incentive for crossing the Jordan and to
keep fighting? The only thing that Solomon offers within the confines of his discussion is “the freedom and joy of
resurrected life...an experiential understanding of the Cross,” which he equates with (1) Moses motivation for
suffering affliction (Heb 11:25) and (2) with the OT saints enduring torture so “that they might obtain a better
resurrection” (Heb 11:35).196 To a misthologist, Solomon’s nikological shortcomings are appalling!
        These texts are not limiting the motivation for victory to the present experience of victory. The writer of
Hebrews is not advocating victory for victory’s sake. The biblical context immediately goes on to attribute Moses
motivation for enduring suffering to his “looking to the reward” (Heb 11:26). Thus, the context explicitly states
that Moses’ motivation was misthological. Moreover, contrary to Solomon, the “better resurrection” in Heb 11:35
is not the resurrection of identification teaching experienced in this life. The biblical context is clearly talking about
a postmortem resurrection experienced in the hereafter rather than the here-and-now. A misthological resurrection
is obviously in view, more specifically, a misthological out-resurrection (see TOD). The out-resurrection is needed
to save believers from the outer darkness.197 The reason these OT saints endured torture, even to the point of being
killed in this life, was so that they could have a better resurrection in the next life—a misthological resurrection in
which they would be well rewarded for their suffering. Failing to acknowledge misthology as the principal biblical
motivation for nikology led Solomon to some erroneous conclusions.
                                       Glorification Versus Co-Glorification
       Whereas Solomon uses a train diagram to depict the crossing of the Red Sea and Jordan River, culminating
in unconditional acceptance and soteriological glorification, I use a version of the following chart in TOD,
culminating in conditional misthological glorification. For the sake of differentiation, misthological glorification is
referred to as co-glorification herein (cp. Rom 8:17b).198 While Solomon’s train diagram is good to a point, it falls

                                       Illustration 35. Achan’s Lack of Rest




       To simplify the discussion from TOD, we can surmise that Achan plays a very interesting role in typological
discussions about nikology and misthology, especially in relation to kingdom rest (Heb 3-4). Based on 1Cor 15:50
and the OT background, misthologists have noted that (1) inherit ≠ enter, (2) inherit > enter, and (3) inherit >
inhabit. That is, (1) the inheritance is not to be equated with entering the kingdom. (2) Inheriting the kingdom refers
to a greater experience than merely entering the kingdom. Also, inheriting the kingdom refers to a greater experience
then merely (3) inhabiting the kingdom. Furthermore, two verses in Jn 3:3,5 establish that entrance into the future
kingdom is unmerited. Present entrance assures future entrance (i.e., present  future), and this soteriological
entrance is based simply upon the new birth. Therefore, the fact all believers will enter the future kingdom is
normally based on four points: (1) inherit ≠ enter; (2) inherit > enter; (3) inherit > inhabit; (4) present  future.
       Some misthologists consider Achan a representative of unfaithful believers who soteriologically enter the
future kingdom (soteriologically: Achan = enter), using the equal sign to serve as an abbreviation for typological
equality. Heb 4:8 and the OT background, to which the writer of Hebrews is referring, supports this interpretation:
“For if Joshua had given them rest, He would not have spoken of another day after that.” (Achan was part of the
them in Heb 4:8 who entered the land with Joshua.) But the rest was not experienced when the Israelites entered
the land or were fighting. Rest was not experienced until much later (Josh 11:23; cp. 22:4; 23:1). To inherit the land
is to possess it (Josh 18:2-3). To possess the land is to have rest (Josh 21:43-45). Therefore, to inherit the land
means to possess it and have rest; these terms refer to more than merely entering the land. This was anticipated in
Dt 3:18-20 where the Israelites were told in advance that they would not have rest when they entered the land but
when they took possession of it (so also Dt 1:8; Num 13:30; 14:24). Therefore, Dt 12:8-10 is not referring merely
to entering a place; it is referring to entering that place and taking possession of it so that it may become a place of
rest—a resting place and an inheritance (rest = misthological inheritance, cp. Col 3:24). Achan is a legitimate part
of the typological background. Nevertheless, even though the biblical account of Achan strengthens and greatly
Chapter 8. Nikology-Misthology 101                                                                            Page 139
illustrates this misthological position, a misthological rest is entailed whether the typology of Achan is included.199
        Notwithstanding, including Achan is a natural part of the typology and is useful to picture the point made by
the writer of Hebrews. Achan represents those who enter into spiritual warfare (polemology) but fail to win that
war. Those who died in the wilderness represent carnal believers who do not engage in spiritual warfare and thus
fail to achieve misthological rest. Achan shows that it is also possible for spiritual believers to fail to enter the
misthological rest. The typology shows Achan’s entrance into the land of war (nikologically: Achan = enter), not
into the land of rest (misthologically: Achan ≠ enter). Achan is a legitimate part of the typology.200
        As depicted in Illustration 35. Achan’s Lack of Rest, those who died in the wilderness typologically highlight
the doctrinal fact that unfaithful believers will not enter the place or the experience of rest. Those OT believers who,
like Achan, died in Canaan further show that even if one progresses in the practical sanctification to the point of
experiencing the victorious Christian life, he or she still may fail to achieve the intended misthological goal if he or
she fails to persevere and meet the requirements for the reward. Faithfulness is required for entrance into the
heavenly rest. The rest represents the rulership in Heaven. But such victorious perseverance is not required for
entrance into the land of Heaven itself. Taken together, these groups picture the fact that all blood-bought believers
will enter the kingdom, but unfaithful believers will be limited spatially and experientially in both its millennial and
eternal expressions. They will never obtain the rest either spatially or experientially. The outer darkness is
permanent exclusion from this type of rest. As already demonstrated elsewhere in my writings (see TOD), the rest
is a reward. This conclusion is not dependent upon the inclusion of Achan.
                                              Warning to Nikologists
       At junctures in my writings, I have issued various warnings to soteriologists and misthologists. For example,
some soteriologists teach excellent soteriology but are very defective in their teaching on nikology and misthology.
They teach legalism or self-effort as the means to live the Christian life and thus fail to teach a biblical nikology.
We may reasonably expect that they will do very poorly at the Bema (e.g., Gal 5:2-4).201 Other soteriologists
downplay the importance of the Bema, even saying that the Bema is merely an educational experience. They take
away from the warnings of Scripture. We may justifiably conclude that they will do very poorly at the Bema as a
result (e.g., Rev 22:19; cp. Dt 4:1-2; Prov 3:5-6). Even misthologists who tone down the implications of the Bema
can fall under this same misthological condemnation. The same is true of nikologists. Teaching a sound soteriology
and sound nikology is not necessarily enough to do well at the Bema.
       Indeed, Achan provides a suitable typological warning to those nikologists who belittle rewards.
Typologically, Achan entered the Promised Land and experienced victory. Typologically, we would conclude that
he knew and practiced the principles of nikology. Yet, unlike Moses, he was distracted by earthly treasure and failed
to press on for heavenly treasure. In the end, he lost his life. Many believers will likewise lose their soul/life when
they stand at the Bema (Mk 8:36-38). Either (1) they did not run the race, or (2) they did not run the race according

the kingdom. What they failed to enter is the rest, that is, the rulership of the land. If Achan is included in the
typological consideration of what it means to enter the rest (which is my preference), then Achan is part of a two-
part typological illustration of the fact that unfaithful believers will not inherit the future kingdom, and they will be
excluded from certain places within that kingdom.

to the rules, or (3) they did not finish the race (1Cor 9:24-25; Gal 6:9; 2Tim 2:5; 4:7). Soteriology, nikology, and
misthology are three primary points of emphasis that need to be mastered if one is to finish the race well.
                                       Illustration 36. Achan’s Lack of Rest




       Put into baseball terms, the rules of the race require us to round all three bases properly in order to reach
home base. We need sound theology in terms of soteriology, nikology, and misthology to reach co-glorification.
Even so, typology suggests that there are exceptions to this rule. The carnal believers below the age of twenty did
not die in the wilderness but were allowed to enter the Promised Land, fight, and win an inheritance. A misthological
age of accountability is thus suggested. Accordingly, we might postulate that those believers who have not had time
to move from immaturity to maturity in this life might have the opportunity to participate in co-glorification to
whatever degree might be possible for immature saints. But for those who have had opportunities to mature, much
more will be required (Lk 12:48). Nikologists who fail to mature misthologically might not be suitable candidates
for composing a misthologically mature Bride, especially if they are without excuse, having had ample time and
opportunity to mature in this area.
                                              Corrected Train Track
       My first misgiving with Solomon’s train-track illustration is that he depicts the negative downside as working
and the positive upside as resting. Adding the positive character traits Solomon lists from Gal 5:22-23 into our lives
takes a lot of work, however, as Peter makes clear:

      Now for this very reason also, do your very best [epichoregeo] to
      • add to your faith, moral virtue
      • and to your moral virtue, knowledge;
      • and to your knowledge, self-control,
      • and to your self-control, perseverance,
      • and to your perseverance, godliness;
      • and to your godliness, brotherly kindness,
      • and to your brotherly kindness, love (2Pet 1:5-7; TM).

      As explained and illustrated more thoroughly in BRH and MCMC, whether epichoregeo is translated as add
or supply makes little difference since Peter’s supplementation is addition. The important thing to note is that faith
comes first, and works are added to this faith by the believer to develop these character traits and derive a virtuous
Chapter 8. Nikology-Misthology 101                                                                            Page 141
faith as a result. These works must be added to faith in order to earn a rich misthological entrance into the kingdom
as the resulting reward (2Pet 1:11). This virtue is added by the believer’s hard work. To be sure, it is God-enabled
effort. Nevertheless, it is the believer’s effort (i.e., the believer’s hard work) that goes into adding these virtues to
the believer’s faith. Rewards at the Bema are based on the believer’s works.
                                  Illustration 37. Add/Supply Works to Your Faith




        Even in the Pauline context, if one wants to obtain a misthological harvest, walking according to the Spirit
requires sowing according to the Spirit by doing good without growing weary. If it were easy, Paul would not be
concerned about us growing weary: “The one who sows to the Spirit shall from the Spirit reap eternal life. And let
us not lose heart in doing good, for in due time we shall reap if we do not grow weary” (Gal 6:8-9). The victorious
Christian life is not a passive rest from work. Solomon clarifies this point in his discussion. Still, it would be good
if his illustration avoided this misimpression.
        To be sure, Jesus promised soteriological rest and nikological rest, respectively, in Mt 11:28-29. However,
soteriologists and nikologists do not have a monopoly on the biblical promise of rest. As discussed in TOD, the
Bible also teaches misthological rest (Ps 95:11; 132:13-14; Is 11:9-10; 2Thess 1:6-10; Heb 3:11-4:11; Rev 14:13;
21:3). The passage in Heb 3:11-4:11 is often considered a picture of nikological rest by exchanged-life advocates.
But a misthological interpretation is superior.202 As noted in FFG, it is ironic that passive nikologists, who
anathematize doing your best and who perceive the rest of Heb 4:11 as being a passive temporal victory, fail to note
that the rest in that very verse is achieved by doing your best. As a matter of fact, the CJB translates it as: “Let us
do our best to enter that rest.”203

    See my discussion in TOD. Also see Hodges, BKC; Tanner, GNTC; and Dillow (ch. 11) in Final Destiny. Dillow
has a chart similar to mine.
203
    See “Do your Best” in FFG for extended discussion and defense of doing your best, by the power of the Spirit,

Illustration 38. Train to Glory   Degrees of Love and Forgiveness
