        Those people in the natural state do not have eternal life, so Christ is outside of their life (as represented by
the Cross outside of the circle). Sin, as an entity, is on the throne of their life (as represented by the S on the throne
in the circle). The issue they need to be concerned about is justification. The carnal person represents believers
who, typologically, have crossed the Red Sea by simply trusting in Christ for the free gift of eternal life. Now Christ
is in their life (as represented by the Cross in the circle). They are still immature, thus carnal, and still controlled by
Sin (as represented by S on the throne). They tend to live by their emotions (as represented by the train’s feeling-
engine), and their emotions are out of whack (as represented by the variously sized dark ellipses). The transition for
becoming spiritual believers is typologically signified by the crossing of the Jordan River. Once believers reach
the bottom and give up on trying to live the Christian life in their own strength, they can cross the Jordan and start
living by faith in identification facts (as represented by the train’s facts-engine). As a result, they become
increasingly filled with the Spirit (as represented by the filling-passenger-car), and Christ is increasingly in control
of their life (as represented by the Cross on the throne). Sin is still in their fleshly bodies (thus in the circle) but has
been dethroned and no longer reigns over them. Their emotions tend to fall in line (as represented by the feelings
following on the train track and by the uniform black circles). This is all pretty much standard exchanged-life
nikology, with which I fully concur. The energy to perform is supplied by the Spirit in conjunction with the Scripture
not being used in a legalistic manner. Solomon does well to explain this in his discussion, but his illustration needs
to be tweaked to show that the train has not yet reached the train station and thus is not at rest. My illustration
supplies this tweak. We rest from legalism and from straining in self-effort, but we do not rest from our Spirit-
enabled works.
        The second problem with Solomon’s illustration is that it terminates in unconditional glorification. He is
correct to picture our entrance into glory as not being conditioned on our performance. Believers have unconditional
security, assuring them of entrance into the future kingdom. Soteriological glorification is unconditional for all
those who have crossed the Red Sea. All believers will have a home in glory. They will enter the millennial and
eternal kingdom.204 Therein lies a major problem with the implications of Solomon’s illustration, though. Since this
level of glorification is free, it is guaranteed to both carnal believers and spiritual believers. The logical implication
of his illustration, surely unintentionally, gives the false impression that in the hereafter it does not matter how
believers live in the here-and-now. This half-truth is far from the full truth.
        Many carnal believers and even some spiritual believers will end up in the outer darkness when they reach
glory. Sin will be removed from their redeemed, glorified bodies (as represented by the removal of the S from
within the circle). Christ will be on the throne of their lives (as represented by the Cross on the throne). Even carnal
believers will be spiritual in the sense of not having Sin in their bodies and in having Christ on their throne, and
even some spiritual believers will be carnal in terms of misthological maturity. Therefore, those in the outer darkness
will be carnal-spiritual believers (as represented by the joint designation in the dark section of the illustration).
Glorification salvation will consist of the outer darkness for them. They will be in the future kingdom but excluded
from many of the misthological opportunities they could have achieved if they had qualified for co-glorification
salvation. They will have mixed emotions (as represented by the mixture of uniform and nonuniform black shapes
in the circle). On the one hand, they will be happy that they are in the kingdom and thankful for what degree of
intimacy they have with the Lord and for whatever degree of reward they may qualify. On the other hand, they will
regret their lost opportunities for misthological service and for higher degrees of intimacy with the Lord.
        Bridal believers succeed in crossing over every obstacle hindering them from reaching co-glorification with
Christ. The Battle of Ai is used typologically in the illustration to represent successfully having done everything
necessary, both morally and doctrinally, to achieve victory misthologically at the Bema. Every tear will be wiped
away from their bridal eyes (Rev 7:17; 21:4). They will have no regrets (as represented by the uniform black circles
in the larger circle). As will be true of all believers, Sin will be removed from their redeemed, glorified bodies (as
represented by the removal of the S from within the circle), and Christ will be on the throne of their lives (as
represented by the Cross on the throne). However, bridal believers will not only be ontologically sinless but
misthologically sinless. Also, the spirits of bridal believers will be far more beautiful and Christlike than those of
other believers. Bridal spirits will not only be sinless but also display a spiritual maturity and beauty in splendid
beaming glory (as represented by the various colors used for their bridal title). They will be the pearl of great price
(as represented by the semi-transparent bridal-pearl overlay). An enlargement of their circle is provided below the
graph in Illustration 38 for the sake of emphasis.
204
   Some misthologists believe that unfaithful believers will be excluded from the millennial kingdom but be allowed
to enter the eternal kingdom. I have refuted those misthological arguments elsewhere and need not do so here.
Page 144                                                                         Degrees of Love and Forgiveness
                                                    Bridal Worth
       Assurance is crucial for victory. Solomon wisely points out that we need both soteriological assurance and
nikological assurance. We were saved by faith, and we are to live by faith. And assurance is the essence of faith.
Accordingly, we should also have misthological assurance. If we are rounding all three bases and adding these
virtues to our spirit by the Spirit, then we have solid grounds for misthological assurance. We should be confident
that the Holy Spirit is doing everything necessary in our spirit to qualify us as bridal saints. We should have
confidence in the Holy Spirit. We should be able to sing with confidence that Jesus’ love is our reward. The song,
Prize Worth Fighting For, by Jamie Kimmett captures the essence of why we fight the good fight and also expresses
the confidence that we should have that we qualify for this reward:

                                      When every days just another struggle
                                        And every choice is an act of war
                                           Gotta pray gotta press on
                                         To the prize worth fighting for
                                       When it feels like I'll never make it
                                       When my hearts crying out for more
                                           Gotta pray gotta press on
                                         To the prize worth fighting for

                                             Your love is my reward
                                           The prize worth fighting for
                                            Jesus You’re worth my all
                                             Your love is my reward

                                             Your love is my reward

       We can run the race not only because we believe that we can win but also because we believe that we are
winning. By spiritual nature, we are winners (1Jn 4:4). By the Spirit’s nurture, we can be what we are by nature—
winners. As a result, we can rest assured that Christ’s love is our misthological reward. We are worth everything to
Jesus. We don’t have a right to feel unworthy. If we are running the race in the prescribed manner—with justified
soteriological, nikological, and misthological assurance—we can be confident that the Lord and the Holy Spirit are
doing everything necessary to make us worthy. The only way we could be worthless is for His love for us to be
worthless. His love for us makes us valuable, and, conversely, He loves us because we are valuable. We can walk
by faith in the intellectual persuasion that we are misthologically valuable.
       Jesus ran the race with the same attitude. He said, “For this reason the Father loves Me, because I lay down
My life that I may take it again” (Jn 10:17). Jesus could sing,

                                        When every days just another struggle
                                        And every choice is an act of war
                                           Gotta pray gotta press on
                                         To the prize worth fighting for
                                       When it feels like I'll never make it
                                       When my hearts crying out for more
                                           Gotta pray gotta press on
                                         To the prize worth fighting for

                                             Your love is my reward
                                           The prize worth fighting for
                                           Father You’re worth my all
                                             Your love is my reward

                                             Your love is my reward
Chapter 8. Nikology-Misthology 101                                                                            Page 145
         By amending one word in the above stanza, it becomes representative of Jesus, our representative. Kimmett
addresses our song to Jesus as we run our race, which is entirely appropriate. Yet, when Jesus was running His race,
He was addressing His song and prayer to the Father. The Father’s love was the reward Jesus was fighting for!
Moreover, Jesus was confident that He was winning the Father’s love. One reason Jesus came down to Earth to
become a man was so that He could know what it is like to earn the Father’s love as a man. And man, did He
succeed! So can we. As we lay down our lives by practicing identity truths, we too can say, “For this reason the
Father loves me, because I lay down my life that I may take it again.” We will take up our life again in glory,
specifically misthological co-glorification via the out-resurrection.
         Jesus is loved by the Father as a glorified Man. Being loved infinitely as a Man by an infinite Person makes
Jesus infinitely valuable as a Man. Conversely, Jesus is loved infinitely as a Man by this infinite Person because
Jesus is infinitely valuable as a Man. It is morally right for the Father to love Jesus infinitely as a Man. Jesus, as a
Man, is worth this infinite reward. Jesus won the Father’s love for Him as a Man as His reward. We are to fix “our
eyes on Jesus, the author and perfecter of faith, who for the joy set before Him endured the cross, despising the
shame, and has sat down at the right hand of the throne of God” (Heb 12:2). What joy was set before Jesus? The
joy of having us as a Bride is an obvious answer. The author of Hebrews makes this clear from Heb 2:6 onward.
Man, that is, redeemed misthologically glorified humanity, is Jesus’ prize, the reason He died. But from Heb 1:3
onward, the writer of Hebrews makes it clear that this is not the only prize Jesus won. He also won the Father’s
loving approval as a redeeming misthologically glorified Man. Jesus won His Father’s love in a way that He did
not have it before. Jesus testified to this misthological reality when He said, “For this reason the Father loves Me,
because I lay down My life that I may take it again” (Jn 10:17).
         The Father’s love for Jesus as a redeeming Man was won by Jesus’ redeeming sacrifice. The Father’s love is
everything to Jesus. The Father already loved Jesus infinitely as His divine Son. But the Father could not love Jesus
infinitely as a Man until Jesus became a Man and did something worthy of the Father’s infinite love for Him as a
Man. For the Father to love Jesus infinitely as Man, the Father’s love for Jesus in this manner and degree had to be
justified. The Father would not and could not love Jesus infinitely as a Man until the Father was justified in doing
so. God’s love, like ours, exists in different planes. As to His divine character and nature, “Jesus Christ is the same
yesterday and today, yes and forever” (Heb 13:8). But as a Man, “Jesus kept increasing in wisdom and stature, and
in favor with God and men” (Lk 2:52). The Father’s love for Jesus as a Man grew. The Father’s love for Jesus was
conditioned not only on who Jesus was but also on what Jesus did and who Jesus would become. As Jesus grew
stronger in His human spirit, His human spirit became more beautiful and valuable. Jesus’ human spirit was
perfected by what He suffered: “For it was fitting for Him, for whom are all things, and through whom are all things,
in bringing many sons to glory, to perfect the author of their salvation through sufferings” (Heb 2:10). His suffering
on the Cross completed the misthological perfection of His human spirit. The Father is now justified in seating
Jesus, as a glorified Man, at His right hand. Likewise, we are spiritually perfected by the things we successfully
suffer (Jam 1:2-4). Therefore, it is misthologically right for us to sit beside Jesus.
         The Father must be just (dikaios) in all His actions and affections. For example, He had to be justified in
justifying us (Rom 3:26). God’s righteous nature demands that He be just in what He does. He cannot do something
if it is unjust. Is the Father justified in loving Jesus infinitely as a Man? Is Jesus worth the Father’s love as a Man?
You bet! As a matter of fact, you can bet your life on that fact—not only soteriologically but also misthologically.
Peter reminds us that the same misthological principle applies to us: “After you have suffered for a little while, the
God of all grace, who called you to His eternal glory in Christ, will Himself perfect, confirm, strengthen and
establish you” (1Pet 5:10). For the Father and Son to love us infinitely, they must be justified in doing so. They
must, through the Spirit, make our spirits infinitely valuable through what we suffer. As a result of making us
infinitely beautiful, the Father, Son, and Spirit have the moral right to love us infinitely—both volitionally and
emotionally.205
         We catch a glimpse of this perception in Paul’s misthological affection for the Philippians: “For I am
confident of this very thing, that He who began a good work in you will perfect it until the day of Christ Jesus. For
it is only right for me to feel this way about you all, because I have you in my heart, since both in my imprisonment
and in the defense and confirmation of the gospel, you all are partakers of grace with me” (Phil 1:6-7). Because of
their sacrificial giving to support his ministry, Paul expected misthological perfection to be worked out on their
behalf. As a result of their sacrifice, Paul said that he had the right to think/feel (phroneo) this way about them.
Some translations render phroneo as feel in this verse, others as think. There is no dichotomy between the two here.
205
      Conversely, it would be unjust for God to forget to reward our work and love (Heb 6:10).
Page 146                                                                            Degrees of Love and Forgiveness
Paul’s feelings were in harmony with his thinking, and his feelings were just/right (dikaios). His feelings were
justified because they were in accord with his thinking, and his thinking was in accord with the misthological reality
of the situation. Paul’s misthological feelings for the Philippians reflect God’s misthological feelings for us. God
has the moral right to feel this way about us because it is the right thing to do, because it is in accord with reality.
God is not neurotic. His feelings are in accord with reality. And the trichotomous reality is that overcomers are truly
this beautiful and valuable in their innermost being. Their spirits are more valuable and beautiful than we can
imagine (i.e., beyond anything that we can think, cp. Eph 3:20). “Eye has seen, nor ear heard, nor the heart of man
imagined, what God has prepared for those who love him” (1Cor 2:9; ESV). If our reward is beyond anything we
can imagine, then our beauty and value must be beyond anything we can imagine. I Can Only Imagine by MercyMe
is a great song:

                                             Surrounded by Your glory
                                              What will my heart feel
                                             Will I dance for you Jesus
                                              Or in awe of You be still
                                            Will I stand in your presence
                                             Or to my knees will I fall
                                               Will I sing hallelujah
                                            Will I be able to speak at all
                                                 I can only imagine
                                                 I can only imagine

       I love both the song and the movie. Actually, more precisely, though, it seems that we can’t really imagine
just how wonderful it will actually be. Frankly, it is hard for me to imagine that I can’t imagine how wonderful it
will be. I have a pretty good imagination in that regard, as attested in my writings. If the misthological reality is to
surpass what I can imagine, then it must be truly mind-blowing. I am prepared to have my mind blown when it is
met with that reality. Our renewed minds should be able to handle the reality at that time without blowing a mental
fuse. Yet, in view of what we will be, perhaps another song could be written along these lines:

                                             Surrounded by my glory
                                              What will my heart feel
                                             Will I dance for you Jesus
                                              Or in awe of me be still
                                            Will I stand in your presence
                                             Or to my knees will I fall
                                               Will I sing hallelujah
                                            Will I be able to speak at all
                                              If I could only imagine
                                              If I could only imagine

       As the Bride of Christ, we will either be struck with dumbfounded awe or burst forth in praise to Jesus over
just how beautiful we have been made by Him and for Him. It will be right for us to feel this way about ourselves.
In fact, it would be wrong for us not to feel this way about ourselves. To feel otherwise would be to undervalue His
workmanship. After all, we must be beautiful enough to justify Hell. Truly, our forthcoming beauty must be beyond
anything we can imagine. Contrary to the opinion of some, you are not wasting your energy to run the race to win
misthological perfection. We can live like we are misthologically loved precisely because we are running the race
through the beautifying power of the Spirit. With that reality in mind, we should spread our wings and fly. We can
be who we were meant to be. We can be loving, both of ourselves and others, because we are loved by the Lord up
above, whose Bride we are meant to be. There is a right way to love ourselves and others and a wrong way to love
ourselves and others. The right way is to try to imagine just how beautiful we and they can be if we and they submit
ourselves to the Lordship of Christ through the power of the Spirit. Perceive your potential value and their potential
value. Love both yourself and them in view of this potential value. If you are already doing this, then trust the Spirit
and Scripture that you are already well on your way to becoming the most beautiful creature in existence, much
more beautiful than the most beautiful of angels. Your beauty is already being realized in your spirit. Your glorified
Chapter 8. Nikology-Misthology 101                                                                          Page 147
body will be a fitting vessel through which your beautiful spirit will radiate. Currently, “we have this treasure in
earthen vessels” (2Cor 4:7): We have an enlightened heart (2Cor 4:6) and spirit of faith (2Cor 4:13) and renewed
inner man being made into the eternal weight of glory through light affliction (2Cor 4:17). The Lord “will transform
the body of our humble state into conformity with the body of His glory” (Phil 3:21). We will be in awe of His glory
and our misthological co-morphing (summorphos) glory. Jesus morphed (morphe); we are undergoing a
metamorphosis (metamorphoo, 2Cor 3:18) and will co-morph with Him (see MMP3).
                                             Nikological Shortcomings
        As I mentioned in the introduction of this excursus, Sam came by on the 3/20/2019 and mentioned some of
the shortcomings of the counseling he and Sue were receiving from Grace Life International (GLI). I was the one
who had recommended GLI to them. Because of the deficiencies in the GLI nikology, I subsequently had
recommended that they investigate Grace Fellowship International (GFI). As it turns out, the next day Dianne and
I went to Pigeon Forge for a belated celebration of our 36th wedding anniversary. The first thing I wanted to do was
find the GFI office, since it was in Pigeon Forge, and find out if they had any material that integrated nikology with
misthology. So Dianne and I visited the GFI office the next day (3/21/2019).
        We had the pleasure of meeting John Woodward while there. I had read his book, Man as Spirit, Soul, and
Body, the year before. It is an excellent book on trichotomy. I highly recommend it. It was through that book that I
discovered Solomon’s ministry. Also, as a result of Woodward’s discussion of trichotomy, I had read Franz
Delitzsch’s magnum opus on the subject, A System of Biblical Psychology, which was also a very profitable reading
experience. My own magnum opus, The Outer Darkness (TOD), was originally supposed to be a three-volume book
and have both a chapter and an appendix on trichotomy: balancing nikology with misthology. Unfortunately, in
order to reduce TOD to a two-volume book, I had to remove that material from TOD and place it into a separate
book, Exchanged Life Trichotomy (ELT), which, regrettably, I have not yet published because of the pressing needs
regarding other writing projects. Nevertheless, as a result of Sam and Sue’s need for this integrated material, I
wanted to resume research in that area and move it further to the front of my other writing projects. This research
led me to Woodward’s book on trichotomy, and in turn to GFI, a very timely find.
        When I visited the GFI office, I asked Woodward if GFI had any material integrating nikology with
misthology. The only book he had to offer in that regard was his own book, Blessed Reassurance. He graciously
gave me a free copy. Woodward also recommended Bruce Wilkinson’s book, A Life God Rewards, as being
representative of what GFI believes about rewards. I ordered a copy from Amazon so that I might better understand
GFI’s misthological perspective.206 Also, while at the GFI office, I purchased a couple more books by Solomon.
        The forward to Woodward’s book, Reassurance, affirmed that we can have assurance due to eternal security.
Naturally, since my ministry is entitled, Unconditional Security Ministries (www.UnconditionalSecurity.org), I was
pleased by this forward since I have written extensively in defense of eternal security. Moreover, in the opening
sentence of his first chapter, Woodward said that he went to Florida Bible College (FBC). I was excited! Over forty
years ago I was introduced to Free Grace (FG) theology by a graduate from FBC. I was struggling with Lordship
Salvation (LS) at the time. I found FG to be a refreshing relief to my LS. The only FG book I had at that time was
by The Gift of God by Richard A. Seymour, a teacher at FBC. Further, as the Lord in His providence would have
it, I was won over completely to FG theology by Stan Ponz, a teacher from FBC who led a Bible study at WCU—
the university I was attending—forty years ago (see ATTG). Stan put me in touch with Grace Evangelical Society
(GES), and I joined GES at the ground level. I had lost touch with Stan over the years but was happily reunited with
him at a GES conference in 2017. Stan informed me that he was president of FBC now. Wonderful! In fact, we both
had book tables at the GES conference. I had even met Seymour at a FG conference several years previously.
        Accordingly, my expectations for Woodward’s book were very optimistic. Unfortunately, the book did not
live up to expectations. To be sure, there are numerous positives in it since it affirms eternal security, defends
personal assurance, believes in personal accountability at the Bema, and has repeated affirmations of the importance

206
   Wilkinson’s book turned out to be a very elementary discussion of rewards. For the most part, it is good insofar
as it goes. He correctly acknowledges that believers can suffer loss and shame at the Bema (pp. 68-70), which is
better than some interpreters in this regard. But he makes the typical mistakes. For example, he asserts that regret
and shame imposed at the Bema will not be eternal because of Rev 21:4 (p. 56). But the wiping away of tears in
this verse is a misthological promise to overcomers, not a blanket promise to all believers (See MMP1 and RAE).
Wilkinson likewise wrongly thinks that Jesus simplistically loves all believers equally and unconditionally (p. 56).
But this notion is shown to be false in the present study as well as elsewhere.
Page 148                                                                            Degrees of Love and Forgiveness
of rewards, etc. Wonderful! But the negatives include making personal assurance contingent upon performance,
making security contingent upon perseverance in the faith, limiting carnality to a temporary condition,
misinterpreting a number of misthological texts as soteriological texts, and worst of all, teaching LS—on both the
frontend and backend!
       The logical extrapolation of Woodward’s LS position would be that if you do not have enough good works
to prove that you are saved, then you were never really saved. If you do not persevere in the faith, then you were
never really in the faith. For faith to be saving it must include a repentance that follows through in producing enough
good works to prove that you were genuinely saved. Otherwise, you are just a verbal-professing believer, not a life-
possessing believer. Woodward approvingly quotes from Richard Seymour’s book, The Gift. If someone wishes to
get Woodward’s book, then I would suggest that they get Seymour’s book as well and compare and contrast the
two discussions. Woodward’s Reassurance is a poisoned version of Seymour’s Gift. For my specific interaction
with Seymour, see TOD. For my conceptual interaction with Seymour’s position, see 3DUS. Seymour is a soft-
shelled securitist. I am a strong-hardcore securitist. I believe that a believer can commit soulical apostasy and remain
saved soteriologically; Seymour does not. Nevertheless, we are both under the same FG umbrella.

         The city is not Heaven; it is only a part of Heaven….The New Jerusalem is obviously the most glorious

Seymour is on the brink of suggesting that this city is itself a reward, which is a thesis developed in TOD. He
certainly implies that living in this city is a reward limited to faithful believers since he pictures it as the mansion
prepared for obedient believers. Unfaithful believers will be given other mansions (i.e., other cities) in which to
live. The outer darkness can be pictured as living in the kingdom outside of the Heavenly City. Seymour’s Gift is a
worthy read. Woodward’s Assurance, in contrast, ironically fails to provide assurance. Secure and Sure by Robert
Wilkin is a much better read for a discussion of assurance. Other GES books and materials are readily available at
www.faithalone.org. Those GES resources are excellent in soteriology and misthology and even helpful to a
considerable extent in nikology. However, the GFI material at www.gracefellowshipinternational.com provides
additional material that is very helpful for nikology. But one will have to weed out GFI’s soteriological and
misthological deficiencies in order to integrate the two. Otherwise, one might wind up in the outer darkness. Thus,
the present book seeks to provide some assistance with this integration.
        On the following Saturday, after getting back from Pigeon Forge, Dianne and I met with Sam (3/30/2019).
He shared a link from GLI: http://www.gracelifeinternational.com/freebies/christian-break-fellowship-with-god.
Unlike some GFI material, the GLI material fuses union with communion, relationship with fellowship, claiming
that if you have one, you have the other. Conversely, if you lack one, you lack the other. Yet it then turns around
and concedes that you may have one without the other. However, in that case, it is supposedly you who have turned
your back on God, not God who has turned His back on you. You are out of fellowship with God; He is not out of
fellowship with you. Your sins cannot separate you from God. You are just acting as if they have. Since your sins
already have been forgiven, you do not need to confess them (supposedly per Col 2:13; 3:13; Eph 4:32). The suitable
word for this theology is baloney. We might as well call a spade a spade, whether it is in evaluating a counselee or
a counselor. Baloney counseling will probably result in many being misthologically separated from God in the outer
darkness. Essentially, considering what the Bible has to say about misthology, GLI is providing antinomian baloney.
        As for GLI’s claims regarding Col 2:13, that text already has been considered herein and in 4DF. That text
should not be ripped out of its context to make a baloney prooftext. In the previous chapter, Paul warned:

         He has now reconciled you in His fleshly body through death, in order to present you before Him holy
         and blameless and beyond reproach—23 if indeed you continue in the faith firmly established and
         steadfast, and not moved away from the hope of the gospel that you have heard, which was proclaimed
         in all creation under heaven, and of which I, Paul, was made a minister. (Col 1:21-23).

Being blameless at the Bema is conditioned on your performance. In the subsequent chapter Paul warns:
         22
           Slaves, in all things obey those who are your masters on earth, not with external service, as those
         who merely please men, but with sincerity of heart, fearing the Lord. 23 Whatever you do, do your
         work heartily, as for the Lord rather than for men; 24 knowing that from the Lord you will receive the
         reward of the inheritance. It is the Lord Christ whom you serve. 25 For he who does wrong will
         receive the consequences of the wrong which he has done, and that without partiality. (Col 3:22-
         25).

      As Paul warns elsewhere in his writings, inheriting the kingdom is a reward conditioned on performance.
The passive, will receive, is what some might call a divine passive, indicating what God will actively do: pay back
everyone according to their works. In terms of rewards, God is not going to forgive you for not working if you do
not work. If you do not work, you will not be blameless or beyond reproach. God is going to blame you and reproach
you for not working, and He will deny you the misthological inheritance. What did Paul say to do in the case of a
brother who does not work, forgive him? No. Let him go hungry.

       As for Eph 4:32, Adams notes: “It should go without saying that since our forgiveness is modeled after God’s
(Eph. 4:32), it must be conditional.”210 GLI’s assumptions to the contrary are presumptuous. Two verses earlier, as
noted in 4DF, Paul’s warning that the Spirit can be grieved by our actions proves that the Spirit can remember our
sins and is grieved by our sins (Eph 4:30). How could you grieve the Spirit (Eph 4:30) if you already are forgiven
completely by the Spirit in advance regarding your fellowship? If God neither sees nor remembers your sins
familially, then how can the Spirit be grieved by something He can neither see nor remember?211 If all their sins
were misthologically forgiven (Eph 5:2-8), why does Paul immediately go on to warn them that they would not
inherit the kingdom if they morally walk in the darkness? Paul and John are on the same page (cp. 1Jn 1:7-9; 2:28).
You must walk in the light to have fellowship with God and to receive a misthological inheritance from God.
       Gal 5:4 is a good text to illustrate the various interpretive approaches, and the following translations are
representative of the various options:
•     “You have been severed from Christ, you who are seeking to be justified by law; you have fallen from grace” (NAS).
•     “You are separated from Christ, you who are trying to be justified by law; you have fallen from grace” (NAB).
•     “You have become estranged from Christ...you have fallen from grace” (NKJ).
•     “You have separated yourself from Christ, you have fallen away from grace” (NJB).
•     “You...have cut yourselves off from Christ; you have fallen away from grace” (NRS).

The Greek word for severed, separated, estranged cut off is katargeo. If I were a GLI interpreter, I would appeal to
one of the latter two translations and claim that we cut ourselves off or separate ourselves from God. It is something
we do to ourselves, treating it is a reflexive middle voice. But this would be a false line of reasoning because the
verb form (katergethete) is passive. The middle voice would have a different ending in Greek. The action is
performed on us by someone else, not by ourselves. The bottom two translations of Gal 5:4 are in error. Conceptual
parallels from Jn 15:6; Rom 11:22; and 1Jn 2:28 suggest that God is the one performing the action.

      •        If anyone does not abide in Me, he is thrown away as a branch, and dries up; and they gather them, and cast
               them into the fire, and they are burned. (Jn 15:6)
      •        Behold then the kindness and severity of God; to those who fell, severity, but to you, God's kindness, if you
               continue in His kindness; otherwise you also will be cut off. (Rom 11:22)
      •        And now, little children, abide in Him, so that when He appears, we may have confidence and not be
               shamefully put away from Him at His coming. (1Jn 2:28; TM).

       As noted in TOD, regarding the latter text, this shame is God-inflicted. Such believers will be put to shame
by God, before God, and shrink away in shame from God. Various translations bring out different nuances of what
is being said. The DBY tries to bring out several subtleties: “Be put to shame from before him at his coming.” The
preposition apo is best translated as from. I would translate it thus as: “Be shamefully put away from Him.” He will
shamefully have us cast away from His presence into the outer darkness when He returns if we fail to abide in Him.
The outer darkness is a place for the outcasts who are cast out from before Him at the execution of the Bema
judgment associated with the rapture. Likewise, as discussed at length in FFG, they had fallen from grace both
theologically and experientially, but most importantly, misthologically. Contextually, Christ would profit them
nothing in terms of inheriting the kingdom (Gal 5:2,21).

    As also pointed out in 4DF, why would Paul condition our experiencing God’s fatherly welcome on our behavior,

      From the GLI website, I found an article by Mark Maulding, the President and Founder of GLI. His
conclusion for Gal 5:4 is:

      Anyone who believes that adding law to grace assures someone of being a Christian and going to
      heaven is in bondage. Living under law, you never know if you’ve done enough.
             We can’t fall from grace if we’ve never been under grace. Another way of saying this is that
      those under grace who are truly in Christ cannot fall from grace, even if they are legalistic.212

      Maulding’s first statement is on target. Frankly, Woodward would be a good target. To the degree Woodward
conditions soteriological assurance on performance, he has fallen into legalism and from grace. GLI is better than
GFI in this regard. On the other hand, Maulding’s second statement is self-contradictory.

      1. You cannot fall from grace unless you have been in grace.
      2. They had fallen from grace because they became legalistic.
      3. Therefore, they had been in grace before they became legalistic.

        Yet Maulding concludes just the opposite: that they could not fall from grace even if they became legalistic.
Here, Woodward is superior insofar as he goes: “The issue is what erring believers would fall into if they fell from
grace. They would not fall into damnation, but into legalism” (emphasis his.)213 For that matter, Woodward is even
superior to Seymour in this instance! Seymour allows a fall-into-legalism as a possible interpretation but prefers to
think that those who had fallen from grace were counterfeit believers who had never been in grace.214 Nope.
        Given the state of confusion in the integration of material from originations such as GES, GLI, and GFI, Sam
and Sue’s request for clarification is understandable. Therefore, I have undertaken to provide some assistance in
this capacity in the present discourse. My impression, from what limited interaction I have had with GLI and GFI,
is that they risk misthological exclusion. To the degree they fail to integrate sound soteriology and sound misthology
with their nikology, they risk only achieving present victory and missing out on misthological victory. I would urge
them not to follow Achan’s example. Rather, I would encourage those involved in these originations to do their
homework and work diligently in these areas, even if these areas are outside of their specialties, to be approved
workmen who do not need to be ashamed, accurately interpreting the word of truth (2Tim 2:15). I too will have
further homework to do in nikology, which is not my area of expertise.
        Solomon repeatedly stresses: “The problem was that I was doing in order to be because I didn’t know how
to be in order to do!”215 (emphasis his). The logic is reasonable: BE then DO. We must know what we are before
we can be what we are. Ontology must proceed nikology. In TOD, I expressed that truth with a Be-What-You-Are
list. Knowing who you are must proceed being who you are. But that is not the endgame. Solomon and I have only
expressed the first part of the equation with our respective articulations of this truth. The rest of the equation would
go on to add that we need to perform according to what we are so that we may become what we were meant to be.
The full sequence is: BE-DO-BE.

      1. BE: Ontologically, you are an overcomer. This defines who you are.
      2. DO: Experientially, overcome. This refines who you are.
      3. BE: Misthologically, be an overcomer. This reveals who you are.

You are an overcomer ontologically, so overcome nikologically, so that you can be an overcomer misthologically.
You are approved in Christ soteriologically, so work to be approved by Christ temporally, so that you may be
approved by Christ misthologically. Soteriologically, you are God’s workmanship, so work nikologically, so that
you may be His misthologically approved workmanship: a golden vessel rather than a mere wooden vessel. If we

live by the Spirit, as we practice exchanged-life teaching with misthological objectives built upon FG soteriology,

                                                      Conclusion
       I love the song by Hawk Nelson, Live Like You’re Loved. From my perspective, I understand the song as
ruling out legalistic perfection as being energy wasting. In contrast, the exchanged-life recognition that we are loved
allows us to be loving and live like we are loved and know that we are valuable. Nevertheless, misthologically, I
know that we can increase our love and value beyond anything we can imagine if we will live like we are loved and
valuable even now. The endgame is not merely to live like you are loved and valuable but to increase your love and
value by living like you are loved and valuable:
                                         You’re not the only one who feels like this
                                          Feelin’ like you lose more than you win
                                          Like life is just an endless hill you climb
                                              You try and try, but never arrive
                                                 I’m tellin’ you somethin’
                                                 This racing, this running
                                             Oh, you’re working way too hard!
                                             And this perfection you’re chasing
                                                   Is just energy wasted
                                             Cause He loves you like you are!
                                         So go ahead and live like you’re loved
                                         It’s okay to act like you’ve been set free
                                         His love has made you more than enough
                                        So go ahead and be who He made you to be
                                                 And live like you’re loved
                                                   live like you’re loved
                                                   live like you’re loved
                                                   live like you’re loved
                                         And live like you know you’re valuable
                                        Like you know the one that holds your soul
                                        Cause mercy has called you by your name
                                            Don’t be afraid to live in that grace
       Jesus’ human spirit became even more lovable and valuable because of His sacrifice. Jesus purposefully
became one of us, so that He could develop a misthologically approved human spirit. Enduring suffering was the
only way He could do so. His doing so was conditioned on His misthological conditioning. We likewise must go
through our trials and submit to the Lord’s misthological conditioning of our spirit if we are to meet the conditions
necessary to develop a misthologically beautiful spirit. For this reason, we can say, “Thank You, Lord,” for our
battles against Sin and Satan. These battles make us stronger in spirit and more beautiful in spirit as a result.
       By believing in Jesus for the free gift of eternal life, we get a new sinless spirit via the act of regeneration.
At that point, we are spiritual babies. Our spirit is immature, though ontologically sinless and incapable of sinning.
By living for Jesus and growing strong in spirit, we develop an enhanced spirit that is exceedingly more valuable.
The Lord paid the price not only for our redemption but also for our co-glorification. Nevertheless, we must meet
the conditions in order to receive the benefits of His payment. The Lord has not only earned the right to give us the
gift dimension of eternal life, but He also has earned the right to give us the reward dimension of eternal life. The
gift dimension of eternal life is imparted permanently to our spirit in the punctiliar moment we are born again. The
Chapter 8. Nikology-Misthology 101                                                                          Page 153
reward dimension of eternal life is imparted to our spirit in a linear process, over time, as we allow the Holy Spirit
to infuse our spirit with misthological aspects of eternal life. It takes time to grow such a priceless misthological
pearl. Nevertheless, qualifying as a bridal saint need not take forever. The Holy Spirit has given us a completed
canon. He also has given us numerous gifted teachers. If we avail ourselves of the resources the Spirit has provided,
we can grow very rapidly. The pearls of wisdom and knowledge that Solomon has provided via the Spirit can be
added to the pearls of wisdom and knowledge that other teachers also provide, thereby enabling us to make our
pearls all the more beautiful and valuable, all the more quickly.
       For our valuable beauty to be enough to justify Jesus’ death and Hell, it must be mind-blowing indeed. As
discussed in BRH, He who is infinite suffered finitely so that we who are finite might not suffer infinitely. When
that discussion is expanded to the sphere of misthology, we learn much more. He who is infinite suffered finitely
as a human being so that we who are human beings might become as infinitely loved and valuable as He is as a
divine human being. It is not good that man be alone. Man needs a helpmate, a soulmate. In becoming the perfect
Man, Jesus subjected Himself to need us as His perfect soulmate. It is not good for Jesus to be alone in terms of
having divine companions who share His divine-human nature.216
       As noted in MMP4, the Bible talks about the spirits of righteous men being made misthologically perfect
(Heb 12:23). Their spirits are not only misthologically perfect in the sense of the rewards they have received but
also in terms of the reward they have become.217 They have become Jesus’ reward. Misthologically, we want to be
holistically blameless—in body, soul, and spirit—at the Bema (1Thess 5:23) so that we might qualify to be Christ’s
Bride (Eph 5:27). If we want to be His soulmate, we must grow strong and valuable in our spirits. To be worthy of
His infinite love, we must be infinitely lovable and valuable. Can we, by our finite fleshly efforts, make ourselves
this beautiful? Of course, not! Yet, if nikologically, polemologically, and misthologically we are practicing
exchanged-life trichotomy within a sphere of FG soteriology, then we can be completely confident that the Spirit is
making us into a priceless pearl—so breathtakingly priceless and beautiful that it will justify the Cross and Hell.
The Spirit can do what we cannot—make us infinitely loveable and valuable. Believe it. Live like it. Sing like it.
Then began to feel like it.
216
   See “A Matter of Necessity Addendum” in 3DUS.
217
   Soteriological perfection is granted permanently and statically the moment we believe (note the perfect tense in
Heb 10:14; see FFG). Our spirits are ontologically sinless from that point onward (Jam 1:17-18; 1Jn 3:9). Temporal
perfection pertains to developing spiritual character traits. Our faith can be perfected experientially in the present
(Jam 2:22; Heb 12:2). Our love likewise can be dynamically perfected (1Jn 2:5; 4:12,17-18). Our sinless spirits
become increasingly mature in faith and love as we successfully undergo various trials. At the time of death,
however, our spiritual capacity for misthological maturity will become static, confirmed in that state (1Pet 5:10).
Similarly, Jesus’ sinless human spirit could not sin, but His sinless spirit nevertheless underwent a process of
perfection during His life (Heb 2:10) that became misthologically static at the point of death (Heb 5:9; 7:28).
       By way of analogy, consider this misthological perfection like buying stock in a company. As you mature
nikologically, your spirit is becoming more valuable misthologically. You are acquiring more stock, so to speak.
At the point of death, your opportunity to buy more stock ceases. You must purchase all the stock you can during
your earthly life, thus laying up intrinsic treasure in yourself and for yourself in Heaven. The value of your stock
will increase throughout eternity future. But the amount of stock you own is set by the degree of internal
misthological perfection you presently attain during your life on Earth.
       By way of theory, we might hypothesize that those believers who die below the misthological age of
accountability may be able to inherit low positions of reward in the Heavenly City. They will have an external
reward graciously bestowed on them that exceeds what would have been anticipated otherwise due to the
comparative underdeveloped beauty of their spirit. Conversely, those believers who excel nikologically but fail
misthologically might have a greater internal beauty but fail to qualify for the inheritance. Their external reward
(namely the outer darkness) would fall far below the expectations that would have been anticipated otherwise,
considering the comparative well-developed beauty of their spirit. Alternately, a poor showing at the Bema might
devalue even the potential value of their spirit. In other words, the amount of stock they own might be reduced. Or,
to use yet another analogy, the nikological groves cut into their spirits may not be filled with gold at the Bema if
they fail to pass the test misthologically. Their potential for having misthological beautiful spirits may not be
realized if they do not pass the test. Thus, I am posing that (1) there may be a discrepancy between inner and outer
beauty (like a comparatively unattractive person being clothed in beautiful clothing or a beautiful person in poor
clothing), or (2) a diminished inner beauty, or (3) an unrealize potential beauty.
Page 154                                                                             Degrees of Love and Forgiveness
                                           Illustration 40. Loved in Christ




       Contrary to the song, Two Out of Three Ain’t Bad, Jesus wants us; He needs us; there ain’t no way, He’ll
ever stop loving us. He can’t live without us.218 He endured the Cross to have us. There ain’t no way that the Devil
is going to keep Him from having us (Rom 8:31-39). This statement is true both soteriologically and
misthologically. Be energized by the realization of Christ’s love so that you can overwhelmingly conquer whatever
doubts and obstacles that get in your way to having a full realization. Meditate on these truths. Allow the Holy Spirit
who lives within you to steamroll over your devilish doubts until they beg for mercy. Then, show them no mercy.
Flatten them. Allow Jesus to fight for you (Ex 14:14; Dt 3:22; 20:4; Josh 23:3,10) and through you (2Cor 12:9;
Eph 3:16-20; Phil 4:13; Col 1:10) to vanquish your doubts (Mk 9:24). Do not fear (Ex 14:13; Dt 3:22; 31:8;
Josh 10:25). Be strong and courageous (Dt 31:6; Josh 1:9; Heb 13:5-6).
                                    Illustration 41. Greater Is He Who Is in You




      Soteriological assurance, nikological assurance, polemological assurance, and misthological assurance are
your birthright. Don’t let the Devil rob you of your birthright. Fight back. Knock him flat on his back through the
power of Him who dwells within you (1Jn 4:4). You were born to fight, so fight. Don’t give into your emotions.
Make your emotions give in to you.


218
   God does not need us to survive, of course (Ps 50:9-12). Nor does He need us in order to be perfectly happy—
within the confines of the Trinity. He can live perfectly happy without us within the limitations of that self-contained
sphere. (See 3DUS and MMP3.) However, to maximize reciprocal love with someone who has His same nature in
a partial overlap of that sphere, God needs a Bride. To the fullest extent that it is possible for the creature to become
one spirit with the Creator, God is seeking to become “one spirit” with us (1Cor 6:17). When we join ourselves to
Him soteriologically, we are ontologically joined to Him via regeneration. But, as in earthly marriage, as we get to
know our spouse better over the years, our unity can grow experientially. Experientially partaking of the Spirit and
Scripture under the pressure and heat of trials transforms our spirits from coal into diamonds, so to speak.
                             Excursus: Follow-Up Meeting
                                                Introduction
       Dianne and I met with Sam and Sue and our Sunday School teacher in a follow-up meeting on a Wednesday
evening on 4/24/2019 in our Sunday School Class. I had a primary rough draft of this book printed by then, which
I had given to our Sunday School teacher on Sunday a few days previously. The allegations that Sue made during
the meeting were nothing new. She said that I had called her names, was insensitive to her emotions, and had
mocked her.
                                                       Names
        I asked her, “What names?” I then acknowledged that I had called her carnal and immature but added that I
would stand by that assessment. She responded that I had also said that she was too sensitive. I stand by that
assessment as well. When the teacher asked about what she had said about us, she said that she did not recall what
she had said in the six-page letter. The teacher summarized it as being a slanderous, disrespectful attack against me
and my wife. I pointed out to Sue that my responses to her after that point in time were a response to her six-page
attack and that I would punch back when someone attacks us, especially when they attack my wife. My attitude
when we meet with Sue after she sent us that letter was, The buck stops here. I would no longer tolerate such attacks.
Sue said that she did not recall attacking Dianne. Sam quickly pointed out that even before that six-page letter, Sue
had called Dianne a snake. Unfortunately, Dianne had declined to let me send the teacher copies of the texts that
Sue had sent to Dianne before that meeting.219 Dianne was just relieved to have Sue attacking me rather than
attacking her after our meeting with Sue that Saturday evening (1/19/2019). Dianne wanted me to know how she
felt, being on the receiving end of Sue’s attacks. I was fine with that. In fact, part of my intent, when we meet with
Sue and Sam that Saturday evening after Sue had sent us that six-page letter, was to redirect Sue’s attack from
Dianne to me. I was not going to tolerate her attacking Dianne any further. Now that Sue’s attack had been shifted
from being directed against Dianne to include me also via that letter, I was free to fight back, not only on my behalf
but also on Dianne’s behalf. Sue had called my wife a snake. In response to this and other factors, I called Sue
carnal and immature. Who is really calling whom names? I stand by my assessment that Sue is carnal and immature.
In fact, I will expand upon that assessment at the end of this excursus.220 Sue conveniently forgot at the follow-up
meeting that she had called my wife a snake before I had responded that Sue was carnal and immature. Sue failed
to acknowledge the cause-and-effect relationship between such statements. Because of her attacks against us and
especially against Dianne, I was no longer going to tolerate Sue’s carnality and immaturity as excuses for her
attacks. When someone unjustly attacks me, and especially my wife, I punch back, hard, as attested in this book.
And I make no apology for doing so, and I am certainly not offering any confession of wrongdoing for doing so.
                                            Apology Versus Confession

for having done so and for having sent us the letter. I pointed out that this was not the case and had repeatedly told
her that I was working on this book to explain why. For example, when she had kept sending me those badgering
texts the following Monday, I kept responding simply that I would respond to her allegations in my writings. When
she came to our home that evening and demanded an apology from me, I refused to give it and told her that I would
further explain why in this book. Yet, in the 4/24/2019 follow-up meeting that Wednesday evening with the Sunday
School teacher, Sue said she thought everything was ok between us after she came over to our house that Monday
night back on 1/21/2019 because we had all discussed the situation that night and had concluded by hugging. During
the follow-up meeting, however, I reminded her that, even though we had hugged on that Monday night, I had told

her that very night that the issue was unresolved and that explaining why would require a lengthy explanation which

        At the conclusion of the follow-up meeting, the teacher offered to give his copy of my rough draft of this
book to Sam, but I told him to hold off because, as a result of the meeting, it was clear that I needed to add further
clarification as to the difference between an apology and confession and explain why Sue’s apology did not resolve
the issue. Therefore, I am inserting that explanation within the book here, in the form of this excursus, and will
begin by quoting from Adams’ section entitled, “Don’t Apologize.” He commences that discussion by saying,

             It is time to say it clearly—so that no one may misunderstand: the Bible nowhere advises
      or allows (and certainly doesn’t command) apology.
             Yet, in spite of this fact, Christians (and even Christian counselors) somehow seem to be
      addicted to apologizing and advising counselees to “go apologize” to others whom they have
      wronged. To all such, I have one piece of advice: Stop it!
             “Well, what on earth is wrong with apologies?”
             Fundamentally, two things.
                                                               I
             An apology is an inadequate, humanistic substitute for the real thing. ‘Nowhere do the
      Scriptures require, or even encourage, apologizing. To say “I’m sorry” is a human dodge for doing
      what God has commanded. And (as we shall see) since it is man’s substitute for God’s requirement
      (and has all but replaced that requirement), it has caused a great number of problems in the church. By
      replacing the biblical requirement for dealing with estrangement, it has allowed estrangement in the
      church to continue unchecked, “What is this biblical requirement that has been replaced?” Forgiveness.
             “Forgiveness?”
             Yes. I shall not now develop this point by discussing the numerous passages that speak of
      Christian forgiveness. Instead. I shall simply refer you to other treatments of the subject.
             Thus, confession is always linked with seeking forgiveness. This is what all the great prayers of
      confession do. Apologizing breaks that link.
             As long as Christians continue to say to those they have wronged, “I’m sorry” (or words
      to that effect), instead of “I sinned; will you forgive me?,” and as long as they receive the natural
      response, “Oh, that’s all right” (or something similar), the real solutions to the many difficulties
      that could have been reached through forgiveness will continue to be by-passed. The church will
      labor under the burden of resentments and bitterness on the part of its members.
             “Why do you say that?” you may wonder. Let me explain; and that explanation leads to a second
      point—apology is wrong, not only because it is man’s inadequate substitute for God’s revealed
      method of righting sour interpersonal relationships, but (as such),
                                                               II
             apology elicits an inadequate response. When one asks, “Will you forgive me?” he has
      punted; the ball bas changed hands, and a response is now required of the one addressed. The
      onus of responsibility has shifted from the one who did the wrong to the one who was wronged.
      Both parties, therefore, are required to put the matter in the past. And, the proper response (Luke 17:3)
      is, “Yes, I will.” Like God’s forgiveness (“Your sins and iniquities I will remember against you no
      more”), human forgiveness is a promise that is made and kept.
             When one person says, “1 forgive you.” to another, he promises:

                 1. “I’ll not bring this matter up to you again;
                 2. “I’ll not bring it up to others;
                 3. “I’ll not bring it up to myself (i.e., dwell on it in my mind).”

            The response, “Yes, I’ll forgive you,” then, is a promise that entails quite a commitment—one
      to which the forgiven brother (and God) may hold him, and one that (if kept) will lead to forgetting
      the wrong (not forgive and forget, but forgive to forget) and reestablishing a new, good relationship
      between the parties involved. So, an apology is an inadequate substitute because (a) it asks for no such
      commitment, and (b) gets none.
            An apology keeps the ball in one’s own possession. The other party is required to do nothing
Excursus: Follow-Up Meeting                                                                                  Page 157
      about it (and usually doesn’t). To say “I’m sorry” is, you see, nothing more than an expression of
      one’s own feelings. To say, ‘I have wronged you,” and then to ask, “Will you forgive, me?” is
      quite another thing.
             Therefore, counselors (in advising counselees) must be quite clear: about this matter. When they
      are, and when a proper understanding of this matter once again begins to permeate the Christian
      church, many of the current difficulties we are experiencing will disappear.
             Let’s do our part in hastening that day. (Theology, 221-223; italics his)

       As Adams will go on to reiterate, when sin is involved, it requires confession and forgiveness. “The object
isn’t merely to express your feelings, even regret (p. 225). An apology is merely an expression of emotion, stating
how one feels. But “forgiveness is not a feeling....forgiveness is a promise” (emphasis his, 228). Forgiveness cannot
be granted in the absence of the offender’s repentance and confession. What about cases where a person refuses to
repent? Adams answers, “You may not sit around and wallow in self-pity or become bitter” (p. 229). Nor do you
grant verbal forgiveness to the offender, rather you practice Mk 11:25 by granting internal forgiveness before God:

             Does his failure to repent give you the right to become resentful? No, that is no license for self-
      pity. The words in Mark 11 apply to the third element in forgiveness—precisely this matter of self-
      pity. You must empty the matter from your heart in prayer; you may not dwell on it— you have given
      it to God. Note, especially, you do this before God, not before the offender. You must tell God of your
      willingness to grant him forgiveness, that you want to be reconciled and that you won’t sit and brood
      about how you were wronged. In that sense—and that sense alone—you forgive him: before God. But
      Mark 11 doesn't require you to grant forgiveness to the offender; the passage speaks only about one’s
      own personal attitude. You are not making a formal promise to the offender when you tell God
      you won't be bitter toward him. This passage speaks of forgiveness of the heart. Forgiveness of the
      heart is essential in all cases. The heart and the promise on the lips must conform. The only
      commitment in Mark 11 is a commitment to God; none is made to the offender. It is forgiveness before
      God. At every point, a matter is set to rest as quickly as possible, where it can be. According to Mark
      11:25, one person can resolve at least one part of the problem right away. Counselors must call [i.e.,
      require] them to do so. (Theology, 230; italics his.)

       Confession is an admission of moral guilt. An apology is not. Confession is an acknowledgment that you
have sinfully wronged the other person. An apology is not. Confession puts the ball in the other person’s court. An
apology does not. Confession is made for the purpose of eliciting the other person’s forgiveness. An apology is not.
Therefore, if the offender only offers an apology, all the offended party can offer is heart forgiveness, not formal
forgiveness. Yet the offended party must grant heart forgiveness regardless as to whether an apology is offered.
Thus, heart forgiveness cannot be attributed to the apology. Consequently, the apology accomplishes very little,
frequently amounting to little more than temporary emotional catharsis, and is wrong when used as a substitute for
those occasions where a moral offense has been committed. Sin must be dealt with as sin. An apology is a coverup,
not a confession.
                                  Heart Forgiveness versus Formal Forgiveness
       Sue’s recollection in the follow-up meeting was that she had apologized for writing the six-page letter, and
she had presumed that everything was ok afterward because we hugged and got along relatively well afterward. My
perspective was quite different. When Dianne and I hugged Sue afterward, it was my way of practicing what Adams
calls heart forgiveness rather than formal forgiveness. Or, to use my terminology, I was practicing level-one
forgiveness. I was not granting level-two forgiveness. If I had granted level-two forgiveness, there would have been
no need for me to proceed with writing this book.221 Biblically, however, I could not grant level-two forgiveness

221
    Adams perceives two levels of forgiveness. I perceive at least four. One can grant level-two forgiveness and still,
if necessary, withhold level-three and level-four forgiveness. Level two allows you to release the offender from
bringing up the matter in terms of fellowship. It does not necessarily require that level-three and level-four
forgiveness be granted or that the offense be forgotten. In fact, in some cases, it would be morally wrong to grant
level-three and level-four forgiveness just because level-two forgiveness has been granted. Just as heart forgiveness
is insufficient to justify formal forgiveness, so level-two forgiveness may be insufficient to justify level-three and
Page 158                                                                            Degrees of Love and Forgiveness
because of Sue’s unconfessed sins; namely, she had not acknowledged wrongdoing and asked for our forgiveness.
What Sue sought, more so, was our understanding: for us to understand why she is so angry with us and to excuse
her for it since she was sorry for having to write in this manner. Telling us that she was sorry for having written the
letter or offering some other form of apology does not constitute confession nor give grounds for formal forgiveness.
For this reason, I repeatedly reminded Sue and Sam that I was still working on this book, even after Sue came over
that Monday night. For example, when we went out to dinner to a Mexican restaurant thereafter and had an
enjoyable meal together, I still reminded them that I was working on this book to address the issue.
        Because the matter was unresolved and I was working on this book day and night, my attitude toward Sue
that subsequent Sunday morning (2/10/2019) was still being influenced by her six-page, slanderous letter. She
considered that letter to be water under the bridge. I considered it water over the bridge. The bridge to level-two
forgiveness and fellowship was blocked by her lack of repentance and her lack of confession of wrongdoing. During
the meeting, she described the tone of my response to her that Sunday morning as having a chip on my shoulder.
Her statement is an adequate description of my attitude that morning. But if I had a chip, she had a boulder on her
shoulder, and it came crashing down. Once again, the matter had never been truly resolved. Not for me; not for her.
And Adams gives a good explanation as to why that is the case. Heart forgiveness falls way short of formal
forgiveness. Level-one forgiveness is far below level-two forgiveness.
                                                   Volitional Caring
       At the conclusion of the follow-up meeting, the teacher asked if Sue would acknowledge wrongdoing in the
disrespect that she had shown to me and Dianne in her letter. She would not because she said she could not recall
the details of the letter. He asked if she would acknowledge wrongdoing in calling me uncaring. She would not. At
this point, I interjected, on Sue’s behalf that, given her perspective, it would not be possible for her to do so. She
could not confess to something as a sin that she did not believe to be sin. For that matter, neither could I. She
believed that I had sinned against her in mocking her. I responded that my mocking, if we are to call it that, is not
necessarily a sin. Whether it was a sin would have to be determined by the context in which it was spoken.222 But

level-four forgiveness. Level-three forgiveness would entail releasing the offender from short term consequences.
Level four entails releasing the offender from long term consequences. For example, a local church had a secretary
who embezzled a large portion of their building fund. They should forgive her at level one even if she does not
repent. They should forgive her at level two if she does repent. However, even if they forgave her at level two, they
still had to decide whether to forgive her at level three or fire her. They also faced an additional decision as to
whether to forgive her at level four or turn her over to the authorities for prosecution and imprisonment. Or consider
the case of a child molester. Even if the molester repents and is forgiven at a level two, would one be wise to forgive
him at level three and continue to trust him with children? Certainly, one would not be required to do so. In certain
cases, it may be a sin to do so. Or consider the case of adultery. Confession results in level-two forgiveness. But the
offended party has the liberty to decide whether to grant level-three and level-four forgiveness. Likewise, if the
adultery is repeated even after full forgiveness has been granted at all levels, the offended party is at liberty to
overturn the previous forgiveness and factor his or her present forgiveness on the fact that this a repeated offense.
These are the types of practical ramifications that one may draw from 4DF.

    Another factor of the context that Sue failed to bring up in the follow-up meeting is that my mocking statement
on the return car trip was a defensive statement after she had spent forty-five minutes on an angry offensive. Her
weaponized hurt-feelings were operating at full steam. See Sunday School Car Trip, 109. During the follow-up
meeting, she made several unwarranted jumps in logic. For example, two major assumptions that she made in her
accusations against me were: (1) Since I did not care about hurting her feelings that Sunday morning, when I told
her that some of her questions would be better addressed outside of the Sunday School class, I did not care about
her. (2) Since I did not care about hurting her feelings, I did not care about hurting people’s feelings.
       However, during the follow-up meeting I pointed out that she was making an unwarranted generalization.
Just because I did not care about her hurt feelings on that occasion does not prove that I would be unconcerned
about hurting anyone’s feelings on any occasion. During the follow-up meeting, she said that what upset her was
the tone of my voice and my indifference to her hurt feelings. However, my tone and indifference are attributable
to numerous factors that would not be in play under normal circumstances. She was attempting to use her hurt
feelings as a powerplay to force acquisition to her demands and was frustrated because I would not play along. In
hindsight, I only wished that a I had made a recording of the conversation so that my statements and tone might be
matched against her statements and tone. Truly, mine would have been a molehill compared to her mountain.

that explanation would require a book—hence this book. By the same token, she believed that she was justified in
