       Additionally, this book format allows me to document and express these matters in a manner that the teacher,
Sam, and Dianne can verify as being an accurate representation of the facts. Dianne has read the book. So has the
teacher. And I will be giving a copy to Sam. To be sure, there are things that they would have done differently than
I did. But allowance for variables in counseling approaches has been made herein. The factual data, in any case,
remains verifiable. As to differences in approaches, the teacher asked Dianne if Sue would have to do anything for
fellowship to be restored as far as Dianne was concerned. Dianne said, “No.” Dianne had no demands. When he
asked me, I responded, “Yes.” I had several demands. Namely, Sue would have to repent and confess her sins
documented in this book. However, I do not believe this is possible unless Sue reads the book because she would
first have to be convinced that her actions were sinful and that mere apologies are insufficient in such cases. The
teacher was, understandably, disappointed that the follow-up meeting was unable to resolve the issue. However, I
was not expecting it to resolve the issue. Given the complexity of the situation, my belief was that true resolution
would require a full explanation, which is why I wrote this book.
       The bottom line is that since Sue equates affection with caring, if I am not being affectionate in what I am
saying, then, in her opinion, I am being unloving and uncaring. Therefore, she said that she would not read this
book, regardless of what I say, because she says that I do not care. In other words, I am not showing the affection
she believes is necessary to constitute love and caring. If loving and caring are linked ball-and-chain to affection,
then she has a valid point. However, the thesis of my book is that love and caring are not equatable with affection.
Therefore, her criticizing me as being unloving and uncaring is false, and her rejecting this book as failing to be a
form of love and caring is groundless. Sometimes the caring thing to do is to confront rather than forgive, as
Augsburg has acknowledged in the title of two of his books.
                                             Time and Place for Emotions
        At this follow-up meeting, Sue also criticized me of being emotionally indifferent. As noted in the book,
according to Ecc 3:1-8, there is a time and place for everything, including a time to kill versus heal, tear down
versus build up, throw stones versus gather stones, embrace versus shun embracing, tear apart versus mend, speak
versus be silent, love versus hate, and a time for war versus a time for peace. To this list, one may biblically add,
there is a time to care about emotions versus a time not to care about emotions. When would be a time to care about
emotions? Obviously, at the Bema will be a time when emotions are very important. Our emotions will correspond
completely to reality at that point. The Bible warns us that we may experience some very negative emotions at that
time if we are not prepared for the event, such as: shame, sorrow, and regret. I care very much about what emotions
I will experience at the Bema. A large part of what I do is to prepare for that event so that I can experience positive
emotions instead. Emotions matter—but not always. At the Bema is a time and place where emotions will matter
very much. In contrast, there are many times and places where emotions do not matter. As I type this sentence, I
am emotionally neutral. It does not matter. I don’t care about my lack of emotions at this point.
        In a nouthetic counseling session, in which I am confronting a counselee’s neurotic emotions, is another time
when I do not care about emotions. I am not going to let neurotic emotions dictate the session. Another time when
I do not care about emotions is when confronting self-pity. I am not going to concede to either my emotions of self-
pity or to someone else’s protracted emotions of self-pity. A statement such as, “I don’t care about your emotions,”
must be understood in the context in which the statement is made. As I pointed out at the follow-up meeting, to say,
“I don’t care your emotions,” negates the present tense of the verb in the present circumstances in which that
statement is made. This negation cannot be taken to mean, “I never care about emotions.” Ecclesiastes and common
sense dictate that there is a time and place to care about hurting someone’s feelings, and a time and place not to care
about hurting someone’s feelings. When someone is using emotions wrongly, that is, to justify unbiblical attitudes
or actions, then one must not shrink back from confronting those emotions. That would be the wrong time to care
about emotions. As noted, Dobson stated that crying can be a “weapon. It becomes a tool of protest to punish the
enemy” (p. 52). Sue had repeatedly weaponized her hurt feelings, using them as justification for her angry assaults.
                                               Flushing Down the Toilet
       That night when Dianne and I got home from the follow-up meeting, I went to the bathroom and found a bug
in the toilet. “That is unusual,” I thought, “for a bug to put itself in the toilet.” Normally, when I find a bug, I have

Comparatively, she was attempting to take a speck out of my eye without acknowledging the log in her own eye.
Page 160                                                                               Degrees of Love and Forgiveness
to bring it to the toilet to flush it. I don’t recall that happening before, where the bug placed itself in the toilet. As I
reached for the lever to flush it down the toilet, in my mind I said to the bug, “I’m sorry.” The realization hit me
like a flash, as my hand paused on the lever, that my thinking I was sorry was a weird thought. I don’t normally feel
sorry for flushing bugs down the toilet. Quite to the contrary, I feel some small degree of satisfaction in getting rid
of the bug that has invaded our home. Why did I have such a weird thought on this occasion? As my hand paused
momentarily, I realized that if I came home the next night and found a bug in the toilet, I would simply flush it
again. Obviously, my saying mentally to the bug, “I’m sorry,” was not a confession of sin, not a confession that I
was doing anything wrong, not even, “Sorry; I won’t do this again.” It was nothing more than a fleeting, irrational,
emotional sentiment. And it dawned on me, as I flushed the bug, that this is how Sue is treating me and Dianne. In
effect, Sue is saying, “I am sorry...flush.” We are the bugs she keeps flushing down the toilet with an attitude of,
“I’m sorry...but I’ll do it again as the occasion, or more specifically, my emotions, require it.” This repetitive pattern
might be simplistically summarized as follows:

       “Dianne, you are a bug....”
       “I’m sorry I called you a bug.”
       FLUSH.
        “Dianne, you are a bug....”
       “I’m sorry I called you a bug.”
       FLUSH.
       “Dianne, you are a bug....”
       “I’m sorry I called you a bug.”
       FLUSH.
        “Dianne, you are a bug....”
       “I’m sorry I called you a bug.”
       FLUSH.
        “Dianne, you are a bug....”
       “I’m sorry I called you a bug.”
       FLUSH.
       “Dianne and Marty, you are bugs....”
       “I’m sorry I called you bugs.”
       FLUSH.
       “Oh no! The toilet is stopped up!”

       In my opinion, the Lord used that bug and thought process to provide a graphic illustration of the situation.
Up until Sue sent that six-page letter, she repeatedly had been able to use her vent-apology-vent-apology-vent-
apology routine, thinking things were fine, when, in fact, they were not. She was using her vent-apology cycle as
an emotional catharsis. Perhaps this is the only recourse she knows. In any event, as we have seen, Solomon,
Dobson, and Adams are opposed to using venting as an emotional catharsis. And as Adams correctly has pointed
out, offering an apology is an “inadequate, humanistic substitute for the real thing.” I broke Sue’s flushing routine
by stopping up her toilet, so to speak. She could no longer flush us down the toilet. I put a stop to it in the manner
prescribed by Solomon, Dobson, and Adams.
       As already noted herein, Solomon says that neurotics must take responsibility for their actions: “The
individual has total responsibility for the sins he commits. He is also responsible for permitting these damaged
emotions to continue uncontrolled.” Dobson says, “The best approach is to force a crisis that will bring the matter
to a head....precipitate a crisis of major proportions.” Adams concurs: “In such cases, it may be necessary to
precipitate some sort of responsibility crisis which has been pending but which the client has avoided until now.”
Some might consider the crisis-precipitating response herein to be making a mountain out of a molehill. However,
responsible counseling requires precipitating a responsibility crisis of major proportions when necessary. This was
the only way to stop Sue from her pattern of flushing us down the toilet like bugs. You can’t flush a mountain down
the toilet. Nor, for that matter, can you flush a full-grown alligator who can snap back.
Excursus: Follow-Up Meeting                                                                                   Page 161
                                        Illustration 42. No More Flushing!




        The urban legend of people flushing baby alligators down the toilet, who then survive and thrive, serves to
illustrate the point. You might be able to flush baby alligators, but you are not going to be able to flush a full-grown
alligator that can resist your flushing attempts. Sue considers my actions abusive. Actually, her actions are offensive
and mine are defensive. She wants the freedom to give a punch, but she cries foul if someone punches back. She
considers it abusive if someone punches back after she has provoked a counterjab to her offensive, self-destructive,
self-sabotaging foray. I may not be able to put an end to her sabotage cycle,223 only she can do that, but I can put an
end to her flushing cycle, and I am certainly doing that, with the only way possible—punch back!
                                                   Spiritual Abuse
       At the follow-up meeting, Sue said that she was reading a book describing spiritual abuse and said that I was
being spiritually abusive. I have thoroughly documented the matter and explained my rationale herein. Make up
your own mind. To put the matter colloquially, Sue needs to read a book that will foster her victim mentality like I
need another hole in my head. To put the matter factually, I would counterchallenge that she is actually the one who
is being abusive with her venting and tongue lashing and then getting upset when someone says, “Enough.” Plus,
she is being evasive with her apologies.
       The primary repetitive issue is that Sue keeps committing basically the same sin against us and thinking that
her apologies get her off the hook. They do not. Additionally, Sue has been repeatedly abusive toward Dianne, yet
Sue does not charge Dianne with being spiritually abusive in return. Why not? I would perceive one reason to be
that since Dianne is willing to allow Sue to abuse her, Sue has not found grounds, yet, to accuse Dianne of being
spiritually abusive. Even at the meeting, even though the teacher presented clear evidence that Sue had treated
Dianne disrespectfully in Sue’s slanderous letter and even though Sam testified that Sue had called Dianne a snake,
when the teacher asked Dianne what she required for fellowship to be restored with Sue, Dianne said that she
required nothing. Dianne was willing to accept Sue unconditionally with open arms. In other words, Dianne was
willing to allow Sue to continue to abuse her if that is what it takes to have fellowship with Sue. However, I am not

                                            Classifications of Carnality
      Since Sue wrote her six-page letter demanding our counsel and then came to our house and accused me and
Dianne of being unloving and uncaring since we were not providing her with counsel that she demanded via her
venting, I will end this excursus by providing the requested counsel via a summation.
                                        Illustration 43. Types of Carnality




       Some carnal believers are antinomian. They are not necessarily immoral in their lifestyle (though some are),
but they do not believe that they are accountable to God for their actions. For instance, some FG teachers deny that
God punishes our sins and deny any negative results at the Bema or deny permanent negative results flowing from
the Bema. Thankfully, Sue does not fall into this category.
Excursus: Follow-Up Meeting                                                                                   Page 163
       Many carnal believers are simply immature, and many of these immature believers have not developed
spiritually even though they have had plenty of time to do so. They still do not walk by the Spirit. Many of the
believers addressed by Paul in 1 Corinthians would fall into this category, as well as those addressed by the author
of Hebrews. They are professionally immature. Sue falls into this protracted-immaturity category.
       Many carnal believers are legalistic. They tend to focus on performance and live by a list of rules. Some of
these believers are immature. They may be new believers, for example. On the other hand, many of them are
professional legalists. They have been studying the Bible for years and are diligently severing the Lord. Pastors and
church leaders and dedicated church members and Sunday School teachers frequently fall into this category. But
they serve the Lord out of fleshly effort rather than relying on the power of the Spirit. Sue also falls into this
professional-legalistic category.
       New believers and young believers are carnal because of their immaturity, and some of them tend to be
weaker believers. They are very subject to stumbling in trying to figure out the fuzzy areas where the Bible allows
a range of freedom. They need further Bible instruction before they are free to wade into the deeper end of the pool
regarding permissible areas. Sue does not fall into this category.
       Some carnal believers are pseudo weaker believers, however. They tend to appeal to alleged weakness to
mask their legalism. Since they are legalists, this group is diagrammed as a subset of the legalistic category. They
are professional weaker believers who appeal to weakness to justify their legalism and to impose their will on others.
Sue falls into this professional-weak-believer category.
       Most of these professional weaker believers are mature legalists. They are mature in the sense of having a lot
of Bible knowledge and seeking to serve the Lord. Thus, most of the Venn diagram for pseudo weaker believers is
placed outside of the immature category. However, since Sue is somewhat of an anomaly, being immature in some
capacities while mature in others, she is pictured on the line between the immature and legalistic categories,
straddling the fence as being immature in some areas while mature in other areas.
       The term backslider normally refers to one of four situations: (1) Some believers mature spiritually and then
fall back into an antinomian lifestyle that is even worse than what it was originally. Peter describes such antinomian
backsliders in 2Pet 2:18-22. (2) Other believers leave a legalistic mindset, start running the race well, and then lapse
back into legalism. Paul deals with legalistic backsliders in Galatians. (3) Many believers do not fall back into such
overt forms of carnality. They just lose their first love, or become lukewarm, or become complacent, or get distracted
by other things, or hide their talent. Jesus describes these types of believers in Mt 13:22; 25:25; Mk 4:19; Rev 2:4-
5; 3:14-20. We might just label them as immature backsliders since they fall back into a state of immaturity. (4)
Other carnal believers abandon the faith. They lapse back into a state of unbelief. Various passages describe these
apostate backsliders (e.g., Lk 8:13; 1Tim 4:1; 2Tim 2:18; Heb 6:4-6). Some interpreters refer to them as
“unbelieving believers.” In terms of their born-again spirit, they can still be classified as believers even though at
the soulical level they no longer believe. The previous three categories of backsliders are represented by the overlaps
of those respective diagrams. The part lying outside the overlaps represents the fourth category.
       Earlier in her life, before her rejection by her dad, Sue had an uncomplicated walk with the Lord. Her
subsequent struggle with rejection syndrome resulted in her playing the part of the prodigal. Her struggle with the
rejection syndrome is causing her to have problems finding her way all the way back home into her Heavenly
Father’s arms. Therefore, she would have to be diagrammed as still being partially in a backslidden condition.
Thankfully, though, she is working on finding her way back home. Presently, though, placing her on the border as
being partially backslidden is necessary.
       The complexity of Sue’s situation is increased, of course, because it involves four categories of carnality—
protracted immaturity, mature legalism, professional weakness, and partial backsliddeness—plus a need to
understand four-levels of forgiveness, in addition to a need to discern the volitional-affectual and unconditional-
conditional duality nature of love and caring. Necessarily, since God’s forgiveness operates at four levels, our
forgiveness must operate at four levels, if we are to practice Eph 4:32, forgiving each other just as God forgives us.
Also, since God’s love is volitional-affectual and unconditional-conditional, our love should be also.
       Still, the need to deal with these multiple levels is by no means insurmountable or uncommon. In fact, they
are part of the fabric of our reality, a reality created by our Creator so that we can learn to be more like Him. When
functioning normally within a biblical paradigm, we typically operate like this without difficulty. For instance,
Dianne and I love each other with a volitional-affectual and unconditional-conditional duality that comes naturally.
We don’t think about it in such complicated terms. It is only when the car engine is not working properly that you
need to know how to run a diagnostic on it and take it apart and fix it as necessary. Otherwise, you just drive the
car without thinking about how it works. Because Sue’s engine is not working properly, I had to at least run a
Page 164                                                                            Degrees of Love and Forgiveness
diagnostic on it. After all, she demanded my counsel. Well, she got it.
       As noted by the diagnostic diagram, overlapping forms of carnality are not uncommon. Understanding the
nature of the problem, via the diagram, should provide a good diagnostic tool to assist with addressing the problem.
Providing a diagnosis is a part of rendering treatment. Paul did it for the legalistic Galatians. Hebrews does it for
the immature believers addressed in that epistle. Jesus does it for backsliders addressed in both the first book of the
NT and the last book of the NT. Sue calls this name calling. I call it rendering a biblical diagnosis, following the
example of Paul, Hebrews, and Jesus, and thereby providing biblical counsel, just like Jesus does:
      5
        I know thy works, that thou art neither cold nor hot: I would thou wert cold or hot. 16 So then because
      thou art lukewarm, and neither cold nor hot, I will spue thee out of my mouth. 17 Because thou sayest,
      I am rich, and increased with goods, and have need of nothing; and knowest not that thou art wretched,
      and miserable, and poor, and blind, and naked: 18 I counsel thee to buy of me gold tried in the fire,
      that thou mayest be rich; and white raiment, that thou mayest be clothed, and that the shame of thy
      nakedness do not appear; and anoint thine eyes with eyesalve, that thou mayest see. 19 As many as I
      love, I rebuke and chasten: be zealous therefore, and repent. (Rev 3:15-19).

       The believers being rebuked by Jesus were in a state of denial, giving themselves a positive self-assessment,
not in accord with reality. Jesus wisely provides a diagnosis of their problem before prescribing a course of
treatment. Plus, He reminds them that He rebukes and disciplines them because He loves them. Following Jesus’
example, my diagnosis is that Sue is a professionally weak, backslidden believer who, ironically, is struggling with
both spiritual immaturity and mature legalism and who is blind to the full scope and implications of her spiritual
condition. Sue is in at least a partial state of denial. At times she will acknowledge that she is suffering from a
rejection syndrome, but at other times she will deny the implications of that assessment and her symptoms of that
condition. Full resolution will not be possible until the full implications are accepted and dealt with accordingly.
                                                      Conclusion
       Addressing these overlapping forms of carnality should be standard fare for those specializing in this area of
counseling. The complexity of Sue’s condition by no means makes it insurmountable. These matters can be
corrected with proper teaching and discipline through the Spirit’s enablement. Hopefully, although Sue has rejected
my counsel after insisting that I give it, she will pursue counsel from others who are competent to counsel in these
areas. As seen in this survey, Spirituotherapy and Noutherapy are up to the task, provided they are supplemented
with augmenting material, especially pertaining to soteriology and misthology.224 May the Lord bless Sue in

224
    Adams, for example, does very well in discussing counseling and depending on the Spirit, but he makes some
fundamental mistakes in the fields of soteriology and misthology. As a Calvinist, he conditions salvation from Hell
on perseverance in the faith and, secondarily, on faithfulness (i.e., on the works necessary to keep faith alive).
Theology, 267-270. However, perseverance is a matter of misthology, not soteriology. Then he uses what amounts
to the fruit-proves-root argument to say that our works do not save us but that our final salvation will be according
to our works, which again conditions our final salvation on our works so that in the end we are saved by works in
this mistaken soteriology. I have refuted such arguments elsewhere (see MCMC, WWWS). Indeed, Adams refutes
himself. He correctly acknowledges that the first stage of the sheep-and-goat judgment determines who are the
Christians. The second phase of the judgment factors in works. Therefore, since works are not used in the first
judgment, the first phase is soteriological and works have nothing to do our soteriological destiny. Works are used
only in the second phase of the judgment—to determine the degree of rewards (pp. 302-303)—a point I have made
in my writings (see WWWS). Adams wrongly argues that our works have no merit since we are, supposedly, not
rewarded because of our works but only according to our works (303-304). See my MCMC and TOD for rebuttal
of such misthological nonsense. Other than these pages, though, his book tends to be a very good read, but these
pages are very significant exceptions and underscore the need for soteriological and misthological supplementation.
         As I mentioned previously, while I was at the GFI office, I picked up a couple of more books. I have only
had time to read the first half of one by Turner, and it has been a major disappointment. For example, Turner takes
a soteric view of 1Jn 1:9. His argument is so flawed, it makes me wonder if he has any grasp of Greek. He says,
“The Greek wording indicates that 1 John 1:9 is not a command (imperative) to do anything but is descriptive (a
present subjunctive). The word forgive is a perfect passive verb which indicates that the forgiveness has already
happened and remains in effect. We can rightly restate the verse this way: If we confess our sins he is faithful and
Excursus: Follow-Up Meeting                                                                                   Page 165
resolving her issues and finding competent counsel so that she can quit crying, “Victim,” and start crying, “Victor.”
She does not need victim counseling; she needs victor counseling. May she choose her counselors wisely.

just in having forgiven our sins and having cleansed us from all unrighteousness.” Lee C. Turner, The Grace
Discipleship Course (Sun City, AZ: Grace Discipleship Ministries, 1992), 24; bold black emphasis his. And in the
forward, Solomon says that the material in Turner’s book is consistent with Solomon’s books (p. 4). Both statements
are ridiculous. Turner is teaching an unconditional soteric forgiveness regarding 1Jn 1:9 whereas Solomon teaches
a conditional familial forgiveness. For Solomon to equate the two is like equating a hippopotamus (river horse) with
a regular horse and entering a hippo in a horse race. The Greek structure is that of ean (if) followed by a subjunctive
verb and is thus a third-class conditional sentence. It denotes probable future, and some grammarians would say
that such statements might be translated as when. In that case, the first half of the statement (the protasis) can be
translated as, when we confess. However, this does not change the conditional nature of the sentence. It does not
turn it into a descriptive sentence. It is prescriptive: “Although the statement lies in a conditional clause, it has the
force of a command or obligation: we ought to confess our sins” (NICNT; so also WBC).
       As for the second half of the sentence (the apodosis), it can be in any mood or tense. The mood or tense, or
voice for that matter, do not change the conditional nature of the syntax. A standard example in a Greek text would
be: If he studies, he will learn. See Ray Summers, Essentials of New Testament Greek (Nashville, TN: Broadman
Press, 1950), 110. This cannot be misconstrued to say, If he studies, he will have learned!
       Also, contrary to Turner, forgive and cleanse are not perfect passive subjunctives but aorist active
subjunctives, which destroys his whole argument. The forgiveness and cleansing have not already been granted.
This aorist denotes punctiliar action, not that the action has already been completed independently of the stipulated
confession. PNCT comments are potentially misleading in stating that the aorist subjunctives “portray forgiveness
and purification as complete, rather than ongoing actions.” The statement is true—if you keep in mind that it is
talking about a future forgiveness that is conditioned on confession. When we confess, we are forgiven immediately,
provided we are walking in the light. PNCT goes on to acknowledge the conditional nature of the syntax and clarifies
that the action is not completed until the condition is met: “God is faithful to believers in that he is carrying through
on his commitment to forgive and purify those who confess their sins.” The aorist subjunctive is merely denoting
punctiliar rather than linear action. In that sense, the forgiveness is completed when/as the confession is made.
       Just for curiosity, I did a quick search and found that ean is followed by an aorist subjunctive in 218 verses
in the NT. There are only four verses in the NT in which it is followed by a perfect subjunctive (1Cor 13:2;
1Cor 14:11; 1Tim 3:15; 1Jn 2:29), and in each of those occurrences, it is a perfect form of the verb eido (know). If
you would like to confirm for yourself that forgive and cleanse are active subjunctives, not passive, you can look
up the verse at www.BlueLetterBible.org, then click on the interlinear for TOOLS and select parse for each verb.
       Like GLI, Turner fuses union with communion, making soteric baloney. NAC likewise tries to pull off a
soteric interpretation of 1Jn 1:9 by claiming, “Those who confess their sins by believing in Jesus who have
fellowship with the Father and fulfill the condition for fellowship.” Grammatically, this is only a theoretical
possibility. Wallace notes that we typically falls into one of three categories of usage: “editorial (referring just to
the author [we = me]), exclusive (referring just to the author and his associates [we = me & my associates = us]),
or inclusive (referring to the author, his associates, and the readers [we = us & you].” But to take the we in 1Jn 1:9
as referring to unbelievers would mean “you, but not me” [we = you] “which is highly unlikely and apparently
otherwise unexampled in the NT.” (GGBB, 699, n. 34). Pronounally, such a theoretical we must be twisted to mean
the opposite of what it would normally mean and something it never means in the NT! Contextually, the soteric
interpretation is faulty because you in the epistle is addressed to believers. And the purpose of the epistle is that
these believers may have fellowship with the apostolic circle (1Jn 1:3), thus that the exclusive fellowship that John
and his associates were experiencing might be inclusive of these believers. Soterically, this interpretation is faulty
because confession is associated with walking, not believing (1Jn 1:7,9), and you cannot condition salvation from
Hell on the way a person walks/lives! Biblically, we apparently have no examples in the Bible where anyone is
called upon to confess their sins for a soteric purpose! A search for confess* sin* in the NAS yields these pertinent
results: Lev 5:5; 16:21; Num 5:7; 1Kgs 8:33, 35; 2Chr 6:24, 26; Neh 1:6; 9:2; Ps 32:5; 38:18; Dan 9:20; Mt 3:6;
Mk 1:5; Jam 5:16; 1Jn 1:9. In the OT, confession of sin is expected on the part of God’s people who are already in
a covenant relationship with God. In the NT, John the Baptist was practicing believer’s baptism and confession for
forgiveness (cp. Mt 3:6 and Mk 1:5 with Jn 1:7). Jam 5:16 is likewise talking about believers confessing their sins
so that they might be forgiven in a nonsoteric manner. So, if 1Jn 1:9 is talking about unbelievers confessing in order
to be forgiven soteriologically, it is the single exception in the Bible to a well-established biblical usage otherwise.
Page 166   Degrees of Love and Forgiveness
                            Excursus: The Choice of Anger
                                                   Introduction
      Some counselors say that no one can make you angry; rather, anger is a choice. For example, you could
choose some other emotional response if you wanted. What about those passages that warn us not to provoke the
Lord to anger and which can thus also be translated as do not make the Lord angry? Does this mean that we control
God’s emotions? Not likely. More likely, we can do something that may cause the Lord to choose to get angry.
Whether He gets angry remains His choice. Likewise, unless the biological mechanism of flight-or-fight is
involuntary invoked in our case, thus involuntary invoking fear (in the case of flight) or anger (in the case of fight),
even we humans still have a considerable degree of freedom as to whether we choose to get angry. We certainly
have a choice as to what action we take in response to our anger.
                                              Anger is One Choice
      Augsburger is one of those counselors who believe that no one can make you angry. Moreover, in his book,
Caring Enough to Confront, he allows the possibility that responding in anger and/or humor may be the most loving
and appropriate response(s) that a counselor might be able to take in dealing with an angry counselee:

             A great freedom comes as I own my thoughts, feelings, words and emotions: (1) I become free
      to choose my actions, and (2) I become free to choose my reactions. My actions are mine; your actions
      are yours. I am responsible for my behavior; you are responsible for yours. In reality, no one can
      make another angry. If I become angry at you, I am responsible for that reaction. (I am not
      saying that anger is wrong. It may well be the most appropriate and loving response that I am
      aware of at that moment.) But you do not make me angry. I make me angry at you. It is not the
      only behavior open to me. There is no situation in which anger is the only possible response. If I
      become angry (and I may, it’s acceptable), it’s because I choose to respond with anger. I might
      have chosen kindness, irritation, humor or many other alternatives (if I had been aware of these
      choices). There is no situation that commands us absolutely. For example, I have the choice to
      respond to another’s threat with blind obedience, with silent passivity, with vocal refusal, with
      firm resistance or with anger, if that seems appropriate. When childhood experiences are limited,
      a person may mature with a limited set of behaviors familiar or practiced. Some have only two ways
      of coping with another’s attack—anger or submission. If these are the only ways modeled by the
      parents or the family, they may be the only awareness-choices in the person’s behavioral repertoire.
             If I have grown enough in life so that more than one pattern of behavior is available to me, then
      I can freely select the responses that seem most appropriate to the situation—anger or patience;
      toughness or gentleness; clear confrontation or warm, caring support. I want to be able to
      respond in any of these. I am “response-able” for choosing my responses to you; for the way I react
      to you; for how I see you. And from the way I see you—friendly or hostile, accepting or rejecting,
      welcoming or threatening—emerge my feelings. Feelings are the energies that power the way I choose
      to see you or perceive you. I am responsible for how I see you—and from that, for the way I feel about
      you.
             You cannot make me angry; I choose to be angry. You cannot make me discouraged or
      disgusted or depressed. These are my choices. You cannot make me hate. Hate is a choice.225

      In his book, Anger Is a Choice, LaHaye likewise believes that anger is a choice and gives practical advice as
to how to prove it:

            Studies indicate that the best thing to do with anger is simply to let go of it. Don’t dwell on it.
      To assist in this process, it is helpful to ask yourself, What are you wanting out of your anger? What
      are you trying to accomplish? Another important question to ask yourself is, How long are you going
      to remain angry? This question really puts the brakes on. It helps you to become aware that your anger

225
  David Augsburger, Caring Enough to Confront: How to Understand and Express Your Deepest Feeling Toward
Others, Kindle Edition (Grand Rapids, MI: Revell, 2009), 54-55.
Page 168                                                                               Degrees of Love and Forgiveness
         is a choice. You can choose how long you are going to be angry. What will it be? A few minutes? An
         hour? A day? A week? A month? A year? A lifetime? (P. 162)

                The truth of the matter is that anger in itself is neither good nor bad. It is just anger. It is an
         emotion. The problem is not the experience of feeling the anger—at least at first. The problem with
         anger is the direction in which it leads you—or, better stated, the direction you choose to allow your
         anger to go....
                You see, anger is a choice. You decide which way it will turn. (Pp. 11-12.)

               You see, we do have a choice when it comes to mental health. We can choose to interpret the
         events of life from either a positive viewpoint or a negative viewpoint. (P. 49.)

                You need to realize that you are responsible to choose how you will respond in anger-producing
         situations. No one “makes you angry.” Anger is your response to others’ actions. You make you
         angry. (Pp. 142)

       Basically, it comes down to a choice. You have control over your anger, or your anger has control over you.
You choose which case it will be. In the appropriate situation, anger is a valid choice and shows toughness. A
situation calling for tough love would be one such situation in which anger is not only a valid choice but possibly
the best choice. In fact, some would say that Paul issues an imperatival command in Eph 4:26, instructing us to “be
angry” on occasions, though he limits it by adding, “do not sin,” in doing so, and he limits the duration of its
intensity to nightfall.226 Even then, though, you have a choice as to how to express that anger. Mocking laughter
would be but one of various avenues by which to express that anger depending on the circumstances.227
                                                 Anger Versus Wrath
         LaHaye shares an interesting distinction between anger and wrath:

                Archibald Hart addresses this point in his book Feeling Free when he says, “The need to
         differentiate between anger (the feeling) and hostility/aggression (the behavior arising out of the
         feeling) is even more important when we turn to understanding the New Testament’s approach to the
         problem of anger. The Apostle Paul presents us with what at first seems to be an impossible paradox:
         ‘Be ye angry [orgizo], and sin not; let not the sun go down upon your wrath [parorgismos]’ (Ephesians
         4:26).
                “How can one be angry and sin not? The New English Bible translation makes a little clearer
         what the Apostle Paul was saying and provides us with a very up-to-date understanding not only of
         the nature of anger but also of its solution: ‘If you are angry, do not let anger lead you into sin; do not
         let sunset find you still nursing it.’
                “My understanding of what Paul is saying here is that it is not the anger itself (as feeling) that
         is wrong, but that anger has the potential for leading you into sin. The point is that it is the
         translation or conversion of angry feelings into aggressive and hostile acts that leads us into sin.
         To feel anger, to tell someone that you feel angry, and to talk about your anger are both healthy and
         necessary. As long as you recognize the anger as your own and avoid hurting back the object of
         your anger, you are keeping it as a feeling—and all feelings are legitimate! What you do with
         your feelings may not be, and this is where you can fall into sin!” (P. 98-99.)

        Paul allows orgizo here, but not parorgismos. “There is a noteworthy change in the word used here. It is more
strictly ‘provocation’ (parorgismos; the corresponding verb is used in 6:4)” (TNTC). Parorgismos is the verbal
form of parorgizo which, in turn, is the combination of para (beside) + orgizo (angry). Perhaps, we might think of
parorgismos as (1) being beside oneself with anger; (2) provoking one’s angry feelings; (2a) volitional anger beside
(2b) emotional anger; (3a) animosity or desire for vengeance in addition to (3b) angry feelings (cp. Rom 12:19;
NICNT). The thought is that Paul rules out indulging your anger (WBC). Even so, having lingering feelings of

226
      See Anger Versus Wrath, 168.
227
      See Nouthetic Mocking, 100.
Excursus: The Choice of Anger                                                                              Page 169
anger are not necessarily sin, but one must exercise one’s volition to prevent these feelings from turning to poison.
Bitterness is ruled out and anger (orge) is not to be retained (Eph 4:32). Having a forgiving spirit means that you
relax “your death grip on the pain you feel” (LaHaye, 110). In a section entitled, “How Do I Change My Emotions,”
LaHaye explains, “The will does not have direct control over the emotions; the will has direct control only over the
mind....The only way you can change your emotional feelings is by changing what your mind thinks about” (p. 130).
                                                Sabotage Cycle
      The situation described by LaHaye of Claire is reminiscent of Sue’s situation. For one thing, both had several
marriage proposals but, more importantly, both have a cyclic pattern that sabotages relationships in which they
provoke rejection:

             Claire has had many boyfriends. Several have even proposed marriage. She has been engaged
      three times—but she just can’t seem to find the right guy. Each relationship seems to be more stormy
      than the one before. Why can’t Claire settle down? Why does she go from one crisis to another in her
      relationships?
             Claire has been hurt in her relationships. She has been very disappointed. Her hurt is so great
      that she has determined not to be hurt again. She has designed a program to escape hurt, although she
      may not be consciously aware of it. Her plan is to move in and out of relationships. She begins a
      relationship and progresses to the point where she could be hurt again, as she was in the past. Then
      she sabotages the relationship. She does something, or does not do something, to provoke discord.
      When her partner responds to the discord, she says, “It’s just as I thought. He really doesn’t
      care.” Then the relationship begins to degenerate and fall apart, which confirms for her his lack
      of caring. The cycle keeps repeating itself.
             Claire may eventually get married, but even in marriage she may still keep up the
      “sabotage cycle.” Jay Haley, one of the founders of family therapy, illustrates this point: “When
      one spouse continues the marriage even though treated badly by the other, a compulsory type of
      relationship occurs. If a husband puts up with more than is reasonable from his wife, the wife
      may begin to assume that he must be staying with her because he has to, not because he wants
      to, and the marriage is in difficulty. Sometimes a spouse will appear to test whether he or she is
      really wanted by driving the other to the point of separation. It is as if they say, ‘If my mate will
      put up with anything from me, I am really wanted.’ “However, if the spouse passes the test and
      puts up with impossible behavior, the tester is not reassured about being wanted but becomes
      convinced the spouse is doing so because of an inability to leave. Once this pattern has begun, it
      tends to be self-perpetuating. A wife who believes that her husband stays with her because of his
      own inner desperation rather than because he wants her will dismiss his affectionate approaches
      as mere bribes to stay with her rather than indications of real affection. When she dismisses her
      husband’s affection, he tries even harder to please her and so increases her belief that he stays
      with her out of desperation rather than choice. When the husband can no longer tolerate the
      situation, he may make a move to leave her. The moment he indicates he can do without her, the
      wife begins to feel she may be a voluntary choice and be attracted to him again. However, such
      a wife will then test her husband again by extreme behavior. When he responds permissively she
      again feels he is unable to leave her, and the cycle continues.” (Highlighted emphasis mine, 60-61.)

       Solomon has quoted extensively as describing the self-destructive tendencies of those perceiving reality
through the lens of rejection syndrome. In the following quote, he describes how it tends to operate between two
friends: one friend unconditionally loving the friend caught in the rejection syndrome:

            If the friend is able to weather the negativism, scorn, intolerance, and rejection and continue to
      show unconditional love, the [rejection-syndrome] person will cease his offensive and back off. It is
      almost like a dog chasing a car. If he ever caught it, he wouldn’t know what to do with it! When a
      person has never had love, he doesn’t know what to do with it when he gets it. Therefore, he
      finds some way to get the loving person to reject him so he will exhibit a behavior or emotion
      with which he can cope. Then, he blames the rejection on the other person and everything is
      back to status quo. (Rejection, 40.)
Page 170                                                                            Degrees of Love and Forgiveness
                                              Sympathy Con
      LaHaye describes how those using anger may seek to use it to con us into showing sympathy:

              “When someone is driven to take his hostile feelings out on us, we suffer and we feel
      helpless. If we complain, we are told, ‘But I can’t help it. That’s how I feel about it.’ The speaker
      is giving us a double message: he wants us to know he doesn’t like us, but he doesn’t want us to hold
      him personally responsible. He cons us into sympathizing with his apparent, conscious dilemma.
      What can we do if the other guy is driven by feelings he can’t control?....
              “What we need is a fresh point of view. We need to turn his cause-and-effect relationship upside
      down: instead of supposing that he does what he does because of his feelings, let us imagine that
      he has the feelings he has because of what they allow him to do. This changes our perspective
      and affects our hearing. Rather than searching for outside reasons to explain his actions, we listen
      for the inner origin of his feelings and for what he says and does in the name of these feelings.
              “Take a man who yells. ‘You make me so mad I could strangle you!’ His anger gives him reason
      to do violence, to satisfy an aggressive urge he would never feel justified in following without the
      presence of the anger. And his anger is clearly the fault of the other person. He never says, ‘I make
      me so mad I could strangle you.’ If we listen for ways in which he might be profiting from the emotion,
      or even creating it, we may learn something. When the man says, ‘The more I think about it, the madder
      I get,’ it becomes pretty obvious who is generating the anger. “Nobody can be run by his feelings
      unless, at some level of his being, he wants to be run by his feelings. (Pp. 67-68.)

       By such means, Sue tried to use her hurt feelings to control the conversation. She told me and Dianne that
she did not like us in her six-page letter, but she wanted to blame her uncontrollable emotions for her dislike and
thus not be held accountable for her dislike. She did not want to be held personally responsible for her dislike of us
when she came to our house but wanted our sympathy for her dislike of us. She got no sympathy from me. Then
she blamed me for hurting her feelings when I showed no sympathy. Fine. The difference between our situation and
that described by LaHaye is that I did not feel helpless. Tough love is not helpless.
       When she ranted the next day about me being cruel because I showed her no pity, I saw it from an alternate
point of view, as suggested by LaHaye. She was trying to avoid personal responsibility by attributing her hurt
feelings to me. While what she was saying in effect was, “You hurt my feelings,” what she was saying in reality
was, “I am angry. I make me so mad and hurt that I am going to rant against you all day.” She was attempting to
use her hurt feelings to justify her angry texting. However, she is the one responsible for her hurt anger and for her
ranting texts. She is not run by her angry hurting unless, at some level of her being, she wants to be run by her angry
hurting. She wants to be run by her angry hurting so that she can, in her mind, justify hurting others. But my
response was counterproductive to her attempt. For I responded that if she wanted to hurt my feelings, she was
going to have to try harder. I was not feeling the least bit helpless.
                                                     Conclusion
       Some counselors believe that anger is simply a choice. Even from the get-go, you could have chosen some
other emotion. Others, while taking the same basic outlook, allow for a difference between anger as a
feeling/emotion versus anger as an act/behavior/volition. What we have direct control over is volitional anger, not
necessarily our emotional anger. Nevertheless, we have indirect control over emotional anger by volitionally
determining what we think about or how we think about the situation. Thus, at the very least, we have a volitional-
intellectual-emotional choice over the intensity and duration of our emotional anger. We can certainly choose what
we do in response to our angry feelings. Frequently, those caught in the rejection syndrome have chosen to be angry,
resulting in a self-destructive, sabotaging cycle in which the rejected provoke further rejection. Instead of taking
responsibility for their feelings, they seek sympathy by portraying themselves as victims. Those counseling angry
counselees may need to counter the counselee’s anger with anger of their own. Anger and/or humor may be the
most appropriate and loving response a counselor can give to an angry counselee in certain circumstances.
Responding to Sue in humorous mocking could be perceived as a commendable approach on such an occasion,
perhaps enabling her to come to her senses and escape the snare of the Devil who has her caught in the snare of
rejection syndrome (2Tim 2:26).
                 Excursus: The Royal Court in Ps 45:6-15
                                                     Introduction
       The present book has referred to the Bride as a Goddess. This excursus will supply further argumentation for
that thesis. Whereas LTPW defended the thesis that the Queen/Princess in Ps 45:9-13 refers to the misthological
Bride of Christ, it did not use that passage to defend the Goddess thesis. The present discussion will build upon and
advance the previous discussion in LTPW by following through the implications of the passage to conclude that the
Bride is a Goddess worthy of worship. In LTPW, some suggestions were mentioned as to the other ladies of the
court who accompany her, namely the daughters of the kings, the virgin companions. An additional option, a more
plausible interpretation, will be briefly suggested in the present excursus. Suggestions for other court participants
will be made as well. First, let us recall the passage:
      6
        Your throne, O God [Elohim], is forever and ever. The scepter of Your kingdom is a scepter of
      uprightness; 7 You have loved righteousness and hated wickedness. Therefore God [Elohim],
      your God [Elohim], has anointed You with the oil of gladness beyond Your male companions
      [fellows]; 8 Your robes are all fragrant with myrrh and aloes and cassia. From ivory palaces
      stringed instruments make You glad; 9 daughters of kings are among Your ladies of honor; at
      Your right hand stands the Queen in gold of Ophir. 10 Hear, O Daughter, and consider, and
      incline your ear: forget Your people and Your father’s house, 11 and the King will desire Your
      beauty. Since He is your Lord, bow to Him. 12 The people of Tyre will seek Your favor with gifts,
      the richest of the people. 13 All glorious is the Princess in Her chamber, with robes interwoven
      with gold. 14 In many-colored robes She is led to the King, with her virgin companions following
      behind her. 15 With joy and gladness they are led along as they enter the palace of the King.
      (Ps 45:6-15; TM)

This Psalm is describing a royal wedding. Four principle participants are noted, and one auxiliary group:

      1.   The Groom is referred to as: God [Elohim], King, and Lord.
      2.   The groomsmen are referred to as: His male companions, His fellows.
      3.   The Bride is referred to as: Queen, Daughter, Princess.
      4.   The bridesmaids are referred to as: daughters of kings, His ladies of honor, Her virgin companions.
      •    The wealthy are referred to in an auxiliary capacity as: people of Tyre, the richest people.
                                                     Mystery
       The identity of the Groom and Bride is a biblical mystery. Like any good mystery, discerning their identity
can only be done by discerning the clues and following them to their logical conclusion. Wilkin is on the right track
when he follows these clues and thus concludes that the Groom is Christ and the Bride is the church. His article is
readily available online and need not be repeated here. For present purposes, his summational affirmation that this
OT passage in Ps 45:10-11 is talking about Christ’s love for the church and its mystery nature will suffice.
       Wilkin cites Walvoord’s opinion that the mystery of the church was not partially revealed in the OT. Thus,
the passage would really not be a mystery in Walvoord’s opinion. However, Wilkin correctly counters this
erroneous notion, showing that the mystery of the church and Bride were concealed in the OT and revealed in the
NT. Wilkin provides NT demonstration and then gives this explanation: “A mystery is something which cannot be
understood without further information. If you read a murder mystery, obscure clues are given early on as to who
the murderer is. But those early clues are not sufficient to solve the mystery. Only when sufficient revelation is
given can the mystery be solved.”228 Excellent. Unfortunately, however, Wilkin takes a soteriological view of the
Bride. But once again, we need not detain ourselves with that mistaken assessment in the present discussion, for I
have thoroughly refuted that argument elsewhere.229

                                            Bridesmaids and Groomsmen
       Pertinent to our present study is Wilkin’s statement that Hodges believes the King’s daughters in Ps 45:9
refer to Israel. Due to a possible parallel in Mt 25:1-12 and in harmony with Chafer, my inclination until now has
been to consider these virgin daughters to be faithful Jewish believers during the tribulation (see my discussion of
the Matthean passage in TOD and my citation of Chafer in LTPW). This interpretation has much to commend it.
On the other hand, this Jewish-tribulational-believer interpretation of the King’s daughters is problematic if the
Bride includes OT believers, a proposition which I am only now considering in dialogue with a couple of friends.
In that case, would not the Bride include the faithful Jewish tribulational believers? But this case would nullify this
interpretation of the OT passage since the OT passage distinguishes the Bride from Her companions. In LTPW,
Beirnes is quoted as correctly believing that the Bride in Ps 45 is misthological. He rightly believes the Bride is
only composed of the faithful portion of the NT church, but he then interprets the King’s daughters as referring to
the unfaithful portion of the church. Ordinarily, this interpretation would also be very plausible. Yet, in this context,
this unfaithful-NT-believer interpretation of the King’s daughters is problematic in that unfaithful believers will be
in the outer darkness, not attending the feast.230
       A third interpretation of the King’s daughters that merits consideration observes that the author of Hebrews
applies Ps 45:6-7 to Jesus in contradistinction to angels (Heb 1:8-9). The entire passage in Hebrews is demonstrating
the superiority of Jesus, as God-Man, being superior to angels. Reading Ps 45:7 in the context of Hebrews ch. 1
gives the very strong impression that the King’s fellows/companions/partakers (metochoi) are male angels of the
royal court. The King’s daughters and honorable ladies in Ps 45:9, accordingly, would be female angels. These male
and female gods and goddesses (elohim) of God’s angelic court are being described as attending this wedding with
this imagery.231 Our God is “God [Elohim] of gods and goddesses [elohim]” (Dt 10:17; Ps 136:2; TM). Since faithful
believers will be elevated in nature and position above angelic gods and goddesses, it would make sense to conclude
that the Bride of Christ will be a Goddess (an elohim), not coequal with the Trinity Elohim, of course, but as close
in substance and station as possible. Overcomers will be like Christ, being superior to angelic gods and goddesses
both ontologically and functionally; therefore, overcomers must be considered a higher class of Gods and Goddesses
(elohim), greater in nature and glory, than their angelic counterparts. To distinguish between these two classes of
elohim, the angelic elohim will be designated herein as lower-case gods and goddesses.
                                        Illustration 44. Three-Tiered Elohim

    Even more problematic is the interpretation of Phillips. He believes that the Bride refers soteriologically to all

       Three basic tiers of elohim are discernable in the passage. At the top tier, is the redeemer-creator Elohim, the
triune God. In the passage, two top-tiered Elohim are easily distinguishable: “God [Elohim], your God [Elohim],
has anointed You.” The anointing Elohim would be the Father; the anointed Elohim would be the Son. They are
upper-class Elohim and designated as such herein with the upper case when using the Hebrew word Elohim.
Naturally, the upper case “G” is used for them in English as well as in designating them as God. The passage is
addressing the latter Elohim and describing His wedding.
       The Son is described as the Lord and King of the Bride. This Bride is being welcomed into the divine council
of elohim by virtue of Her marriage to the King. Only overcomers, not all believers, will have their names confessed
by the Son before the angels (Lk 12:8; Rev 3:5). Basically, the Father is performing a wedding for the Son. In
today’s terms, these overcomers are welcomed into the divine council with the Lord responding, “I do,” at the
wedding ceremony. This confession, in the presence of the other divine council members, is the misthological
confession that was promised to those believers who confess Jesus before men on earth and overcome earthly
obstacles. These believers will become Gods and Goddesses: middle-class elohim. The mixture of upper-case
English and lower-case Hebrew is being used to show their middle-class status.
       The groomsmen and bridesmaids, to use today’s terminology, are the lower class elohim (gods and
goddesses) differentiated as such by the lower case for both English and Hebrew. The Bride and bridesmaids have
something in common: They both come from royal families. They are royalties. The bridesmaids are daughters of
kings. The Bride is likewise the daughter of a king, denoted in the text as such by her being called a princess. The
Bride and her virgin companions share the same royal pedigree. By nature, they are royal. They share the same
royal nature; nevertheless, this princess is being elevated in status to that of Queen. This passage is using very
picturesque imagery to describe the shared yet elevated nature and stature of the Bride. Overcomers will share
rulership with other high-ranking angels. Even so, overcomers will outrank these high-ranking angels and be
ontologically and functionally superior to them. Both the Bride and these angels will be spiritual beings. Both will
be godlike. But overcomers will be more godlike than these gods and goddesses. Therefore, overcomers must be
considered Gods and Goddesses.
       “The richest of the people” are mere humans who bring gifts to the Bride in Ps 45:12 and would correspond
to the royalty of Earth bringing riches so that they may enter by the gates into the bridal city and visit these bridal
Gods and Goddesses—elevated divine-human beings in their cooperate heavenly city/home where they dwell with
God (Rev 21:24-26).
                                   First Objection to the Angelic Interpretation
        NINCT counters that angels could not be intended in Ps 45 because the Groom’s companions are inferior to
the Groom. However, that explanation is inferior, for that is the very point being made in both the OT and NT
contexts! The Son is superior to the angels. Likewise, the Groom is naturally superior to His companions at His
own wedding! In contrast, the beauty, not the inferiority, of the Bride is stressed in the OT context. Similarly,
Hebrews assures faithful believers that they will be fellow heirs, that is, co-heirs with Christ (Heb 11:9; cf.
Rom 8:17b). They are not inferior heirs. Granted, the author of Hebrews elsewhere uses metochoi of believers
(Heb 3:1,14; 6:4; 12:8) and calls them sons and brethren (Heb 2:10-11). However, that association with companions
is not intuitive in Heb 1:9, where the NT context is focused on Christ being superior to angels. Also, associating the
Groom’s companions with such believers would be problematic in its corresponding OT background context
because the faithful believers would be the Bride—not the court companions! Hebrews shows that angelic metochoi
are inferior both to Christ and His human co-metochoi. WBC’s comments on Heb 1:9 are decidedly superior to
those of the NINCT in concluding that the companions are angels:

      The preposition παρά [para], taken in the comparative sense (“more than,” “beyond”), reinforces the
      contrast being drawn between Christ and the angels. Although angels participate in the implementation
      of God’s will (cf. vv 7, 14), and in this sense are μέτοχοι [metochoi] of the Son, God has assigned to
      him a superior office. Their function is to serve; his is to rule. They are subject to constant change; he
      does not change, and his rule reflects a commitment to righteousness, which explains why God has
      crowned him with joy (cf. 12:2). Both verses of the extended quotation express the fundamental
      contrast between the divine Son of God and the angels. (Lane, WBC.)
Page 174                                                                           Degrees of Love and Forgiveness
To reinforce WBC’s comments, further note the use of para in this context:

        •       The Son “has become as much better than the angels, as He has inherited a more excellent name than
                [para] they” (Heb 1:4). More than [para] they = more than the angels.
        •       You, Son, are “anointed with the oil of gladness more than [para] Your fellows” (Heb 1:9; TM). More
                than [para] Your fellows = more than the angels.
        •       Man and the son of man are made “for a little while lower than [para] the angels” (Heb 2:7). Lower
                than [para] the angels = lower than the angels.
        •       “But we do see Him who has been made for a little while lower than [para] the angels, namely, Jesus”
                (Heb 2:9). Lower than [para] the angels = lower than the angels.

      Para is used of a comparison of the Son, man, the son of man, and Jesus in comparison with angels in the
book of Hebrews, never in a comparison of Christ with fellow believers. This consistent usage, especially in this
angelic context, argues strongly that the comparison of Christ with His fellows, via para in Heb 1:9, is just one
more comparison of Christ with angels. Christ will be God among gods; the Bride will be Goddess among
goddesses. If you want to be a human-divine god or goddess, then be an overcomer (cp. Ps 82:6 with Lk 6:35).
                                               Worship the Queen
       A very interesting promise is made by Jesus to overcomers in Rev 3:9 that may have some bearing on our
understanding of the Goddess status of the Queen. Many translations render proskuneo in this verse along the lines
of: bow down, prostrate, give homage, do obeisance. The NAS is typical of this approach: “Behold, I will cause
those of the synagogue of Satan, who say that they are Jews, and are not, but lie—behold, I will make them to come
and bow down at your feet, and to know that I have loved you.” Overcomers are promised that this group of human
beings will bow down before them. Many other translations, however, provide a much stronger rendering (ASV;
BBE; ERV; GNV; KJV; NKJ; PNT; RWB; TNT; WEB). The NKJ is typical of this alternate approach: “I will make
them come and worship before your feet, and to know that I have loved you.” This bowing down is a form of
worship. That a group of human beings would be forced to worship glorified overcomers in Revelation is
astonishing since twice John is told not to bow down in worship before an angel:

            •     And I fell at his feet to worship him. And he said to me, “Do not do that; I am a fellow servant of
                  yours and your brethren who hold the testimony of Jesus; worship God. For the testimony of Jesus
                  is the spirit of prophecy.” (Rev 19:10).”
            •     And I, John, am the one who heard and saw these things. And when I heard and saw, I fell down to
                  worship at the feet of the angel who showed me these things. 9 And he said to me, “Do not do that;
                  I am a fellow servant of yours and of your brethren the prophets and of those who heed the words of
                  this book; worship God” (Rev 22:8-9).

        John is instructed to worship God rather than an angel. No surprise there. This is consistent with our
expectations. Since the Ten Commandments, humans have been instructed to worship God alone, no other god or
goddess. Twenty-three of the twenty-four times proskuneo is used in Revelation, NAS renders it consistently as
worship, as we would expect. But in Rev 3:9, it is inconsistent and renders it as bow down. Why? Because there is
a conflict in our expectations. Which expectation should triumph, the expectation to render it as worship or the
expectation that we should only worship God? Could it actually mean worship? NICNT does not believe so: “To
come and worship is simply an Oriental metaphor that in this context involves no more than the acknowledgment
that the church is the object of Christ’s love and that with his return their faith in him will be vindicated.” WBC
likewise concludes: “This is an allusion to Isa 60:14, ‘all who despised you shall bow down at your feet,’ and
perhaps also to Isa 49:23, ‘They shall bow down before you and lick the dust of your feet.’” Phillips suggests other
possible background texts, such as this one: “Joseph found it true in his day. There came a day when the sun and
the moon and the eleven stars bowed down to him” (JPCS). In the case of Joseph, even the members of his family
would be forced to bow down before him and acknowledge his regality. Such commentators believe that simple
bowing is imposed. Barnes (BNNT) hedges his bets:

            So far as the word is concerned, it may refer either to spiritual homage, that is, the worship of
      God; or it may mean respect as shown to superiors. If it is used here in the sense of divine worship
Excursus: The Royal Court in Ps 45:6-15                                                                     Page 175
      properly so called, it means that they would be constrained to come and worship “before them,” or in
      their very presence; if it is used in the more general signification, it means that they would be
      constrained to show them honor and respect. The latter is the probable meaning; that is, that they would
      be constrained to acknowledge that they were the children of God, or that God regarded them with his
      favor. It does not mean necessarily that they would themselves be converted to Christ, but that, as they
      had been accustomed to revile and oppose those who were true Christians, they would be constrained
      to come and render them the respect due to those who were sincerely endeavoring to serve their Maker.

        Commentators who acknowledge that this passage is describing actual worship at the feet of the Bride are on
the right track.232 The fact that this promise is made to overcomers in the book of Revelations suggests that their
opponents will be forced to acknowledge the regality of overcomers in a way that normally is reserved for God
alone and thus is inappropriate to render even to angels. Because overcomers will be a higher order of being than
angels, however, and co-rulers with Christ, it does not appear to be out of character for overcomers to be worshipped
as Gods and Goddesses.
        One day every created being will have to bow at the name of Jesus and acknowledge that He is Lord (Phil
2:9-11). The worship that everyone, including the Bride, will—willingly or unwillingly—render to Jesus will be
shared by Jesus with the Bride. Those who oppose the Bride will be forced to come and bow down at Her feet and
pay Her homage as a divine being. Opponents will be constrained to show these Gods and Goddesses the honor and
respect due to such divine beings, to acknowledge that they are the divine Bride of Christ. This type of worship for
those running the race is inappropriate now, for they are mere mortals (Acts 10:25-26). But once they have won the
race and become a race of immortal Gods and Goddesses, this type of worship will be entirely appropriate for them
then. In fact, at least in certain cases, it will be demanded. Christ will necessarily be worshipped as God. His Bride
will necessarily be worshipped as a Goddess. Christ will demand it. This worship accorded to the Bride at Christ’s
demand is yet more proof of Her superior divine nature and stature among the elohim.
        Joseph was second only to the supreme ruler—Pharaoh—who exalted Joseph to the fullest extent possible.
Pharaoh even forced all others to bow the knee to Joseph (Gen 41:42-43). Joseph’s wife, Asenath, is named in
Scripture (Gen 41:45). She went from being the daughter of a priest to become the wife of the second highest ruler
of the land. Talk about a change in status! Typologically, Asenath represents the Bride of Christ. The focus in
Scripture is on Joseph, as it should be, because he represents Christ, and the typological focus of the wedding in
Ps 45 is accordingly on the Groom who represents Christ. The NT fills in more details about Christ’s Bride. From
it, we learn that Christ will share His nature, glory, and throne with His Bride! Overcomers will sit on His throne
(Rev 3:21) and be co-glorified with Him (Rom 8:17). These facts alone should have led commentators to deduce
that the worships ascribed to overcomers in Rev 3:9 is more than a mere homage to human beings. Overcomers
cannot be co-glorified with Christ if He alone receives divine homage. Overcomers must share in the divine homage
paid to Him if they are to be co-glorified with Him. Some believe that Joseph’s wife sat with him at his side and
thus shared in some degree in his glory. This deduction is very reasonable. What is unreasonable is for commentators
to fail to deduce that because Jesus’ Bride is co-gloried with Him as His co-ruler, she must be co-worshipped with
Him, at least to some degree.
        Pharaoh’s great royal wife/principle wife/queen would appear before crowds and was pictured “enthroned
on a dais decorated with all of Egypt’s enemies bound, literally under the king.”233 Pharaohs made monuments of
their wives for good reason: “There is no question that a revered, respected and occasionally worshipped wife
brought nothing but glory to her husband.”234 To dishonor Pharaoh’s wife would be to dishonor Pharaoh. Since
Joseph was to be treated as Pharaoh, surely Joseph’s wife was to be honored as Pharaoh’s wife. To do otherwise
would be to dishonor Joseph. This is not rocket science. If a king has a queen whom he dearly loves and who sits
with him on his throne, then it does not take a genius to figure out that if you dishonor her, you dishonor him. If he
232
