shares his throne with her, his honor with her, his rulership with her, and expects you to bow at His feet, then he
will expect you to bow before her feet. Would you really want to risk your life by not bowing before her?
Overcomers will be honored with worship; to do otherwise would be a dishonor to Christ. Since overcomers will
be worshipped, we may deduce that they are Gods and Goddesses—a higher rank of divine beings than angelic
gods and goddesses. Inversely, we may appropriately reason that since overcomers are a higher rank of divine
beings than angelic gods and goddesses, overcomers may be worshipped. The probability of the latter deduction
gives way to virtual certainty in the face of the previous deduction. Would you really want to bet your misthological
life by refusing to acknowledge that deduction? Refusing to honor the Queen exegetically, might result in the
displeasure of the King misthologically.
                                  Second Objection to the Angelic Interpretation
       A second objection for identifying the groomsmen and bridesmaids as male and female elohim is the
argument that elohim are all masculine or neuter. If the above argument that overcomers are elohim is accepted,
though, then this second objection is invalid because overcomers will not be neutered in Heaven or all male.
Believers will retain their sexual gender in Heaven. Thus, second-tier elohim will be male and female.
       In his latest book on angels, Heiser addresses what he considers to be a dual myth about angels: (1) they have
wings, and (2) they are female too.235 To refute the notion that angels have wings, he makes a distinction between
various classes of elohim: “In biblical literature, cherubim and seraphim are never sent to people to deliver
messages. That task belongs to angels. Cherubim and seraphim are heavenly throne guardians” (KL 3115).
Although cherubim and seraphim have wings, he would not consider them angels/messengers, thus maintaining
that angels do not have wings. This part of his argument requires that we restrict ourselves to the biblical data and
accept his definition of angels. However, Heiser does not limit his analysis to the biblical data but incorporates other
textual data as well throughout his book. Accordingly, if we are going to appeal to extrabiblical data, then my
positive documentation in MMP4 of female angels and winged angels in various encounters is superior to his weak
argument from silence. For that matter, even Heiser acknowledges that biblical and extrabiblical sources talk of
goddesses (KL 469, 2380).
       Additionally, Heiser acknowledges that some scholars regard the winged women in Zech 5:9 as winged
female angels:
         9
          Then I lifted up my eyes and looked, and there two women were coming out with the wind in their
         wings; and they had wings like the wings of a stork, and they lifted up the ephah between the earth
         and the heavens. 10 And I said to the angel who was speaking with me, “Where are they taking the
         ephah?” 11 Then he said to me, “To build a temple for her in the land of Shinar; and when it is prepared,
         she will be set there on her own pedestal.” (Zech 5:9-11)

He rejects the argument that these winged women are angels, noting that the Hebrew word for women is never used
to describe angels. This is a weak argument from silence. The biblical text does not say that angels are always men
either. Just because female massagers are not explicitly encountered in the Bible does not prove that they do not
exist. Heiser points out that Zechariah is talking to a male angel about these winged women, so the male angel is
distinct from the winged women. So what? Once again, just because God is sending a male messenger on such an
occasion does not prove that there is no such thing as a female angel. The text still presents us with the enigma of
winged women. Heiser concedes:

                One could actually make a more reasoned case for the women being cherubim. In addition
         to their creaturely attribute of wings, Zechariah 5:9 notes, “The wind [rûaḥ] was in their wings.” The
         term rûaḥ is frequently translated “Spirit”/“spirit.” This is the same “locomotion” of the winged
         cherubim in Ezekiel 1:12, 20; 10:17. Like Ezekiel 1, the context is oriented to Babylon, the source of
         cherubim iconography. (KL 3131-3141)

MISH makes this “reasoned case” at length and supports it with multiple strands of collaborating biblical data. Yet,
even though Heiser acknowledges that this is a more reasoned case, he fails to give it serious consideration, brushing
it aside with this false conclusion:

235
      See Heiser, “Angels Have Wings...And They’re Women, Too” (KL 3106).
Excursus: The Royal Court in Ps 45:6-15                                                                       Page 177

             Since Zechariah 5:8–11 cannot validate that angels are winged creatures, the passage also fails
      as evidence that angels can appear as women (biblically speaking, at least). If the women are not
      angels, then Zechariah 5:9 cannot teach us that angels can appear as women.
             The assumption presupposes the idea that angels have gender. They do not—indeed they cannot
      be gendered, since they are spirit beings and gender is a biological attribute. When angels assume
      visible form or flesh to interact with human beings, Scripture always has them male. The flesh they
      assume is gendered because it is flesh, not because that corporality is an intrinsic part of angelic nature.
      (KL 3141; emphasis his.)

       Actually, if the passage is teaching that cherubim can be female, then Heiser’s argument falls flat on its face.
For, in that case, sexuality is an intrinsic part of the nature of cherubim since they were not appearing in a fleshly
form. For that matter, they were not assuming visible form to interact with Zechariah. Rather, the male angel was
showing Zechariah these female cherubim in their intrinsic spiritual form so that Zechariah might make some
spiritual deductions. One such deduction would be that such spirit beings have gender. And if cherubim are not
genderless, then the supposition that angels must be genderless is groundless.
       Other inconsistencies in Heiser’s extended argument are apparent. For example, he notes that 1Cor 11:10
allows the possibility of heavenly angels lusting after human females (KL 2482). Would this not presuppose that
these are male angels in their spiritual state and that sexuality is intrinsic to their angelic nature? After all, the
masculine form of the Greek text describes these angels in the heavenly realm as male. Heisler’s appeal to the
masculine form of the text is a two-edged sword. He tries to use it in Zech 5:10 to bolster his case, but in 1Cor 11:10
it slaughters his case. For a far more extensive interaction and refutation of Heiser and others in this regard, see
MSIH. Adam and Eve were created in the image of the elohim of the divine council, as Heiser acknowledges
regarding Gen 1:27. Yet the text further affirms that Adam and Eve were created as male and female in the image
of the elohim of the divine council. Heiser is oblivious to many of the shortcomings in his own arguments when it
comes to the sexuality of the elohim. Otherwise, his arguments regarding the angels tend to be well reasoned.
However, I am not inclined to make such a thoroughgoing distinction between angels and other supernatural beings
serving God.236 For example, Jesus said that He could call “twelve legions of angels” (Mt 26:53). He was not
236
    As discussed in MSIH, when God created man, He made him “a little lower than the elohim [Heb]” (Ps 8:5).
While the NAS translates this occurrence as God, the NKJ translates it as angels. The latter is undoubtedly correct,
as proven by Heb 2:7, where the NT writer quotes the LXX, which in this verse translates elohim into Greek as
angels. The most natural conclusion is that God created humankind like the angels, not like the sons of God, in
making them male and female in the image of elohim. In other words, God making humankind as male and female
in Gen 1:26 cannot be limited to making mankind like male archangels or even male angels in general; the verse
must be inclusive of female angels. Furthermore, the expression sons of God in Ps 8:5 is not necessarily limited to
male angels. The only place other than Gen 6:1-4 where sons of God (or its semantic equivalent) appears to refer
exclusively to a male is Dan 3:25, and even there it must be inferred as being a theophany of the Lord Jesus as the
Angel of the Lord. Apparently, Gen 6:1-4 is the only place sons of God is used exclusively of male gods. Using this
expression on that occasion is natural because sons of God, like sons, can refer exclusively to males—if the context
warrants that conclusion, which is the case in Gen 6:1-4. Furthermore, both the high status and the divine function
as image bearers are underscored by the phrase sons of God. In that context, high ranking male divinity is having
sexual relations with lower ranking female humanity. Otherwise, when the expression sons of is used in relation to
divinity, it is gender inclusive, just as the expression sons of is normally used generically when used in relation to
humanity: sons of men, sons of my people, sons of your people, sons of their people, sons of his people, sons of the
sojourners, sons of the east, sons of Zion, sons of the desolate one, sons of the married woman, and sons of Israel
(Gen 11:5,15; 23:11, 29:11, 29:1; 32:32; 36:31; Lev 19:18; 20:17; 25:45; Num 22:5; 1Kgs 4:30; 8:39; Job 41:34;
Ps 33:13; 149:2; Is 11:14; 54:1; Eze 3:11; Dan 12:1; Joel 2:23; etc.). For instance, the expression sons of Israel is
used exclusively of Israel’s twelve male sons in certain contexts (cf. Gen 42:5,11,13; 42:32; 45:21; 46:5,8; 49:2;
50:25; Ex 1:1), but the expression is used inclusively in Gen 36:31, where the rulership of various kings over sons
of Israel was not limited to male Jews! Limiting the expression to male dependents would be no more warranted
than limiting it to Israel’s original twelve dependents. When not referring to those specific original twelve
dependents, the expression is presumably, and sometimes explicitly, referring inclusively to the people of Israel
(Ex 1:7,9,12-13; 2:23,25; 3:9-15; 4:29,31; etc.). When God brought the sons of Israel out of the land of Egypt, for
Page 178                                                                           Degrees of Love and Forgiveness
suggesting that He could call 1,200 messengers! Legions is a military term. These angels would act in a military
capacity. Some angels are warrior angels. Possibly these warrior angels are male. But this does not mean that all
angels are male or even that female angels could not function as guardian angels. Also, in the days in which Scripture
was recorded, female testimony was not highly regarded. God sending male messenger angels in that historical
context is to be expected. His doing so does not prove that female angels do not exist.
                                                       Conclusion
       Elohim is a very broad term that can be used to refer to all spiritual beings living in the spiritual realm of
God’s economy and functioning in all levels of God’s bureaucracy. Ps 45:6-15 is best taken as describing the top
three tiers of these elohim participating in a royal wedding taking place between the top two tiers of elohim. Jesus
is marrying His Bride. High ranking angels are attending.
       This Bride will be composed of overcomers (Gods and Goddesses) who will live with the Lord in the
Heavenly City, functioning at the very highest levels of God’s economy and bureaucracy. Third-level elohim (gods
and goddesses) will retain their high-ranking status but be lower in rank, function, and nature than these human-
divine Gods and Goddesses.
       For simplicity’s sake, these third-tiered elohim can simply be referred to as angels. High ranking humans will
be lower in status than the third-tiered elohim. These high-ranking mortal humans are pictured in the Psalm as
merely being wealthy in terms of earthly possessions, not as being royalty. Not all humanity will be higher than the
angels. Overcomers, however, will certainly be higher than the highest angels.
       The intrinsic sexuality of the third-tiered elohim is maintained in the Psalm by picturing them as groomsmen
versus bridesmaids. The intrinsic sexuality of the second-tiered elohim is necessarily inferred from numerous
considerations, only a few of which have been presented herein. MSIH may be consulted for a more exhaustive
defense of that thesis. Even so, enough evidence has been provided in the present book to justify the conclusion that
loving-and-forgiving believers who walk by the Spirit rather than by their emotions, thus keeping their emotions
under control, are well on their way to hearing, “Well done,” and qualifying to be Gods and Goddesses in God’s
new world order.




example, He did not leave the female dependents of Israel behind! Therefore, in the over 600 times this expression
is used, sexually inclusive language vastly dominates the lexical range. Or to mention another common example,
when the Bible says that God knows “the hearts of all the sons of men,” it does not mean to exclude women so as
to imply that women are so mysterious that even God cannot figure them out! Obviously, sons of men refer to all
the inhabitants of the earth (Ps 33:13-14). Psalms repeatedly uses sons of men inclusively as referring to both male
and female humanity. Similarly, Psalms would likewise intend for sons of God and sons of the Most High to be
understood inclusively as referring to both male and female divine beings on the divine council (Ps 29:1; 82:6;
89:6). The Bible uses the term elohim (gods) to refer generically to supernatural gods and goddesses, and ben elohim
(sons of God) can refer to God’s human sons and daughters. In a note, Heiser defers to an article where he gives
this summation: “If we understand elohim to be a term that refers to the realm in which an entity normally resides
(i.e., the spiritual world, as I have done in Chapters 3-4 of The Unseen Realm), then all spirit beings, including the
disembodied human dead (1 Sam. 28:13) are elohim. Those elohim who serve Yahweh in any capacity are part of
his bureaucracy (council) in the broadest sense.” See http://www.moreunseenrealm.com/?page_id=10. Accessed
2/8/2019. The take away is that angels are part of the elohim and part of the divine council in some capacity. Also,
as an aside, note that since the human dead are part of the elohim, the female human dead are part of the elohim.
Some elohim are clearly female.
